# 道德真经集义（明危大有）

**The *Zhenjing* of *Daode* with Collected Meanings**

**Title:** *The Zhenjing of Daode with Collected Meanings*
A compilation by Master Wēi Dàyǒu, in ten volumes.
Original source: *Zhengtong Daozang* (Orthodox Daoist Canon), *Dòngshén* (Cavernous Gods) section, *Yùjué* (Jade Precepts) category.

---

**Preface to the *Zhenjing of Daode***

The *Daode Jing*, both *Shang* (Upper) and *Xia* (Lower) chapters, comprises over five thousand words. Within it are encompassed the Dao of preserving and cultivating life’s essence, and without, the affairs of governance and harmonizing the world—none are omitted. This is the learning of *inner sage, outer king* (nèishèng wàiwáng).

The *Shǐ* (historians) placed it between *Shēn* and *Hán*, and the world thus came to call it the *Yellow Emperor’s Legalism*, which is vastly different from what the Daoist tradition truly holds—this cannot but be flawed.

When the Zhou declined and the Way of the Sage waned, its intent to save the age and benefit the people was embedded within, yet it was not solely about self-cultivation. Cao Pǎn, Marquis Gāo, found that *clearing and stillness, non-action* bore fruit in governance, and his application was not far from the practical principles of the age. How much more so for the teachings of withdrawal from the world, which were thereby greatly expanded!

Some, failing to grasp the profound origins of its meaning, dismiss it outright as empty, bizarre, and fanciful—how could such be true understanding of the Dao?

Over a hundred schools of commentary and exegesis have emerged since antiquity, each holding to their own interpretations. Yet the finest are those whose words and logic are comprehensive and coherent; if meaning is obscured or reasoning flawed, even elegant phrasing is of no worth.

Master Wēi Dàyǒu of Xū River, upright and resolute in his purpose, examined the works of all schools, selecting the most excellent to compile into this collection. He sought to have it engraved and printed for transmission, his diligence commendable. If those who truly savor its meaning seek beyond the words and embody it in body and mind, then the Dao of preserving life’s essence will flourish within, and the affairs of governance and harmony will manifest without.

Is the Dao divided? At his request, I thus presume to inscribe this preface.

In the year *Zhāoyáng*, the *wǔ* year of the *Dīng* cycle, on the *zhōu* day of the twelfth month, the 43rd-generation Celestial Master, Zhang Yǔchū, a disciple of the Three Caverns, respectfully composes this preface.

The *Daode Jing* is the ancestral scripture of our Dao. Its essence is *wuwei* (non-action, naturalness), its practice is *humility, retreat, kindness, and frugality*, and within it are all the methods for cultivation, governance, and harmonization—none are lacking.

The True Person Gé Xuān said: The five thousand words are the very source of *Daode*, encompassing all that is vast without exclusion, all that is minute without omission—the natural scripture of Heaven and humanity.

Master Ni Wénjié said: The five thousand words of Laozi are indeed the essential method for self-cultivation and governance, the finest remedy for saving the age and benefiting the world.

From this, it is clear that it is not merely the ancestral scripture of the Dao—how could the scriptures of the Three Teachings (Daoism, Confucianism, Buddhism) possess any other principle apart from this?

In the present age of sage and enlightened rule, it is especially revered, and a Daoist Bureau has been established to oversee its teachings. Future scholars must first master its doctrines before receiving ordination certificates. Yet alas, the world is in decline, and even its reading is unknown—how much less its meaning?

Master Wēi Dàyǒu, during his leisure from ritual duties, compared the commentaries of various schools, which sometimes differed, sometimes aligned, with merits and flaws alike. Scholars could not help but be confused. Thus, he gathered the interpretations of *Hé Shànggōng* and *Hé Xīnshān* and over ten other schools, selecting those whose elucidations were detailed and clear, whose logic was sound and consistent without strain, compiling them into two volumes—*The Daode Jing with Collected Meanings*—so that future scholars might know the definitive interpretation and avoid confusion from conflicting views.

As for those who, through understanding the meaning, illuminate the scripture, and through the scripture, construct the Dao, finding joy in the practice of *wuwei*—such persons are not absent.

In the year *Dīngmǎo* of the *Hóngwǔ* era, Master Wēi Dàyǒu of Xū River, incense burning, respectfully records this.

**Names of the Commentators in the *Collected Meanings***
*Hé Shànggōng* (Riverbank Master), also known as *Hé Shàng Zhàngrén* (Riverbank Old Man).

---
**The *Daode Jing with Collected Meanings* (Volume One)**
Compiled by **Wēi Dàyǒu of Xū River**

**Chapter One**
*The Dao that can be spoken is not the eternal Dao;
The name that can be named is not the eternal name.*
*The nameless is the origin of Heaven and Earth;
The named is the mother of all things.*

**Commentary by Hé Zhī:**
The Dao is the primal *qi*, formless and void, a chaotic unity of nature. From it arise the two polarities (*èryī*), and all things derive their form. It cannot truly be named, yet we forcibly call it *Dao*. Thus, the opening line rightly begins with *Dao* alone, standing apart—just as in the passage *"The Dao is boundless, yet it acts"* (Chapter 4), where *Dao* is first proclaimed in reverence. By this, we see its supreme height and boundless greatness. Since the Dao establishes its name, both are declared together.

The lines *"The Dao that can be spoken"* and *"The name that can be named"* refer to the Dao’s methods being divided among the world, and names serving as their markers. *"The eternal Dao and eternal name"* speak of the void generating nature spontaneously, nature generating the Dao, and the ineffable beyond naming. If the Dao is truly eternal, then its name is eternal as well. What can be spoken and named are but temporary expressions. To say *"can"* implies both possibility and impossibility—how then can it be eternal? This is the paradox of forced naming. To say *"eternal"* denies both possibility and impossibility—how then can it change? This is the natural way of things.

The Dao is its essence; the name is its function. Both existence (*yǒu*) and nonexistence (*wú*) are names. The Dao originates in nonexistence—vast and empty—thus naming the beginning of Heaven and Earth. Things arise from existence—vibrant and flourishing—thus naming the mother of all things.

**Commentary by Lǐ Zhī:**
The Dao is the void and natural, the eternal Way. Originally, it cannot be spoken of; the Dao that *can* be spoken is not the eternal Dao. The primal *qi* of origins transforms into all heavens, adapting to time and change. The Dao itself is formless; the names we give it are not its true names. Heaven and Earth move and transform, nurturing all things, manifesting in tangible forms and names.

**Commentary by Lín Zhī:**
The Dao cannot be put into words. The moment speech is applied, it falls into the second order of meaning. Thus, one must observe it without desire to grasp its subtlety, and with desire to perceive its boundaries. Though these two approaches differ in name, they both arise from the same source and are called *Xuan* (mysterious).

**Commentary by Hé Zhī (continued):**
*"Constant nonexistence"* is not absolute nonexistence—it has always been so, hence *"constant."* As in *The Liezi*: *"The Great Ease"* saw no *qi*; *"The Great Beginning"* was the start of *qi*; *"The Great Commencement"* was the start of form; *"The Great Purity"* was the start of substance. Following this sequence leads to confusion, hence it is called *"mixed and indistinct."* Observing it in nonexistence reveals its profound mystery.

*"Constant existence"* is not temporary existence—it is always so, hence *"constant."* As in *The Liezi*: *"The Ease transforms into Oneness; Oneness transforms into Seven; Seven transforms into Nine; Nine transforms back into Oneness."* Oneness is the beginning of form. It is also said: *"When there is form, it returns to formlessness."* Observing it in existence reveals its inevitable return. The character *jiào* (徼) in both Laozi and Liezi commentaries means *"return"*—the return to its resting place. As in *The Zhuangzi*: *"To reach the void and fulfill one’s seeking, to roam freely and grasp its end."* Here, the one and the none are observed together, and only what one does not seek to observe is not the Dao.

These two approaches share the same origin but differ in name. The *Daode Jing* states: *"All things under Heaven arise from existence; existence arises from nonexistence."* Thus, their origin is the same, though their names differ. The subtle nonexistence is not nonexistence; the manifest existence is not existence. Hence, *"The eternal can make the nonexistent exist; the nonexistent can make the eternal manifest."* Existence and nonexistence share the same name as *"eternal,"* thus both are called *Xuan*. Existence and nonexistence are one; nonexistence is inherently *Xuan*, and existence is also *Xuan*, hence *"they are the same in mystery."* The *Daode Jing* says: *"All things arise together; I observe their return."* The three observations—*"subtle," "boundaries,"* and *"return"*—later reveal that *"return"* encompasses both *"subtle"* and *"boundaries."*

**Commentary by Lín Zhī (continued):**
The lines *"constant nonexistence, constant existence"* teach the ultimate realization. The word *"desire"* here carries deep meaning—it signifies *"seeking."* One seeks this ultimate truth. Though existence and nonexistence appear as two, they share the same source despite differing names. If one constantly observes both nonexistence and existence, they are both called *Xuan*. *Xuan* is the marvel of creation. By observing thus, Laozi does not exclusively uphold emptiness. If he did, he would not say *"they share the same source"* or *"both are called Xuan."*

**Commentary by Ní Zhī:**
Existence and nonexistence are both eternal aspects of the Dao. *"Constant nonexistence"* is the Dao before manifestation; *"constant existence"* is the Dao in manifestation. The Dao is eternally nonexistent yet eternally existent—existence and nonexistence generate each other and cannot be separated. The Dao’s nonexistence invites observation of its subtlety—the refined essence within. The Dao’s existence invites observation of its boundaries—the coarse manifestations at its edges. The world mistakenly claims Laozi speaks only of nonexistence, yet in this opening chapter, he presents both existence and nonexistence equally, never favoring one over the other. This reveals his intent in composing the text. Many commentators mistakenly split the lines into *"without desire"* and *"with desire."* Desire is the obstruction of the Dao—without it, one may proceed. How could there ever be *"with desire"*?

「○ Master Lü said: To observe the subtle is to gaze within. The subtle is the divine—divine in its transformation of all things, yet expressed through speech. Thus, the practice of inward observation restrains the restless mind, halts the cravings of desire, gathers the divine radiance, consolidates the pure *qi*, and solidifies the embryonic true essence.

The *Dao De Jing* of the Supreme Sage contains the methods of inward and fixed observation, while the *Dao* of immortals and true ones teaches the techniques of *shōushì fǎn tīng* (withdrawing sight and reversing hearing). The *Zhēnjié* states: Inward observation is the root of spiritual immortality. *Jiào* (boundaries) refers to the edges and limits, as well as to the return (to the source).

○ Master Dong said: All that is distant and without ultimate reach must be profound in hue—its essence is the boundless depth of the human heart, where no form is manifest. This is the meaning of *yānmò* (profound silence) in the *Guan Yin Zi*—the unmeasurable, the indivisible. Thus, he speaks of *Tiān* (Heaven), *Mìng* (Fate), *Shén* (Divine), and *Xuán* (Mysterious), all converging in the term *Dao* (Way).

○ Some say: Why do all the schools interpret the passage as *"constant nonexistence"* and *"constant existence with desire"* when you alone take *"constant nonexistence and constant existence"*? I say: The schools’ interpretation is not without logic, but it fails to align with the deeper *Xuán* (Mysterious) meaning of the subsequent text. If existence is paired with desire, how can it be called *Xuán*? Again, those who cling to desire perish themselves—yet to perish is to attain *Xuán*, is this possible?

Another school suggests that *"constant existence with desire"* refers to the practice of skillful application, which is not without brilliance, yet it is also overly strained.

It is better to take *"constant nonexistence and constant existence"* as absolute—simple yet profound in meaning. Thus, I adhere to this reading.

*"Mysterious yet again mysterious—
the gateway to all subtleties."*

○ Master He said: The subtleties are indeed subtle, yet the boundaries are also subtle—hence, *"all subtleties."* The gateway refers to their origin. Many read this chapter only in terms of Heaven and Earth, yet the Sage’s intent is to be grasped from the heart.

○ Master Lü said: The *Xuán* (Mysterious) is the ultimate of subtlety. Again, *Xuán* is Heaven—*Xuán* within *Xuán* is Heaven within Heaven. The *Yùluó Xiāotái* (Jade Tower of Obscurity), the *Yùshān Shàngjīng* (Jade Mountain Upper Palace), in the human body are the Heavenly Valleys and Divine Precincts of the brain and blood. They are the crystalline chamber of vital fluids, the source of the essence, and the dwelling of the hundred spirits—where the Great Lord of Self-Sustaining Life resides. When one observes with the divine essence the Heaven within Heaven, the embryonic immortality forms itself, the Heavenly Gate opens, and all spirits enter and exit through it. Hence, it is called *"the gateway to all subtleties."*

○ Master Chái said: The unfolding and closing of *Yīn* and *Yáng* (the cosmic forces), the emergence and retreat of essence and function—what more does this gate encompass?

○ Master Liú said: *"Mysterious yet again mysterious"* speaks of the origin of all things in their natural state—this is the gateway through which all subtleties pass. This chapter first discusses the *Dao*, then Heaven and Earth, and finally the human heart—it is now complete. Scholars should reflect upon it in silence.

○ Master Dong said: Both principle and phenomenon arise from this gate—hence, *"the gateway to all subtleties."*

**Chapter Two:**
When all under Heaven know beauty as beauty,
then it is ugliness.
When all under Heaven know goodness as goodness,
then it is not goodness.

○ Master He said: The *Dao* is formless yet all-encompassing, a symbol without substance. It molds and transforms, and thus all the beauty of Heaven and Earth is contained within it. The interplay of *Yīn* and *Yáng* (cosmic forces) nurtures virtue and completes nature—those who know this are better off forgetting what they know. How much more so when all under Heaven claim to know it!

Thus, to know beauty as beauty is not as good as to not know it as beauty—this is the ultimate beauty.
When people know what is beautiful, beauty harbors ugliness, for their knowledge is shallow.
To know goodness as goodness is not as good as to not know it as goodness—this is the ultimate goodness.
When people know what is good, goodness harbors not-goodness, for their knowledge is superficial.

○ Master Lü said: The sage who embodies the *Dao* and fulfills virtue blends with all things, equal to their natural state. When all under Heaven know his beauty as beauty, I do not know it as beauty; when they know his goodness as goodness, I do not know it as goodness. In perfect harmony with all things, transcending duality, how could there be beauty and ugliness, goodness and not-goodness?

The world fails to awaken to the truth that things were not always manifest. Lost in private perception, some take appearance, sound, and color as beauty, while others take wealth and honor as goodness. Once the deluded mind arises, they may praise what is ugly or what is not goodness. What is now rejected may later be embraced; what was once approved may now be condemned. Then, what choice is there between beauty and ugliness, goodness and not-goodness?

○ Master Wú said: The names of beauty and ugliness, goodness and not-goodness, arise in relation to one another. Because ugliness exists, beauty exists; because not-goodness exists, goodness exists. When all know this as beauty, then that is ugliness; when all know this as goodness, then that is not-goodness. To erase both, to return them to the void, then neither beauty nor ugliness, goodness nor not-goodness, exists.

○ Master Lín said: This chapter embodies the meaning of *"having yet not dwelling"*—when beauty is known, ugliness arises; when goodness is known, not-goodness arises. Beauty unknown as beauty, goodness unknown as goodness—then there is no ugliness, no not-goodness.

Thus, existence and nonexistence generate each other,
difficulty and ease complete each other,
long and short define each other,
high and low depend on each other,
sounds and responses harmonize each other,
before and after follow each other.

○ Master He said: To speak of nonexistence is to imply existence;
to speak of existence is to imply nonexistence—this is their generation.
Difficulty arises from ease,
ease is shaped by difficulty—this is their completion.
A foot may be short where a cubit is long,
a high bank becomes a valley—this is their interdependence.
The crane’s call is answered by its mate,
as sounds and responses follow one another—
just as before and after, goodness and not-goodness,
beauty and ugliness, stand in constant opposition under Heaven.
The shallow in knowledge and perception,
knowing beauty and goodness,
are not as good as those who do not recognize them—
this is the ultimate goodness and beauty.

○ Master Lǐ said: Existence arises from nonexistence,
nonexistence arises from existence.
Difficulty transforms into ease,
ease transforms into difficulty.
The long brings forth the short,
the short brings forth the long.
Once there is high, there must be low—
sounds must harmonize in response,
before must follow after, after must follow before.
Thus, existence and nonexistence,
difficulty and ease,
are mutually dependent.
Beauty brings forth ugliness,
goodness brings forth not-goodness.»

○ **Master Lü said:** Once the Two Principles [Yin-Yang] are established, all things exist in pairs, thus they generate one another through existence and nonexistence, perfect one another through difficulty and ease, take form through long and short, incline through high and low, harmonize through sound, and follow through before and after—all motion arises from deliberate action.

○ **Master Lin said:** These six sentences all illustrate the meaning of beauty and ugliness, goodness and not-goodness from above.

○ **Ouyang Xiu said:** The following of before and after, the forming of long and short—when extended broadly, this is the principle of all things.

Though the *Dao De Jing* speaks as if of emptiness and nothingness, its teachings on governing the people are profound indeed. Therefore, the Sage acts in non-action and governs through silent instruction.

○ **Master He said:** The Sage knows nonexistence and grasps the pivotal mechanism of knowledge, thus while others engage in affairs, the Sage dwells in non-affairs, finding amusement in the interplay of things—what concern does the Sage have for affairs?

Others speak endlessly, yet the Sage practices speechless speech. Though never uttering words, standing beside others, the Sage transforms them—what concern does the Sage have for speech?

○ **Master Lü said:** Existence and nonexistence, difficulty and ease, long and short, high and low, sound and voice, before and after—though these exist in pairs, they inevitably entail entanglement. Thus, the Sage abandons things and departs from people, standing alone above all things, not pairing with anything. As the *Classic* says: *"Standing alone yet unchanging, nothing can pair with it."* Therefore, the Sage practices non-action, does nothing, forgets speech and forgets images, silently resonating with Nature—how could such a one rely on mere sounds?

The *Zhuangzi* speaks of the utmost action as departing from action, the utmost speech as departing from speech—hence, *"The Sage acts in non-action and governs through silent instruction."*

All things arise yet the Sage does not claim them, they are born yet the Sage does not possess them, the Sage acts yet does not rely on it, completing the work yet does not dwell upon it. Only because the Sage does not dwell upon it does it never depart.

○ **Master He said:** The Sage acts, and all things observe; all things act, yet how could the Sage refuse responsibility for their arising? Though the Sage does not claim them as possession or rely upon them, the work belongs to the Sage—this cannot be rejected, for it is inevitable, not the Sage’s intention.

○ **Master Lin said:** Heaven and Earth generate ten thousand transformations without ceasing—how could they refuse their labor? All things fill Heaven and Earth, yet Heaven and Earth never claim possession. As spring brings life and autumn brings death, yet Nature never boasts of its power—thus, *"They are born yet the Sage does not possess them, acts yet does not rely on it."* The meaning lies in completing the work yet not dwelling upon it, hence these three phrases illuminate it.

*"Action"* is akin to the *Yijing*’s *"Kun"* nurturing all things—this is the meaning of Shun and Yu ruling without claiming ownership. Those who possess what they have cannot truly possess it; those who do not possess what they have can truly possess it. The *Book of Documents* says: *"Having virtue, yet losing it."* Thus, *"Only because the Sage does not dwell upon it does it never depart."*

○ **Master Li said:** Not refusing, not possessing, not relying, not dwelling—each forgets the other, and existence and nonexistence stand annulled. Thus, the True One endures eternally and never departs.

○ **Master Lü said:** The Sage embodies the Dao within, yet his mind labors not; his responses to things are without measure. Thus, all things arise together, and he responds spontaneously—like a valley echoing sound, beauty and ugliness alike come to him without refusal. Hence, *"All things arise together yet the Sage does not refuse."*

Self-formed, self-colored, self-generated, self-transformed—each fulfills its small or great nature, completing its essence. Who possesses it?

The *Guan Yinzi* says: *"Things are not mine, yet I cannot but respond; I am not myself, yet I cannot but nourish."* Thus, responding to things yet never possessing them, nourishing oneself yet never possessing oneself—hence, *"They are born yet the Sage does not possess them."*

Benefiting ten thousand generations yet not claiming it as benevolence, covering Heaven and Earth yet not claiming it as greatness—hence, *"The Sage acts yet does not rely on it."* The work covers the world yet seems not to emerge from the Sage; to acknowledge it would be confusion—hence, *"The work is complete yet the Sage does not dwell upon it."*

If there is dwelling, there is departure—this is true of past and present. Within oneself, there is no dwelling; thus, things cannot displace it.

Only because the Sage does not dwell upon it, does not possess it, does not rely on it—thus, soaring with things above all things yet remaining unchanged, never departing.

○ **Master Wu said:** *"Action"* refers to things about to arise. Not refusing, not possessing, not relying, not dwelling—these are the non-actions of Heaven and Earth. Not departing means enduring forever. Heaven and Earth do not dwell upon completing their work, thus their work endures eternally and never departs.

○ **Master Dong said:** This chapter guides learners to a place where name and trace are both forgotten.

**Chapter Three:**
Do not exalt the worthy, so the people do not contend.
Do not treasure rare goods, so the people do not become thieves.
Do not display what is desirable, so the heart does not become disordered.

○ **Master Li said:** If one does not boast of one’s own virtue, the people become pure. If one does not treasure rare goods, the people become wealthy. If one does not display what is desirable, the heart becomes settled.

○ **Master Wu said:** *"Exalt"* means to honor; *"treasure"* means to value highly; *"display"* is akin to showing. If one does not exalt, does not treasure, it is as if one does not show what is desirable, thus the people’s hearts do not contend. Not becoming thieves is the same as not becoming disordered.

○ **Master Lin said:** *"Exalt"* is the same as *"boast."* If I boast of my virtue, then contention will arise throughout the world. Yu alone did not boast, and thus no one contended with him—this is the meaning.

If I value rare goods as treasures, then all people will have desires to obtain them, and their ruin will lead to theft. Only if one does not show what is desirable will the heart naturally settle.

Therefore, the Sage empties the mind, fills the belly, weakens the will, and strengthens the bones.

○ **Master Li said:** *"Empty the mind"* is to preserve one’s nature. *"Fill the belly"* is to preserve one’s life.

Weakening the will, this is to preserve the spirit. Strengthening the bones, this is to preserve the form.

○ **Master Lü said:** Earlier, it was said that one should not value rare goods, not show what is desirable—this is the same as emptying the mind. Not exalting virtue is the same as weakening the will. If one says only that the mind must be emptied, there is fear of sinking into dull emptiness. If one says only that the will must be weakened, there is fear of failing to stand firm. Therefore, the Sage’s cultivation of the self must include emptying the mind, filling the belly, weakening the will, and strengthening the bones. The empty must be paired with the full, the weak with the strong. A luminous spirit hall clears and brightens; a vacant chamber generates purity—untainted by a single speck of dust—this is emptying the mind. Once emptied, if one cannot inhale and exhale the Great Harmony, chew the essence of *Hāngzǐ*, drink the motherless *Xuānyīng*, or consume the celestial *Tiānguān* of the Great *Dàfán*, how can one fill the belly? Merely guarding the emptiness of nature—how could one then transcend the mortal coil, ascend in broad daylight?

Thus, it is clear that emptying the mind and filling the belly must not be neglected.

To humble oneself and remain lowly, to govern oneself with meekness—this is weakening the will. Though the will is weakened, if one cannot speak of the Dao in action, there will be nothing to establish oneself upon. How then could one achieve what the *Yijing* says: *"Heaven’s movement is vigorous; the superior man strengthens himself with unremitting effort"*? Those who refine internally may twist and turn against the flow, ascending through the Three Passes to nourish the brain—when the brain is full, the bones grow strong. Weakening the will and strengthening the bones must not be neglected—this is also clear.

○ **Master He said:** Emptying the mind is purifying the origin and transcending the root. Filling the belly is accumulating essence and piling up *qi*. Separating the principles of emptiness and fullness, the spirit is preserved. Weakening the will is concentrating *qi* to achieve softness. Strengthening the bones is hearing the Dao and diligently practicing it. Following the yielding of *Kūn* and the vigor of *Qián*, virtue is preserved.

○ **Master Chái said:** If one does not exalt oneself, does not value goods, and has no desires, then the mind naturally becomes empty and luminous, and the belly’s *qi* naturally fills. When the mind is empty of all things, the will becomes gentle and harmonious. When the belly is nourished, the bones grow strong. If one constantly keeps the people without knowledge or desire, then those who are wise dare not act. To act without action is to govern without failing.

○ **Master He said:** When spirit and virtue are both complete, there is naturally a transformation without words, guiding the people without their knowing it. What was once known is now forgotten; what was once desired is now abandoned. As *Zhuangzi* says: *"In harmony with the unknown, virtue does not depart. In harmony with the desireless, this is called pristine simplicity."* When the people return to their natural simplicity, how could they dare to act selfishly or scheme for others? The common people dare not act; the ruler acts without action. The subtle effect of perfect governance leaves no trace to follow.

○ **Master Lin said:** In ancient times, the people were pure and simple, knowing nothing, desiring nothing. Though some among them had clever and scheming minds, even if their knowledge took root, they dared not act upon it. The Sage governs the world in this way. The Sage, in the world, has no place for the mind. In governance, all is done through *wuwei*, and thus nothing remains ungoverned.

○ **Master Lü said:** *Wuwei* is the Dao. The Sage proclaims the Dao from above, and the people respond in harmony from below. Morning and evening, in times of chaos or order, the Dao is the only path. Without the Dao, one does not act; without the Dao, one does not speak. All actions must align with the Dao of *wuwei*. If one acts with deliberate action, the mind labors and the body grows weary, ending in exhaustion. But if one acts through *wuwei*—even if one consumes elixirs of refined essence, breathes celestial *qi*, rinses and swallows cloudy nectar, or refines the mysterious pearl—ascending to the heavens and coiling upon the earth, wielding marvelous transformations, with the body perfectly attuned—then it is said: *"To act without action is to govern without failing."*

○ **Master Dòng said:** This chapter speaks of forgetting exalted virtues, erasing thought, and returning to *wuwei* in harmony with the ultimate truth.

**Chapter Four**
The Dao is boundless yet applied—sometimes it does not fill.
Deep as the source of all things.

○ **Master Wú said:** *"Or"* is a word of doubt, not certainty. The Dao’s essence is empty; those who use the Dao must also remain empty and unfilled. If filled, it is no longer the Dao. *"Deep"* means unfathomable. *"Source"* is the ancestral root, the lineage’s foundation. The Dao is the foundation of all things, hence *"the source of all things."* *"Seems"* is another word of doubt, used in praise.

○ **Master He said:** *"Boundless"* means empty. The Dao’s highest use lies in its emptiness. *Zhuangzi* knew that only when one understands the useless can one speak of use. If filled, the center is not empty, and its use is limited. *"Seems"*—how difficult it is to speak of it directly! This is the utmost emptiness.

○ **Master Lin said:** *"Boundless"* means empty. Though the Dao’s essence is empty, its application is inexhaustible—sometimes filled, sometimes not, adapting to the moment. *"Seems"* is a word of doubt used in praise. *"The source of all things"* is what *Zhuangzi* called the Great Master.

○ **Master Lǐ said:** *"The Dao is boundless yet applied—sometimes it does not fill"* means not being self-satisfied. Those who are not self-satisfied will inevitably benefit, blunting sharpness and resolving conflicts through the emptiness of the center and forgetting the self.

○ **Master Lü said:** Heaven’s first creation is water, whose use lies in the center, hence *"boundless."* The character for *"boundless"* combines water and center, speaking of the true One’s water within *qi*. This is called *"boundless."* Boundless is *qi*; boundless is harmony. The True Person of Jin Hua said: *"Boundless harmony enters the body to vitalize; the spirit of the grain knows the boundless use of ascension to Kunlun."* This is the true speech of the Dao’s boundless application. Yan Junping said: *"Boundless takes emptiness as its dwelling; harmony takes non-action as its home. Using emptiness and non-being, one naturally avoids excess."*

---

Because it does not overflow, it is profound—like the origin of all things.
For the profound is the deepest and unfathomable, the most tranquil and unmoved.
The origin of all things is none other than the Dao itself.
The origin is the ancestor: from One it generates Two, from Two it generates Three, and from Three it generates all things.
Thus, is the Dao not the ancestral origin of all things?

It blunts the sharp, resolves the tangled, harmonizes the radiant, and aligns with the mundane.

Master Wu said: The spoken is always dull, the luminous must dim—
just as the full inevitably overflows.
The Dao does not seek fullness, so it blunts the sharp and does not wish it dull,
it harmonizes the radiant and does not wish it bright—
these four lines speak of the Dao’s non-overflowing use.

Master Lin said:
To blunt the sharp is to smooth away all jaggedness.
To resolve the tangled is to order chaos with serene precision.
To harmonize the radiant is to shine without revealing.
To align with the mundane is to be unstained yet unconcerned with purity.

Master Li said:
To blunt the sharp and resolve the tangled,
to harmonize the radiant and align with the mundane—
this is the emptiness of the center and forgetting the self.

Master Lü said:
To blunt the sharp is to crush the rigid, sharp *qi* of fame and gain,
to dwell in simplicity, revealing no edges.
To resolve the tangled is to untangle the clamor of disturbance,
to remain silent and detached, not engaging with things.
The radiant refers to brilliance; the mundane refers to worldly affairs.
As *Zhuangzi* says: *"The vast and settled emit celestial light."*
The Confucian says: *"Filled and luminous is called great."*
Now, using boundlessness as principle, it is like the lush light of Heaven,
the essence of sun and moon flowing into the limbs—
within, already filled and luminous, so naturally does radiance emerge,
purely manifesting on face and back, differing from common folk.
When boundless harmony is truly realized within,
the six *qi* also manifest externally—
one must only be gentle, yielding, and unassuming,
blending into the world like dust, coexisting without distinction,
otherwise people will point to you as arrogant, eccentric, and unorthodox.

Master He said:
How is it empty? By blunting the sharp, resolving the tangled,
harmonizing the radiant, and aligning with the mundane.
These four vanish without trace—
then the heart is like the Great Void,
the *grain spirit* responds with emptiness,
the Dao acts through boundlessness.
This chapter twice speaks of "as if,"—
these are living phrases, to be savored deeply.

Deep and as if it exists.
Master Wu said:
*Deep* means tranquil stillness—
the Dao’s essence is empty, hence its presence here.
As if it exists, yet nothing truly abides.

This line speaks of the Dao’s emptiness.

Master Lü said:
*Deep* is clear and luminous, as if it exists yet does not—
the form of neither presence nor absence.
Gazing upward, it grows ever higher;
probing it, it grows ever firm;
glancing ahead, it is before you—
suddenly, it is behind.

Master Lin said:
As if present, as if absent,
as if real, as if void—
thus, *deep and as if it exists.*

I do not know whose child I am,
the ancestor before the Sovereign.

Master Wu said:
*"I do not know whose child I am"*—a question.
*"The ancestor before the Sovereign"*—an answer.
*Sovereign* speaks of Heaven’s form; *ancestor* speaks of Heaven’s ruler.
Heaven too arises from the Dao, hence *"the ancestor before the Sovereign."*

Master Li said:
*The ancestor before the Sovereign*—before the *Taiji* divided.

Master Lü said:
The *Huangting Jing* asks, *"Who’s child am I within my body?"*
This is *"I do not know whose child I am."*
It is the self’s true nature, eternally present since antiquity,
aging not in ancient times—hence *"the ancestor before the Sovereign."*

Master Dong said:
This chapter speaks of the wondrous root of boundless emptiness,
and its unfathomable use.

**Chapter Five**
Heaven and Earth are not *ren*, treating all things as straw dogs.
The Sage is not *ren*, treating the people as straw dogs.

Master Wu said:
*Ren* means to cherish with intent.
A straw dog is grass bound into a dog’s shape, used in rain rituals.
Once the ritual ends, it is discarded—no further care is given.
Heaven and Earth cherish nothing, yet allow all things to arise and complete themselves.
The Sage cherishes no one, yet lets the people act and rest as they will.
Thus, the straw dog is the analogy—
for the Sage’s heart is empty, with nothing to cling to.
If the Sage cherished the people, the heart would not be empty.

Master Lin said:
*Nurturing* is *ren*—
Heaven and Earth nurture things yet claim no merit, forgetting themselves with things.
The Sage nourishes the people yet claims no virtue, forgetting himself with the people.
*Not-ren* means not possessing *ren*.
A straw dog is used in sacrifice, then cast aside—
this illustrates forgetfulness without attachment.

The Classic says:
*"The highest virtue lacks virtue, hence it has virtue."*
Not-*ren* is like not possessing virtue.
*Zhuangzi* says: *"Great *ren* is not *ren*."*
And again: *"In the age of perfect virtue, they love one another without calling it *ren*."*
This is the same meaning.

Master Lü said:
Heaven and Earth, without favor, yet great favor arises.
The Sage, not-*ren*, yet great virtue is fulfilled—
because they are without self.
Heaven covers without partiality, Earth bears without favor,
the Sage transforms without attachment—
when it should be born, it is born; when it should die, it dies.
Only the Original *qi* moves—
Heaven, Earth, and the Sage never interfere with their own hearts.
It is like the straw dog.

*Shu* is grass; bound *shu* forms a dog for sacrifice,
honored with reverence when in use—
wrapped in fine silk, adorned with embroidery,
the officiant fasting and solemn in attendance.
But once the ritual ends,
passersby tread upon it, cooks burn it—
merely discarded, of no further use.

「Master Lü said:
At the times of spring’s birth and summer’s growth,
Heaven and Earth seem to bestow boundless love—
yet when autumn and winter come,
and all things wither and fall,
Heaven and Earth cannot save what they once gave life to.

The Sage, in times of pity and nurturing,
appears to act with great compassion—
yet when death arrives,
the Sage cannot rescue what must perish.

It is like honoring the straw dog before sacrifice,
then discarding it after the ritual ends.
Before the sacrifice, it must be revered—
after the sacrifice, it must be cast aside.
Thus, the straw dog serves as a metaphor for the birth and death of all things and people,
showing Heaven, Earth, and the Sage’s lack of partiality.
It is through their *not-ren* that their greatness is revealed.

Master Li said:
Heaven covers without favor, Earth bears without favor,
transforming and nurturing the people and all things—
this can be called the utmost *ren*.
But to say they are not-*ren* is to forget their own nature.

The Sage loves the people and governs the state,
and likewise cultivates the self and preserves life—
between Heaven and Earth, it is akin to the *túshuǐ* [1].

Empty yet unyielding,
moving yet ever producing—
the more one speaks, the more one exhausts,
yet better to hold fast to the center.

Master Wu said:
The *túshuǐ* is an instrument for blowing wind and igniting fire.
Between Heaven and Earth, it is like the *túshuǐ*—
discarding form, embracing the Great Void [2],
containing all things without limit.
The *shuǐ* symbolizes the Original *qi* [3],
its *yīnyūn* [4] and flowing movement.

To say it is "unyielding" means its motion is direct,
and "ever producing" means life is endless.
Only its emptiness as a *tú*,
and its transformative function as a *shuǐ*,
—where transformation is constant—
thus its motion as *shuǐ* and its endless generation of life.

In Heaven and Earth, it is so—
and in humans, it is the same:
when the heart is empty of all things,
and the *qi*’s pathways are unobstructed,
then the Original *qi* moves with regularity,
and the life generated from the void grows ever more abundant.

"Exhausted" means swift depletion,
and "exhaustion" refers to *qi*’s depletion.
If a person speaks too much, their *qi* is consumed,
thus hastening its exhaustion.

Better to keep the heart empty,
firmly guarding the spirit [5],
so that nothing from outside enters,
and nothing from within escapes—
then the void within has no bounds,
and the life generated from it also knows no end.

Master Lin said:
The *túshuǐ* is used and wind arises,
its form though empty, yet when applied, it is unyielding.
Move it, and wind is produced—
the more it moves, the more it yields.
Between Heaven and Earth,
its generation of all things is likewise—
how could it harbor a heart for the wind?

Heaven and Earth, in giving life to all things,
how could they harbor a heart for them?
Thus, this metaphor is used.»

Excessive speech leads to swift depletion—
better to guard the center,
for Heaven and Earth’s Dao cannot be exhausted by words.
Too much speech only hastens one’s own exhaustion.
Better to remain silent and forget speech,
as Confucius said:
*“I wish to speak not at all—
what words could Heaven have?
The four seasons pass in their course,
and all things are born from them.”*
This is the very meaning.

○ Master Li said:
This chapter concludes the meaning of *chōng* (emptiness) and its application.
Between Heaven and Earth, it is like the *túshuǐ*—
its emptiness within,
its unyielding stillness—
no mind.
When moved, it yields ever more—
endless responsiveness to change.
“Better to guard the center”
is nothing but this emptiness within.

○ Master Lǚ’s text records:
The True Person of the Marvelous Dao asked Laozi:
*“What is the most essential chapter for cultivating the Dao and refining the body?”*
Laozi replied:
*“It is ‘Guarding the Center, Embracing the One, Deep Roots, Firm Foundation.’”*
The True Person asked:
*“What is meant by ‘guarding the center’?”*
Laozi said:
*“The center is the Central Palace.
Consider the infant in the womb—
its navel cord connects to the mother’s,
drawing her *qi* in darkness.
When she inhales, it inhales;
when she exhales, it exhales.
For ten months, its *qi* is full, its spirit complete.
At birth, it detaches from the cord—
just as a fruit, once ripe, falls from the tree.
The space three inches deep between the navel is called the Central Palace,
also the Yellow Court.
In men, it is the *Qi Sea*;
in women, the womb.
I was once taught by the Dao Sovereign of the Highest:
‘Diligently guard the center,
never let it stray.
Keep the outer from entering,
the inner from escaping.
Return to the Original Source—
all matters will then be complete.’
‘Diligently guard the center’ means
fixing one’s mind on the Original Sea.
‘Not letting it escape’ means
letting the breath’s coming and going
settle naturally within the Central Palace,
never letting it disperse outward.
‘Returning to the Original Source’ means
the navel is the origin of twelve thousand five hundred breaths,
the root of the *qi* for the five *zàng* and six *fǔ*.
By returning the breath to its source
and guiding it with the spirit,
one makes the breath steady and still.’*

**Chapter Six: The Immortal Valley-Spirit, Called the Mysterious Female**

○ Master Lǚ said:
*“Valley”* refers to Heaven’s Valley,
*“spirit”* to the original spirit of the body.
Heaven’s Valley contains the transformations,
holds the void and emptiness;
Earth’s Valley contains all things,
bears mountains and rivers.
Humans, sharing the same origin as Heaven and Earth,
also possess a Valley.
Within it dwells the True One,
the abode of the original spirit.
Thus, the head has nine palaces,
corresponding to the nine heavens.
The central palace is called the *Níwán* (Mud Ball),
also the Purple Treasury.
Beyond the nine palaces lies another,
with many names:
*Liáotiān* (Boundless Heaven),
*Tiānguān* (Heavenly Pass),
*Yùjīngshān* (Jade Capital Mountain),
*Kūnlún’s Peak*,
*Tàyuān Pool*,
and *Tiāngǔ* (Heavenly Valley)—
the palace where the original spirit resides.
Its emptiness is like a valley,
and the spirit dwells within—
thus it is called the *Valley-Spirit*.
When the spirit remains, life continues;
when it departs, death follows.
Even during the day, as one interacts with things,
or at night, when lost in dreams,
the spirit is unsettled in its dwelling.
If it leaves and does not return,
wandering without return,
then the path of life and death is severed.
Thus, it is said:
Humans cannot live without the spirit’s life,
nor die without the spirit’s death.
Yet the Valley-Spirit does not die—
because of the *Mysterious Female*.

*Xuán* (Mysterious) is the Yang,
Heaven.
*Pìn* (Female) is the Yin,
Earth.
The Mysterious is Heaven’s color—
pure Yang, the void *qi* of emptiness,
light and clear, floating in Heaven.
Its character is composed of *wú* (none) and *huǒ* (fire),
arising from the void,
dwelling in the Mysterious Original Palace—
thus it is called *Mysterious*.
The Female is Earth’s nature—
pure Yin, the substantial *qi* of the valley,
heavy and turbid, sinking into Earth.
Its character is composed of *qì* (breath) and *mǐ* (rice),
arising from substance,
dwelling in the Female Original Treasury—
thus it is called *Female*.
The two *qi* rise and fall between inhalation and exhalation.
There is a method to regulate them:
Reverse their natural flow,
so that Yin does not grow excessive,
and restore their origin,
so that Yang does not weaken.
Transform the having into the void,
the void into having—
using the substantial *qi* of the Original Earth
to ascend and expel it,
and the void *qi* of Heaven’s Original
to descend and return it.
When Heaven’s *qi* returns to the body,
the turbid Yin naturally dissipates,
restoring one to pure Yang.
Thus it is said:
*“Return the Heavenly *qi* above
to govern the nine heavens’ souls.”*
Therefore, the Mysterious Female’s two *qi*
each hold profound meaning—
unless one meets a sage
who transmits oral instructions,
they cannot be known.
If one knows the origin of the two *qi*,
then one knows the mystery of the Mysterious Female.
Nourish the *Tàihé* (Great Harmony),
inherit the *Qi Mother*,
to nourish the spirit.
The spirit depends on its darkness,
returning to the Valley—
thus it does not die.
Is this not the function of the Mysterious Female?
Thus it is said:
*“The Valley-Spirit does not die—
this is called the Mysterious Female.”*
From the *Língshū Shénjǐng Neijing*:
*“Heaven’s Valley, the original spirit—
guard it, and it will remain true.
Above and below, the Mysterious Female—
child and mother in harmony.”*
It is also said:
*“The Mysterious Female is the vessel of Heaven and Earth;
seize it with the spirit,
obtain it with the true—
the way of ascent and descent.”*

○ Master Hé said:
*“Valley”* is emptiness,
the dwelling of the spirit.
From vigor to old age to death,
the spirit scatters from dissipation,
yet people do not know to rest quietly and nourish it.
Even if one sits in repose all day,
if the mind wanders outward,
the Valley will not be empty,
the spirit will not remain—
how then can one avoid death?
Only by seeking the essence in the profound darkness,
seeking things in the hazy uncertainty,
with form and spirit merging perfectly,
uniting with the Dao’s truth,
can one achieve longevity.
*Xuán* is Heaven—emptiness;
*Pìn* is Earth—stillness.
If one accumulates emptiness to communicate with the spirit,
attains stillness to nourish the true,
then the Dao of the Mysterious Female’s immortality is realized.

○ Master Li said:
*“The Valley-Spirit does not die”—
the void and spirit remain unobscured.
*The Mysterious Female*—
one Yin, one Yang.

「The Gate of the Mysterious Female,
this is called the root of Heaven and Earth.
Master Lü said:
Since the Mysterious Female’s essence is defined by ascent and descent,
then the *Gate* refers to the doorway through which the two primal forces—Yin and Yang—
neither emerge nor vanish.

Before the *Dirt Pellet Palace* (泥丸宮) lies the *Bright Hall* (明堂),
which extends downward to the nostrils,
hence the nostrils are the *Gate of the Mysterious Female*.
The nostrils connect the Six Palaces (六府) with the passage of *qi*,
where the light and subtle *qi* interfaces with Heaven,
thus it is called the *Heavenly Root*.

Above the *Mystery of the Primordial Mother* (牝元宮) lies the *Yellow Court* (黃庭),
which ascends through the *Heavy Chamber* (重楼) to the mouth,
hence the mouth is the *Gate of the Mysterious Female*.
The mouth connects the Five Organs (五臟) with the passage of *qi*,
where the heavy and turbid *qi* interfaces with Earth,
thus it is called the *Earthly Root*.

The common folk do not exhaust its source,
nor probe its origin,
and so they merely call the nostrils *Mysterious Female* (玄)
and the mouth *Primordial Mother* (牝).
Then what else could the *Gate of the Mysterious Female* be named?
This is due to their failure to grasp its subtlety.

○ Master He said:
When emptiness is perfected and stillness is steadfastly maintained,
entering through this Gate,
the roots of Heaven and Earth are fully attained.

Heaven bestows *qi*, Earth bestows *jing* (精),
when gathered, they form spirit—
this is their root.
If the root is not plucked,
then life may endure long.
Hence it is said: *"My life lies in me, not in Heaven and Earth,"*
and again: *"I share one breath of *qi* with Heaven and Earth,
governing myself by preserving the root—this is the Way."*

○ Master Ni said:
*Mysterious Female* (玄) is the Yang—
the *Heavenly Stem* (乾).
*Primordial Mother* (牝) is the Yin—
the *Earthly Receptacle* (坤).
The *Gate* is the path of emergence and return,
the opening and closing of *Heavenly Stem* and *Earthly Receptacle*.
The *Root* is like the root of a tree—
the origin of life.

As the *Yì* (易) says:
*"The Great Polarity* (太極) *is the origin of the Two Forces* (兩儀)."
And again:
*"The *Heavenly Stem* and *Earthly Receptacle*—
these are the Gates of the *Yì*."*

○ Master Li said:
*"One closing, one opening—
generating Heaven, generating Earth."*

○ Master Liú said:
*Mysterious Female* (玄) is the Yang,
*Primordial Mother* (牝) is the Yin.
The *Gate* is the keyhole of the two *qi*—
as the previous chapter says: *"the Gate of all wonders,"*
and as the saying goes: *"Who can exit without passing through the door?
Why not follow this Way?"*

Before Heaven and Earth existed,
the *Original Breath* (元氣) was first—
this is called the root of Heaven and Earth.

Continuous and unceasing,
it is used without exhaustion.
Master Lü said:
Continuous and unceasing—
like a thread that never breaks.
When the two *qi* of the Mysterious Female enter their root,
if the closing is too extreme, they lose it to haste;
if they are left unrestrained, they lose it to turbulence—
both are incorrect.
To achieve true continuity without interruption,
as if present yet not present,
as if absent yet not absent,
the true breath arrives at perfect equilibrium,
like the turtle’s retreat,
like the serpent’s repose—
never reaching exhaustion or urgency.

The *Zhuāngzǐ* (莊子) says:
*"The ancient true ones,
their breathing profound and deep,
breathing with the heel of their foot.*
*The *Língshū* (靈樞) of the *Inner Canon* (內經):*
*"Mingled chaos and numinous response,
ascending clouds to refine the root—
continuous and unceasing,
using it—how could it be exhausted?"*

○ Master Hé said:
Continuous and everlasting,
boundless and inexhaustible—
it seems to exist yet does not.
If it were exhausted,
then life would be labored and depleted.
The *Grand Historian* (太史公) said:
*"In all people,
the essence of life is spirit,
the vessel is form.
If spirit is overused, it will be depleted;
if form is overtaxed, it will be impaired.
When form and spirit separate, death ensues."*

○ Master Wú said:
Continuous and unceasing—
long-lasting without interruption.
*Store*—
spirit’s abiding presence.
*Exhaust*—
labor and depletion.
If *qi* is used with ease, it nurtures and grows daily;
if *qi* is used with agitation, it is consumed and depleted.
The statement that spirit endures within,
then *qi* is not exhausted.

○ Master Li said:
*"One closing, one opening—
a ceaseless flow,
no one knows its measure.
In motion and stillness, no confusion,
no strain of effort,
eternally generating and transforming."*

The *Lièzǐ* (列子) fully records this chapter,
and then says: *"The Yellow Emperor’s Book says..."*
According to the *Inner Transmission* (內傳) of *Gěxiān Gōng* (葛仙公),
when the Yellow Emperor was emperor,
the *Laozi* (老君), as *Guǎngchéngzǐ* (廣成子),
explained this scripture to him.
Thus the emperor wrote it down,
quoting this chapter as follows.

Heaven and Earth endure long,
because they can endure long.
Heaven and Earth are able to endure long
because they do not generate themselves—
thus they endure long.

○ Master Hé said:
Heaven and Earth are unified in generating things,
yet do not generate themselves.
The scripture says:
*"Generating yet not possessing,
enduring yet not controlling—
this is called the Mysterious Virtue."*
All speak of Heaven and Earth not generating themselves,
yet being able to endure long—
thus it is said:
*"Ample and profound—
thus they bear things;
High and luminous—
thus they cover things;
Endless and enduring—
thus they complete things."*
The three phrases all concern generating things,
yet do not mention Heaven and Earth generating themselves.

○ Master Li said:
Heaven does not generate Heaven,
Earth does not generate Earth—
thus life does not cease.

○ Master Lín said:
This chapter uses Heaven and Earth to illustrate
the sage’s lack of contrivance.
Heaven and Earth generate all things,
naturally and effortlessly,
without contrivance—
thus ten thousand years are like one day.

Therefore, the sage places his body last yet it is first,
places his body externally yet it endures—
not because he has no selfish desires,
but because he fulfills them.

○ Master Hé said:
The sage aligns with Heaven and Earth without boundary,
accepts the bearing and covering with selflessness,
laboring for the world.
Is it not because Heaven and Earth share the same root with me,
and all things share the same body with me?
All take what is before themselves,
but I alone take what is after—
by taking what is after, others are placed before.
All take what is solid for themselves,
but I alone take what is empty—
my form is placed externally,
while the Way is preserved within.

And again:
*"The sage takes the Dao as his body,
yet has no body—
what selfishness could there be?
This is how he harmonizes with Heaven and Earth without end."*

○ Master Wú said:
*Placing last*—
humility, abasement below.
*Placing first*—
exaltation, elevation above.
*Placing externally*—
clearing stillness, non-action.
*Enduring*—
longevity, abiding in the world.
*Selflessness*—
placing his body last, placing his body externally.
*Fulfilling selfishness*—
his body is first, his body endures.
The sage does not desire to fulfill selfishness,
yet naturally achieves the effects of his body being first and enduring.
If one were to assume that all people have contrivance to fulfill their selfishness,
then it would be possible to speak of it as such.
But the sage’s actions are not contrived.»

○ Master Lǐ said:
The sage does not claim his own sainthood,
thus he aligns with Heaven and Earth in **virtue/power** without end.

○ Master Lín said:
In the sage’s cultivation of the self,
there is no **mind of distinction** between first and last,
no **mind of distinction** between inner and outer,
thus none can surpass him yet he remains ever present.
Hence, he is without selfishness yet achieves the effects of his selfishness.
This is not the selfishness of public and private gain,
for if it were, one could not say he has no **mind of distinction**.
These words reveal Lǎozǐ’s intent to guide people toward goodness,
and when the **Release** [Buddhist schools] expound upon them,
they speak of *true emptiness, real existence*—
*true emptiness* is the meaning of **selflessness**,
*real existence* is the meaning of **achieving the effects of his selfishness**.

○ Master Héshàng Gōng said:
When people speak of selfishness,
they mean to enrich themselves.
The sage, without selfishness, enriches himself naturally,
thus achieving the effects of his selfishness.

---
*Chapter Eight of the Daoist Canon: The Collection of Meanings*
**上善若水** [The highest virtue is like water],
water benefits all things yet does not contend,
dwelling where people deem vile,
thus it is close to the Dao.

○ Master Lín said:
This chapter again uses water to illustrate the meaning of **no mind of distinction**.
**上善** [The highest virtue] refers to the utmost goodness in the world,
its principle being akin to water’s nature.
Water’s goodness lies in its ability to benefit all things—
yet how often does it regard itself as capable?
Following the current without resistance,
it does not contend.
Embracing the low and the moist,
it does not deem what others abhor as vile.
When observed thus, water is near the Dao.
**几** [Close], meaning **near**.

○ Master Wú said:
**上善** [The highest virtue] is the utmost extreme of virtue—
the virtue of those who follow the Dao.
Why is it like water?
Water’s virtue lies in its irrigation and cleansing,
its function of benefiting all things,
yet it does not vie for high or pure places;
instead, it dwells in the vile and lowly spots people despise,
thus it is close to the virtue of those who follow the Dao.
**几** [Close], meaning **near**.

○ Master Lǚ said:
None surpass the **highest virtue** in embodying
the mystery of **Heaven’s One** [天一],
the essence of **Dao’s One** [道一],
or fulfilling the function of **uppermost goodness**.
Water, whether square or round, solid or fluid,
conforms to its natural suitability—
this is how the sage examines the Dao,
models its goodness,
and responds to change with the times,
like water’s nature—
sometimes firm, sometimes yielding,
sometimes square, sometimes round,
neither too much nor too little,
thus it is said: **The highest virtue is like water**.
The **perfected person** [至人] unifies the Dao through One,
and One through the Dao,
so water is used to illustrate the **heart** [mind],
and the **heart** [mind] is used to craft the Dao.
Moreover, the *Shāngtóng Qì* [《参同契》] states:
**"One"** is the **pivot of the Dao** [道枢],
knowing white yet guarding black—
all interpretations by **Péng Zhēn** [彭真人] are of **One**.
Indeed, **golden elixir** [金液] and **jade elixir** [玉液]
are the **pivots of the Dao** for the **golden elixir method** [金丹之道枢],
irrigating the five **zàng organs** [五脏],
nourishing the three **tián fields** [三田],
swilling and descending with ease,
circulating and flowing without obstruction—
the *Huángtíng Jīng* [《黄庭经》] says:
*"Swilling the elixir essence, calamities do not adhere."*
Within **qi** [炁] lies **true One’s water** [真一之水],
and within water lies **true One’s qi** [真一之炁],
thus the **Flowering Pool** [华池] is the source of **uppermost goodness’s** benefits.

Dwelling in the **virtue of goodness** [善地],
the **heart** [mind] is the **virtue of depth** [善渊],
giving is the **virtue of generosity** [善仁],
speech is the **virtue of trustworthiness** [善信],
governance is the **virtue of order** [善治],
action is the **virtue of capability** [善能],
movement is the **virtue of timeliness** [善时].

○ Master Hé said:
The opening line, **"The highest virtue is like water"**,**
does not merely state that water is good;
it earnestly urges all people to emulate it,
to resemble water in possessing all these virtues of the utmost goodness.
**Like water** is **like the Dao**.
Thus, the **virtue of goodness in dwelling** is akin to water’s relation to the earth—
water flows when it meets the earth,
the earth softens when it meets water;
how could it hoard its place of dwelling?
The **virtue of goodness in the heart** is like water’s relation to the depths—
people do not reflect in flowing water,
but in still water;
how could they lose their original mind?
The **virtue of goodness in giving** is like water’s relation to the seven [orifices]—
it measures and pours for others,
how could it indulge others’ desires?
The **virtue of goodness in speech** is like water’s trustworthiness—
it rises and falls with the tides,
how could it go back on its word?
The **virtue of goodness in governance** is like water’s relation to order—
it adjusts like a balance scale,
how could it raise or lower its hand arbitrarily?
The **virtue of goodness in action** is like water’s relation to capability—
it is capable of both square and round,
how could it not exhaust all means and adapt to all changes?
The **virtue of goodness in movement** is like water’s relation to the times—
it halts when still, flows when moving,
how could it not respond to circumstances and follow natural course?

○ Master Lǐ said:
Dwelling in the **virtue of goodness** [善地] is to benefit others.
The **heart** [mind] being the **virtue of depth** [善渊] is to contain all things.
Giving as the **virtue of generosity** [善仁] is to nurture life.
Speech as the **virtue of trustworthiness** [善信] is to respond to all things.
Governance as the **virtue of order** [善治] is to transform all things.
Action as the **virtue of capability** [善能] is to accomplish all things.
Movement as the **virtue of timeliness** [善时] is to follow all things.
Only because it does not contend,
there is no blame against it.

○ Master Lín said:
The seven virtues mentioned above
all describe the goodness of those who follow the Dao,
yet they do not regard themselves as capable—
thus, in the world, they contend with none,
nor do they invite blame or resentment.
This is the meaning of:
*"Only because you do not contend,
no one in the world can contend with you."*

Many commentators take this as the **seven lesser virtues of water**,
so their explanations often strain the meaning—
not the original intent of Lǎozǐ.

○ Master Hé said:
To encompass these several virtues,
to begin with **non-contention** and end with **non-contention**,
is the utmost of goodness.
Who can emulate it?
Thus there is no blame against it.
The unvirtuous invite blame,
and even if virtuous, if they cannot avoid contention,
they too may invite blame.
But if one is both virtuous and without contention,
what blame could there be?
This is why water’s virtue resembles the Dao.

○ Master Lǐ said:
Water embodies **gentleness and harmony**,
dwelling in the low—
benefiting all things yet not contending,
thus there is no blame against it.

○ Master Wú said:
Only the **uppermost goodness** of those who follow the Dao
does not vie for high places yet willingly dwells in the low,
thus people do not blame them.
**尤** [Blame] means **resentment and censure**,
so those who desire to rise above others
cannot avoid a **mind of contention**,
and where there is contention, there is blame.

---
*Chapter Nine of the Daoist Canon: The Collection of Meanings*
**To hold and fill it** [持而盈之],
**is not as good as letting it be** [不如其已].
**To grasp and sharpen it** [揣而锐之],
**cannot be long preserved** [不可长保].

「Master Lín said:
This chapter speaks only of how advancement is not as good as retreat,
thus using *holding and filling* [持而盈之] and *grasping and sharpening* [揣而锐之] as metaphors.
A vessel that is filled to overflowing must spill—
to hold it is difficult,
yet it is easier to preserve what is not filled.
**已** [already/stop] means the intent of not filling it.
**揣** [grasp] means **治** [refine], **锐** [sharp] means **铦** [keen].
To refine a vessel to its utmost keenness and sharpness,
without fail it will break—
thus, it is better to preserve what is not sharpened for long.

○ Master Wú said:
**持** [hold] means to **捧** [carry].
**已** [already/stop] means to **止** [cease].
This chapter states that the Dao does not desire to be filled,
for when filled, it easily leads to overflowing—
yet it is better to cease and avoid filling.
The **锋** [edge] cannot remain sharp,
for when sharpened, it easily leads to **到** [bluntness],
and its sharpness cannot be long preserved.
To fill is also to invite the loss of what is filled—
this is the reason as well.

○ Master Liú said:
To be filled is to be bound to emptiness—
be warned against fullness.
To be sharpened is to be bound to dullness—
be warned against excess.
Gold and jade filling the hall,
no one can guard them.
Wealth and honor with arrogance,
one invites their own downfall.
When one’s achievements are complete and name established,
to retreat is the Dao of Heaven.
Master Lín said:
When wealth and honor reach the point of gold and jade filling the hall,
one cannot long preserve them.
To dwell in the rank of king or noble yet reach arrogance and fullness,
one brings upon oneself the consequences of that.
Thus, those who wish to preserve their achievements
and safeguard their reputation must know to retreat early—
only then does one align with Heaven’s Dao.
**功成** [achievements complete] and **名遂** [reputation established]
are terms that vary with greatness and smallness,
yet one can still preserve them—
hence the words *complete* and *established*.
If one does not know when to stop,
then when is it ever complete?
When is it ever established?
These four characters must be carefully examined.

○ Master Wú said:
In the world, there are those who have gold and jade filling their halls
yet cannot guard them—
why is this?
It is because wealth and honor breed arrogance,
and one invites their own downfall.
Thus, when achievements are complete and reputation established,
to retreat is in accord with Heaven’s Dao—
this is the saying of *not desiring to be filled*.
Gold and jade filling the hall means wealth,
arrogance means those who fill themselves—
their misfortune is self-inflicted, not by others.
Achievements complete and reputation established means honor,
to retreat means to not fill oneself—
Heaven’s Dao is empty yet not filled,
thus the sequence of the four seasons.
Those who achieve success depart.

○ Master Lǐ said:
Achievements complete and reputation established,
yet one retreats—
this is the warning against excess and the encouragement of humility.

○ Master Lǚ said:
*The Book of Changes* says:
*"To know advancement and retreat, survival and perishing,
yet never lose one’s true path—
who but the Sage can do so?"*
Lù Xīshēng said:
To carry a great vessel and be filled to overflowing,
even if one fears it, it is better to cease early.
To occupy a high position and be extreme in one’s conduct,
even if one worries, it is better to withdraw early.
As for following the wanderings of the Red Pine [赤松],
or drifting away in a small boat [扁舟],
this also clearly shows the meaning of *not desiring to be filled*.

○ Master Liú said:
Gold and jade must accumulate—
be warned against greed.
Wealth and honor must lead to licentiousness—
be warned against pride.
Achievements complete and reputation established must bring danger—
be warned against not knowing when to stop.
The words of the Master Lǎo are deeply meant to save people—
not saying that those who abandon worldly affairs and dwell in the mountains and forests
can enter the Dao.
Even while residing in the realm of wealth, honor, achievements, and reputation,
all can be diligently practiced.

---
**Chapter Ten**
To carry the **魂** [hun/soul] and embrace the **一** [One],
can one avoid separation?
To focus **炁** [qi] and attain **柔** [gentleness],
can one be like an infant?
○ Master Hé said:
To embrace the One without separation,
the child can guard the One,
and the One also guards the child.
To focus **炁** [qi] and attain **柔** [gentleness],
to be like an infant—
the scripture says: *"The depth of virtue is compared to an infant,
bones weak, sinews soft, yet grip firm."*
To focus **炁** [qi] is to preserve **纯气** [pure qi].
To attain **柔** [gentleness] is to reach **至和** [ultimate harmony] without deviation.
This is how the Great Person never loses the heart of an infant.

○ Master Lín said:
**载** [carry] is like a cart carrying goods.
An infant has not yet encountered sights or sounds,
thus its **炁** [qi] is focused.
**致** [attain] means **极** [to reach the utmost],
**柔** [gentleness] means **顺** [to comply].
If one can be like an infant in focusing **炁** [qi] and attaining gentleness,
then one can embrace the One.
Hence it is said: *"To focus qi and attain gentleness,
can one be like an infant?"*
This is the Master Lǎo’s rhetorical question.
It means: *"Can people truly do this?"*
The following sentences are all in this vein.

○ Master Lǚ said:
A strong **魂** [hun/soul] is the companion of life,
a robust **魄** [pò/soul] is the companion of death.
The **魂** [hun/soul], belonging to **阳** [yang], delights in clarity and emptiness,
while the **魄** [pò/soul], belonging to **阴** [yin], thrives in agitation and disturbance.
The **魄** [pò/soul] is the **阳** [yang]'s thief,
and moreover, the nature of the **魄** [pò/soul] is to:
indulge people in sensory pleasures,
pursue superficial splendor,
be lost in drowsiness and slumber,
and roam freely—
draining one’s vital essence and **精气** [jing-qi],
leading people to perish,
so that after the body decays,
it may enjoy their sacrifices.
Thus, the Sage governs **神** [spirit] with **炁** [qi],
and restrains **魄** [pò/soul] with the form,
following the method of Earth’s use—
settled, tranquil, and deeply rooted,
steadfast and unmoving.
Though the **阴魄** [yin-pò/soul] may desire to agitate,
stirring one’s thoughts and worries,
can it truly succeed?
Hence it is said: *"To carry the魄 [pò/soul]."*
The original method of carrying it
is to embrace the One without separation.
To embrace the One is like a mirror containing clarity—
how could clarity ever separate from the mirror?
Yet one fears that the carrying may not be thorough,
so though pure and unchanging,
it is called *not two*—
only then can the spirit be complete and unharmed,
the essence be used without depletion,
and never for an instant be separated.
Hence it is said: *"To embrace the One,
can one avoid separation?"*
Moreover, if the spirit is not governed,
the qi will be disordered;
if the spirit is governed,
the qi will not act recklessly.

The Perfect Person excels in regulation and control,
focused on sincerity and purity.
They nourish it with **虚** [emptiness],
and rest it with **踵** [retreat].
Pure, refined, and gentle,
their cultivation of **炁** [qi] is like that of the **灵龟** [spiritual tortoise],
and their nurturing of the spirit is like that of an infant.
An infant embodies the depth of virtue,
dwelling in ignorance of what to do,
moving without knowing where to go—
hence it is said: *"To focus qi and attain gentleness,
can one be like an infant?"*

○ Master Lǐ said:
To carry the **魄** [pò/soul] is like the metaphor of a cart carrying goods.
The **魄** [pò/soul] loves movement,
loves to roam freely,
loves sharpness and vigor—
thus it is called *campaigning魄 [pò/soul]*.
The **魄** [pò/soul] belongs to yin;
when yin is excessive, it harms yang,
and when emotions are excessive, they enslave the nature.
Those who can subdue and restrain it
embrace the One without separation,
attain gentleness without flaw,
and practice *non-action as the female*—
non-knowing makes the yin **魄** [pò/soul] unable to indulge its emotions.
When the **魄** [pò/soul] is subdued and yin is consumed,
the spirit becomes serene and the nature tranquil.»

---

Without constraint, it moves naturally of its own accord,
like returning with treasure-laden hands—
from embracing the One downward,
purely the meaning of carrying the **pò/soul**.

To purify and remove the **玄鉴** [xuánjiàn/mysterious mirror],
can one be without flaw?

○ Master Hé said:
When Guān Yīn Zǐ knows the heart is without substance,
then he knows all things are without substance.
When he knows all things are without substance,
then he knows the Dao is without substance—
thus he does not revere words of mysterious profundity.
Those who ponder mysterious profundity only harm their spirit.
This speaks of washing away the **玄览** [xuánlǎn/mysterious vision],
removing the heart’s blemishes,
thus attaining flawlessness.

○ Master Lǚ said:
Not being swayed by the **pò/soul**,
internally not being controlled by **qi**,
cleansing all thoughts and discarding all desires—
this is called **玄览** [xuánlǎn/mysterious vision].
The **玄览** [xuánlǎn/mysterious vision] observes its subtlety,
yet it is not what the eyes can see.
Excluding the utmost truth,
all else is screened away—
exterior and interior, hollow and clear,
naturally empty and resonant.
The spiritual repository is serene and still—
only then can one attain **玄览** [xuánlǎn/mysterious vision].
Once **玄览** [xuánlǎn/mysterious vision] is attained,
it becomes an obstacle to **玄览** [xuánlǎn/mysterious vision] itself—
when **玄览** [xuánlǎn/mysterious vision] is removed,
then there is no flaw.

○ Master Lǐ said:
Seeing nothing, hearing nothing—
dust cleansed, the mirror bright.

○ Master Dòng said:
If the vision of mysterious profundity is not removed,
it is like untying bonds.
If traces of purification remain,
it is like an obstacle to awakening.
When there is no flaw,
the love of dharma fades and duality is extinguished.

To love the nation and govern the people—
can one practice non-action?

○ Master Hé said:
The Yellow Emperor’s writings on enriching the nation and securing the people
are methods of refining **qi**.
The Sage takes the body as the nation,
the heart as the ruler,
**精氣** [jīngqì/essence-qi] as the people—
when the people are at ease, the nation prospers;
when the people scatter, the nation weakens.
When the heart practices non-action, **qi** harmonizes—
when **qi** harmonizes, all treasures gather.
When the heart acts, **qi** becomes disordered—
when **qi** is disordered, brilliance disperses.

○ Master Lǚ said:
The perfected one governs the body
just as one governs a nation—
loving the people is how one secures the nation,
loving **qi** is how one preserves the body.
Those who love the body well
take the heart as ruler,
blood as ministers,
**qi** as the people.
Unifying nature, nurturing **qi**,
filling every passage,
treasuring and regulating it,
letting it flow naturally—
then what need is there for action?

Governing the nation and the body—
their principles are one and the same.

○ Master Lǐ said:
Unmoving, unshaken—
the Dao flourishes, the times are clear.

The opening and closing of the **天門** [tiānmén/Heaven’s Gate],
can one avoid being **雌** [cí/female]?

○ Master Hé said:
*Zhuangzi* says: *"There is an exit and an entrance,
entering and exiting without seeing its form—
this is called the Heaven’s Gate."*
It also says: *"If the heart rejects this,
the Heaven’s Gate will not open."*
This speaks of the Heaven’s Gate as the **玄門** [xuánmén/mysterious gate].
The transformation of opening and closing—
guarding stillness, guarding softness—
is to guard **雌** [cí/female].
○ Master Lín said:
The Heaven’s Gate is the natural principle between Heaven and Earth.
○ Master Niè said:
The Heaven’s Gate is **乾** [qián/Heaven],
*Yijing* calls it the "door"—
where there is opening, there must be closing,
opening and closing mutually functioning.

Some say: *"能無雌"* [néng wú cí/can one avoid being female]
should be *"能為雌"* [néng wéi cí/can one be female],
yet following the context above—*"能無雌乎"* [néng wú cí hū/can one avoid being female]—
it means one does not desire flaws.
To say *"能無為乎"* [néng wú wéi hū/can one practice non-action]
is to say one does not desire action.
Now to say *"能無雌"* [néng wú cí/can one avoid being female]
is to say one does not desire **雌** [cí/female].
Laozi values softness above all—
yet to say *"無雌"* [wú cí/avoid being female]
is to unify in **雌** [cí/female] while having no **雄** [xióng/male],
meaning one can close but cannot open—
this is not the Heaven’s Gate.
Thus, the meaning of *"無雌"* [wú cí/avoid being female]
is to supplement yin with yang.

Some manuscripts say *"能為雌"* [néng wéi cí/can one be female],
but only Master Niè says *"能無雌"* [néng wú cí/can one avoid being female].
Yet judging from the context above and below,
*"無雌"* [wú cí/avoid being female] aligns more smoothly with the meaning.
Some say: *"雌"* [cí/female] is stillness without stagnation—
thus *"無雌"* [wú cí/avoid being female].
Others say: *"無雌"* [wú cí/avoid being female] means
no yin perversity to intervene—
this also makes sense.

Clear and open in all directions—
can one avoid knowing?

○ Master Hé said:
Clear and open in all directions—
this is awakening, this is illumination.
If one can be as if without knowing,
then one awakens yet does not illuminate,
sees yet remains constantly still—
this is what is called *"無知"* [wú zhī/avoid knowing] as true knowing.

○ Master Lǚ said:
By nature, Heaven is luminous and transparent,
its light extending to the four horizons,
illuminating the eight extremes.
Substantial yet appearing empty,
present yet appearing absent—
its wisdom encompasses all things,
yet returns wisdom to foolishness,
its brightness surpasses the three luminaries,
yet returns to darkness.
Making people act daily without knowing—
thus it is said: *"明四達"* [míng sì dá/clear and open in all directions],
can one avoid knowing?

○ Master Lín said:
Clear and open in all directions—
omniscient, yet taking *"無知"* [wú zhī/avoid knowing] as knowing,
then one can embrace the One.

○ Master Lǐ said:
Excluding sharpness, screening out wisdom,
harmonizing light with dust.

To nurture and store life—
to give birth without possessing,
to act without relying,
to grow without dominating—
this is called **玄德** [xuándé/mysterious virtue].

○ Master Hé said:
If one does not act in two ways toward things,
then one’s ability to give birth to things is unfathomable.
The Sage models himself after Heaven and Earth,
loving and nurturing all things—
to give birth is the Dao,
to store is virtue.
Though giving birth and not giving birth are the same,
though acting and not acting are the same,
though being an instrument of growth and not growing are the same—
the utmost emptiness, the extreme of stillness,
the mysterious virtue of Heaven—
how could one cling to, rely on, or dominate,
and burden the wondrous virtue of the mysterious?

○ Master Lǐ said:
To nurture and store life,
without possessing, without relying, without dominating—
this is to forget one’s own origins.

○ Master Wú said:
To give birth—
though one gives birth, in truth there is no mind in giving birth,
thus it is said: *"不有"* [bù yǒu/not possessing].
To store—
though one acts, in truth there is no mind in acting,
thus it is said: *"不恃"* [bù shì/not relying].
Like an official in charge—
though one governs the people, in truth there is no mind in governing,
thus it is said: *"不宰"* [bù zǎi/not dominating].
This is how one attains the mysterious and unfathomable virtue.

○ Master Lín said:
To nurture and store life—
this speaks of the transformations between creation and nurturing all things.
Creation and nurturing never rely on this as having,
never rely on this as ability—
though one is the longest of all things,
one never has the mind to dominate all things.
Only then can it be called the mysterious and profound virtue.
The intent of this chapter
largely centers on *"無為而為"* [wú wéi ér wéi/non-action as action],
natural and effortless.

---

**Chapter Eleven**
Thirty spokes converge to form a single hub,
yet when the space between them is empty,
the use of the wheel arises.
Mold clay into a vessel,
yet when the space within it is empty,
the use of the vessel arises.
