Pierce doors and windows to make a room,
yet when the space within it is empty,
the use of the room arises.
Thus, having is for utility,
but emptiness is for function.

Master Lǐ said:
When spokes converge with the hub,
it is for the wheel’s practical utility—
this is like gathering all dharmas into the mind,
and the wondrous completeness of the spirit.
Without spokes converging with the hub,
how could it be called a wheel?
Without dharmas converging with the mind,
there is no way for the spirit to pass through.
The hub is hollow within,
so the wheel can move and run.
The mind is hollow within,
so the spirit can transform and adapt.
Thus, emptiness is the true utility,
and utility is the empty function.
Emptiness and utility interpenetrate,
free from obstruction in coming and going—
this is the essence of the previous chapter’s teaching on storing and nurturing the vital essence.
When there is nothing to store,
spokes and hub are both forgotten,
and the wheel returns to non-being—
just as when mind and dharmas are both forgotten,
the spirit returns to emptiness.
The same principle applies to vessels and rooms.

Master Wú said:
Spokes are the rims of the wheel,
the hub is its center,
and emptiness refers to the hollow space within.
*Cao* (埏) means blending clay,
and *zhi* (埴) is the sticky portion of clay.
Having this wheel, this vessel, this room—
all are for the benefit of the world,
so it is said: *"having is for utility."*
Wheels, vessels, and rooms all rely on their inner emptiness for function,
so it is said: *"emptiness is for utility."*
A person’s solid body contains *qi*,
which sustains life—this is called *"utility."*
A hollow mind contains nothing,
yet it is the source of *qi*—this is called *"utility."*
Thus, these two aspects are used as analogies.

Master Lín said:
All three are metaphors—
emptiness is their function,
while wheels, vessels, and rooms are solid utilities.
Yet the very reason they become wheels, vessels, or rooms
lies in their emptiness within.
This metaphor vividly illustrates the character *"wu"* (無),
a stroke of ingenious insight.

Master Lǚ said:
The wheel moves because of its emptiness,
the vessel holds because of its emptiness,
and the room is spacious because of its emptiness.
By elaborating on these three aspects,
it is valued to use things as analogies for oneself.
To preserve what is, one must rely on vessels,
but to preserve what is not, one must rely on the Dao.
The perfected one assumes the form of what is
while refining the spirit of what is not.
Without what is, there is no way to apply its utility,
without what is not, there is no way to achieve its function.
The form is for preserving life’s utility,
while emptiness is for attaining the spirit’s function.
Action is for the utility of things,
non-action is for the utility of function.

Master Hé said:
What is solid cannot be used by what is solid—
this refers to the form.
Yet the form is used by emptiness—
this refers to the spirit.
The hub, with its single hollow aperture,
moves the multitude of thirty spokes—
is it the wheel itself that is the wheel?
No, it is the wheel’s emptiness that makes it a wheel.
Clay is shaped into a vessel,
but if not fired, it quickly decays.
The vessel is indeed formed by blending clay,
yet if its interior is not hollow,
where will things be received?
Is it the vessel itself that is the vessel?
No, it is the vessel’s emptiness that makes it a vessel.
Even if a palace is beautiful,
sealed with walls and barriers,
what use is it?
To enter the hall, one must pass through the door—
is it the room itself that is the room?
No, it is the room’s emptiness that makes it a room.
The body is like a thing,
just as the wheel, vessel, and room are mere physical implements.
The Dao and the mind are like the hub and the window,
possessing the wondrous clarity of spirit.
The *Yì* says:
*"What is above form is called the Dao,
what is below form is called the instrument."*
It is not merely that the Dao precedes instruments,
for even within a single instrument,
the Dao inherently resides.
When people see the instrument’s solid existence,
I see the Dao’s wondrous emptiness—
then, in the midst of boundless utility and empty passage,
there is something that transcends the instrument itself.

---

**Chapter Twelve**
The five colors blind the eyes,
the five tones deaf the ears,
the five flavors dull the palate,
galloping and hunting make the heart mad,
rare treasures obstruct moral conduct.
Master Lǐ said:
This follows upon the previous chapter’s teaching on empty utility—
when one empties utility, one is not dazzled by sounds and colors.
Thus, the five colors blind the eyes,
for colors and sounds, flavors and things
are all *root-dusts* (根尘),
and all worldly people fall victim to their theft.
Only those who follow the Dao do not succumb to deception,
for they act only when it is proper—
then the six thieves become the six passages.

Master Lín said:
*"Blind the eyes"* means to be confused by sight,
*"deaf the ears"* means to be confused by hearing,
*"dull the palate"* means to lose the true taste,
*"make the heart mad"* means instability,
and *"obstruct moral conduct"* means hindering virtue.
These five aspects all involve indulging in externals
while losing the essence of the inner self.

Master Lǚ said:
Gazing at colors, listening to tones, tasting flavors—
all are burdens upon true nature.
Even before an infant knows its own desires,
what is sound or color,
what is flavor or taste?
But once the senses open,
it knows of green and black hues,
of the splendor of brocade and embroidery,
and chases after things in competition,
unable to penetrate the sight of colorless color.
What difference is there between this and blindness?
True sight is not merely external vision,
seeing only what is singular—
only the perfected one examines the eyes,
for they are the sun and moon of *Taiyi* (太乙).
In the midst of stillness and emptiness,
one can gather sight and turn the gaze inward,
with spirit-light radiant and clear—
even in the deepest darkness,
one alone sees the dawn.
How could this be blindness to the five colors?

What are the five tones?
They are *gong, shang, jue, zhi, yu* (宮商角徵羽),
the sounds of Zheng and Wei music,
which make people indulge in pleasure,
disturb their true nature,
and impair their spiritual clarity.
Unable to return to the hearing of soundless sound,
what difference is there between this and deafness?
True hearing is not merely external listening,
but attaining harmony within.
Only the sage discerns the ears,
for they are the *listening gates* of the Celestial Sovereign.
In profound stillness,
one listens with *qi*—
then the celestial resonances hum,
and the celestial melodies echo,
sometimes like the sound of gold and jade,
sometimes like the music of zither and strings.
Once this sound is heard,
even if external winds shake the seas
or thunder splits the mountains,
one does not hear them—
how could this be deafness to the five tones?

The five flavors—what are they? Sour, bitter, sweet, salty, and spicy.

Boiling the *lin* and butchering the *feng*,
feasting on dishes the size of a *fangzhang*,
exhausting extravagance to its utmost—
this makes the tongue indulge in cravings without end,
clouding the spirit and defiling the true self.
Unable to taste the flavorless flavor within,
what difference is there between this and *cang*?

*Cang* is loss—
to taste within is not to crave those external flavors,
but to taste one’s own essence alone.

Only the Perfect One knows the mouth,
for it is the crimson well of the Vermilion Palace.
Thus, they nourish the *hángxiè*,
consume the *yuying*,
drink the *jinyè*,
and sip the *qiánlí*,
to irrigate their celestial root.

Therefore it is said:
*"Plain and flavorless—heaven’s and humanity’s sustenance."*
When the *Zi Dan* presents his dishes—
*yellow* as the *langgāo* and *yùshuāng*—
how could the delicacies of *gāoliáng* ever surpass this?

Hunting is the common practice of the state:
spring searches, summer patrols, autumn hunts, winter expeditions—
never missing its season.

But to indulge in endless revelry,
to pursue ruinous excess,
day and night without rest—
how could one not drown their true nature in deceit?

The Perfect One’s inner heart is serene and unmoved,
cutting off the harm of sounds and profits,
not indulging their nature,
not flaunting their virtue—
*Taiyi* is clear and tranquil,
where could wild emotions arise?

Ah! The *zhàochéng* pearl,
the *liánchéng* jade—
the world’s treasures,
the people’s prized possessions.
If one demands them without restraint,
exceeding their due,
a single misstep
will cause all their conduct to fade and vanish.

The Perfect One regards all things as *chányì*,
sees *Sōngyue* as a mere blemish,
untainted by desire,
self-soaring in the Dao—
even rare treasures could never obstruct their path.

---
*He Shi* says:
*"In the* Zhuangzi*, five colors blind the eyes,
making vision unclear;
five tones deafen the ears,
making hearing dull;
five flavors corrupt the mouth,
making taste* cang.
*Rushing and retreating unsettle the heart,
making nature soar wildly—
this sentiment aligns with this chapter.*

Once there was a man who loved hunting,
claiming it made one forget death—
was this not the madness of the heart?

There was a man who, at dawn, rushed to the market to seize gold.
When the pursuers questioned him,
he said: *"When taking the gold,
I saw only gold,
not people."*
Is this not a treasure that obstructs one’s path?

The chapter’s use of *order* and the *Zhuangzi*’s *make* both describe
how when things grow heavy, the will grows light;
how deep enticement leads to shallow gain—
unaware, enslaved by others,
losing one’s sovereignty.
People do not know the Dao,
and thus fall to this.

*Cang* is *difference*—loss, not *brightness*.

---
*Zhào Shi* says:
If one does not indulge in the pleasures of ears and eyes,
nor the delights of mouth and stomach,
and does not seek gain—
then naturally, there will be no loss.
Thus, the sage nourishes the *fù* rather than the *mù*,
and so discards the former to take the latter.

*Lǐ Shi* says:
To nourish the *fù* rather than the *mù*—
the inner realm does not stray,
the outer realm does not intrude—
retracting sight, returning hearing.

---
*Lín Shi* says:
The *fù* is the inner self,
the *mù* is the outer.
The sage attends to the inner and not the outer,
thus it is said: *discard the former, take the latter.*
The former refers to the five above,
the latter, the Dao.

---
*Lǚ Shi* says:
*Kūn* is the *fù*, bearing all things,
*Lí* is the *mù*, gazing outward.
The *fù* has capacity,
receiving within to nourish its substance.
When *Dao-qi* is full and surging,
the inner self is whole in spirit.

The *mù* perceives the outer,
fixating on all colors,
disturbing the true dwelling.
Thus, the sage cultivates the inner,
not the outer,
seeking within rather than without.

Retracting sight, returning hearing—
restoring the command, returning to origin—
discarding the spirit of the *mù*,
taking the essence of the *fù*—
thus it is said: *discard the former, take the latter.*

---
*He Shi* says:
To indulge the emotions exhausts the spirit,
to adorn the outer harms the inner.
Thus, to nourish the *fù* is to focus solely on the inner,
while to indulge the *mù* is to let the outer dominate—
the eyes see, the heart moves.
Many things can lure one away,
but if forgotten by the *mù*, light overflows without bound.
If the *fù* is filled, there is a master within.
To take this is the Dao;
to discard that is the world.

---
**Chapter Thirteen**
*Honors and humiliations are like sudden shocks,*
*great calamities are like threats to one’s life.*

*Lǚ Shi* says:
Honors and humiliations are the natural course of gain and loss,
while the *yòushēn* is the greatest calamity to true nature.
Honor is followed by humiliation,
humiliation arises from honor.

Fine titles and rich emoluments,
silk robes and jade food—
these are indeed grounds for honor,
yet when they come, one rejoices,
when they depart, one grieves,
and humiliations abound—
how could this not be shocking?
Thus it is said: *honors and humiliations are like sudden shocks.*

The body is not inherently noble;
what is noble is *xìng* and *mìng*.
Humanity is not inherently plagued by calamity;
what is calamitious is *dé* and *shī*.
Thus, in life,
one stumbles upon cold and heat,
entangles in right and wrong,
is constrained by fear and urgency,
struggles in advance and retreat.
If one only worries over gains and losses from external things,
it is as if one abandons the calamities of true nature upon the body—
thus it is said: *great calamities are like threats to one’s life.*

---
*Wú Shi* says:
*Noble* means *weighty*—
wealth and riches are considered great benefits,
yet in truth, they are great calamities.
Thus, those who know the Dao do not prize them,
while those who prize them are ensnared by them—
*ensnared* meaning unable to extricate themselves,
and thus burdened by them.

What is meant by *honors and humiliations are like sudden shocks*?
Honor is lowly—
to receive it is shocking,
to lose it is shocking—
this is called *honors and humiliations are like sudden shocks.*

*Lǚ Shi* says:
What are honors and humiliations?
Are they not the sway of kin and authority,
bending the body and lowering the head,
refining speech and waiting for opportunity,
hoping for praise and elevation,
spirit and breath in harmony,
nursing the remnants of wine,
listening and obeying—
this is called *honor*?

Once honor is received,
one is placed among those who serve,
always fearing its loss—
how could one not be its subordinate?
Thus it is said: *honors and humiliations are like sudden shocks*—
honor is lowly.

Yet honor is the root of humiliation, and fortune the seed of calamity.
When honor descends upon one,
it comes with lofty carriages and four steeds,
towering halls and carved walls,
jades at the girdle and chimes at the belt,
rank elevated to its utmost,
favor extending to one’s descendants—
nothing is withheld.
Once obtained, one rejoices as if struck by shock.

When humiliation arrives,
suddenly grace wanes and favor fades,
slander spreads like seeping moisture,
morning whispers turn to evening dismissal,
deep blame and heavy punishment follow—
calamity comes without turning back.
One loses it abruptly, trembling as if struck by shock.
Thus, one honor and one humiliation,
like shadow following form,
both are causes for shock.

Only the Sage is not enslaved by form.
He regards honor as humiliation,
flees fame and abandons the world,
preserves his body and avoids harm,
equalizes praise and blame,
unifies life and death,
does not scheme externally,
does not fret internally,
does not indulge his will in high rank,
does not conform to vulgarity in poverty.
Looking back on gains and losses of honor and humiliation—
how could they shock him?

**He Shi says:**
What is meant by *honor and humiliation*?
Honor is lowly—
for when fame arises, slander follows—
this is called lowly.
High office brings danger—
this too is lowly.
Honor itself is lowly;
to obtain it is to lose—
for one knows its lowliness without waiting for humiliation.

**Lin Shi says:**
This is the meaning of *fearing gain and fearing loss*.

What is meant by *treating great calamity as one’s own body*?
The reason I have great calamity
is that I have a body.
But when I have no body,
what calamity do I have?
**Lü Shi says:**
What the world values as *treating great calamity as one’s own body*
is this: when a person is born,
he shares the same root as Heaven and Earth,
and all things draw sustenance from it.
The petty man pursues profit,
the scholar pursues fame—
though their power and influence differ,
to have a body makes them one in this regard.
The world says that without a body, there is no calamity—
this is to lose the Master’s intent.

How could the Perfect Man’s *great calamity of having a body*
mean abandoning form and losing his physical self,
sinking into dull emptiness?
It means he is ensnared by things and fixated on self,
not embodying the Dao.

Thus, his heart is like dead ashes,
his form like withered wood—
though honored, he is not humiliated;
though humiliated, he has no calamity.
He transcends his body as if it were not his,
forgets his mind as if it were not his,
discards illusion and attains the eternal truth,
abandons awareness and sits in forgetfulness.
Then the jade talisman preserves his spirit,
the golden elixir refines his form—
form and spirit both attain marvelous harmony,
merging with the Dao’s true essence.
No longer afflicted by having a body,
how could he suffer great calamity?

**He Shi says:**
What is meant by *treating great calamity as one’s own body*?
*Noble* means *weighty*—
the body is a great calamity,
worthy of weighty regard, not to be treated lightly.
The body is small, yet the calamity is great;
when the body is forgotten, calamity ceases—
this is clear.
Thus it is said:
Ears and eyes, sounds and colors—
these are the child’s lingering debts.
The nose and mouth crave fragrance and flavor—
these are sources of resentment.
The body is the root of vexation:
pain, itching, cold, heat—
because I am bound to the body, I know great calamity.
Yet once formed, I cannot suddenly make it cease to be a source of calamity—
what benefit is there?
The Sage summarizes this in one phrase:
*Transcend the body while the body exists.*
Why not consider that form is not mine to possess,
that name is not mine to retain?
What I am born from
comes from the natural void—
structured by *qi* and taking shape.
Thus, though I receive form,
the form is not mine.
Form is lent to me as form,
shown to me as appearance.
Therefore, those who attain the Dao
transcend the physical frame to conquer themselves,
transforming their essence and cultivating purity,
diligently practicing the Dao of boundless formlessness—
then what others fear,
I have no fear of.

Moreover, the body is indeed the root of calamity,
yet having a body means having things—
this is what invites calamity.
**Liezi said:**
*The body is not mine to possess.*
Though born, I must preserve it—
things are not mine to possess,
though I have them, I must abandon them.
The body is the master of life,
things are the master of nourishment—
though I preserve my body,
I cannot possess it;
though I do not abandon things,
I cannot possess them.
To possess things and the body
is to arrogantly claim Heaven’s body and Heaven’s things—
how could such a one be a Sage?

To make Heaven’s body and Heaven’s things public—
this means not claiming them as one’s own,
but offering them to the world—
then the body can be forgotten,
and calamity can be forgotten.

Thus, to honor the body as the world’s
is to entrust it to the world.
To love the body as the world’s
is to commit it to the world.
**Wu Shi says:**
The Son of Heaven’s eminence,
the wealth of the Four Seas—
all stem from treating the body as the world’s.
Those who know the Dao
cherish and treasure this body,
not daring to use it for the world’s sake,
preferring to have no world rather than lightly use their body.
Only thus can they be entrusted with the world.
*Entrust* is like entrusting a command over a hundred *li*;
*commit* is like committing a six-foot orphan.
Shun and Yu held the world yet did not possess it—
thus the succession of Tang and Yu.
Those who take honor and humiliation as glory,
who value great calamity as great profit—
these are vulgar men.
How could one entrust the world to them?

**He Shi says:**
If one values and loves this body more than the world itself,
such a person will not be moved by the world,
and thus can be entrusted with its care.
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*The true Dao governs the body;
its coarse husk governs the world.*
Yet today’s common scholars
often endanger their bodies and abandon life for things—
is this not tragic?
This is like the parable of the *hidden pearl used to strike a sparrow.*

**Lin Shi says:**
The words *entrust* and *commit*
imply the world is not possessed.

**Dong Shi says:**
This chapter clarifies the abandonment of false emotions
and restores true nature.
It says:
Abandon honor, and humiliation will not reach you;
forget the body, and calamity will not come.
The world is a great thing—
yet it is not a burden.
What of lesser things?

**Chapter Fourteen.**

The unseen is called *yí* (平),
the unheard is called *xī* (希),
the untouchable is called *wēi* (微).
These three cannot be grasped or questioned,
thus they merge into one.

**Lin Shi says:**
This chapter describes the Dao’s unapproachable nature.
*Yí* means level—flat, without trace, hence unseen.
*Xī* and *wēi* imply what is faint and invisible.
The three terms, though distinct in name,
all depict the Dao’s inability to be heard, seen, or seized.
*Bó* means "to grasp."
These three—*yí, xī, wēi*—
though named, cannot be distinguished,
for they are all the Dao itself.

**Wu Shi says:**
*Yí* means level and flat—
level, it vanishes without trace, hence unseen.
*Xī* means sparse—
sparse, it leaves gaps with no sound, hence unheard.
*Wēi* means faint—
faint, it is dim and boundless, hence untouchable.
Though one might strive to describe *yí, xī, wēi*,
the words fail—
thus they merge indistinguishably into one.

**Lü Shi says:**
The colorless color—
even if Zhīzhū or Zhīyǔ sharpened their eyes at noon,
rubbed their brows, and strained to gaze,
they would see nothing.
The soundless sound—
even if Zhīyú or Shīguāng cupped their ears at night,
leaned forward, and listened intently,
they would hear nothing.
The shapeless form—
even if Guìyù or Wǔdīng strained their strength,
clenched their fists, and lunged with all their might,
they could not grasp it.
Eyes find no light to illuminate,
ears find no sound to perceive,
hands find no form to seize—
thus it is called *yí*, *xī*, *wēi*.
Though named differently,
in essence, they are one.
Like all sounds merging into one wind yet differing in tone,
or seven orifices sharing the same *qi* yet serving distinct functions—
these three cannot be grasped or questioned,
thus they merge into one.
Yet when considered together,
they blend seamlessly as one,
so that though sight, hearing, and touch are applied,
they remain indistinguishable.

Its upper reaches are not bright,
its lower depths are not dark.
Endless and nameless,
it returns to nothing—
this is called the form of no-form,
the image of no-image—
this is called *hūhuǎng* (惚恍).

**Lin Shi says:**
"Not bright" means not illuminated,
"Not dark" means not obscured.
"Upper" and "lower" are not to be confined—
only that this Dao is neither seen above nor below.
*Shéngshéng* (绳绳) means "prolific."
Prolific yet nameless,
its end returns to nothing—
thus it is the form of no-form,
the image of no-image—
also *hūhuǎng*.

**Wu Shi says:**
"Upper" and "lower" refer to what the *Yijing* calls
"form above form" and "form below form."
*Shéngshéng* means "continuous without interruption."
*Fù* means "to return."
"Without substance" refers to the Dao—
returning to its origin,
it aligns with the Dao of nothingness,
as in *Zhuangzi*’s "virtue reaching back to its beginning."
The Dao has no substance,
yet its form and image are not like those of things—
thus "form of no-form," "image of no-image."
It seems both present and absent—
hence *hūhuǎng*.

**Lü Shi says:**
All shaped and colored things have limits,
but the Dao precedes Heaven and Earth,
endures from ancient to modern times.
It disperses as *chōngqì* (冲气),
spreads as the *tàixū* (太虚),
coexists with the Two Principles (yin-yang),
yet its brilliance does not dazzle—
suddenly, it rests beneath the nine depths,
hidden like tiles and rubble,
yet its obscurity is not dim.
It cycles endlessly, unbroken,
continuous without end,
nameless, untraceable,
its virtue complete yet unseen.
Though one might try to liken it to things,
it cannot be—
thus "its upper reaches are not bright,
its lower depths are not dark."
Endless and nameless,
it returns to nothing—
vast yet solemn,
deep yet unfathomable,
beyond comparison or measure.
Within the formless,
there is the form of no-form;
within the nothing,
there is the image of no-image.
If one says it has form,
what form does it truly have?
If one says it lacks image,
what image does it truly lack?
Thus it is said:
Mysterious presence without presence,
true absence without absence—
*hūhuǎng*, ethereal and misty,
indeterminate between presence and absence—
hence "form of no-form," "image of no-image," *hūhuǎng*.

Approach it, and you see no head;
follow it, and you see no tail.
Hold fast to the ancient Dao
to govern the present.

「To know the ancient beginning is called *Dao*’s principle.

Lin Shi says: To approach it and see no head is its beginningless nature. To follow it and see no tail is its endless nature. To hold fast to the ancient *Dao* is to speak of its emergence from nothingness. By grasping its original nothingness to govern the present, one may then comprehend the *Dao* of the ancient beginning.

*Ji* means "principle and order." *Dao ji* is akin to "human principle," or the fundamental *Way* of kingship.

○ Wu Shi says: Because it is *hūhuǎng* and indeterminate, neither approaching nor following can reveal its limits. The ancient is what precedes, the present is what follows. All things originate from the *Dao*, hence "to hold fast to the ancient *Dao* to govern the present."

The ancient beginning is the *Dao*.

○ He Shi says: From "to hold fast to the ancient *Dao*" onward, this passage exhausts the theme of "no beginning, no end," yet introduces the character "have" (*you*), thereby showing that though myriad transformations differ, one may govern all by holding fast to the One. The *Dao* governs and the king rules—great measures are not severed—all rooted in the ultimate *wu* (nothingness).

The Classic says: "Hold fast to the great image, and all under heaven will follow." Indeed, one cannot govern *you* (having) without *wu* (nothingness) governing it. The "holding" is to take this and proceed.

*End of Volume Two of the Collected Meanings of the Dao-De Jing*
*Beginning of Volume Three of the Collected Meanings of the Dao-De Jing*」

**Chapter Fifteen of the *Jade Decisions of the Wu River*: The Ancient Virtuous Scholar**

The ancient virtuous scholar was subtle, mysterious, and profound—so deep as to be unknowable.
Only because he was unknowable did he feign outward forms.

○ **Master Wu** says: *"Scholar"* refers to one who embodies the *Dao*. *"Edging through"* means *communing*—one who penetrates the mysteries of all things is without limit in his profundity. Subtle yet not manifest, his communion is profound and hard to discern, like the depths of water beyond measure. His depths are unfathomable, so he feigns outward semblances to show others. The seven descriptions below are these.

○ **Master Lin** says: This chapter depicts the virtuous scholar, profound in the *Dao*, yet beyond all recognition. His heart is empty, unknowing—his outward bearing arises from no contrivance, hence *"feigning outward forms."* From *"joyous as"* onward, this is the true depiction of the virtuous one’s bearing—most refined.

○ **Master He** says: The scholar’s *Dao* is subtle and mysterious, elusive and unobstructed—who could fathom it? Since it lies beyond perception, even naming it is a forced analogy, hence the comparisons below.

Joyous as one crossing a river in winter,
Hesitant as one fearing four neighbors,
Solemn as a guest,
Dissolving as ice about to thaw,
Thick as uncarved wood,
Vast as a valley,
Muddled as turbid water.

○ **Master Wu** says: *"Joyous"* and *"hesitant"* are names of beasts—*"joyous"* belongs to the elephant clan, *"hesitant"* to the puppy. The elephant knows ahead but moves cautiously; the puppy follows then turns back, hence *"hesitant"* means lingering without progress. Crossing in winter is fearful of cold; fearing four neighbors is wary of enemies—thus, the scholar does not dare lead the world, and his bearing is such.

*"Solemn"*—dignified and composed.
*"As a guest"*—following without presuming.
*"Dissolving"*—scattered and unfixed.
*"As ice about to thaw"*—melting yet not clinging.
*"Thick"*—sincere and unadorned.
*"Uncarved wood"*—raw material not yet shaped.
*"Vast"*—open and receptive.
*"As a valley"*—empty yet responsive.
*"Muddled"*—unrefined in appearance.
*"As turbid water"*—embracing both beauty and flaw without self-purification.

○ **Master He** says: In the twelfth month, the carts are built, and people do not yet fear crossing rivers. The difficulty of winter crossing mirrors the scholar’s caution—*"joyous as"* resembles this. *"No easy path"*—ears pressed to walls—this is the fear of four neighbors. The scholar’s secrecy is like *"hesitant."* The host does not inquire, the guest does not act first—the scholar does not lead others, hence *"solemn as."* Water freezes into ice, ice melts back to water—the scholar is unobstructed by things, hence *"dissolving as."* *"Thick as"* is like uncut wood not yet divided; *"vast as"* is like a valley not yet filled; *"muddled as"* is like turbid water receiving filth. Each description vividly portrays the likeness of the virtuous scholar’s *Dao*—hard to exhaust in words.

Who can make turbid water still and gradually clear?
Who can make the settled gradually move?

○ **Master Wu** says: This follows the *"muddled"* of above, asking: *"Who can make it turbid?"* Turbidity is the time of motion—when motion follows stillness, clarity emerges gradually. Again, from *"settled,"* it asks: *"Who can make it still?"* Stillness is the time of rest—when rest gives way to motion, life arises gradually. *"Settled"* means steadfast; *"move"* means activity. Only because of turbidity does clarity arise; only because of stillness does motion arise. Thus, the vast is not full yet full, the thick is not shaped yet shaped, the dissolving is not fixed yet fixed, the solemn is not the host yet the host, the hesitant is not the first yet the first—this can be understood.

○ **Master He** says: This *Dao* never fails to encompass both essence and function, permeating motion and stillness—it is not mere empty talk. To harmonize its light, to blend with dust, to mingle with the world’s turbidity—yet there is a quiet, detached clarity. To close the mouth, seal the gate—though settled and at rest, there is a sudden, spontaneous vitality. The two *"gradually"* emphasize natural, unforced progression. This speaks of how stillness can make turbidity gradually clear, and motion can make the settled gradually vital—motion and stillness in mutual use.

○ **Master Lin** says: These two lines simply mean clarity within turbidity, stillness within motion.

To preserve this *Dao* is to avoid fullness.
Only by avoiding fullness can one embrace decay;
Only by embracing decay can one avoid renewal.

○ **Master Wu** says: *"Renewal"* means completion. All things decay and become deficient, or renew and become complete. Decay and deficiency are signs of non-fullness; renewal and completion are signs of fullness. Those who preserve this *Dao* do not seek fullness, hence they embrace decay rather than renewal.

The seven bearings within this chapter are all decaying yet unrenewed.

○ **Master He** says: To preserve this *Dao* is to avoid fullness—this is emptiness. The *Dao* is boundless in its use; sometimes it is not full. Only because it is not full does it contrast with things. While others rush toward renewal and completion, I delight in decay, not hastening toward renewal.

○ **Master Lin** says: *"Avoid fullness"* is emptiness; *"decay"* is authenticity. To decay without renewal is to remain unchanged for a thousand years as one day—only thus can one achieve the *Dao*’s ultimate fulfillment, hence decay without renewal. *"Renewal"* is one phrase; *"completion"* is another.

○ **Master Li** says: Building upon the *Dao*’s essence from the previous chapter, it elucidates how later learners preserve sincerity, maintain reverence, and constantly guard their solitude—unfixed by appearances, yet embracing unity, emptiness, and daily practice. Until all attachments are extinguished, all illusions dissolved, until stirred yet not turbid, settled yet not clear—this is the *"subtle, mysterious, profound, and hidden"* communion.

Chapter Sixteen: **Attain the utmost emptiness, steadfastly guard the utmost stillness.**

○ **Master Wu** says: *"Attain"* is the process of reaching the utmost degree. *"Emptiness"* refers to the absence of substance, where external things do not penetrate within. *"Utmost"* means to exhaust its degree. *"Guard"* is to firmly hold within and resist without, like defending a city. *"Stillness"* means motionlessness, where the inner mind does not stray outward. *"Steadfast"* is the unflagging strength of this stillness.

○ **Master Lü** says: Emptiness upon emptiness, the spirit finds its dwelling; stillness upon stillness, the spirit finds its rectitude. *Liezi* states: *"Stillness is emptiness; in emptiness, the spirit finds its dwelling."* The *Sutra of Xisheng* says: *"If a person can be empty and without action, they do not seek the Dao, yet the Dao naturally returns to them."* ○ **Master He** says: *Liezi* also said, *"There are those who are born, and those who give birth."* Further, *"To give birth is to return to non-birth."* The sage perceives the subtle principle of birth and return: whatever emerges from the *Dao*’s mechanism must reenter it. Thus, I do not swell with all things; I observe how they begin with *Gèn* and must end with *Gèn*. Hence, I remain still and do not follow them together. *"Return"* means to abide in the Dao and remain constant. All things, myriad and teeming, each return to their root. Returning to the root is stillness; stillness is the return of life. ○ **Master Wu** says: *"Myriad and teeming"* describes the living and growing motion of all things. In spring and summer, the vital *qi* ascends from the roots to the branches and leaves—this is motion. In autumn and winter, the vital *qi* descends from above and returns to the roots—this is stillness. The *qi* of heaven generates life and forms all things, called *mìng* (命). When it returns to its original place of generation, it is called *"return of life"* (复命).

○ **Master Lü** says: All things arise from *nature* (性), like the roots of grasses and trees. They manifest through *Zhèn* (震), appear in *Lí* (离), explain in *Duì* (兑), labor in *Kǎn* (坎), and finally return to their roots. Grasses and trees have branches above and roots below, but humans are different: limbs are below, and the root is above—this is the *Níwán Gōng* (泥丸宫). The *Sutra of Yellow Court* (《黄庭经》) calls the brain *"Níwán"* (泥丸), the sacred domain of the *Dao*’s essence, where spirit and *qi* converge. If one can observe inwardly here, all spirits will gather, all *qi* will flow, and the *Tāixiān* (胎仙) will be perfected. Hence, *"All things, myriad and teeming, each return to their root."* The noble practitioner who constructs the Dao must begin by exhausting principles, proceed by perfecting nature, and conclude by returning life. None can attain the return of life without exhausting principles and perfecting nature. Now, clarity and openness in all directions—this is exhausting principles. To attain the utmost emptiness and steadfastly guard the utmost stillness—this is perfecting nature. *Guāngchéngzǐ* (广成子) with no seeing, no hearing, embraces the spirit in stillness, and when stillness is steadfast, clarity follows. Your spirit will then guard the form, the form will endure and grow, and enduring growth means life returns.

○ **Master Li** says: To practice emptiness and steadfast stillness, returning life to its root—this is the pure mystery of the spirit. If one attains this with a single, unblinking gaze, the study of a lifetime is complete. *"Return of life"* is constancy; knowing constancy is enlightenment. To not know constancy is to act recklessly—disastrous. Knowing constancy brings tolerance; tolerance brings fairness; fairness brings kingship; kingship brings heaven; heaven is the Dao; the Dao is enduring. Thus, one’s life is never in peril. ○ **Master Lin** says: To attain the return of life is to grasp the constancy that does not change. To know the enduring, unchanging Dao—this is enlightenment. Because people do not know this enduring, unchanging Dao, they indulge in reckless thoughts and actions, all of which are disastrous deviations from the Dao. Knowing constancy, one’s heart aligns with the vastness of heaven and earth—what cannot be embraced? Once embrace is achieved, what matter remains unfair? Kingship over all under heaven is this fair Dao. To rule with fairness is to rule with heaven. Heaven is the Dao, hence *"kingship is heaven; heaven is the Dao."* Enduring is constancy; if one attains this constant Dao, their entire life is without deviation—how could there be any peril? ○ **Master He** says: Only by knowing the constant cycle of growth and decay in all things does one grasp the principle of *swelling and waning* (盈虚消息). Thus, one does not cling to minutiae, and *"constancy becomes tolerance."* Knowing that beauty and ugliness, right and wrong can be embraced, one remains boundless and without bias—hence *"tolerance becomes fairness."* Fairness without self-interest is the kingly Dao. Kingship without bounds is the heavenly Dao. Enduring without cessation is the constant Dao of heaven.

The Dao endures through countless ages without flaw—what peril could there be? This is the ultimate attainment of *emptiness and tranquility* (虚静).

○ **Master Wu** says: Peril refers to endangering oneself. The character *殆* (dai), with the radical *歹* (evil), is glossed as *dangerous*, *near*, or *close*—most words with this radical carry the sense of *death*. Peril is danger approaching death. *没* (mo) means *to perish* or *terminate*.

**Chapter Seventeen**
The *Supreme Sage* (太上) is known only as existing.
The next is one whom the people love and praise.
The next is one whom the people fear and revile.

When virtue/power (信) is insufficient, there arises unbelief.

○ **Master Lü** says: The *Supreme Sage* (太上) is one whose teaching is *wuwei* (无为), whose governance is without exertion, harmonized with the Dao itself—nothing can be added above, hence *"the Supreme Sage."* Though he holds his position, he does not exalt himself, does not value nobility, but lets all things follow their natural course, each settling in its place, like the realm of *Hua Xu* (华胥之国). Thus, the world hears no talk of governance yet governs itself; the people, though aware of their ruler, say *"What has the Emperor’s power to do with me?"*

The *Zhou Shijing* (周诗) states:
*"Unknowing and unknowing, they follow the Emperor’s norms"*—hence *"the Supreme Sage is known only as existing."*

Moreover, in the ancient times, the people used knotted cords for record-keeping, lived in nests, and dwelled in caves, unaware of the passage of seasons or years—this may be called the utmost governance. *Fu Xi* and *Shen Nong* (伏羲神农) emerged, and the people began to till and eat, to weave and wear. They turned toward *ren* and *yi* (仁义), toward *li* and *yue* (礼乐), and the distinctions of praise and blame began to arise.

For a ruler, if his benevolence extends to the people, those who receive it regard him as a parent; if his righteousness is embraced, they praise him like the sun and moon. The more vivid the principle of love and praise becomes, the farther it strays from the ancient Dao.

The *Nanhua Jing* (《南华经》) says:
*"Shun’s conduct was humble, and the people delighted in it"*—hence *"the next is one whom the people love and praise."*

In later ages, governance grew artificial and deceitful, *ren* and *yi* were lost, *li* and *yue* abandoned, and the wise and foolish deceived one another. The world declined, yet the Dao persisted through political means, standardized by punishment, and the ruler’s authority was displayed to make the people *cang* (僶, yield) and obey—thus, the people did not fear his might but turned to deceit. When the people deceive, the heart to revile their ruler arises—hence *"the next is one whom the people fear and revile."*

The *Five Constant Virtues* (五常) take *virtue/power* (信) as their foundation. The *Rú* (鲁) saying declares:
*"Without virtue/power, the people cannot stand."* When virtue/power is complete, the world is secure; when lost, the world is in peril.

If *ren* and *yi* (仁义) vie with sincerity, gain and loss follow in turn, and cunning schemes flourish, deceit arises—this is the insufficiency of virtue/power. Once virtue/power is insufficient, the people’s hearts of unbelief emerge. As when the *Zhōu* (周) people established oaths and the people began to rebel—hence *"when virtue/power is insufficient, there arises unbelief."*

○ **Master Lin** says: The *Supreme Sage* (太上) refers to the ancient times. The *lower* (下) means the world below. In the ancient times, the people of the world saw only that their ruler existed, yet all forgot themselves in the Dao’s transformation. Later, the people began to regard their ruler with love and praise. Again later, they began to fear him. Again later, they began to revile him. This shows how the world’s Dao declines ever downward. When the *Supreme Virtue* (上德) decays and virtue/power (诚信) is insufficient, then the people’s hearts of unbelief arise. This is the meaning of *"the Shang* (商) people made oaths and the people began to rebel; the *Zhou* (周) people established assemblies and the people began to doubt."*

○ **Master He** says: When the Sage is above, he embraces the Dao without words, yet his benevolence reaches all things. The officials and courts appear empty, the halls and courts devoid of people—no labor, no wrongful punishments. All under heaven reveres his virtue, and at such times, the people know their ruler yet know nothing else, like fish forgetting each other in the rivers and lakes. This is the Dao of the *Supreme Sage.*

The next is when the great Dao is abandoned, yet *ren* and *yi* remain—then the ruler and the people are close and praise each other within the framework of *li* and *yue*, like fish nibbling and moistening one another.

The next is when governance becomes scrutinizing, and the people become wary—then the world begins to fear the law’s severity. Again the next is when laws are established and fraud arises, punishments carried out and deceit flourishes—then the people begin to deceive their ruler, to despise him, and to revile him afterward. This is like fish devouring one another.

*Zhuangzi*, in his insight into the ancient great Dao, saw that morality (道) and virtue (德) were first made clear, then *ren* and *yi* followed, and rewards and punishments were the last concern—he indeed perceived this truth.

The world’s Dao declines like a river’s current; it is difficult to restore the era when the people said *"What has the Emperor’s power to do with me?"* Yet when the *Supreme Sage* and *Way* are mixed with other influences, human hearts follow suit, and thus arise the shifts of affection, fear, respect, and contempt—all stemming from those above. This is the extreme of virtue/power’s insufficiency in moving the people.

Again it is said: *"Only when my virtue/power is insufficient do the people doubt me."*

○ **Master Li** says: The *Supreme Sage* (太上) cannot but be *wuwei*—this is the teaching of love and praise, the silent instruction. Those who are feared are governed by punishments and prohibitions; those who are reviled are rulers who have lost the people’s virtue/power. When the ruler loses virtue/power among the people, the people do not believe him. *"Ah, how precious are his words!"*

When deeds are accomplished and affairs settled, the people all say of him: *"He is but nature."* Master Li says: *"Ah, how precious are his words!"*—this is the silent instruction. Silent instruction, achieved through *wuwei*, means punishment is not enforced yet the people submit themselves. When his accomplishments and affairs are complete, he returns to simplicity and purity—then love, praise, fear, and revile all fade from the people’s minds. The people dwell in peace and delight in their customs, forgetting their origins, hence *"the people say of him: ‘He is but nature.’"*

○ **Master He** says: The Sage’s reason for saying *"Ah, how precious are his words!"* is that he dares not use *sheng* (声) and *se* (色, sounds and appearances) to transform the people. Only when he attains the silent instruction of *wuwei* can his deeds be accomplished and affairs settled, through the natural transformation of the people—making them praise him yet not praise themselves—this is how he saves the world’s endless decline.

○ **Master Lü** says: The ancient transformation was rooted in the principle of *wuwei*, in silent instruction alone. Its regard for words was thus: The *Yijing* (《易》) states:
*"Without words yet believed, lies in virtue and conduct"*—thus one knows that the Sage’s single word becomes the law for all under heaven. He himself knows his words are precious, hence he diminishes them again and again, remains silent yet again and again, though he still values his words and does not utter them lightly. Today’s people merely value words, yet they are but empty rhetoric—hence *"Ah, how precious are his words!"*

「The ruler of the Dao/Way makes the people improve daily and transform monthly, shifting to goodness and distancing from crime, allowing them to dwell in peace and take joy in their customs, while he governs with ease and observes from afar. The flourishing of the people and the winds of pure transformation can be restored, and when his work is accomplished and affairs settled, the common folk all say that they have found his natural Dao/Way.

**Chapter XVIII:**
When the Great Dao/Way is abandoned, there arise *rényì* (仁義, virtue and righteousness).
When *zhìhuì* (智慧, wisdom) emerges, there arises great deceit.
**Master Lin** says: When the Great Dao/Way is practiced, *rényì* is inherent within it. Once the names of *rényì* are established, the Dao/Way begins to fade. Hence it is said, *"When the Great Dao/Way is abandoned, there arises rényì."* It is like the sun rising daily, and only then does the deceit and falsehood of the world appear.
**Master Wu** says: The lower understanding of the previous chapter—that which is known and praised—is the Great Dao/Way. That which is cherished and commended is *rényì*. That which is feared or reviled is *zhìhuì*. From the Great Dao/Way, descending once and then again, already three grades are formed; when *zhìhuì* further transforms into great deceit, there are four grades in total. However, when the Great Dao/Way is abandoned, *rényì* arises, its change is still somewhat gradual. When *zhìhuì* emerges, great deceit swiftly follows, its change is far more abrupt.

**Master Lü** says: The Great Dao/Way is the *wuwei* Dao/Way of antiquity. In its flourishing, *rényì* is practiced within it, yet the people use it without knowing its origin—it is simply the Dao/Way. The Sage’s learning first seeks *dàodé* (道德, virtue/power of the Dao) before *rényì*, whereas the learning of the common world first seeks *rényì* before *dàodé*. Yet they do not realize that *yīnyáng* (陰陽, yin and yang) is the Dao/Way of Heaven, and *gāngróu* (剛柔, firmness and softness) is the Dao/Way of Earth. *Rényì* is the Dao/Way of humankind; its essence was never truly separate from the Dao/Way, nor did the Dao/Way ever abandon *rényì*. How is it that later generations cling to *rényì* as fixed laws, causing the pristine and natural Dao/Way to dwindle, and bringing about the harm of obscuring it? It is far better to conceal *rényì*, discard deceit, return to the origins, and align with nature, then the Dao/Way will not be abandoned.

Hence it is said:
*"When the Great Dao/Way is abandoned, there arises rényì,"*
*"When wisdom emerges from rényì, thieves and robbers arise from poverty,"*
*"When rényì is hidden, wisdom emerges,"*
*"When wisdom emerges, great deceit arises,"*
*"When wisdom is insufficient, it is followed by deceit."*
Hence it is said, *"When wisdom emerges, there arises great deceit."*

**Master He** says: In the *Zhuangzi*’s age of supreme *dàodé*, uprightness is natural yet not known as *rényì*, mutual love is natural yet not known as *rén* (仁, benevolence), sincerity is natural yet not known as *zhōng* (忠, loyalty), fittingness is natural yet not known as *xìn* (信, trustworthiness). Thus actions are performed without trace, and deeds are done without record. From this perspective, the names of *rén*, *yì*, *zhōng*, and *xìn* do not stand, yet their substance remains—this is the age of supreme *dàodé*, the practice of the Great Dao/Way.

When the *liúqīn* (六親, six relations of kinship) are not harmonious, there arise filial piety and compassion.
When the state is in chaos and disorder, there arise loyal ministers.»

「Master Lin says: Only when the *liúqīn* (六親) are not harmonious does the name of filial piety arise; only when the state is in chaos and disorder does the name of loyal ministers become known. These two lines are both allegorical.

**Master Wu** says: The *liúqīn* (六親) are father, son, elder and younger brothers, and husband and wife. If respect for rank and seniority follows its proper *Dao/Way*, and there is no discord, then filial piety is merely their natural duty, and they do not recognize it as *xiào* (孝, filial piety). The blind elder (瞽叟) did not act as a father, but when Shun (舜) was acknowledged as the sage-king, only then did the people know of the filial piety of the Great Shun (大舜之孝子). The state belongs to the ruler, and the family belongs to the minister; if the ruler and minister follow their proper *Dao/Way* without chaos or disorder, then loyalty is merely their natural duty, and they do not recognize it as *zhōng* (忠, loyalty). When King Jie (桀) of the Xia (夏) ruled unjustly and evil consorts gathered, only then did the people know of the loyal ministers like Bi Gan (比干) and the Three Ren (三仁).

**Master Lü** says: It is better to let the people transform naturally without governance than to wait for the state to fall into disorder and then display loyal ministers.

**Master Lü** further explains: When King Jie (桀) of the Xia (夏) stood, Longfeng (龙逢) became eminent; when King Zhou (纣) of the Shang (商) perished, Bi Gan (比干) shone forth.

Chapter Nineteenth: *"Abandon the Sage and discard wisdom, and the people will benefit a hundredfold. Abandon benevolence and discard righteousness, and the people will return to filial piety and kindness. Abandon cleverness and discard profit, and thieves and robbers will vanish."*

**Master Wu** says: *"Abandon"* means to reject and not practice; *"discard"* means to reject and not employ. The Sage and wisdom refer to *zhìhuì* (智慧, wisdom). Cleverness and profit refer to *dàwěi* (大伪, great deceit).

**Master Lü** says: Form, measure, law, and model—these are the works of the Sage. Strategy, calculation, manipulation, and intrigue—these are the wisdom of the Sage. If one has a mind to practice wisdom, how could they know the utmost wisdom? The Perfect One fears that the people will be confused by the Sage’s wisdom, losing their true nature even more. They value returning to the root, abandoning the Sage’s artifices, discarding wisdom, and letting the people be like innocent children, devoted solely to agriculture and sericulture. The benefit to the people is far beyond a hundredfold, hence the saying: *"Abandon the Sage and discard wisdom, and the people will benefit a hundredfold."*

Filial piety and kindness are the nature of Heaven; benevolence and righteousness are human inclinations. Heaven’s nature arises from spontaneity and cannot be altered. Benevolence, loving all things, makes the people close to it; righteousness, fitting all things, makes the people praise it. When the hearts of closeness and praise arise, the harmony of life and nature is lost. The Perfect One abandons them before they arise and discards them before they become reality, allowing people to return to their original state and restore their natural simplicity. Thus, the *Dao/Way* of filial piety and kindness is complete. Hence the saying: *"Abandon benevolence and discard righteousness, and the people will return to filial piety and kindness."*

Carving, polishing, and intricate craftsmanship—these are the cleverness of things. Precious gems, jade, measures of grain, and scales of balance—these are the profits of things. If one competes for cleverness, they will not care about shame; if one pursues profit, they will not care about righteousness, giving rise to thieves and robbers. If one can return to their true nature and reside in simplicity, then where would thieves and robbers come from? Hence the saying: *"Abandon cleverness and discard profit, and thieves and robbers will vanish."*

**Master Lin** says: Once the names of the Sage and wisdom arise, the harm to the world follows. It is better to abandon and discard them so that the world naturally benefits. Once the names of benevolence and righteousness arise, distinctions between filial and unfilial, kind and unkind, emerge. It is better to abandon and discard them, forgetting them in the *Dao/Way*, so that all people return to the natural state of filial piety and kindness without distinction. Cleverness and profit give rise to thieves and robbers; it is better to abandon and discard them, as in the saying: *"Do not value hard-to-obtain goods, so that the people will not steal."*

**Master Li** says: This elaborates on the meaning of the previous chapter, where the Sage, wisdom, benevolence, righteousness, cleverness, and profit are all abandoned and discarded, never to be employed again. Then the people will follow their natural transformation and return to simplicity.

**Master Lin** says: Some say that when the Old Master (老氏) abandons benevolence and discards righteousness, does he not contradict Confucius? Indeed, he traces the origin of *Dao/Way* to its source, asserting that though benevolence and righteousness, rites and music, can be used for governance, it is superior to govern the people through the *Dao/Way* and let them forget it in its midst.

These three teachings are insufficient as mere ornamentation; thus, they are given to those who belong to the realm of *Dao/Way* and *virtue/power*. To see one’s true substance and hold fast to simplicity, to have few thoughts and few desires.

**Master Lü** says: *"Abandon the Sage and discard wisdom"* is the first; *"abandon benevolence and discard righteousness"* is the second; *"abandon cleverness and discard profit"* is the third. All three belong to the sequence of *Dao/Way*, yet they do not obscure its reality.

The *Dao/Way* cannot be spoken of; any speech about it is merely its semblance. Those who attain understanding hear only its semblance and lose its truth. Though they record it in writing and pass it down through teaching, they still fear that people will doubt its semblance. Thus, it is known but not employed, spoken of but not understood. To discard ornamentation and uphold simplicity, there must be another path to follow, so that doubts dissolve like melting ice. Hence the saying: *"These three teachings are insufficient as mere ornamentation."*

*"Seeing one’s true substance"* means purity without admixture. *"Holding fast to simplicity"* means stillness and unity without change. To guard oneself with utmost fairness, not to love external beauty—this is having few private inclinations. To practice moderation and control emotions, to be serene and content—this is having few desires. Thus, to govern the state in this manner, one can elevate the people to the realm of benevolence and longevity, the age of *Xīhuáng* (羲皇, the mythical sage-king of antiquity). This is not difficult to achieve.

To govern oneself in this manner: one’s spirit becomes clear and pure, unaffected by external things—is this not *"seeing one’s true substance"*? One’s spirit does not diminish, preserved in the chaos of unity—is this not *"holding fast to simplicity"*? In eating, resting, and daily life, there is nothing but fairness and correctness—is this not having few private inclinations? To dwell in the tranquil and distant, severing all entanglements—is this not having few desires? To exchange these for that, what is there to prevent?

**Master He** says: It is not that the Sage’s wisdom, cleverness, and profit cannot be used in the world; rather, their names are mere ornamentation, not their true essence. If the world is allowed to settle in its natural life and nature, then these three can be dispensed with as mere names. The world not settling in its natural life and nature, while people only revere the names of these three and cling to them, is the greatest confusion. Ornamentation is insufficient; if they belong to the realm of *Dao/Way* and *virtue/power*, then it becomes reality. Hence, the Sage says: *"Those who practice cleverness, wisdom, and the Sage’s ways, I regard them as having cast off the *Dao/Way*. Thus, I harmonize all things without calling it benevolence, bestow blessings upon countless generations without calling it righteousness, cover and load the heavens and earth, carve and fashion all forms without calling it cleverness."* Hence the saying: *"Those who discard benevolence and righteousness are few; those who profit from benevolence and righteousness are many. The practice of benevolence and righteousness is merely insincere, and relies on the tools of greedy and acquisitive people."* Thus, one person’s judgment benefits the world, but this is merely the false use of benevolence and righteousness. How could this be true benevolence and righteousness? It is far removed from the *Dao/Way*.»

「Yet the common folk fail to grasp its intent, deriding the rejection of the Sage’s ways and abandonment of cleverness as absurd, and scoffing at the abandonment of benevolence and righteousness as a deviation from the norm. Why do they not ponder the *Dao/Way*’s profound purpose, which seeks to guide all under Heaven to cultivate the *Hun-Cheng* (混成) *Dao/Way*? By discarding superficial adornments and returning to simplicity, they may attain their natural state. Hence the saying: *"Hold steadfast to one and do not mix with another; govern the inner and do not embellish the outer. Clarity enters into emptiness, and non-action returns to primal simplicity. Embrace nature, preserve the spirit."* When one’s selfishness and desires diminish, they merge into boundless selflessness and desirelessness. Once purity and sincerity are restored, the *Jiao-Jing* (浇竞) of strife is naturally dispelled. Then, the radiant Sage has no need for artificiality, the subtle *Dao/Way*’s intentions require no contrivance, the benevolence of universal love finds no place to dwell, the righteousness of governance has no need to intervene, and the tools of profit and cleverness are set aside. All under Heaven thus blends harmoniously into the great order. This is the ultimate intent of the Sage’s teachings.

The common folk are unworthy to recognize this truth—how can they dare to lightly judge it?

○ *Master Wu* said: The people of the *Dao/Way* outwardly appear as plain *xu* (素), inwardly uphold the uncarved *pu* (朴). *Xu* (素) is silk yet to be dyed, *pu* (朴) is wood yet to be shaped. Pure and simple, what need have they of artificiality?

**Chapter Twenty: Absolute Learning and No Worry**
How far apart are *"Absolute Learning"* and *"the One and the That"* (唯之与阿)?
How distant are *"good"* and *"evil"*?
What people fear, one must not fail to fear.
Vast and boundless, its end is beyond reckoning!

○ *Master Li* said: *"Absolute Learning"* means abandoning the learning of ordinary people and taking up what they do not learn—rejecting the world’s learning. Common folk are only concerned with learning for practical gain, augmenting their knowledge, ever fearful that their progress in the *Dao/Way* is not precise. The Sage, however, discards all acquisition and solely attends to the flavor of the *Dao/Way*, thus having no worries. *"How far apart?"* means they are not distant at all. *"How distant?"* means they are too far apart. What people fear, one must not fail to fear—good and evil are indeed things to be feared.

○ *Master He* said: This *"Absolute Learning"* is none other than the *Dao/Way*. Natural and effortless, it reaches the point of transcending form and name—this is *"Absolute Learning"*. One’s anxieties and thoughts should then be utterly forgotten. Though it is said there is no worry, what then is there to fear? Even without deliberate thought or outward form, one must still hold reverence and caution, for it is the resonance of *"the One"* (唯) and *"the That"* (阿). How distant they seem, yet in reverence, how slight is the neglect! Good and evil are but a single thought—how vast the divide between beauty and ugliness! *Zhuangzi* says that *"the beautiful becomes lasting, the evil cannot be rectified"*—how can one be too careful?

Here, even within *"no worry"*, there are things to be feared. Though the Sage forgets the world, they dare not treat it frivolously. Speaking of *"Absolute Learning and no worry"*, the forms of *"no form"* appear, the sounds of *"no sound"* are heard, the tastes of *"no taste"* are savored, and the teachings of *"no speech"* are imparted. With *"the whole cow in one’s eye"*, what is there to worry about?

From *"the One and the That"* regarding good and evil, the eyes dare not gaze indiscriminately, the ears dare not listen recklessly, the mouth dare not speak falsely, the body dare not move wantonly. To see a louse as large as a chariot wheel—how can one not fear? *Zhuangzi* says: *"How small it is! Yet this is how it belongs to humanity."* This is the saying of those who fear what people fear. *"How vast it is! Standing alone in its own nature."* This is the saying of *"Absolute Learning and no worry"*.

The *Dao/Way* reaches its utmost extent in effortless action, and its breadth is boundless. The *Dao/Way* begins with goodness itself, neither grand nor minute, neither arising nor ceasing. To seek its source, one finds it utterly beyond pursuit—what further use then for one’s mind?

○ *Master Dong* said: It is vast and boundless, with no known limits.
The multitude bustles in joy, as if feasting on the grandest sacrificial meat, as if ascending the Spring Terrace to gaze.
I alone remain tranquil, yet unseen—like an infant before it can laugh.
○ *Master Wu* said: *"Bustling in joy"* is the appearance of harmony and delight. *"Tranquil"* is stillness. *"Unseen"* is like the faintest crack in a tortoise shell’s divination. The common folk who engage in learning seek pleasure in outward things, like feasting on the grandest sacrificial meat, which delights the palate, or ascending the Spring Terrace to view, which satisfies the eyes. I, however, remain tranquil and still, my desires yet unopened, with no trace of ambition visible—like an infant unable to laugh or smile, entirely unaware of the world’s pleasures.

○ *Master Lin* said: The common folk take joy in the world’s flavors, like feasting on the grandest sacrificial meat or ascending the Spring Terrace to gaze, while I alone find contentment in guarding simplicity and stillness. My thoughts are a hundredfold unformed, like the infant before it can laugh. *"Unseen"* is form, is budding—this heart does not bud nor move, hence *"unseen"*.

An infant is born, and a child grows slightly older. The infant’s heart is entirely without knowledge.

○ *Master He* said: The common folk are like those feasting on the grandest sacrificial meat, delighting their senses and bodies, or ascending the Spring Terrace to indulge in unrestrained viewing—bustling in joy, finding ample delight. I, however, am yet unseen, like an infant just born, before reaching the stage of laughter. One cannot perceive my head if they approach me.

*"Leaning and resting"* (乘乘兮), as if having nowhere to return.
○ *Master Wu* said: *"Leaning and resting"* means dwelling in things, yet having nowhere to return—it means not clinging to them.

○ *Master He* said: *"Leaning and resting"* with nowhere to return—dwelling in things while the mind wanders freely, like riding the wind and treading the void, finding no place to anchor. One follows them without seeing their trail.

It is also said: *"Leaning and resting"* is like riding the wind, like driving the clouds—both are metaphors of riding the wind and harnessing the floating.

○ *Master Lü* said: To blend with the world and move unnoticed, to merge with things and ride their waves, the heart fuses with form, and the body releases its burdens, becoming one with the spirit. Like a hollow boat drifting east and west, or a leaf fluttering high and low, one does not know if the wind rides them or they ride the wind. No one knows where they are going, hence *"Leaning and resting"* with nowhere to return.

The common folk all have excess, while I alone seem abandoned.
○ *Master Lin* said: The common folk all possess a heart of seeking and filling, while I alone appear to abandon and discard—this means that in my pursuit of the *Dao/Way*, I have no heart of excess.»

○ **Master Lü** said: The common folk follow what they know, drowning in wealth and honor, believing they have abundance in their joy. But the Perfect One is not so. He discards fame and profit like chaff, treats high office and lowly matters alike—knowing that both body and world are illusory. His heart is as empty as space, forgetting all things. Thus it is said: *"The common folk all have excess, while I alone seem abandoned."*

○ **Master Wu** said: *"Abandoned"* means *lost*. The common folk rejoice in their abundance, but I, having nothing, feel a quiet loss.

○ **Master Dong** said: *"Abandoned"* means *forgotten*—having yet not having. Oh, how simple-minded I am! Stupid and unknowing.

○ **Master Lü** said: The most eloquent in the world do not excel in speech but in silence; the wisest do not excel in cleverness but in foolishness. Those who act foolish all day do so because they do not deplete their spirit.

○ **Master Wu** said: *"Stupid"* is like the original chaos (*Hun Dun*), dark and indistinct, unable to distinguish—just as in *Zhuangzi*, *"foolish"* means without knowledge.

○ **Master Lin** said: *"Stupid and unknowing"* describes the undifferentiated chaos. The common folk are bright and discerning, while I alone am dim and confused. The common folk are sharp and probing, while I alone am dull and obscured.

○ **Master Lü** said: The common folk expend their spirit outward, dazzling and radiant, seeking only to hear more, to increase their knowledge, to show off their learning—like parading the sun and moon. But the Perfect One’s wisdom encompasses all things, his inner light illuminating like a hidden treasure. Though he wears coarse robes, he holds jade within; outwardly, he lacks brilliance. Thus it is said: *"The common folk are bright, while I alone am dim."* The common folk are strict and rigid, boastful and self-made, clinging to their narrow views, lost in trivialities, scrutinizing the depths of the sea yet unable to stop. But the Perfect One follows Heaven and the Dao, wandering freely, vast and inclusive, beyond measure—dim, silent, and unfathomable. Thus it is said: *"The common folk are sharp, while I alone am dull."*

○ **Master Lin** said: *"Dim and dull"* is the same as chaos. The common folk are bright and sharp, while I alone am dim and dull—this is how I differ from them.

○ **Master Wu** said: *"Bright"* is already clear, *"sharp"* even more so. *"Dim"* is unclear, *"dull"* even more so. The common folk take knowledge for wisdom, but I alone am ignorant and foolish. Drifting like the sea, whirling like endless wind.

○ **Master Lin** said: Like sailing on the vast ocean, the wind howling without end—this is the meaning of *"Leaning and resting, with nowhere to return."*

○ **Master Wu** said: Like floating on the sea, drifting without direction, or the endless wind rising without rest. The common folk all have purpose, but I alone seem stubborn and crude.

○ **Master Wu** said: The common folk all have purpose—that is, they know where they are going. *"Stubborn"* means numb like a paralyzed face, unaware of pain. *"Crude"* means rustic. I alone am dull and ignorant, like a distant fool. Those who dwell in bustling cities know much, while those in remote, barren places know little.

○ **Master Lin** said: *"Purpose"* means *action*. The common folk all act, but I delight in seeking nothing, thus appearing stubborn and crude.

○ **Master Dong** said: *"Purpose"* means *use*. *"Dull"* means *without knowledge*. *"Crude"* means *wild*. All people know the use of what is useful, but not the use of what is useless. I alone differ from them, cherishing to *"feed at the Mother."*

○ **Master Wu** said: This sentence summarizes what came before—from the common fear of excess to my own abandonment, speaking of how scholars strive for many talents, while I alone possess none; from my simple heart to my stubborn crudeness, speaking of how scholars seek much knowledge, while I alone know nothing. Thus I differ from them. What I value is the profound virtue of the Dao—the Dao being the Mother of all things. Thus it is said, *"Feed at the Mother."* To feed at the Mother is like nursing at a wet nurse. Sima [Qian] said: *"Nurturing the Original Harmony."*

○ **Master Lin** said: What makes me different is my taste for the Dao—the Dao, named the Mother of all things. *"Feed"* means *taste*. To cherish feeding at the Mother is to value seeking taste in the Dao.

○ **Master Li** said: The Sage abandons all things, attending only to the Dao, feeding at the Mother, guarding the feminine and embracing unity—thus there is no worry.

○ **Master Lü** said: The Dao is the root of all things. As fish depend on water—if fish lose their water, they die—what would happen if humans lost the Dao? One must know that spirit is the child of *qi*, and *qi* is the Mother of spirit. Without the Mother’s nourishment, one cannot live; without *qi*’s sustenance, one cannot thrive. Thus it is said, *"Feed at the Mother."* If one continuously draws from this nourishment above and below, then the body will not decay, and the spirit will not be depleted. Hence, *"Feed at the Mother"* is most precious.

○ Some say: *"Purpose"* is used to explain *"cherishing to feed at the Mother,"* but the interpretations differ—how can this be? It is said: Though the words differ, their meaning both return to *tasting the Dao.*

**Chapter 21**
The bearing of great virtue follows only the Dao.

○ **Master Lü** said: The constant Dao is nameless; only virtue reveals it. Supreme virtue has no origin—it arises from following the Dao. The Dao is formless, but when it moves, it becomes virtue, taking shape. Never for a moment departing from the Dao, hence *"follows only the Dao."*

○ **Master Wu** said: *"Great virtue"* is like abundant virtue. *"Bearing"* refers to what exists and is visible. *"Follows"* means *conforms to*.

---

**All things derive from virtue.**

○ **Master Lü** said: The virtue that gives rise to this manifestation is born from the Dao. The visible forms and *qi* are the *bearing of virtue*.

○ **Master Wu** said: *"Great"* is indeed *"abundant."* Those who understand the Dao follow it alone, yet even those who see beyond it possess the bearing of great virtue. *"Virtue"* (德) means *that which is obtained*—what one obtains within oneself is virtue. Since the Dao is invisible yet virtue is visible, virtue is thus called the *bearing of the Dao*.

**The nature of the Dao** is but *dim and vague*.

○ **Master Lü** said: The Dao is obscure and difficult to speak of—what substance can be likened to it? Those who speak of its "nature" force a name upon it to probe its profound essence. Since the Dao is formless, from what can it be said to arise? Now, if one seeks the Dao through things, it cannot be obtained; if one seeks the Dao apart from things, it has no boundary. Examining its *dim and vague* essence, one finds that *dim* seems to be *having*—a having within the void—while *vague* seems to be *not having*—a not having within the having. Dwelling in the void is not void, hence *space is form*; dwelling in form is not form, hence *form is space*. Thus, the essence of *dim and vague* can be imagined. Hence it is said: **The nature of the Dao is but dim and vague.**

○ **Master Wu** said: *Dim and vague*—having yet not having—cannot be seen. Hence it is said: *dim and vague*. Within the *vague*, there is *form*; within the *dim*, there is *substance*. Within the *obscure* and *profound*, there is *essence*. This essence is most true, within which there is *faith*. Within the *faith*, there is *truth*.

○ **Master Lü** said: The *form* within *dim* appears to have form, yet its source is nowhere to be found—thus it cannot be seen, nor can it be heard. If sight and hearing cannot grasp it, how can one measure the *obscure and profound*? To call it *not having* is to say it wondrously encompasses all things; to call it *having* is to say its form cannot be perceived. The *form of the Great Void* is having yet not having—from it, transformation arises, and all things emerge. The *substance of the Great Void* is not having yet having—from it, wind and thunder are born, and frost and dew descend. When there is *form*, the names of things are established; when there is *substance*, they arise from the utmost *essence*. Obscure yet deep and boundless, vast yet silent, *having within the void*, it encompasses the roots of all transformations, conceals the *essence of the Great Void*, and nurtures the *faith of Nature*. Thus all classes of things are born, and the Three Realms are complete. *The Broad Virtue Master* says: **"The essence of the ultimate Dao is obscure and profound; the extreme of the ultimate Dao is dark and silent. Its origin is such."**

The heart is the palace of *dim and vague*, the kidneys the storehouse of *obscure and profound*. The heart harbors the spirit, the kidneys the *essence*. The *essence and spirit* of the body dwell within the *dim and vague*. Hence it is said: *vague yet obscure*, within which there is *form*; *dim yet vague*, within which there is *substance*; *obscure and profound*, within which there is *essence*. If the *essence* is refined, it weaves through all directions, wondrously uniting the body. In alchemy, it is called the *Great Medicine*; in the *I Ching*, it is called *Central Trust* (中孚). All changes cannot alter it, and Nature cannot displace it. The marvel within marvels, the truth within truths, it responds instantly when stirred—thus it can give birth in spring and cause death in autumn, never failing. Applied to the body, it ascends as *ancestral qi*, circulates as *Yi flow* (沂流), and transforms from yellow to red. Its Dao follows the seasons, hence it is said: **its essence is most true, within which there is faith.**

○ **Master He** said: In ancient times, a scholar of profound mysteries expounded this chapter thus: *"Form"* is not always *form*, and *"essence"* is not always *essence*. Heaven obtains clarity through it, Earth obtains tranquility, and humanity obtains vitality. Those who can embrace the *mysterious* and hold to the *One*, seeking *essence* in the *obscure and profound* and *substance* in the *dim and vague*, achieve a perfect union of form and spirit with the true Dao. When one speaks of *form*, *substance*, and *essence*, all are within the *dim and vague* and *obscure and profound*—having within the void. Yet it is said that this *essence* is most true, and that there is *faith*—meaning there is no void without having, nor having without void.

○ **Master Wu** said: Former sages spoke of the *void and boundless* without eyes, yet all things stood arrayed within it. From ancient times to the present, its name does not fade, for it surveys all *beauty*.

○ How do I know that all *beauty* is such? **Thus.**

○ **Master Lü** said: Since the dawn of antiquity, down to the present, it has ever existed, manifesting its power in each season. It has never been named, yet we force the name *"true essence"* upon it—enduring through the ages, unchanging, it is the eternal nature of the *One*. Sitting and observing the myriad beauties of Heaven and Earth, witnessing life and death’s completion, hence it is said: **from ancient times to the present, its name does not fade, for it surveys all beauty.** Yet all beauty undergoes ceaseless change—how do I know that all things draw their resources and endowments from it? **It is because the Dao reaches everywhere, thus it governs all transformations.**

○ **Master He** said: This Dao precedes Heaven and Earth yet does not endure long; it persists through antiquity yet does not grow old. Though it is ever nameless, its name cannot be erased—this is the *"Dao of the Constant"* from the first chapter. It is called the *Dao of the Constant Dao*. Sitting and observing all beauty as it emerges, *beauty* (甫) means *perfection*—all perfection, for the Dao is complete. Hence it is said: **the void and boundless, yet all perfection follows it; this is the Dao of Heaven and Earth, the virtue of the Sage.** How do I know that all perfection is such? **Thus, by this Dao.** To survey all perfection is to survey all principles beneath Heaven—each must pass through the mind.

○ **Master Lin** said: The name of the Dao is present in both ancient and modern times; it cannot be erased for even a day, yet all goodness arises from it. *All perfection* means *all beauty*; *survey* means *to pass through and observe*. All goodness comes and goes through this Dao. **Thus, by this Dao.**

○ **Master Wu** said: *"Does not fade"* means *eternally preserved*; *survey* is *to pass through*; *beauty* is *perfection*—all things are *beauty*. Yet all things’ beauty varies with time and perishes; only the *Great Virtue*, arising from the Dao, endures from ancient times to the present, unchanging and unperishing. Hence it is said: **from ancient times to the present, its name does not fade, for it surveys all beauty.**

Thus, it is so. How could I know that all things’ changes and perishings are without constancy, as they are? **By this Great Virtue’s enduring Dao, I know.**

---

**Dao/Way and Virtue Classic: Collected Meanings, Volume III**
**Dao/Way and Virtue Classic: Collected Meanings, Volume IV**
**Chapter XXII of the *Xu Jiang Wei Da You* Collection: "The Crooked Becomes Whole, the Twisted Becomes Straight"**

The **crooked** becomes **whole**,
The **twisted** becomes **straight**,
The **hollow** becomes **full**,
The **ruined** becomes **renewed**,
The **few** become **many**,
The **many** bring **confusion**.

**Master Lü** said: The Sage hooks deep and reaches far, yet all movement follows principle. Where principle abides, none beneath Heaven can compete with it.

**To cultivate one’s nature**, nothing surpasses *clarity of mind*.
**To preserve one’s body**, nothing surpasses *avoiding harm*.
Thus, by bending and curving one’s talents,
By adjusting speech and silence,
By conforming to what is fitting,
And not contending with things,
One does not lose the **Great Whole**,
So one achieves the **Virtue of Non-Contention**—hence it is said: **The crooked becomes whole**.

The **keen** and **clear** perceive all things,
The **wise** discern their distinctions,
Yet they abandon them without complaint,
And endure adversity without pity.
Their **straightness** is like an arrow,
Their **truth** is like a taut string.
Ever yielding yet harmonizing with the waves,
Like the inchworm’s gradual unfolding,
They bend and submit without losing their **straightness**—
