Thus they may be called **Great Straightness**—hence it is said: **The twisted becomes straight**.

Rivers and valleys are **hollow** and low,
Yet water flows toward them.
Though one might wish not to be **full**,
It cannot be avoided.

In humility and modesty,
All **virtues** gather to one—hence it is said: **The hollow becomes full**.

The **Perfect Sage** fragments his **virtue**,
Ruinously thins his body,
Makes **Great Whiteness** seem like shame,
And **True Authenticity** appear as flaw—
Then all beneath Heaven gladly embrace him without tiring.
His Dao is ever **renewed**.

The *Yangzi* [text] asks: **"How does the ruined become renewed?"**
To **renew** is to **inherit**,
To **ruin** is to **augment**—
Thus one may be said to **renew his virtue daily**,
Without being harmed by external things—hence it is said: **The ruined becomes renewed**.

The Dao does not value complexity.
The more one hears and sees,
The less one adheres to its simplicity.
The **easy** is easy to follow,
The **simple** is easy to obey—
**Ease and simplicity** attained,
All beneath Heaven’s principles are attained.

Those who **master life’s preservation**,
With **few thoughts, few deliberations**,
**Few desires, few fixations**,
Hold fast without losing—
Thus they attain it—hence it is said: **The few become many**.

Human nature loves victory,
And prizes excess.
Yet the Dao does not desire excess—
Excess brings **chaos and disturbance**.
Many paths lead to lost sheep,
Many methods lead to lost Dao.
Cultivating the body through **excessive thought** scatters the spirit,
**Excessive scheming** drowns wisdom—
This is not the way to **embrace Unity**—hence it is said: **The many bring confusion**.

**Master He** said: This chapter begins with **the crooked becomes whole** and ends with **whole and restored**—thus one sees the Dao’s **naturally unified Great Whole**, like rivers and valleys to the sea, unobstructed though they twist a million times.

It is not that the **crooked** can force the **whole**—
Rather, the **Great Whole**’s inherent nature transcends Heaven,
And the **crooked** fulfills its forms.

To know the **Great Whole** of Heaven and Earth,
All is contained within me.
Yielding to things, harmonizing with their waves,
I act thus in this world—
**Straightness** cannot be followed directly.
How could I ever violate the Dao for empty praise,
Like those of later ages who despise the **crooked**?

Thus, the four quarters are balanced and equal—
The Dao’s **straightness** is its core,
Yet **twistedness** preserves its **wholeness**.
The six **empties** flow freely—
The Dao’s **fullness** is its core,
Yet **hollowness** preserves its **wholeness**—
**Humility receives benefit**.
Enduring ancient times without aging—
The Dao’s **renewal** is its core,
Yet **ruin** preserves its **wholeness**—
**Hidden yet clear**.

**The few become many**—**Unity**.
**The many bring confusion**—**Diversity**.

**Master Lin** said: Only by mastering the **crooked** can one achieve the **whole**,
Only by mastering the **twisted** can one achieve the **straight**,
Only by mastering the **hollow** can one achieve the **full**,
Only by mastering the **ruined** can one achieve the **renewed**,
Only by mastering the **few** can one achieve the **many**—
These all mean:
By embracing **insufficiency**, one achieves **excess**,
By embracing **true emptiness**, one achieves **true presence**.
**The few become many**—
Yet these three words are further refined into two sentences:
**The few become many, the many bring confusion**.

Thus the Sage **embraces Unity** as the model for all beneath Heaven.

**Master Lü** said: **Unity** is humanity’s true nature.
Yet the Dao’s **Unity** has many kinds—
One must not fail to know them.

There is the **Unity** of the Dao,
The **Unity** of **non-Unity**.
There is the **Unity** of the Spirit,
The **Unity** of **True Authenticity**.
There is the **Unity** of *qi*,
The **Unity** of **One without Unity**.
There is the **Unity** of Water,
The **Unity** of **Heaven’s One generating Water**.

This **embrace of Unity** refers to the **Unity** of **True Authenticity**.
To know **Unity** is to know **non-Unity**.
In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor journeyed to Mount Éméi to meet the **Celestial True Sage**,
Who said: *"I beg you to teach the Dao of **True Unity**!"*
The **Celestial True Sage** replied: *"This is the most profound teaching of the Dao family.
Its scripture is hidden within the **Five Cities**,
And I have heard it says:
**Unity** lies within the **Northern Pole’s Great Abyss**,
With the **Hall of Brightness** before,
And the **Vermilion Palace** behind.
Majestic are the **Flowering Canopy**,
And the **Golden Hall’s arched roof**—
This is the **True Unity**’s essence."*

When it speaks of the **Northern Pole’s Great Abyss**,
It refers to the **True Unity**’s divine chamber—
Located in the **ear’s aperture**,
Before the **Hall of Brightness**,
Behind the **Jade Pillow**,
Beneath the **Flowering Canopy**,
And above the **Vermilion Palace**—
The **Vermilion Palace** is the **Dirt Pellet** [泥丸],
And the **Celestial Sovereign** [天帝] is the **Uppermost Red Child**’s dwelling.
It is said the ears belong to the kidneys—
Hence it is the **Northern Pole’s Great Abyss**.

The *Xisheng Jing* [西升经] states:
*"When one attains **Unity**,
All affairs are complete.
Knowing it and acting upon it,
One may follow the **True Authenticity**’s method—
Hence it is said: **Embrace Unity as the model for all beneath Heaven**."*

**Master He** said: The Sage **embraces Unity** as the world’s model.
**Unity** is the Dao’s origin—
The **Great Whole**’s naturally unified essence.

Not self-displaying, thus **clear**,
Not self-asserting, thus **manifest**,
Not self-praising, thus **functional**,
Not self-exalting, thus **enduring**.

For only in **non-contention**,
None beneath Heaven can contend with it.

**Master Wu** said: **Self-displaying** is like **self-flaunting**,
**Clear** refers to the **Spirit’s clarity**.
Not **self-displaying** means
Using **obscurity to achieve clarity**.

**Master Wu** said:
*"Self-asserting"* is like *"self-praising"*—*"manifest"* refers to its illumination.
*"Not self-asserting"* means
Obscure yet ever more luminous.

To boast of one’s deeds is *"self-praising"*—*"deeds"* refers to accomplished works.
To arrogate one’s superiority is *"self-exalting"*—*"superiority"* means surpassing others’ abilities.

Not self-displaying, not self-asserting,
Not self-praising, not self-exalting—
Yet with *"non-contention"* as its culmination,
These four are all metaphors for *"non-contention."*

**Master Lin** said:
Not self-displaying, not self-asserting, not self-exalting—
All express the intent of *"not possessing what one possesses."*
If I contend not with the world,
Then what is there to contend for?

**Master Li** said:
From *"not self-displaying"* onward,
All admonish against excess and advocate humility.
Through humility and submission,
There is no contention—
Thus preserving one’s foundation.
The ancients’ saying *"yielding preserves wholeness"*—
Was it mere empty words?
Truly whole, and thus it returns.

**Master Wu** said:
The phrase *"yielding preserves wholeness"*—
Was an ancient saying, which Laozi cited
As the opening of this chapter.
Within the chapter, *"Embrace Unity"* is the guiding thread,
And *"yielding preserves wholeness"* below, along with the five *"non-contention"* points,
Totaling ten matters—
All encompassed by *"yielding preserves wholeness."*
Thus, it is restated at the chapter’s end:
*"The ancients’ saying ‘yielding preserves wholeness’—*
Was it mere empty speech without substance?
Rather, because it is sincere and complete,
Its effects return to wholeness."*

**Master Dong** said:
Laozi merely transmitted, not composed—
Sayings like *"yielding preserves wholeness"* were ancient texts.
This reinforces that his words were not baseless,
Urging with deep sincerity.
If scholars truly grasp the Way of yielding and bending,
Then the Dao’s completeness and reality
All return to them.
The Dao is inherently human—
Thus *"return"* means restoring to its origin.

---
**Chapter 23**
*Rare speech aligns with Nature.*
A gusting wind does not last the morning,
A sudden rain does not last the day.
Who does this?
The heavens and the earth.

「The heavens and the earth cannot endure even this,
Yet would humanity die from it?
**Master He** said:
The text states: *"Listening yet not hearing is called 'rare'"*—
This "rare" refers to what?
It means *nature* itself, the speech of the Great Dao.

Here, the sentence should end with "rare" alone,
Not to be paired with "rare speech" against "much speech"—
That would be a shallow understanding.

Those who study the Dao/Way cultivate their mind in stillness,
Merge their *qi* with emptiness,
Boundless and formless, silent and void,
Thus the opening line speaks of *rare* alone.

The "rare" of the Great Sound—
This is *nature* itself.
Saying *rare*, saying *nature*—
This is the mystery of the Dao.

A gusting wind and sudden rain do not last the day—
Gusting winds and sudden rains,
These are the *qi* of reckless haste,
Thus they vanish quickly.

The *qi* of gusting and suddenness is not *nature*,
Then it cannot endure.
The heavens and the earth cannot even endure this,
Yet how much more so for humanity?

**Master Wu** said:
*"Listening yet not hearing is called 'rare'"*—
"Rare" means speechlessness.
Those who attain the Dao forget speech,
Following only their own *nature*.
A gusting wind is a violent, swift wind;
From dawn to midday is what is meant by *lasting the morning*.
Sudden rain is a fierce, hasty rain;
From dawn to nightfall is what is meant by *lasting the day*.
To act rashly and speak without restraint,
Like the heavens and the earth’s gusting winds and sudden rains—
These are all contrary to *nature*’s constancy,
Becoming strange and unnatural.

The heavens and the earth, contrary to *nature*’s constancy,
Cannot endure even this,
Not even lasting the morning or the day—
Yet how much more so for humanity,
If it cannot follow *nature*’s constancy?
Is this possible?

**Master Li** said:
Gusting winds and sudden rains—
This is a metaphor for reckless movement and hasty progress,
Far from lasting.

**Master Lü** said:
Gusting winds and sudden rains—
These are metaphors for things that do not last,
For the *Tàishàng* warns against
Excessive joy, anger, sorrow, and delight—
When emotions erupt without measure,
Movement and stillness lose their proper order.

The "rare" is the Dao/Way of what cannot be seen.
Thus, those who engage in the Dao—
The Daoists align with the Dao,
Those of *virtue/power* align with *virtue/power*,
And those who stray align with loss.»

**Master Wu** said:
To engage in the Dao means to take the Dao as one’s affair.
The Dao refers to those who possess the Dao,
*Virtue/power* refers to those who possess *virtue/power*,
And loss refers to the common and lowly.

What they do cannot but have some failing.
This aligns closely with *Zhuāngzi*’s *Qíwù Lùn* ["Equating Things"], which means to harmonize with them, merging as one without contradiction or distinction.
Only by following their own nature and speaking rarely can one achieve this,
So all—high, middle, and low—are seen as equal, without contradiction or distinction.

Indeed, those of the Dao and *virtue/power*,
Are one with me,
And mutually verify and confirm each other—
How could there be separation?

As for those of loss,
Though others may call them lost,
They themselves believe they are correct,
And indeed, they have their own natural correctness.

How could one not follow their own correctness,
And instead rashly speak against it?

*Zhuāngzi* says that silence is equating,
But equating with speech is not equating,
And speech with equating is not equating—
Thus, there is no speech,
Which is also Laozi’s intent of rare speech, natural harmony, and mysterious unity.

**Master Li** said:
Those who engage in the Dao
Are gentle and harmonious in *virtue/power*,
Merged with all things,
Naturally resonating and responding to them,
And things naturally responding in kind.

Again, those of the Dao align with the Dao—
A unified vision as one body.
Those of *virtue/power* align with *virtue/power*—
Meaning they do not harbor dualistic views.
Those of loss align with loss—
Without discrimination.

**Master Lü** said:
The Perfect One aligns with the Dao, with *virtue/power*, and with loss,
Yet does not cling to things.
They know not of the Dao’s existence,
Nor of *virtue/power*’s existence,
Nor of loss’s existence—
Transcending the physical and material,
Free from worries of gain or loss.

Those who align with the Dao,
The Dao also finds them.
Those who align with *virtue/power*,
*Virtue/power* also finds them.
Those who align with loss,
Loss also finds them.

**Master Wu** said:
Those of *virtue/power*,
See me as one with them,
And mutually verify and confirm each other—
What separation could there be?

As for those of loss,
Though I see them as one with me,
Perhaps they cling to their own narrow correctness,
And through this, awaken me to the broader truth—
Thus, all three kinds of people
Confirm my understanding through their own ways.

Yet only those who truly possess the Dao can achieve this.
If the Dao’s essence is deficient within oneself,
Then one’s treatment of others will not reflect its true nature,
And distinctions of right and wrong will be forcibly imposed—
How could one achieve such mysterious unity?

**Master Li** said:
Self-perception, self-affirmation, self-praise, and self-boasting—
All are rooted in private intent.
Private intent solidifies into harm to the public—
How could this endure?

Thus, those who possess the Dao do not dwell here.
"Excess food, superfluous movement"—
Like leftover food or parasitic growths,
They are what the world despises.

**Master Lü** said:
The Perfect One’s Dao-heart is boundless and void,
They gaze upon things with playful detachment,
Treating life and death as a single thread,
And possibility and impossibility as a single continuum.
They look back upon those who self-perceive, self-affirm, self-praise, and self-boast—
Like discarded leftovers or superfluous actions,
What heaven rejects, what earth transforms,
What humanity despises—
And much less so for the Perfect One,
Who exhausts principles and fathoms nature,
Reaching the pinnacle of the Dao—
They never consider such things,
And should avoid them for life.

**Master Lin** said:
Self-perception, self-affirmation, self-praise, and self-boasting—
All are cases of holding onto one’s own while failing to transform.

**Master Wu** said:
Those who cling to self-perception
Will never truly be enlightened,
Those who cling to self-affirmation
Will never truly shine,
Those who cling to self-praise
Will never achieve true merit,
Those who cling to self-boasting
Will never have lasting virtue—
Just as those who stand on tiptoe
Cannot truly stand,
And those who stride too widely
Cannot truly walk.

"Excess food, superfluous movement"—
Some say: *xíng* (行) should be read as *xíng* (形), for ancient characters were interchangeable.

**Master Sima** said:
Discard the surplus of food,
And thus one may avoid causing others revulsion.
Remove the superfluous growths of flesh,
And thus one may avoid causing others ugliness.

**Chapter Twenty-Five:** There is a thing that blends into unity,
Born before heaven and earth.

**Master Lü** said:
The *Taiyi* (太易) is the state before *qi* (炁) was seen.
The *Tai’chu* (太初) is the beginning of *qi*.
The *Taishǐ* (太始) is the beginning of form.
The *Taìsù* (太素) is the beginning of substance.
When *qi*, form, and substance are all present yet not yet separated,
It is called *hùn lún* (浑沦).
Neither clear nor turbid, it remains serene and ever-present;
Neither high nor low, it cannot be divided by myriad streams.
Heaven and earth arise from it,
And all things transform through it.
The perfected one embodies it,
Thus returning to the root and recovering the vital breath,
Restoring one’s original nature to its beginning.

Within the daze of *huánglǔn* (恍惚),
Ten thousand harmonies merge into one,
The true essence of all things is grasped,
And their original form is revealed.
Before the heavens and earth were formed,
Before the *hún dùn* (混沌) took shape,
This is the source of the Dao.
In humans, it manifests as the nature of *miào jué* (妙觉),
Enduring through countless *kālpas* (劫) without interruption.
Hence, it is said: *"There is a thing that blends into unity,
Born before heaven and earth."*

**Master Lin** said:
*"There is a thing that blends into unity"*—this is the Dao.
The *Wuji* (无极) gives rise to the *Taiji* (太极),
Its birth precedes that of heaven and earth.
It speaks of heaven and earth arising from this Dao.

**Master Wu** said:
*"There is a thing that blends into unity"*—the "thing" refers to the Dao,
And "blends into unity" means complete without division or distinction.
*"Born before heaven and earth"*—just as the first chapter speaks of *"the beginning of heaven and earth"*,
And the fourth chapter speaks of *"the time before the Emperor of Forms"* (象帝).

Silent and boundless,
It stands alone yet never alters,
Circulates endlessly yet never falters,
Capable of being the mother of all under heaven.

**Master Lü** said:
Silent—without sound,
Boundless—without form.
Empty and profound, vast and obscure,
Neither past nor future,
Standing alone without counterpart,
Never changing or transforming—
What could pair with it?
Thus it can traverse the eight *huáng* (纮),
Endlessly adapt and respond,
Revolving without error.
All things have rise and decline,
The Dao knows no limits.
Things cannot see its movement yet it moves,
Cannot see its rest yet it rests.
Moving then resting, resting then moving,
Like an endless ring.
Things, great or small,
All turn toward the Dao.
Obtaining it brings wholeness,
Losing it brings harm.
Life and completion depend upon it.

**Master Lin** said:
Silent and boundless—
It cannot be seen.
Standing alone yet never altering—
It endures eternally without change.
Circulating endlessly yet never faltering—
It moves with vigor yet never ceases.
Capable of being the mother of all under heaven—
The origin from which all things arise.
Silent—utter stillness.
Boundless—vast emptiness.
It speaks of its formlessness and soundlessness.
Standing alone beyond all things,
Without equal or opposition,
Yet never varying or transforming—
Hence, *"never alters."*
Circulating through all things,
Reaching everywhere without end,
Yet never exhausted or failing—
Hence, *"never falters."*
Nurturing all things—
Hence, *"the mother of all under heaven."*

I do not know its name,
So I call it *Dao*.
Forcing a name upon it, I call it *Great*.
Great—it passes away,
Passing away—it grows distant,
Distant—it returns.

**Master Lin** said:
I do not know its name,
And no name can truly capture it.
Since it cannot be named, I call it *Dao*—
A substitute name.
Saying *Dao* is insufficient,
I force another name upon it—*Great*.
*Great* is also insufficient,
So I name it *Passing* (逝)—
Passing, like the movement of going,
Cannot be pursued or chased.
*Passing* is still insufficient,
So I force another name upon it—*Distant*.
Distant—it cannot be approached or clung to.
*Distant* is still insufficient,
So I force another name upon it—*Returning*.
Returning—it means returning to the root.

**Master Li** said:
*Great*—without bounds or measure,
*Passing*—without territory or frontier,
*Distant*—without limits or edge,
*Returning*—it gathers and brings back.

**Master He** said:
*Returning*—it means returning to the state of no-thing.

**Master Chái** said:
*Returning*—it means the Dao’s use returning to its original body.

Hence:
The Dao is great,
Heaven is great,
Earth is great,
The Sage is also great.
Within the realm, there are four *Great Ones*,
And the Sage is one of them.

**Master Wu** said:
The Sage refers to those who hold the position of enlightenment.
Though the Dao is the greatest of all time,
Nothing can compare to it.
This is why, when speaking of heaven, earth, and the Sage in relation to the Dao,
It is said there are four *Great Ones*—why?

Because:
Heaven obtains this Dao and thus becomes heaven,
Earth obtains this Dao and thus becomes earth,
The Sage obtains this Dao and thus becomes the Sage.
This is how they become great—
Because they possess this Dao.

**Master Li** said:
Humans only know of the four *Great Ones* within the realm,
But do not realize that their own self is even greater.

**Master Lü** said:
The perfected one likens it to oneself.
The Dao is the numinous and mysterious void,
Heaven is the *Shén gōng* (神宫) of the celestial valley,
Earth is the *Zhēn tǔ* (真土) of the Yellow Court,
The Sage is the *Yuán shén* (元神) of true nature.
The *Yuán shén*—this is the heart,
Never departing from the four-square inch space.
Penetrating heaven and earth,
Spanning all directions—
This too can be called *Great*.

Humans follow Earth,
Earth follows Heaven,
Heaven follows the Dao,
The Dao follows *Zìrán* (自然).

**Master Lü** said:
The Sage is profound and silent, like stillness,
Sharing virtue with the *Yīn* (阴).
In governing, it shows no partiality—
This is following Earth.
Earth is steadfast and unchanging,
Firm yet balanced, like the center,
Sharing movement with the *Yáng* (阳).
This is following Heaven.
Heaven is exalted and just,
Revealing true signs of the Sage,
Responding to all transformations,
Yet only virtue aids it—
This is following the Dao.
Only the Dao lets things follow their nature,
Achieving effortless spontaneity.
Hold fast without losing it,
And it merges with the numinous.
The Dao’s body is complete and free-flowing,
Entering and exiting without hindrance—
This is what is meant by *Zìrán*.
Who could say it is not so?
Thus it is said: *"Humans follow Earth,
Earth follows Heaven,
Heaven follows the Dao,
The Dao follows Zìrán."*

**Master Wu** said:
The reason humans are great
Is because they obtain this Dao and align with Earth—
Hence, *"follow Earth."*
The reason Earth is great
Is because it obtains this Dao and aligns with Heaven—
Hence, *"follow Heaven."*

---

Heaven’s greatness lies in its unity with the Dao—
Hence, *"follow the Dao."*
The Dao’s greatness lies in its *Zìrán*—
Hence, *"follow Zìrán."*
There is no *Zìrán* apart from the Dao—
*Zìrán* is simply the nameless.

**Master Lin** said:
Its meaning is only that the Dao reaches its utmost in *Zìrán*—
Such an exposition may be called a profound theory.

**Master He** said:
The "humans follow Earth" refers to the Sage.

**Chapter Twenty-Six**
The heavy is the root of the light,
The still is the sovereign of the restless.
Thus the Sage never walks a day without his baggage train.

**Master Wu** said:
*Root* is like *foundation*; *restless* means *movement*.
*Sovereign* means *master*—
Speaking of the light relying on the heavy as its foundation,
And movement being governed by stillness as its master.

The Sage’s journey:
When traveling in peace, he rides in a light carriage,
When leading an army, he rides in a war chariot—
Both are light vehicles.
Behind the light carriage comes the baggage train,
Carrying bedding, clothing, food, and supplies—
This is the heavy carriage.

Though he rides in a light carriage,
He never strays from the heavy baggage train all day,
Not daring to rush forward in his light carriage
And abandon what follows behind.

This is what is meant by *"the light rooted in the heavy."*

**Master Lü** said:
The heavy is what bears things,
The light is what is borne by things.
To govern the light, one must not lose sight of the heavy.
The still is what commands things,
The restless is what is enslaved by things.
To govern the restless, one must not lose sight of the still.
For stillness brings the power to control the light,
And stillness provides the means to restrain the restless.
Thus, the ruler of a state
Should establish depth, weight, and tranquility as his foundation,
Then the people will be at ease without fear of disorder.

Even as he walks all day,
He never dares to leave behind the heavy baggage train—
For it contains his clothing and food.
If he abandons it and strays far,
He risks exhaustion along the way.
How could one, in establishing oneself and following the Dao,
For even an instant forget stillness and weight?

The Perfect Man, in his own conduct,
Holds weight and stillness in highest regard,
And carefully avoids lightness and restlessness.
The heavy is the head,
The light is the limbs,
The still is the spirit,
The restless is the emotions.
Taking the virtue of stillness and weight,
And discarding the emotions of lightness and restlessness,
Then the sacred embryo rests in ease,
And all day long, he moves as if riding in a comfortable carriage.

Though the Great Dao is smooth,
He does not dare to rush forward lightly.

**Master Lin** said:
*Root* speaks of the light arising from the heavy,
*Sovereign* speaks of the restless being mastered by stillness.
Though there are splendid sights to observe,
His dwelling is serene and detached.

**Master Wu** said:
*Burn* means *leisure*; *dwelling* means *residence*.
Even in realms of honor and splendor,
Where one might wander in observation,
He remains detached, untethered by any concern—
This is what is meant by *"movement governed by stillness."*

**Master Lin** said:
What his mind perceives
Is the utmost beauty in all under Heaven—
Hence, *"splendid sights."*
Yet he resides in it with ease,
Thus standing beyond lightness, heaviness, restlessness, and stillness.
*Burn* means *ease*; *dwelling* means *residence*—
As in my writings: *"ease in movement, breadth in dwelling."*

**Master He** said:
Though there are towering heights and splendid sights,
His leisure is serene and unburdened—
Nothing in all things can disturb his mind.
What more is there to governing the world?

The *Listed Child* observed the Dao without seeing—
The utmost of stillness and weight.
How could a ruler of ten thousand chariots
Make his body light to the world?
Lightness leads to losing his ministers,
Restlessness leads to losing his sovereignty.

**Master Wu** said:
The state’s power rests on the heavy governing the light,
Military strategy relies on stillness overcoming movement—
Just as in a person’s cultivation of body and mind,
One must root oneself in the heavy
And govern with stillness.

**Master Lin** said:
If a ruler of ten thousand chariots
Does not know his own weight,
He cannot stand detached from things.
He will inevitably fall into lightness and restlessness.
Losing his ministers means he cannot subdue the world,
Losing his sovereignty means he has abandoned what he governs—
To make his body light to the world
Is to treat the world as heavy and himself as light.
Not to make his body light is to know the Dao,
And to know the Dao is to know *Zìrán*.

**Master Lü** said:
For the Perfect Man, taking from himself—
The heart is the sovereign,
*Qi* is the minister.
Lightness causes the heart to move and deplete his *qi*,
Restlessness causes *qi* to dissipate and unsettle the heart.
Internally, he cultivates himself;
Externally, he commands things.
The body of stillness and weight,
The division of nature and principle—
Thus it is said: *"Lightness leads to losing his ministers,
Restlessness leads to losing his sovereignty."*

**Chapter Twenty-Seven**
The adept’s path leaves no tracks,
The adept’s speech has no flaws,
The adept’s strategy requires no calculations,
The adept’s closure needs no locks yet cannot be opened,
The adept’s binding needs no cords yet cannot be undone.

**Master Wu** said:
*"Shàn"* means to excel in one’s affairs.
*"Zhé"* refers to the tracks of a chariot.
*"Jì"* means footprints.
*"Xiá"* means flaws or blemishes.
*"Zhé"* means blameworthy errors.
*"Jì"* means the counting of numbers.
*"Chóucè"* are counting rods—calculating tools made of bamboo.
*"Guānjué"* are door-blocking bars: the horizontal piece is called *guān*, the vertical *jué*.
*"Shéngyuē"* means cords: when twisted together they form a rope (*shéng*), when used to bind things they are called *yuē*.

The traveler must leave tracks upon the earth,
The speaker must have flaws that can be pointed to,
The calculator must use counting rods,
The door-closer must use bars,
The binder must use cords.
Yet these are the deeds of ordinary men.
But when the Daoist observes them,
How could they be called *shàn*?

Indeed, the one who excels in movement
Does so by *not* moving and thus leaves no tracks.
The one who excels in speech
Does so by *not* speaking and thus has no flaws.
The one who excels in calculation
Does so by *not* calculating and thus needs no rods.
The one who excels in closing
Does so by *not* closing and thus has no bars—yet what is closed cannot be opened.
The one who excels in binding
Does so by *not* binding and thus has no cords—yet what is bound cannot be undone.

These five matters are used as analogies
To introduce the idea of the Sage’s excellence in salvation.

**Master Lü** said:
The numinous has no fixed form,
The Dao has no fixed body.
It arrives without movement,
It is swift without haste.
The Perfect Man observes the Dao of Heaven,
Follows Heaven’s actions,
Acts through *wuwei* (non-action),
Moves where nothing moves,
Yet without leaving his courtyard,
His virtue spreads and permeates—
Like wind sweeping through all things,
What tracks could there be to follow?

Moreover, those who cannot speak well
Are burdened by their words,
For it is clear they have not forgotten speech.
Flaws are the defects of jade,
Errors are the faults of speech—
How could they know the purpose of speech?
The Perfect Man, with his virtue of daily renewal,
Expresses himself in teaching the world,
Harmonizing with *Zìrán*,
So how could there be flaws or blame to mar him?
Thus it is said: *"The adept’s path leaves no tracks,
The adept’s speech has no flaws."*

The Dao cannot be sought through measurements.
Now, if one uses counting rods to know,
How could it be the self-attained mystery?
But the adept in calculation is otherwise:
Guarding the One in *wuwei*,
He can embrace all things,
Moving freely without falling into numbers,
Letting great and small, many and few,
Each fulfill their proper measure,
Without waste or loss—
What need has he for counting rods?
Thus it is said: *"The adept’s strategy requires no calculations."*

Moreover, the adept in closing
Has six openings and four exits,
Yet even if Heaven and Earth shift in secrecy,
Ghosts and spirits respond in vain—
They cannot find the door,
Nor enter by any means.
For his form is whole, his essence restored,
His orifices sealed, his gates closed—
No realm can penetrate.
Though no bars are used, it cannot be opened.
Thus it is said: *"The adept’s closure needs no locks yet cannot be opened."*

Ah! The adhesion of glues, the congealing of water,
The merging of affairs—
Even without cords, they cannot be undone.
The Perfect Man is simple, empty, and boundless,
His spirit concentrated, his *qi* settled,
Serene and tranquil—
Even Heaven and Earth cannot violate him.
Thus it is said: *"The adept’s binding needs no cords yet cannot be undone."*

**Master Li** said:
If one is careful in speech and action,
There will be no tracks, no blame.
The calculations of the heart—
Can they be reckoned?
The closures of the heart—
Can they be opened?
The bindings of the heart—
Can they be undone?

**Master Lin** said:
These five are all metaphors.
Their meaning is simply this:
If one takes *Zìrán* as the Dao,
There is no force to contain it,
No place to advance it.
Thus the Sage always excels in salvation,
And so there is no abandonment of people.
He always excels in saving things,
And so there is no abandonment of things—
This is called *"clinging to the bright."*

**Master Wu** said:
The Sage’s salvation of people and things
Is to *not* save as salvation—
Just as in the earlier analogies,
To excel is to *not* do the deed.

Indeed, where there is salvation,
There must be abandonment.
Suppose the Sage saves a hundred thousand people,
A hundred thousand things—
Yet outside that number,
All are abandoned and unsaved.
But the Sage’s excellence in salvation is otherwise:
He saves none at all.
If he saves none,
Then he abandons none.
One does not see him saving this while abandoning that,
So there is no one abandoned and unsaved,
No thing abandoned and unsaved.

**Master He** said:
The Dao pervades the world,
Encompassing all things.
All desire to be transformed by it,
All desire to be completed by it—
Yet no tracks are seen,
For its response is numinous and wondrous.

From the *Guān Yīn Zǐ*:
*"Dark and dense within the Great Transformation,
Nature is One alone.*
He who knows that Nature is One
Has no self, no other—
Thus all things fulfill their nature without remainder,
The Sage’s Dao of *wuwei*—
This is called *clinging to the bright.*"

The luminous and clear nature
Is what all share,
Yet it is not fully bright.
The Sage guides them with flexibility,
Restoring their luminous Heaven
To an unbroken, undying state—
Those who follow are the *shàn* (excellents),
Thus it is called *clinging to the bright.*

**Master Lü** said:
The Perfect Man withdraws his gaze, returns his hearing,
The empty chamber gives birth to whiteness,
The settled space emits light—
Like a lamp’s brilliance,
Transmitted without end,
Thus it is called *clinging to the bright.*

**Master Dòng** said:
To rescue people in peril
Is only to rescue their forms—
And even then, it may not be comprehensive.
How could this be called *shàn*?

Only those who cling to *wuwei*,
Lost in their nature’s darkness,
While I possess the capacity to contain them—
I envelop them with my prior enlightenment,
Awaken them so their Heavenly light shines forth,
Like a lamp’s transmission without end—
The more I give to others, the more my own brightness grows,
Boundless in measure.
Thus the *shàn* become teachers for the un-*shàn*,
And the un-*shàn* become resources for the *shàn.*

**Master Wu** said:
The *shàn* are those who excel in their affairs.
A *teacher* is one whom others revere and follow as a model.
A *resource* is like wealth given to others,
So they may rely on it and achieve success.

Because of their lack of virtue, others attain my reputation for virtue—
thus it is called *resource*.

**Master He** said:
The un-virtuous learn from the virtuous and receive their teachings,
while the virtuous use the un-virtuous as a means to manifest their transformation—
this is how the Dao endlessly generates and sustains itself.
This is called *transmitting brightness*.

**Master Lin** said:
If there were no un-virtuous in the world,
who would know the virtuous for their virtue?
Thus, the un-virtuous are the *resource* for the virtuous.
*Resource* means relying on them to distinguish oneself.

**Master Lü** said:
*Teacher* and *resource* are those who aid others and are also aided in return—
a reciprocal relationship.

**Master Ni** said:
That the virtuous are teachers to the un-virtuous is certain,
but that the un-virtuous are *resources* for the virtuous—
Confucius said:
*"When three men walk together, there is one I can take as my teacher.
I follow his virtues and correct my flaws."*
Even Confucius regarded the un-virtuous as teachers.
How much more so for the wise?

*Resource* means *aid*—
by observing their flaws, I reflect and correct my errors to follow virtue.
This is called *resource*.

To not esteem one’s teacher,
to not cherish one’s *resource*—
even the wise may be utterly lost.
This is called the *ultimate subtlety*.

**Master Lü** said:
Though one follows a teacher to find direction,
upon sudden enlightenment to the ultimate truth,
subject and object are forgotten—
neither esteem nor attachment remains.
As in Liezi, who taught by Lao Shang and befriended Boyao,
later saying:
*"I do not know if my teacher was truly my teacher,
or if my friend was truly my friend—
within and without, all is complete."*
Thus it is said:
To not esteem one’s teacher,
to not cherish one’s *resource*—
only then can one perceive the nature of nature,
open the Heaven of Heaven,
and penetrate deeply into the Dao,
so that even the wise are utterly lost in its crossing.
Though they devise clever schemes, they find no application;
though they speak profound words, they find no understanding.
This is truly the *ultimate subtlety*—
thus it is said:
Even the wise may be utterly lost,
this is called the *ultimate subtlety*.

**Master Li** said:
Forgetting teacher and *resource*,
discarding cleverness and abandoning wisdom,
acting the fool all day.

---
**Chapter Twenty-Eight**
Know the *heroic* yet hold to the *receptive*—
be the valley of the world.

To be the valley of the world,
ever abiding in virtue,
returning to the infant.

**Master Lü** said:
The *heroic* is strong and active,
the *receptive* is gentle and still.
The sage knows his own exalted dignity—
if he indulges in rigid dominance, calamity arises.
Better to embrace the yielding and weak,
to be humble and good at submission,
so that the world’s virtue returns to him,
like water flowing downward.

To be the valley of the world,
overflowing like a gentle stream,
spreading to the four directions—
what cannot it receive?
Yet still maintaining his constant virtue,
pure and unadulterated,
like an infant before speech—
this is called *perfect virtue*.
So too for oneself.

The *heroic* is the yang within yin,
in man, the *essence*.
The *receptive* is the yin within yang,
in man, the *spirit*.
Knowing the *heroic* preserves one’s essence,
holding to the *receptive* nurtures one’s spirit.
When the spirit is luminous, all things come;
when the heart is empty, harmonious *qi* returns—
like water flowing into a valley,
bearing all filth,
comparable to the innocence of a babe.

**Master Wu** said:
*Heroic* means strong and forceful,
*receptive* means gentle and weak,
*infant* means without knowledge.

**Master Lin** said:
The word *know* implies action without action—
knowing the *heroic* yet holding to the *receptive* is not seeking victory.

*Constant virtue* is the *constant Way*—
*not departing* means without interruption.
*Infant*—without knowledge.

**Master Li** said:
Knowing the *heroic* yet holding to the *receptive* is not arrogance.

**Master He** said:
When the Heavenly gates open and close,
can one truly be *receptive*?
I would rather remain still and humble—
weakness overcomes strength.

**Master Ni** said:
First, one must *know* the *heroic*,
then speak of holding to the *receptive*—
it is not that one does not know the *heroic*’s strength,
but that one can embrace the *receptive*’s gentleness—
this is what is precious.
If one only knew the *heroic* and held to the *receptive* without understanding,
even the world’s foolish and weak could claim to know the Way—
how could this be?

Thus, one must first know and yet be able to refrain—
this is the mark of the *Daoist*.

The *infant* is a metaphor for the beginning of the Way.

---
Know the *bright* yet hold to the *dark*—
be the model of the world.

To be the model of the world,
ever abiding in constant virtue without error,
returning to the *Unbounded*.

**Master Lü** said:
*Bright* illustrates the Dao’s manifestation,
*dark* illustrates its return.
The sage knows his heart is pure and unblemished,
wise yet not acting—
only then can he embrace its depths,
holding wisdom like foolishness,
concealing his light like darkness,
applying it to himself and others,
becoming the world’s model.
Once established as the standard and measure,
upright yet not arbitrary,
public yet not partial,
acting with constancy—
no deviation or fault remains.
In the body, *bright* is the metal’s domain,
*dark* is the water’s reservoir.
Knowing metal and water generate and complete each other,
as the foundation of life,
one must refine the elixir,
preserve the primordial essence,
circulate and transform—
achieving the true body,
becoming the world’s model for cultivation.
Moreover, the constant virtue is self-aware and effortless,
direct and comprehensive—
no flaw, no error,
spirit in perfect accord—
how could it ever be exhausted?
Thus it is said:
*Bright* illustrates clarity,
*dark* illustrates silence.
Though one knows one’s own clarity,
one must also hold to silence.
Like darkness with nothing to see,
thus becoming the world’s model—
virtue remains constant.

**Master He** said:
Do not tread upon the *bright*—
this ensures eternal preservation.
I would rather take the *dark* as virtue,
making clarity like obscurity.

**Master Wu** said:
*Bright* means luminous,
*dark* means obscure,
*Unbounded*—without limit.

**Master Li** said:
Those who know the *bright* yet hold to the *dark*—
they do not reveal themselves.
Those who know their *honor* yet hold to their *humiliation*—
they become the world’s *valley*.

As the world’s *valley*,
constant virtue is then complete—
returning to the *Uncarved*.

**Master Lü** said:
The coming of *honor* and *humiliation* is beyond human foresight,
yet they arrive and depart as they will.
At first, there is no entanglement.
The Sage knows the exalted station of their place—
to bask in *honor* yet guard against *humiliation*,
to accept filth and conceal stains,
like the *valley*’s emptiness.
When the *valley* is empty,
nothing is excluded—
no distinction between self and other,
inner virtue overflowing,
one nature pure and unchanging—
returning to the *Uncarved*.

**Master Li** said:
To know *honor* yet hold to *humiliation*—
this is not to exalt oneself.
To guard the *female* and embrace the *dark*—
this is not to reveal one’s own clarity.

**Master He** said:
The greatest *bright* is like *humiliation*,
the most *virtue* is like insufficiency—
I would rather endure filth and misfortune,
for loss brings gain.
The terms *infant* (*婴儿*), *Unbounded* (*无极*), and *Uncarved* (*朴*)—
all speak of the Dao’s return to its origin.

**Master Lin** said:
*Complete* refers to fullness,
*Uncarved* is the *Great Uncarved* (*太朴*)—
the beginning of heaven and earth.

**Master Wu** said:
*Honor* means exalted nobility,
*humiliation* means lowly abasement,
*Uncarved* means raw wood yet to become a vessel.
The meaning of this passage is to preserve constant virtue
and return to the original, beginningless Dao.
Constant virtue is the virtue of *effortless emptiness*—
thus, one prefers the *dark* over the *bright*,
the *female* over the *male*,
*humiliation* over *honor*.
Those who know and uphold it,
though aware of the other’s worth,
yet choose to guard and remain in the lowly—
when they fully embody this *effortless emptiness*,
they may return to the beginningless Dao.
As *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Virtue reaches its peak when it aligns with the original"*—
the terms *Unbounded*, *infant*, and *Uncarved*
all illustrate the beginningless Dao.
When the *Uncarved* is dispersed, it becomes vessels—
the Sage employs it,
and thus becomes the *official* of heaven and earth.
Hence, the great *measure* does not *sever*.

**Master Lin** said:
The *Great Uncarved* is dispersed, and only then do vessels arise—
what is beyond form is called *Dao*,
what is beneath form is called *vessel*.
The Sage uses the *Dao* beyond form to govern what is beneath form,
and thus all things between heaven and earth fulfill their roles.
The Sage governs the Three Powers to command all things—
though their duties overlap,
they still obey my will—
this is the *official* of all things between heaven and earth.
As *Zhuangzi* says:
*"The Sage manages the roles of heaven and earth"*—
this is merely one aspect of the *Dao*’s governance.
*Sever* means *to divide*—
to govern things with the *Dao* is called the *great measure*,
and when measured, *Dao* and *vessel* do not separate—
this is the meaning of *acting without action*,
*effortless naturalness*.

**Master He** said:
The *Uncarved* is the origin of the *Dao*’s effortlessness,
the *vessel* is the name for things that are shaped by effort.
The Sage cannot prevent the *Uncarved* from becoming vessels,
yet skillfully employs them
to manage the roles of heaven and earth,
to lead the people and command all things—
though the *great measure* governs,
it does not *sever*—
it nurtures without harming,
the power of the *Dao* itself.

**Master Wu** said:
Where there is governance, one must use a blade to divide the whole—
yet the *great measure* governs by not governing,
thus it does not *sever*.
The *great scattering* is not scattering,
the *great use* is not using—
this is also like the *great measure*’s non-*severance*.

**Chapter XXIX**
When one seeks to take the world yet acts upon it,
I see their necessity as inevitable.
The world is a *divine vessel*—
it cannot be shaped.
Those who shape it will ruin it,
those who grasp it will lose it.

**Master He** said:
The scriptures speak of three warnings against the world—
to awaken the hearts of traitorous ministers and rebellious officials across ten thousand generations,
those who are blind to *authority* and *right*,
who transgress order and seize power recklessly,
who externally overestimate their strength,
yet internally fail to grasp *command*—
these are those who bring their own destruction,
and I show them their calamity as a warning.
Thus it is said:
*"Take the world by doing nothing"*—
do not harbor even a speck of *action* in your heart.
The world is a *great thing*, a *divine vessel*—
not something human strength can covet.
As King Wen held two-thirds of the world,
how could he ever have had the heart to *take* it?
As Yao’s *yellow house* was not his own doing,
and Shun and Yu ruled the world yet did not claim it—
how could they ever have had the heart to *grasp* it?

**Master Lü** said:
The Sage’s possession of the world is not *taking*—
their governance of the world is not *doing*.
Why so?
Because they never had the heart to *take* or *do*.
Thus they could harmonize with the world’s constant *Dao*,
assist in the natural unfolding of all things—
all things return to them,
not out of necessity, but willingly.
If one harbors the heart to *take* and *act* upon the world,
will they not rely on cleverness and force,
contradicting the *Dao* and losing its natural order?
The *divine vessel* is the supreme treasure,
the domain of heaven and earth’s *divine brilliance*—
it commands all existence,
subdues all movement—
indeed, it cannot be shaped.
If one pursues their own private gain,
interfering in the world’s affairs,
bound by things and claiming them as their own,
acting without knowing when to stop—
they will enter the realm of *worry* and *calamity*,
not only losing the highest truth,
but also their own authenticity—
why bother governing the world at all?
The human heart encompasses all the world’s marvels—
what is prized is its *naturalness*.
Once one acts upon it,
they can no longer remain *without action*—
humanity extinguishes the *Dao*,
reverence destroys *command*—
the *Dao* becomes even more unattainable.
In its original form,
it mirrors heaven and earth,
harmonizes with yin and yang—
this is the *divine vessel*.
The Yellow Emperor gazed southward and lost the *dark pearl*—
the seven orifices were pierced, and *Chaos* died—
this is the *Dao*’s unshapability.
Those who *shape* it desire its completion,
thus they ruin it.
Those who *grasp* it desire its possession,
thus they lose it.
If one remains *without action*, they will not ruin it—
if one does not *grasp*, they will not lose it—
thus they attain the *Dao*!

The **Li Family** says:
*"To act is to have affairs; not to act is to have no affairs. Those who grasp lose; those who act fail—this is the warning against action."*

The **Lin Family** says:
*"The divine vessel of the world—
how can it be attained by human strength?
When the Dao flourishes and virtue reaches its peak,
the world submits to it.
If one harbors the intention to seize the world and forcefully do good,
seeking to gain something,
then the unattainable becomes even more so.
For all things under heaven,
one must not seek them with intention.
Those who act will fail;
those who grasp will lose—
these are the burdens of intention.
All things either move forward or follow,
either warm with breath or chill with wind,
either prevail with strength or falter with weakness,
either succeed with elevation or collapse with ruin.
Thus, the Sage avoids excess,
avoids extravagance,
avoids extravagance—
this is the **He Family**’s teaching:
*What is lost to one is gained by another;
what is completed here is deficient there.
Loss and gain, completion and deficiency—
they cycle endlessly, as in the *Liezi*’s parable:
the praying mantis seizes the cicada,
unaware of the sparrow behind.
Thus, when one moves forward, another follows;
when one breathes warmth, another blows cold;
when one prevails with strength, another weakens it;
when one succeeds with elevation, another ruins it—
they guard each other like shadows to form,
like things guarding things.
How much more so the world, greater than all things,
can one have intention, action, or grasping?
Excess is overreaching—
the Dao takes weakness as its standard,
emptiness as its substance,
and avoids overreaching—
thus, to remove excess is to eliminate frivolous waste.
The Dao takes origin as its refinement,
things as its coarseness—
extravagance leads to insolence,
thus, to remove extravagance is to eliminate arrogant excess.
The Dao takes depth as its root,
simplicity as its measure,
and does not consider the world as extravagant—
thus, to remove extravagance is to avoid arrogance.
These three are all the extravagant hearts of action;
removing these three,
the Sage’s heart is empty—
the world cannot disturb it."*

The **Li Family** says:
*"Strength and weakness, elevation and ruin—
they mutually rely and overturn each other,
like shadows following form.
Where there is success, there is also failure—
thus, the Sage avoids greed for excess,
avoids extravagance,
avoids arrogance and extravagance,
and deeply warns future generations."*

The **Su Family** says:
*"Some move forward, some follow behind;
some breathe warmth, some blow cold;
some strengthen and benefit, some weaken and diminish;
some succeed with elevation, some ruin with collapse—
these are the natural ways of things,
inevitable forces that cannot be avoided."*

The **Wu Family** says:
*"This follows the earlier warning: *‘Those who grasp lose.’*
The Way of governing the world—
those who hold the world,
how can they long preserve it as their own without losing it?
The cycles of gain and loss, survival and ruin,
are like moving forward and following,
breathing warmth and blowing cold,
strength and weakness,
elevation and ruin—
these eight opposites interdependently arise.
The Sage knows these forces must reach this point,
and thus has a Way to respond.
All that is overabundant must decline—
only by preventing overabundance
can one avoid decline.
If one does not exceed in greed, extravagance, or arrogance,
then at the peak of abundance,
one can preserve the world.
*The Collected Commentaries on the Dao De Jing*, Volume Four concludes.
Volume Five begins."*

**Chapter Thirty: Those Who Assist the Sovereign with the Dao Do Not Use Military Might to Subdue the World**

Their affairs tend to return.

**Master Lü** says:
Those of the Dao who assist their sovereign must first establish virtue as their foundation. Armies and weapons are but the final means. The sovereign governs ministers with propriety, and ministers serve the sovereign with loyalty. If the ruler acts without artifice, the people transform themselves spontaneously. The common folk flourish in harmony, unaware of imperial might, yet they engage in warfare—how can this be the Dao?

War is an omen of calamity, and battle is a perilous affair. It is not the instrument of the virtuous. Used only when unavoidable, victory brings defeat, and gain brings harm. How can one justly subdue the weak or violently oppress the good? When beasts are cornered, they strike back; when things reach their extreme, they reverse. This is inevitable.

**Master Ni** says:
It does not say *"the sovereign"* but *"those who assist the sovereign with the Dao,"* meaning that even if the sovereign harbors intentions of war, if the great ministers do not comply, he may not necessarily act upon them.

**Master Lin** says:
To assist the sovereign while prioritizing military strength is not to know the Dao. War is a cruel instrument—if I harm others, others will harm me in return. Thus, it is said that such affairs tend to return.

**Master Wang** says:
Those who assist the sovereign with the Dao still refrain from using military might to subdue the world—how much more so should the sovereign himself, who embodies the Dao?

Where the teacher treads, brambles grow.
After great armies pass, there will surely be years of famine.

**Master Lü** says:
Where armies have marched, both ancient and modern battlefields, few reap lasting benefit. Fields lie fallow, homes stand empty, and brambles thrive—how can one not fear this?

When great armies march, they disrupt the natural order above and drain the people’s strength below. Harmonious *qi* fails to ascend, and killing *qi* floods below. Both people and things suffer violence. The six *qi* become unbalanced, and rain and sun lose their proper timing. Plagues and disasters follow, and famine ensues after war—this is the natural course.

**Master Su** says:
Where war is waged, civil affairs falter, and fields lie untended. After battle, killing *qi* surges, and crops wither.

**Political Harmony Annotation**:
The people’s strength is drained below, hence brambles grow. The natural order is disrupted above, hence years of famine follow.

**Master Lin** says:
In lands where armies are deployed, farmers cannot till the soil, and brambles arise. After war, the harmony of Heaven and Earth is wounded, and years of famine inevitably follow. Thus, the virtuous act decisively but do not seek strength. Act decisively without arrogance, without boasting, without pride—act only when unavoidable, and do not seek strength.

**Master Lü** says:
Though military strength and victory in battle are methods to suppress violence and quell disorder, they must not be discarded—like water and fire, their proper use brings blessing, but misuse brings ruin. Those who skillfully wield arms act decisively but do not seek strength. What virtue cannot transform and what governance cannot reach must be addressed only when unavoidable, pressed only when necessary. When they act, they do so without fail, not relying on strength to take what is not theirs. This is the clear path of virtuous victory.

Act decisively without boasting of your ability—for boasting leads to decline.
Act decisively without praising your virtue—for praise undermines true merit.
Act decisively without growing proud of your power—for pride leads to insufficiency.

Matters of aggression may be addressed, but not to seek strength.

**Master He** says:
Virtue lies not in waging war but in wielding it well. By *"decisive"* (*guǒ*), he means clarity of vision and firm resolve. Decisiveness is secondary to doing good, not to wielding arms for conquest. As the *Classic* says: *"The contest is of heart and strength."* Thus, one acts decisively but does not seek strength. Again, it says: *"Act decisively but do not seek strength."*

To not boast of ability, not to praise one’s deeds, not to grow arrogant—these are the expressions of a virtuous heart. Thus, one acts with decisive resolve, not competing in heart. Only in the matter of extreme military strength does one act decisively to reject it, not competing in force.

**Master Dong** says:
Though the Dao rejects war, Heaven endows the five elements, which cannot be discarded—like water and fire, their proper use is essential. Only when used to suppress violence and aid the distressed does decisiveness follow principle. Whatever principles and righteousness belong to us, we uphold them without bending.

The *Spring and Autumn Annals* says: *"Decisive action means killing enemies to prevent mutual aggression—how dare one seek strength over the world?"*

Decisive action wins through principle; strength wins through force. Decisiveness carries an inherent certainty of victory, yet it does not rely on force or delight in war. Thus, when facing matters, one fears, plans carefully, and acts only when unavoidable—simply avoiding strength.

When things reach their peak, they decline—this is called *not the Dao*. *Not the Dao* must be abandoned early.

**Master Lin** says:
The strong cannot remain strong, the arrogant cannot remain arrogant. Just as all things, once at their peak, must decline—those who do not understand this principle yet seek to dominate the world are acting against the Dao. Knowing this, they must swiftly abandon it. *"Early abandonment"* means to cease and not persist.

**Master Su** says:
Things grow strong, then decline—none escape this. Only those of the Dao, though complete, appear deficient; though full, appear empty. They never grow strong, never decline, never die. To use military might to subdue the world is extreme strength—how can it avoid decline?

**Master He** says:
Why does the Sage retreat in humility, unwilling to seek victory over the world? Because He sees that when the sun reaches its zenith, it declines; when the moon is full, it wanes; when things reach their peak, decline follows. Is this not the natural course of the Dao? If one knows this is not the Dao, then one must swiftly return to it, swiftly abandon what is not the Dao. The causes of ruined states are many, but those who delight in arms are doomed to fall.

With the Dao to govern the people and the state, how could one not take the use of arms as a warning and skillfully employ their consequences?

**Master Dong** says:
When things reach their peak of strength, they grow old; when arms are at their strongest, they meet defeat. Thus, those who rely on military might cannot fully preserve their virtue in victory. To employ strength is precisely what hastens decay and decline—all of which are contrary to the Dao. One should know when to stop early.

**Chapter Thirty-One**
Fine arms are instruments of ill omen. People detest them, and thus those who follow the Dao do not wield them.

**Master Lin** says:
Fine arms mean delighting in warfare, taking pleasure in using arms—this is the nature of an ill-omened person. An ill-omened person engaging in ill-omened deeds makes arms an ill-omened instrument, which the world detests. Hence, those who follow the Dao do not use them.

**Master Li** says:
Not using arms to subdue the world, those who rank arms as ill-omened instruments—this is the Sage’s profound warning to all future rulers of states. With non-action, purity, and tranquil governance, the realm naturally attains peace. Though arms exist, they are not displayed, and the scourge of contention is forever averted.

**Master Lü** says:
Arms are not tools for achieving great peace and governance. Though they may be used to punish tyranny, if one is forced to employ them, how can one avoid dark machinations? Those who adorn themselves with weapons as ornaments are already indulging in their cruelest use. This is to glorify a deadly instrument and hope for the world’s blessing—it is not only hated by people but also feared by all things.

The *Continuation of the Comprehensive Mirror to Aid in Government* records that Song Emperor Taizong said to his close ministers:
*"When I read in Laozi that ‘Fine arms are instruments of ill omen,’ and that the Sage uses them only when unavoidable, I have never failed to reflect on this as a solemn warning three times over.*

*The virtuous in repose honor the left; in war, they honor the right.*

*Arms are instruments of ill omen, not the tools of the virtuous. Used only when unavoidable, tranquility is supreme.*

*To win yet not take pride in it, yet to take pride is to delight in killing. Those who delight in killing cannot fulfill their ambitions in the world.*

**Master Lin** says:
The virtuous always honor the left in repose, yet in war, they honor the right—this shows that even the ancients regarded arms as ill-omened deeds, not something the virtuous would willingly use. They are employed only when unavoidable. Even if forced to wage war, they hold tranquility in highest regard. Tranquility is without relish, signifying the unwillingness born of necessity. Though victorious, they do not rejoice. ‘Not taking pride’ means war is no good deed. If one takes pride in war, then killing becomes a delight—how could such a person ever fulfill their ambitions in the world? As *Mencius* says: ‘Those who do not delight in killing can unite the world.’ This is the same meaning.

**Master Lü** says:
The Dao of Heaven and Earth places yang on the left and yin on the right. Yang embodies virtue, life, and gentleness—thus, in repose, it is honored. Yin embodies punishment, killing, and strength—thus, in war, it is exalted.

**Master Wu** says:
Tranquility means without exultation; simplicity means without excess. To hold war in disdain is to govern supremely. Though victorious, they do not glory in it—war is no noble deed. If one glories in victory, then killing is their delight. Those who delight in killing cannot fulfill their ambitions in the world. This is the final admonition.

*Auspicious rites honor the left; inauspicious rites honor the right. The Assistant General stands to the left, the Commanding General to the right—this is to treat war as a funeral.*

*When many die, one mourns with tears. Victory in war is treated as a funeral.*

**Master Lin** says:
The Assistant General’s rank is subordinate to the Commanding General, yet here the Commanding General stands to the right while the Assistant General stands to the left—this shows the ancients treated war as an inauspicious affair, thus observing funeral rites. Left is yang, right is yin; funeral rites exalt yin. Even if victorious, one should treat it as mourning, weeping for the dead with sorrow. The intent of this chapter is that those who seek victory in life are doomed to misfortune, not blessed.

**Master He** says:
To treat victory as though it were defeat, observing funeral rites—this is the height of compassion. When the Qin army was defeated at Heng and retreated, the Duke of Qin wore mourning attire at the outskirts and wept toward the troops. This was fitting for defeat, but there has never been one who, upon victory, mourned in this way. Only those who deeply embody Heaven and Earth’s love for life and universal compassion could be moved to such grief.

Compassion makes one courageous; sorrow makes one victorious. The Dao resides here. But if one takes arms as fine, killing as delight, and victory as beautiful, then one does not know the Dao, one does not know Heaven.

**Chapter Thirty-Two**
The Dao is ever nameless. Though crude and seemingly small, the world dares not submit to it. If lords and kings can preserve it, all things will come of their own accord.

**Master Lü** says:
The Dao is difficult to speak of—it encompasses all things, penetrating the finest and the vast. How could it be named or compared to simplicity? Crude refers to its pure, unadulterated essence, one without mixture, boundless and all-pervading. Though called "small," it is not small in size, but in its formlessness, its non-action, its subtle and wondrous nature—beyond grasp or imitation. Thus, it is named "small."

All worldly tools and instruments, once named, are subject to the nameless. Only the Dao’s crudeness underpins all things—Heaven and Earth cannot violate it, sages and wise cannot interfere, and yet how could the world submit to it? If lords and kings preserve this crudeness, the world will naturally be governed.

To liken it to oneself: lords and kings are originally like unspoiled children. The unmanifest Heavenly Sovereign is called the Original Spirit. If one guards it without loss, one becomes one with the Spirit.

The Dao’s supreme constancy is unassailable—all ten thousand *qi* bow to it. Hence it is said: **"The Dao is ever nameless."**

Though *pu* (crudeness) is small, the world does not dare to subject itself to it.
If lords and kings can preserve this *pu*, all things will naturally submit.

**○ Master He says:** The phrase *"Dao is ever"* is a complete sentence, like *"Heaven is ever true"* or *"constancy is ever true"*—it means the eternal Dao.

The Great Dao is formless and nameless; yet, when forced to name it, we call it "Dao."
Its namelessness is the Dao’s eternal essence—*pu* itself.
All things that can be named or spoken of are not true constancy, but mere instruments.
Thus, it is said: **"Heaven and Earth, along with all sentient beings, are originally rooted in the Dao, all emerging from the primordial *Tai Su* (Great Substance), the initial stage of emptiness and nothingness."**

*Pu* is the Great Substance, the origin of the nameless.
Before differentiation into the myriad forms of existence, it alone remains within the Great Chaos.
*Pu* is subtle beyond measure—hard to know, hard to see—thus it is called "small."

The Sage discerns the root of constancy and *pu*, embraces the Substance while preserving *pu*.
This is simplicity in essence and ease in practice, few actions yet great achievements,
a heart of non-action, safeguarding the utmost rectitude.
The Dao is both empty and real, void yet present—
with it, one governs the heavy and light,
and may rule the world,
yet how could the world dare to subject itself to such governance?

With it, one dwells in stillness yet commands motion,
and may become the master of all things—
yet all things will naturally submit to such mastery.

**○ Master Li says:** *"The Dao is ever nameless"*—this is the natural state of emptiness and nothingness.
*Pu* is small, yet supremely subtle—
the world does not dare to subject itself to it, for it is the utmost reverence.
All things will naturally submit—none will resist.
**○ Master Lin says:** *"The Dao is ever nameless"*—
though it may be named as "extraordinary" or "nameless," the nameless *pu* is the Dao itself.
Though it seems the smallest, the world cannot but honor it—
who would dare to belittle it?
Thus, it is said: *"the world does not dare to subject itself to it."*

For those who become lords and kings,
if they can preserve this Dao,
then all things will naturally submit and serve them.

Heaven and Earth unite in harmony,
and sweet dew descends.
No one commands it,
yet all is balanced naturally.

**○ Master Lü says:** When Heaven and Earth interact, all things are born.
When human Dao interacts, achievements are fulfilled.
What then if the Sage acts,
and all things respond?
The harmonious *qi* silently aligns—
above, it reaches the Celestial Clarity (*Tai Qing*),
below, it coils around the Great Tranquility (*Tai Ning*).
It transforms into sweet dew,
nourishing all things.
Initially, none know who commands it,
yet it descends and ascends in perfect balance,
entering and exiting through the Mysterious Mother (*Xuan Mu*),
converging at the Yellow Court (*Huang Ting*).
Piercing this meaning,
one establishes the reversal of the infant.
**○ Master He says:** When the Sage aligns with Heaven and Earth in virtue,
timely rains descend and sweet springs flow—
the sweet dew may be attained.
When the Sage unites in heart with all beings,
teaching and transformation flourish,
customs shift—
the world does not await commands but naturally finds harmony.
Sweet dew is the auspicious response of kings—
refined as fat, sweet as honey,
received by pine and bamboo alike.
Because the Sage preserves *pu* in stillness and quietude,
the natural responses are thus.

**○ Master Li says:** When Heaven and Earth unite,
it is virtue alone that supports them,
and sweet dew descends from harmonious *qi*.
Their natural balance is the peace of harmony itself.

From the beginning, there was *pu* and the nameless.
Once names arise, one must know when to stop—
knowing when to stop ensures no peril.
**○ Master Lü says:** With seasons passing and years turning,
things manifest through their functions.
*Pu* differentiates into instruments,
names arise from these instruments.
Once distinctions are set,
rank and hierarchy appear—
one cannot transgress above or below.
If one does not know when to stop,
pursuing names while forgetting *pu*,
abandoning the root for the branches,
then one loses their foundation.

The wise who know when to stop
are both enlightened and sagely,
self-sufficient within,
requiring nothing from without—
how could they face danger or peril?

**○ Master Lin says:** The Dao’s origin is nameless.
Once the myriad things arise,
then the Dao’s name appears.
Creation itself is the scattering of *pu* into instruments.
This name having arisen,
one generates two, two generate three—
where does it end?

Those who know the Dao
must recognize this point and stop—
knowing when to stop ensures no peril.

**○ Master He says:** From the nameless to the named,
from the beginning to the mother—
within every scattering of *pu* into instruments,
there lies a supreme principle that cannot be severed.
Tracing the flow to its source,
exploring the root to its branches,
returning to the beginning while harmonizing,
erasing distinctions into formlessness,
preventing the loss of the Dao—
those who stray and forget return,
yet stopping is perilous, stopping is humiliation.
Thus, it is said: *"Once named, return to the Dao."*

Long-lasting achievements and virtue
bring lifelong freedom from blame—
thus, *"it cannot be named."*

The Dao’s initial stage may be named,
but those who know the Dao’s remainder
also grasp the Dao of the Primordial Beginning (*Yuan Shi*),
and those who know its remainder
align with the pivot of yin and yang—
mixing into one,
they may harmonize with Heaven and Earth.

**○ Master Wu says:** The "beginning" is the Dao,
"making" is creation—
just as one says "to do."
The "named" is virtue.
The Dao is nameless,
yet when it manifests as virtue,
then it is named.
The Dao’s nameless virtue becomes virtue—
once named, one must know when to stop at virtue,
and not descend further.
The intent of this passage is:
when one moves from nothingness to existence,
one must know to stop at virtue—
it cannot be furthered beyond this.

Below the named virtue,
one returns to the Dao and descends to virtue—
is this possible?
Knowing when to stop at virtue,
one is still not far from the Dao.

To liken the Dao in the world
to rivers and valleys compared to the sea.

**○ Master Lü says:** The Dao is the origin of all things,
rivers and valleys are the divisions of water,
the sea is the gathering of water.
Yet the Dao in the world—
above, it ascends to the Celestial Clarity (*Qing Du*) and Purple Micro (*Zi Wei*),
below, it mingles with the humble and the coarse.
Following it brings prosperity,
contradicting it brings ruin—
like the sea, it is compared to this.
If one does good,
it permeates and nourishes all things,
if one does evil,
it surges and brings calamity.
Yet the Dao grows ever higher the more it descends,
the sea grows ever fuller the more it is tilted.
The Sage’s virtue encompasses Heaven and Earth,
nourishment extends near and far—
then the people cannot but return to such virtue.

**○ Master Wu says:** The preceding text speaks of arising from Nonbeing to Being;
this now speaks of returning from Being to Nonbeing—
both must be understood in terms of the Dao.
For the Dao in the world is like the sea and rivers,
while virtue is like the tributaries converging into them.

**○ Master He says:** The Dao in the world
is like the sea receiving all rivers—
the sea does not desire the waters,
yet the waters naturally flow to it.
If one returns to simplicity and non-action,
the Dao is not sought,
yet it naturally comes—
effortless, not forced.
The Sage begins with silent stillness and non-action,
then transcends all entanglements of name and form.
His Dao endures through ten thousand ages without change—
truly, it is the Eternal Constant.

**○ Master Lin says:** The waters of rivers and valleys
must flow into the sea before they rest,
and all things under Heaven
must return to the Dao before they cease.
Thus it is said:
*The Dao in the world is like rivers and valleys to the sea.*

**Chapter Thirty-Three**
*To know others is wisdom,*
*to know oneself is enlightenment.*

「Conquering others is strength,
but conquering oneself is power.
Master Lü says: Knowing others means seeking perfection in things,
controlling life through external means—
this is not as good as knowing oneself without knowing,
enlightening oneself without enlightenment,
which may truly be called **self-knowing** and **self-enlightenment**.
Those who strive externally conquer others with force,
but those who strive internally conquer themselves with the Dao.
To establish a state and proclaim a name,
to grasp authority and wield influence,
to command all under Heaven,
to subjugate the feudal lords—
is this not the use of force to conquer others?
The self-conqueror upholds **virtue/power** and **Dao**,
practices **ren** (benevolence) and **yi** (righteousness),
overcomes self to restore propriety,
humble and self-effacing—
yet people flock to him.
Thus, none under Heaven can conquer him;
what else could this be but **power**?

Master He says: By human nature,
one is clear in knowing others,
but dim in knowing oneself;
strong in conquering others,
but timid in conquering oneself.
So knowing others is merely discerning right and wrong,
advantage and harm—
yet a single moment of knowing wrong,
and the **spiritual radiance** (*lingguang*) ever shines forth,
this is the greatest **self-enlightenment**.
Conquering others is merely a matter of strength and weakness,
courage and cowardice—
yet a single moment of overcoming evil,
and this is the greatest courage under Heaven,
the greatest **self-power**.
How far removed this is from those who strive externally but neglect internally!

Master Wu says: Intelligence can know others,
but this is merely external knowledge.
To know oneself is to exhaust one’s nature internally,
thus called **enlightenment**.
To have strength and conquer others is to rely on external force,
but to conquer oneself is to internally overcome self,
thus called **power**.
The contented man is rich,
the resolute man acts with purpose.
The steadfast man endures long.
The man who dies yet does not perish is long-lived.

Master Lü says: The accomplished man’s work
endures after his death,
benefiting all people—
he does not lose his place,
nor forgets his roots,
his influence lasts far into the future,
so that later generations cannot emulate him.
In his body, he guards the **True One** (*Zhenyi*), embraces unity,
and is not swayed by external things,
never for an instant departing from it—
then he enters the realm of the **Eternal Constant** (*Zhenchang*),
enduring through all ages without change,
unified with the Dao,
thus it is said: *The steadfast man endures long.*
The Sage’s Dao is transmitted through ten thousand ages,
recorded in books and decrees,
later generations revere him as their teacher,
though his person may have passed,
his governance remains as if alive.
The man of the Dao regards life as a temporary dwelling,
and death as his final return—
in his life, he discerns the **tendencies** (*qù*) with clarity,
unified with the Dao in truth;
in his death, he sheds the mortal form and attains **spiritual transformation** (*shenhua*),
like a cicada casting its shell.
Though his body dies,
his true self does not perish—
his longevity matches the **Two Principles** (*Eryi*) in its boundless extent,
thus it is said: *The man who dies yet does not perish is long-lived.*

Master He says: **Self-enlightenment** through self-knowing is possible,
but one must also attain the **knowing of contentment** (*Zhizú*),
then wealth is not bound by the world—
his contentment is **Dao-contentment**, **spiritual-contentment**,
this is called **true knowing**.
To achieve **self-power** and conquer is possible,
but one must also attain **resolute action** (*Qiángxíng*),
then his purpose can overcome all things—
his actions are **diligent action**, **solitary action**,
this is called **true power**.
Thus, the two lines—**knowing of contentment** and **resolute action**—
are connected to the preceding lines on **knowing** and **power**,
and their meaning becomes clear.
**Longevity** lies in not losing one’s place—
the place is the destination of return,
as the *Yijing* says: *"The **stopping** (*Gèn*) Principle stops at its place,
and finds rest in its place"*—
it means that the power to stop is one who stops at its proper place.
All things and affairs have their proper place,
to find it is to be at ease,
thus **longevity**.
To lose it is to go astray;
how could it endure?
**Longevity** lies in death, not in perishing—
death is the meaning of finality,
as the *Liezi* says: *"The Dao is the source of eternal life,
from life comes life—
though it ends, it does not perish eternally."*
As the *Zhuangzi* says: *"A thousand years of life may weary one of the world,
and they depart to ascend as immortals,
riding the white clouds,
reaching the realm of the Emperor."*
This is also the case.
As the saying goes: *"Establish virtue, achieve great deeds, and attain the Three Unperishables"* (*Sān Bùxiǔ*),
this too is the **not-perishing**.

Master Li says: The **contented man** finds joy even in poverty,
the **resolute man** never falters in beginning or end,
the **steadfast man** faces no peril,
the **internally enlightened** transcends birth and death—
the man who dies yet does not perish,
his **True One** eternally abides.

Master Wu says: The Laozi’s Dao
uses **obscurity** (*mèi*) as **enlightenment** (*míng*),
and **weakness** (*ruò*) as **power** (*qiáng*),
yet this chapter speaks of **enlightenment** and **power**—why?
Because Laozi is not obscure internally,
though externally he seems so;
not weak internally,
though externally he appears so.
His **obscurity** and **weakness** are remedies for external affairs,
his **enlightenment** and **power** are prescriptions for internal cultivation—
both are practiced without contradiction.

Master Dong says: The **place** is as the **stopping** Principle’s place in the *Yijing*—
only he who understands the Dao and can act upon it
finds his own place and dwells securely,
thus though external things change without end,
his heart never strays—
this is the way of **no entry without self-possession** (*Wùrù ér bùzìdé*),
the means to **longevity**.
Only he who can truly **know himself** and **strengthen himself**
without losing his place
attains true **longevity**—
this is the **unmoved** and **solitary** that does not perish with the changes of life and death,
standing alone and enduring eternally—
how could it be measured by mere quantity?

**Chapter Thirty-Four**
The Great Dao flows unrestrained,
its movement knows no left or right.
Master Lü says: It is the most exalted, most profound—
its boundaries are unfathomable,
its roots are untraceable.
Only the Dao could be so.
It encompasses the universe without distinction,
fills the boundless void,
its motion and stillness are unceasing,
its coming and going endless,
flowing freely without obstruction,
wandering through all things,
moving left and right without restriction—
its application is vast indeed.
Within the body, the Great Dao is the **original qi** (*yuánqì*),
pervading all hundred bones,
in the joints when full,
in the passages when full—
flowing freely without exclusion,
present in every part.

Master Chai says: The Dao is the mystery of all things,
its presence is never absent.
The text often employs the *"fú"* (*兮*) particle paired with *"qí"* (*其*),
as in *"Huāng fú qí wèicháng"* ("Vast and boundless, its limits are without end")
and *"Pèi fú qí wèizhào"* ("Frail and subtle, its signs are not yet revealed").
Again, in Chapter Fifteen, all lines use *"fú"* paired with *"qí"*—
this is the style of Laozi’s text.
