「○ Master Lin says: Vast and boundless, its presence can be left or right,
without any attachment or obstruction.

○ Master He says: Great indeed is the Sage’s Dao,
nurturing all things, reaching its apex in Heaven.
When speaking of *"fú"* (泛), it is profound as the sea,
lofty yet ever returning to its beginning.

Left is left, fitting to the left.
Right is right, fitting to the right.
The text says: *"Hold the Great Symbol"* (*zhí dàxiàng*),
and all under Heaven will follow—
for there is nothing it cannot traverse.

All things depend on it for birth yet it does not decline,
its work accomplished yet it claims no ownership.

Master Lü says: The power of generation and transformation
originates from Nature alone—
yet the Dao never applies its artifices,
nor boasts of its abilities.
Though Heaven and Earth are vast beyond measure,
they still rely on it,
and to all who seek, it gives.
Though the Six Extremes are vast,
they do not depart from its embrace,
and though the autumn hair is minute,
it awaits its shaping.
Does it not labor endlessly?
Standing alone, it gives life without resistance,
fulfills without possession,
does not dwell on its deeds—
this is the very essence of the Dao.

○ Master He says: All things lean upon it for gestation,
revealing themselves in every place,
while the Sage abides in ease.

○ Master Lin says: All things are born from the Dao,
how could it refuse them?
Once born, how could it claim them as its own?
Clothing and covering all things yet not ruling them,
ever without desire,
this may be called *"small"*—
all things return to it yet it does not rule them,
this may be called *"great"*.

Master Lü says: The Dao returns in the most hidden depths,
manifests in the most transformative ways.
It nurtures and shelters,
as if without beginning or end.
It covers and fosters,
each following its own nature—
like a babe by day relying on its milk,
by night on its swaddling.
Expanding its virtue of love and nurturing,
it acts yet does not rely,
it grows yet does not govern,
letting all unfold naturally and ever without desire.

Within, it observes its mystery,
and nothing escapes its embrace.
All movement cannot fathom its path of return—
this may be called *"small"*, hence it says,
*"all things return yet it does not rule"*.

Ever without desire,
this may be called *"small"*—
the Dao is nameless and hidden,
and its namelessness is vast beyond measure,
profound beyond reckoning.
Before anything existed,
it was without form—
then all things returned to it,
and there was no distinction.
It harmonized without ruling,
thus its greatness may be named.

○ Master He says: *"Clothing and covering"* means to shelter and reveal.
From the *Zhuangzi*:
The Sage encounters it without resistance,
passes through without clinging,
adapts and responds—this is *virtue*,
matches and aligns—this is the *Dao*—
and the meaning of *"not refusing, not possessing, not ruling"* is clear.

Ever without desire is stillness—
the Sage unifies in *non-action* and *non-desire*,
so its heart is without heart.
Gathered, it does not fill a palm’s grasp,
though simplicity may seem small,
all things return to it.
Not ruling means each thing is entrusted to its own way,
scattered yet filling the Six Extremes—
how vast it is!
Thus it is said:
Heaven and Earth have their constancy,
the sun and moon their brightness,
birds and beasts their groupings,
trees and woods their standing.

The Sage lets virtue flow and follows the Dao,
already attaining perfection.
From this perspective,
the Divine Person has no deeds,
the Sage no name—
why would it dwell on dominion?
Thus it says:
*"Its deeds cover all under Heaven yet seem not its own,
transforming and granting yet the people do not rely on it—
though it has no name to be raised,
it lets things rejoice in their own accord,
standing in the unfathomable,
wandering in the nonexistent."*

Master Li says: *"Clothing and covering all things yet not ruling"* means
forgetting its own origin.
*"All things return yet it does not rule"* means
letting all unfold naturally.

Thus the Sage never claims to be great,
and so it achieves greatness.

Master Lin says: Only by being small can one be great—
the Sage’s refusal to claim greatness
is how its deeds are fulfilled.

This is the meaning of *"retaining the feminine,
the Dao becomes the valley of all under Heaven."*

○ Master He says: The Sage never claims to be great,
yet greatness is achieved through this.
Though at first it may seem unclear and unpossessed,
its greatness cannot be concealed in the end—
is this not the Dao?

○ Master Wu says: This chapter begins with the Dao of Heaven and Earth,
then concludes with the Sage—
for the Sage and Heaven and Earth are one.

When the yearly work is complete and all things return,
this is the Dao’s ultimate greatness—
yet Heaven and Earth do not dwell on their deeds,
and all things know no ruler.
Thus, though the Dao of Heaven and Earth is great,
it does not claim greatness itself,
and the Sage is likewise—
hence it achieves greatness.

Master Lü says: Within the realm, there are four great things,
yet the Emperor resides in one of them.
The Sage holds humility as its instrument,
dwells in self-effacement,
is lowly so that others exalt it,
is humble so that others honor it.
It forgets its towering might,
discards its boastful demeanor—
then it can fulfill the Dao’s complete greatness.

**Chapter Thirty-Five**
*Hold the Great Symbol*, and all under Heaven will follow.
Go forth without harm,
and peace and tranquility endure.

○ Master He says: The Great Dao has no form,
so it lacks even the symbol of form—
yet its symbol is vast.
The Dao encompasses Heaven and Earth,
enters the minutest hairs—
it cannot be named or described,
thus it is called the *Great Symbol*.
To say *"hold"* means to uphold this and go forth—
if one can embody the nameless, formless Dao,
and follow Nature’s constant transformations,
then governing the body and the realm,
spanning past and future—
what path cannot it traverse?

Though I say *"all under Heaven is my path"*,
it is not merely that it does not harm—
it also calms the nature of life and death,
secures the blessings of moderation,
and stands serene without further action,
reaching the pinnacle of its journey—
the moment of perfect harmony between Heaven and humanity.

From the *Zhuangzi*:
*"If one attains its Dao,
there is nothing it cannot do."*

The **Lin** says: *"The Great Symbol*—
this is the symbol of the formless.
To traverse all under Heaven*—
it is to hold the Dao and act accordingly.
When one governs all under Heaven with the Dao,
who can harm it? If all under Heaven is unharmed,
then it is tranquil, serene, and boundless.*

The **Zhenghe** annotation reads:
*"Tranquility means no peril,
serenity means no obstruction,
boundlessness means universal harmony."*

Music and feasts—
passing guests pause.
The Dao when spoken of,
is bland and without flavor;
when seen, it is not visible;
when heard, it is not audible;
when used, it cannot be exhausted.

The **He** says: *"Five tones and six pitches suffice the ears,
eight delicacies and nine tripods suffice the mouth—
these are but the most appealing sounds and tastes to mortals,
only enough to amuse passing guests for a fleeting moment,
not the true source of nourishing the spirit or prolonging life."*

When the feast ends and guests depart,
what concern are they to me?
All things that satisfy desire enough to unsettle the heart
are of this kind.

Yet the Great Dao is not so.
With stillness, one refines the self—
music and color have their limits in delight.
With frugality, one sustains life—
richness and taste have their limits in indulgence.
One wanders the heart toward the bland,
and unites the *qi* with the boundless.
Nurturing the formless, the form abides;
guarding the bodiless, the body remains whole.
Flavor without flavor is sweet,
vision without sight is clear,
hearing without sound is acute,
use without end is vast.

It cannot be exhausted—
for exhaustion means completion.
Not that its use is without end,
but that its Dao is inherently boundless.

The **Li** says:
*"Music and feasts—
sounds and tastes both exquisite—
if one abandons the formless,
how long can they endure?"*

The Dao when spoken of,
has no flavor, no form,
no sound, no color—
because it is bodiless,
its application is boundless.

The **Lin** says:
*"Music*—
the joy of bells and drums—
*feasts*—
the abundance of food and drink—
set music and prepare banquets
to welcome honored guests.
When the music ends and the feast is consumed,
the guests depart and it ceases.
Departure*—
this is leaving.
Though the Dao’s flavor may seem most subtle,
though it is unseen when viewed,
though it is inaudible when heard—
this is to say it does not please the ears and eyes—
yet its use spans past and present without end.
This is the meaning: things have limits,
but the Dao is boundless."*

When one speaks of the Dao emerging from words,
it is as if to say:
*"The Dao in its expression."*

**Chapter Thirty-Six:**
*"When one would contract it,
must first fully expand it.
When one would weaken it,
must first fully strengthen it.
When one would discard it,
must first fully promote it.
When one would take from it,
must first fully give to it.
This is called *subtle clarity*."*

The **Li** says:
*"To hold the Great Symbol,
one can perceive the subtlest workings of things—
once expansion is seen,
one knows contraction is coming.
Before the first sign appears,
one already knows.
This is not the *subtle clarity* of all under Heaven—
who else can attain this?"*

The **Lin** says:
*"These eight lines are all metaphors,
expressing only the principle:
where there is gain, there must be loss.
Contract*—
to gather and to slacken—
what is expanded must slacken;
what is strong must weaken;
what is promoted must decline.
Gain must have its loss.
To give*—
to receive;
to take*—
to lose.
Only those who do not understand
take this as joy,
unaware of the principle—
though it seems obscure,
it is truly clear.
Hence, it is called *subtle clarity*.
Subtle*—
as obscure as the dim—
yet its meaning is most evident."*

The **He** says:
*"Heaven cannot be trusted,
principles have no fixed state—
who can unify them?
The sun at its setting is most luminous,
yet Heaven in its withdrawal of light is swift.
*Qi* has its contraction and expansion,
forces have their strength and decline,
cycles have their rise and fall,
things have their giving and taking—
this is the nature of affairs,
not a matter of desire or aversion.
The Classic says:
*'Gusts of wind do not last the morning,
sudden rains do not last the day—
even Heaven and Earth cannot endure long,
how much more so mortals?*
Thus, speaking of *qi*,
when the yang reaches its peak,
the yin arises—
is it not that contraction follows expansion?
Speaking of force,
when things are at their height,
they age—
is it not that decline follows strength?
Speaking of cycles,
when things flourish,
they must wither—
is it not that fall follows rise?
Speaking of things,
what is precious must become base—
is it not that loss follows giving?
When one would act thus,
must first act otherwise—
borrowing human affairs to illustrate Heaven’s principles,
it is but the natural ebb and flow of fullness and emptiness.
Heaven has no partiality—
the Sage has no partiality.
Later generations unaware of this Dao
mistook it for schemes of manipulation,
yet this is but the interplay of circumstances,
where there is a mind for schemes,
the Sage is far removed.
How can this chapter’s grand meaning
be viewed through the lens of contrivance and action?"*

From the *Zhuangzi*:
*"When the eyes are about to fail,
first examine the autumn hairs;
when the ears are about to dull,
first hear the gnat’s flight;
when the body is about to stiffen,
first hasten to bound and leap;
when the heart is about to be confused,
first distinguish right and wrong—
thus, what does not come to pass
is not yet reached.*
The annotator takes this to mean:
*'The extremes of high and low,
the natural cycles—
the Sage abides in the middle,
walking the path of harmony,
fully whole throughout life.'*
This Dao is subtle yet profound,
difficult yet clear."*

The **Liu** says:
*"Expansion must lead to contraction,
strength must lead to weakness,
promotion must lead to decline,
giving must lead to taking—
this is the natural order of things,
called *subtle clarity*—
subtle clarity*—
the most refined and evident.
Some interpret this as a matter of strategy,
yet the Dao of Heaven is beneficent and does not harm—
such is its nature."*

The **Dong** says:
*"The ebb and flow of fullness and emptiness—
a principle of mutual dependence,
though its workings may be subtle and hidden,
its meaning is clear and evident.
Only those who dwell in purity, stillness, and weakness
do not fall into its schemes.
Softness overcomes hardness,
weakness overcomes strength.
Fish cannot be plucked from the depths,
and the state’s sharpest tools
should not be shown to the people."*

The **Li** says:
*"Those who study the Dao,
preserve the formless,
hold to the utmost softness,
compete with nothing—
then naturally know the omens."*

「If one were to employ harshness and violence,
or to rely on stratagems and clever tactics,
and seek to triumph over all things—
this would not be the Dao/Way, indeed.

Just as fish are creatures of water,
if one seeks to differ from other fish
and desires to escape the depths—
is this possible?

Since it is not possible,
then humans too cannot rely on stratagems.
Those who rely on stratagems
go against the natural order—
like fish leaving the depths, they must perish.
The state’s sharpest tools
should not be shown to the people,
as Confucius said:
*"One may share the Dao/Way with them,
but not one’s stratagems."*
The same meaning applies here.

The Sage uses stratagems,
yet does so by reversing the natural order
to align with the Dao/Way—
still, this should not be done lightly.
How much more so for ordinary people—
should they not be warned?

The **He** says:
*"Under Heaven, nothing is softer and weaker than the Dao/Way.
The Dao/Way is soft and weak,
encompassing Heaven and Earth,
penetrating all things.
This is how softness and weakness
overcome hardness and strength.
Thus, when humans dwell in the Dao/Way,
the Dao/Way dwells in humans,
just as fish dwell in water,
and water dwells in fish.
The Dao/Way departs from humans, they die;
water dries from fish, they perish.
Fish that hide their scales and conceal their fins,
inhale and exhale the water,
do not die—
humans who retreat to blessed lands,
inhale and exhale the *qi*,
do not perish either.
The state’s sharpest tools
are the Dao/Way’s hidden principles—
if one were to reveal them lightly,
Heaven’s retribution would swiftly arrive.
The *Great Cave of Heaven Constitution Scripture* states:
*"To reveal what is not meant for humans is called ‘leakage’;
Heaven’s treasures are profound indeed—
its hidden principles should not be recklessly disclosed."
The *Southern Huaxia Scripture* says:
*"There are swords of Gou and Yue,
yet they are kept in their cases,
not dared to be used—
this is how they are preserved,
and the meaning is the same."*

The **River Above Master** says:
*"Sharp tools are stratagems of the Dao/Way.
When governing the state with stratagems,
one should not show them to officials.
When practicing the Dao/Way for oneself,
one should not reveal them to those unworthy."*

The **Lin** says:
*"The depths symbolize the Dao/Way,
and fish symbolize humans—
just as fish cannot escape the depths,
so humans cannot exist outside the Dao/Way."*

**Chapter Thirty-Seven** says:
*"The Dao/Way is ever without action,
yet nothing remains undone.
If lords and kings could uphold it,
all things would transform of their own accord."*

The **He** says:
*"In the *Zhuangzi*,
though all things differ,
their principles and Dao/Way are not exclusive—
thus, they are nameless.
Because they are nameless,
there is no action.
Yet without action, nothing remains undone.
The phrase ‘the Dao/Way is ever without action yet nothing remains undone’
is the great constant of the Dao/Way.

The ‘constant’ is without desire,
to observe its subtlety—
the ‘constant’ with desire,
to observe its transformations.
Only the constant without desire
is without action,
while the constant with desire
is without anything left undone—
yet the Dao/Way is one.

This ‘without action yet nothing remains undone’
is the Dao/Way’s true constant.
The Dao/Way is without presence yet without absence—
if lords and kings could uphold this Dao/Way,
it would manifest through all things,
not requiring transformation yet naturally transforming itself."*

The **Lin** says:
*"This chapter aligns with ‘the Dao/Way is ever without name’—
‘without action yet nothing remains undone’
is the natural unfolding of the Dao/Way.
If lords and kings could uphold this Dao/Way of non-action,
then without seeking to transform all things,
all things would transform themselves."*

The **Lü** says:
*"The body’s imitation,
responding without deliberation—
when the spirit is too fixed,
this is non-action.
Lords and kings,
entangled in the Nine Sovereigns’ ways,
if they uphold and do not lose them,
will refine their essence into fluid,
fluid into marrow,
marrow into essence,
essence into the primal pearl,
and the primal pearl into golden elixir—
what else could this be but the transformation of ten thousand things?
If one desires to act upon this transformation,
I shall restrain them with the nameless *purity*.
The nameless *purity* also desires not to act.
Without desire, there is stillness—
and under Heaven, all will naturally align."*

The **He** says:
*"Though transformation acts from above and responds below,
the mechanism of desire to act or to not act
is something the Sage holds most carefully.
The Sage transforms through divine means,
making people adapt to it—
not rushing ahead of its time,
nor lagging behind—
its mechanism is thus.
The Sage does not claim this as his own merit;
I shall follow the transformation of non-action,
returning to the nameless *purity*."*

The saying *"to restrain"* means
to carve and return to the unadorned—
to stand alone, solid and unyielding in form,
though responsive yet still at its core.
This is what is called the nameless *purity*.

The Dao at its origin
cannot be named at all.
The Sage does not even desire it,
let alone the scattered vessels of *purity*,
which bear no extraordinary name.

If the Dao itself requires no intent,
how much more so that which is not the Dao?
Thus, the Sage’s mind is tranquil—
when the mind is unified and steadfast,
all things submit.
How could the world not align?

○ **Master Wu** says:
To speak of acting with intent is like stirring—
to say that one has not yet attained the purity of non-action,
and yet already waits for things to transform,
suddenly harboring intent toward their transformation—
the moment this intent arises,
it is no longer the Dao of non-action.

*"To restrain"* means to press down and settle,
preventing any disturbance.
The nameless *purity* refers to the Dao of non-action.
When the impulse to act arises,
one must restrain this intentional desire
with this nameless *purity*—
that is, to govern oneself through the Dao.
Once this nameless *purity* restrains the desire,
the desire itself will cease to desire.
The stillness that follows is the return to the origin—
when the desire to act is restrained by the Dao,
the desiring ceases,
and the one who acts becomes still.
Thus, though the Sage acts without intent,
the world will align of its own accord.

○ **Master Lü** says:
Within oneself, the moment a thought stirs,
it is called *emotion*—
when emotion moves, all transformations arise.
Once the thought has slipped through,
one must urgently reflect back,
extinguishing the restless mind,
restoring the pure simplicity,
and restraining it with *purity*—
only then may one unify the nature.
Hence it is said:
*"When transformation stirs to act,
I shall restrain it with the nameless purity."*

Moreover, the Dao is originally formless;
*purity* is but a borrowed term.
The Sage fears that even this may become rigid and fixed,
that clinging to *purity* for use
might lead to exhaustion beyond remedy.
Thus, he declares:
To let the world’s myriad forms be,
the mind must be empty;
to respond to the world’s myriad movements,
the mind must be still.
Only by suppressing desire to empty the nature,
and severing learning to empty the mind,
can one refine the self—
without obstruction, nothing is unattained;
can one govern the nature—
without exception, nothing is unachievable.
Thus, the world will naturally settle and align.

○ **Master Li** says:
Heaven and Earth act without doing,
and all things emerge.
The Sage acts without doing,
and all people thrive in peace.

Speaking of cultivation and refinement,
there is no deliberate action—
in moments of tranquility,
preserve the formless.
At the slightest movement,
one must first become aware,
and upon awareness,
restrain it with the nameless *purity*.
*Purity* is formless;
it is also called *nameless*—
meaning *emptiness*.

*(End of Volume Five of the Collected Commentaries on the Dao De Jing)*
*(Beginning of Volume Six of the Collected Commentaries on the Dao De Jing)*

The **Way** of supreme virtue is not virtue,
thus it has virtue.
The **Way** of inferior virtue does not abandon virtue,
thus it lacks virtue.
The **Way** of supreme virtue acts without action—
yet nothing fails to be accomplished.
The **Way** of inferior virtue acts with action—
yet nothing is accomplished.

The **Way** of supreme benevolence acts yet nothing is accomplished;
the **Way** of supreme righteousness acts yet nothing is accomplished.
The **Way** of supreme propriety acts yet none respond,
and so they roll up their sleeves and force it upon them.

○ **Master He** says:
The ancients dwelled in **mixed chaos**,
and with the world became **dull and tranquil**.
Though people may have wisdom,
they had no use for it—
this is called **ultimate unity**,
where none know to act yet all abides in **constant naturalness**.

When virtue declined,
at the time of **Tang and Yú**,
they began to establish laws for the world,
dissipating **purity** and scattering **simplicity**,
abandoning the **Dao** for the sake of goodness,
and making virtue perilous through conduct.
Then they discarded **nature** and followed the **heart**.

The **heart** gives rise to **heart-awareness** and **mind-knowing**,
yet it was insufficient to govern the world—
so they adorned it with **culture** and enriched it with **learning**.
When **culture** extinguishes **substance**,
and **learning** drowns the **heart**,
then the people began to be bewildered and disordered,
unable to return to their original **nature** and **emotions**.

This chapter’s essence lies entirely here.
Supreme virtue not being virtue—
that is **mixed chaos** and **dull tranquility**.
Inferior virtue not abandoning virtue yet lacking it—
then the doer is ruined, the grasp is lost.

Supreme virtue acting without action—
that is **ultimate unity** in **naturalness**.
Inferior virtue acting with action—
that is what is called “beginning to act for the world.”

Supreme benevolence acting yet nothing is accomplished—
**ultimate benevolence** has no favorites,
and its distance from virtue is still slight.

Supreme righteousness acting yet nothing is accomplished—
when righteousness governs actions,
then inferior benevolence is already one rank below.

If one views **virtue** and **righteousness** through the lens of **moral power**,
then this is what is meant by
“abandoning the **Dao** for goodness” and
“making virtue perilous through conduct.”

When it says “not virtue,” and “nothing is accomplished,”—
all are without **heart**,
acting without purpose is called **Heaven**.
When it says “not abandoning virtue,” and “nothing is accomplished,”—
all are with **heart**,
acting with purpose is called **human**.

Supreme propriety acting yet none respond—
this is already one rank below inferior righteousness,
adorned with **culture** and **rites**.
In **loyalty** and **trustworthiness**, it is too thin;
enriched with **learning**, it is too ornate.
Its distance from **nature** grows ever farther.

Supreme propriety—
that is **excessive rites**.
When rewards are lavish, the response must be lavish,
yet if the response does not match the reward,
then strife arises from it.
Rolling up the sleeves—
a gesture of forcibly pulling away—
is the mark of one who violates propriety.
“Force” (扔) is “pull,”
but the character is incorrect.

○ **Master Lin** says:
“Acting yet none respond”—
this is compelling the people yet they do not follow.
“Force” (扔) is “pull,”
when the people do not follow yet one forcibly drags them by hand.
It is a strong and violent pulling.
This merely describes the act of compelling the people,
hence it says: *“roll up the sleeves and force it upon them.”*

○ **Master Li** says:
The previous chapter spoke of **constant non-action**,
so next it speaks of *“the **Way** of supreme virtue is not virtue.”*
Supreme virtue is non-action,
thus it aligns with the **Dao**.
Inferior virtue is action and does not align with the **Dao**.
**Benevolence** and **righteousness** are still close to virtue,
and can serve as steps toward the **Dao**.
**Rites** are purely action;
when **rites** are used to harmonize all,
they instead fail to harmonize—
for all action must eventually falter.
If one holds to them without faltering,
they can gradually lead one to a better state.
From **benevolence** to return to **virtue**,
from **virtue** to return to the **Dao**,
directly reaching **non-action**.

○ **Master Qin of Huáihǎi** says:
**Virtue** and **power** are the fullness of **benevolence**, **righteousness**, **rites**, and **wisdom**,
while **benevolence**, **righteousness**, **rites**, and **wisdom** are but aspects of **virtue** and **power**,
with the **Dao** as their foundation,
and **benevolence** and **righteousness** as their application.
Thus, when the **Dao** is lost, **virtue** follows;
when **virtue** is lost, **benevolence** follows;
when **benevolence** is lost, **righteousness** follows;
when **righteousness** is lost, **rites** follow.

**Rites** are the thinnest of **loyalty** and **trustworthiness**,
and the origin of disorder.
**Preconceived knowing** is the ornate aspect of the **Dao**,
and the beginning of **folly**.
**Master He** says:
Disorder begins with folly—
this is the utmost of how **rites** and **wisdom** lead to ruin.
The *Zhuāngzi* clearly enumerates these five stages from *“when the **Dao** is lost, **virtue** follows”*,
and says: *“Now they make these things into objects of pursuit,
and to return to the root is not so easy.
How simple it is! How simple it is—
only the **Great Person** can do it.”*

Though it says *“so”*,
it also says: *“Hidden yet cannot fail to act—this is **rites**.
Distant yet cannot fail to dwell upon—this is **righteousness**.
Near yet cannot fail to broaden—this is **benevolence**.
Moderate yet cannot fail to accumulate—this is **rites**.
Central yet cannot fail to elevate—this is **virtue**.
One yet cannot fail to simplify—this is the **Dao**.*
Though these have a sequence of high and low, first and last,
when the Sage employs them all,
what is not the **Dao**?

Once there was a **Master of Mysterious Transformation** who said:
The **Dao** is the subtle essence of all things,
and when spoken of, it is called **virtue**—
the totality of the **Two Poles** (yin and yang).
To attain it at the highest is the **Dao**,
to attain it in the middle is **benevolence** and **righteousness**,
to attain it in the lower is **rites** and **wisdom**.
To attain it in the cold and lose it—
that is not **human**.
This statement is the most concise and clear.

○ **Master Lǚ** says:
The **Dao** is the **substance** of **virtue**,
and **virtue** is the **application** of the **Dao**.
**Benevolence** and **righteousness** arise from the **heart**,
and are worthy of praise.
But when it comes to **rites**,
they grow ever more distant from **nature**,
ornate yet lacking in substance.
To engage in elaborate **rites** and trivial details
without seeking their foundation—
**loyalty** and **trustworthiness** grow ever thinner,
though outwardly they may gleam.
To follow them brings order,
to oppose them brings chaos—
not even a hair’s breadth can intervene.
Is this not the origin of disorder?

Thus it is said:
When the **Three Sovereigns** had the **Dao**,
they did not know that the **Dao** transformed into the **virtue** of the **Five Emperors**.
When the **Five Emperors** had **virtue**,
they did not know that **virtue** transformed into the **benevolence** and **righteousness** of the **Three Kings**.
When the **Three Kings** had **benevolence** and **righteousness**,
they did not know that **benevolence** and **righteousness** transformed into the **wars** of **Qín-Hàn**.
This is what is meant.

Moreover, the common people take **wisdom** as a chisel,
pursuing superficial ornamentation.
Though they think themselves perceptive,
they do not realize that **ultimate folly** begins from here.
Hence it says:
*“Preconceived knowing* is the ornate aspect of the **Dao**,
and the beginning of **folly**.”*

Mr. Wu says: **"Loyalty and sincerity"** are the depth of **virtue**, gradually thinning as they transform—once diminished, they become **benevolence**; twice diminished, they become **righteousness**; thrice diminished, they become **rites**. Thus, the profound **virtue** of loyalty and sincerity grows shallow. **Rites** seek to bring order without chaos, yet they instead become the foundation of disorder—hence, they are called *"the origin of chaos."*

*"Preconceived knowing"* is akin to foresight—it refers to **wisdom**. The **Dao** is like the fruit of a tree: before its birth, its essence lies within, its embryo yet unseen. Once born, **virtue** is its root, **benevolence** its trunk, **righteousness** its branches, **rites** its leaves, and **wisdom** its blossom. All arise from the **Dao**. **Wisdom** seeks to be enlightened yet avoids folly, but instead it gives rise to folly—hence, it is called *"the beginning of folly."*

Mr. Li says: *"Preconceived knowing"* is **discernment**; **discernment** is the first deviation from the **Dao**, hence it is called *"the ornament of the **Dao**,"* and those who follow the **Dao** do not dwell there. Thus, the noble man abides in what is profound, not in what is shallow; he rests in substance, not in ornament. Hence, one discards the former and takes the latter.

Mr. He says: To abide in profundity and not in shallowness means to honor **virtue** over **rites**; to rest in substance and not in ornament means to embody the **Dao** without relying on **wisdom**. The former is called shallow and ornamental; the latter, profound and true. Again, he says: This passage divides the six terms—**Dao**, **virtue**, **benevolence**, **righteousness**, **rites**, and **wisdom**—with **Dao** being nameless and **virtue** named. From **virtue** arise **benevolence**, **righteousness**, **rites**, and **wisdom**, each descent growing more refined, hence this chapter ranks **Dao** as first, **virtue** as second, **benevolence** as third, **righteousness** as fourth, **rites** as fifth, and **wisdom** as sixth.

Mr. Lü says: The sage, witnessing the **Dao** in existence, values purity and profundity, discarding superficiality. He anchors himself in sincerity, rejecting ornamentation. By abandoning the superficial trappings of **rites** and embracing the vast body of the **Dao**, the world is governed. It is like cultivating one’s life: returning to simplicity, earnest as uncarved wood—abiding in profundity. Walking where there is no path, moving without trace—avoiding shallowness. Emerging without gate, entering the boundless—resting in substance. Focusing the spirit, suppressing perception—discarding ornament. To abandon superficiality and take profundity—what could be more attainable?

**Chapter Thirty-Nine**
In ancient times, those who attained **Oneness**—
the heavens attained **Oneness** and became clear,
the earth attained **Oneness** and became tranquil,
the spirit attained **Oneness** and became numinous,
the **valleys** attained **Oneness** and became full,
all things attained **Oneness** and were born,
and the sovereigns attained **Oneness** to uphold the **Dao** for all under heaven.
This is how **Oneness** is achieved.

Mr. He says: In *Zhuangzi*, it is said that *"the Primordial had neither being nor non-being, and the nameless gave rise to **Oneness**."* Before **Oneness** took form, those who attained it in antiquity placed it at the chapter’s beginning, declaring that **Oneness** as the **Dao** has existed since ancient times. **Oneness** is what the **Dao** generates; it is not called "attaining the **Dao**" but "attaining **Oneness**," revealing the **Dao’s** supremacy—its singularity, unpaired, unmatched by anything. *Liezi* speaks of the "uncarved," doubting the solitary; this speaks of **Oneness**’s essence. Hence, it is said: *"Doubt the solitary, for the **Dao** cannot be exhausted."*

Mr. Lin says: **Oneness** is the **Dao**. The heavens, through it, are clear and manifest their forms; the earth, through it, is stable and bears all things; the spirit, through it, is empty yet numinous; the **valleys**, through it, are empty yet full. All things, through it, are born. The sovereigns, through it, uphold rectitude for all under heaven. Their attainment of it is what brings clarity, tranquility, numinosity, fullness, birth, and rectitude.

Mr. Lü says: Those who attained **Oneness** in antiquity—after the Primordial **Dao** had divided—were able to make the heavens clear, the earth tranquil, the spirit numinous, the **valleys** full, all things born, and the sovereigns rectify the world. There is no other reason: they attained the marvelous workings of **One Qi**. **One Qi** is unmatched; harmonized with the world, it is the child of the Great **Dao**, the mother of numinous clarity, the precursor of the Undifferentiated, and the ancestor of all transformations. Constant above and below, unchanging from ancient to modern, hence the sage draws near by examining himself.

The heavens are the rounded symbol of the head; attaining it makes the beard turn black, the eyes bright, the ears sharp—all senses become keen and clear. The earth is the true yellow field; attaining it makes one bear all things, nurture all growth, and remain stable and unmoving. The spirit attains it by chewing the six breaths, condensing the embryonic essence, and then transforming into numinous clarity. The **valleys** attain it, filling the brain and marrow, making the celestial **valleys** empty yet full, generating the true purple essence. All things—the six viscera, five organs, nine orifices, and hundred bones—attaining it make the seven fluids flow freely, the five entrails firm, the **Qi** harmonized and nourishing, thus enabling longevity and enduring vision. The sovereigns are the primordial spirit and heart-mind; attaining it elevates one instantly to sainthood, rectifying the form. There is no other reason: all is the work of **One Qi**.

Mr. Wu says: **Oneness** is the **virtue** of the void. In the previous chapter, it is called *"embracing **Oneness**,"* *"being **Oneness**."* In later chapters, it is said that *"the **Dao** generates **Oneness**."* All refer to this. *Zhuangzi* calls it *"Great Unity"* (*Taiyi*), yet also simply *"**Oneness**."* This is the natural work of the **Dao**; its operation is empty yet not full, yielding yet not grasping, soft yet not rigid—all as stated in the previous chapter, now fully revealed.

The one attained is called attaining this **Oneness** as **virtue**; the *yi* [以] is said because of this, one attains such effects. The clarity of Heaven, the tranquility of Earth, the responsiveness of Spirit, the fullness of the grains, the ceaseless generation of all things—how the sovereigns stand above the world—all are due to attaining this **Oneness**.

Heaven, without this **Oneness** to clarify, would fear splitting. Earth, without this **Oneness** to tranquilize, would fear erupting. Spirit, without this **Oneness** to enliven, would fear fading. The grains, without this **Oneness** to fill, would fear exhaustion. All things, without this **Oneness** to generate, would fear extinction. The sovereigns, without this **Oneness** to uphold their rectitude and nobility, would fear collapse.

Mr. He says: Clarity belongs to Heaven. If one does not know this **Oneness**, this **Dao**, to clarify it, then day would be as dark as night, and clarity would split asunder. Tranquility belongs to Earth. If one does not know this **Oneness**, this **Dao**, to tranquilize it, then mountains would crumble and rivers dry up, and tranquility would erupt in chaos. Responsiveness belongs to Spirit. If one does not know this **Oneness**, this **Dao**, to enliven it, then Spirit would lose its responsiveness and fade away. Fullness belongs to the grains. If one does not know this **Oneness**, this **Dao**, to fill them, then the grains would not be full and would wither away. Generation belongs to all things. If one does not know this **Dao**, this **Oneness**, to generate them, then their generation would fail and they would decay.

As for the sovereigns, with one body ruling above the subjects, attaining **Oneness** and embodying **Oneness**—only then can they uphold rectitude for the world. If not for this **Oneness**, this **Dao**, then those in high positions would be in peril, those with virtue of two or three would face disaster, and the calamity of decline would surely come—how could one not be warned?

Rectitude is uprightness.

Mr. Lü says: Heaven is the accumulated **virtue** of Yang, symbolizing the sovereign. If it lacks clarity, then Yin and Yang would be disordered, the cosmic order would collapse, **Oneness** would not flow, and signs of splitting would appear. Earth is the accumulated **virtue** of Yin, symbolizing the ministers. If it lacks tranquility, then rigidity and softness would falter, mountains and rivers would crumble, **Oneness** would not be preserved, and signs of eruption would manifest. Spirit is the unfathomable interplay of Yin and Yang. If it lacks responsiveness, then calamities and omens would arise, and **Oneness** would not reside, bringing the time of decline.

The grains are empty yet contain all things. If they lack fullness, then the flow of nourishment would fail, and the fear of collapse would arise. **Oneness** would not be full, and the peril of exhaustion would begin. All things have form. If they lack generation, they would wither and dry up. **Oneness** would disperse, and the time of extinction would come.

The sovereign unites the Three Realms and possesses all things. If he lacks rectitude and nobility, then officials would not fulfill their duties, the four seas would not submit, and the calamity of decline would follow.

Moreover, within a man’s body, the most Yang aspect shines above—from the Heavenly Pillar, the first vertebra, to the Jade Capital above the Heavenly Axis—all is Heaven. If it lacks clarity, then the marrow would diminish, teeth would fall out, vision would dim, and the form would decay and split. If his Earth lacks tranquility, then the true soil would dry up, the yellow sprouts would not grow, and the vital essences would erupt and dissipate. If his Spirit lacks responsiveness, then the embryo would wither, the prenatal sage would scatter, the seven fluids would not flow, and they would eventually exhaust and perish. If his myriad things do not generate, then the joints would lack vitality, the organs would weaken, and he would end in stillness and extinction. If the sovereign relies on his high nobility, then the Cinnabar Field would not be upright, the true nature would stray, the heart-shadow would become askew, and he would fear collapse.

Mr. Lin says: *Klie* [裂] means to split apart. *Fa* [发] means to move restlessly. *Xie* [歇] means to fade and lose responsiveness. *Jie* [竭] means exhaustion. The empty can receive; the non-empty stops and cannot receive.

*Ju* [蹶] means to topple. To be in a high and noble position without this **Oneness** is to court collapse.

Thus, nobility depends on humility as its foundation, and height depends on lowliness as its base. Therefore, the sovereigns call themselves "lonely," "widowed," and "without grains." Is this not taking humility as the foundation?

Mr. Lin says: The phrases "nobility depends on humility" and "height depends on lowliness" are merely metaphors. Without lowliness, how could there be nobility? Without the base, how could there be height? Lowliness and humility are the foundation of nobility and height.

The sovereign’s self-designation—"lonely," "widowed," "without grains"—are all words of self-debasement, also used as metaphors for emptiness and selflessness. Is this not so? Saying, "Is this not so?"—how could it be otherwise? These two words are a marvel of literary style.

Mr. Wu says: This chapter speaks of six things in parallel, and below it elaborates on the "noble" and "high" from above, specifically addressing the sovereigns. The sovereign’s position is noble and high, yet the **virtue** of **Oneness** does not seek fullness—only by placing oneself in lowliness can one attain it. "Lonely" is like an orphan without a father, "widowed" is like a woman without a husband, and "without grains" means "not virtuous"—these are all unflattering names no one would wish to bear, yet the sovereigns use them to describe themselves, thus placing themselves in lowliness.

Earlier, it was said, "Lowliness is the base, humility is the foundation," and later only "humility is the foundation"—this is a concise way of encompassing both.

Mr. Li says: Is this not so? How could it be otherwise?

Mr. He says: Nobility originates from humility, height is founded on lowliness. Therefore, the sovereigns call themselves "lonely," "widowed," "without assistance," "without virtue," and "without grains"—thus returning to their roots without losing the **Dao**.

*The Classic of Changes* says: "Constantly uphold **Oneness** in **virtue**, use humility to regulate rites. The steadfastness of constant **virtue**, the power of humble **virtue**." This is the Daoist **Dao** of the "feminine **Oneness**"—guarding simplicity while applying broadly.

Thus, "attaining ten chariots without desiring chariots" [致数车无车], originally "attaining ten praises without desiring praise" [致数誉无誉]—the character *yù* [誉] was mistaken.

Mr. Lin says: "Attaining ten chariots without desiring chariots" [致数车无车]—the original text reads "attaining ten praises without desiring praise" [致数誉无誉], where *yù* [誉] was incorrectly written.

This passage is a metaphor, and if *yù* [誉] is used, the following lines—"like jade, like stone"—lose their connection in meaning.

Mr. Lin says: "The term *shù chē* [数车] refers to the components of a chariot—none of which alone is called a chariot. Similarly, the *shù* [数] of my single body—none of which alone is called *wǒ* [我, 'I']. When the hub and spokes unite, it is called a chariot; when the Four Great Elements unite, it is called *wǒ*. To not desire to be like jade or like stone is to forget both nobility and humility, embracing only Oneness."

Mr. He says: "To focus on *shù chē* while splitting over the distinction of names and numbers, or to fixate on jade and stone while favoring the extremes of nobility and lowliness, is to stray from the Dao of Oneness and origin. As *Zhuangzi* states: *All things are equal in Oneness—what is short, what is long?* Observing things through the Dao, what is noble, what is base? This is the way. Thus, the chapter’s concluding metaphor serves as a warning.

Mr. Chái says: "The wheels, spokes, and other parts are the humble components of a chariot, yet without them, no chariot exists. Counting all these parts, none alone is called a chariot—hence, *shù chē wú chē*. Jade emerging from stone—one does not exalt jade over stone, for the longer it endures, the more it forgets its origin."

**Chapter Forty: The Returning is the Dao’s movement; the yielding is the Dao’s use.**

Mr. Lin says: "Returning is returning to origin, to stillness. Stillness is the source from which movement arises—just as *The Classic of Changes* says: *Gèn* [艮] completes both beginning and end. Only by yielding can one grow strong; clinging to strength alone leads to fracture. Movement relies on stillness as its function; strength relies on yielding as its use."

Mr. Wú says: "The Dao’s utmost stillness returns to the Dao, hence movement arises. The Dao’s original emptiness returns to the Dao, hence existence emerges. Thus, the root of emptiness and indistinctness, the origin from which all things spring—bound by the mechanisms of emergence and return, confined to the realms of movement and stillness—these are the body and function of the Dao.

To grasp the true nature of things, one must uphold their origin. To govern the transformations of things, one must uphold their fundamental principle. Thus, when movement generates life, if one does not align with its transformation, one knows that emergence must return to return. If one does not align with its passage, one knows that movement must return to stillness. Observing things as they emerge, one sees they must ultimately return. Observing things as they move, one sees they must ultimately still. Thus, the heart of Heaven and Earth becomes visible.

In spring and summer, all things flourish in abundance, their roots and shoots bursting forth, their flowers and fruits unfolding outward—emerging and living. Is this not returning to movement? In autumn and winter, all things shed their flowers and bear fruit, withdrawing inward, returning to their innate nature, orderly yet unforced. Is this not yielding in use?

Consider the body: to cultivate longevity, one must recognize the origin of life. To seek immortality, one must understand the mechanisms of emergence and return—this is called returning, this is called movement and stillness. Returning is the restoration of the true essence; movement is the activation of Heaven’s inherent potential.

From the first emergence of *yīn-yáng* [一阳], within the stillness of definition, the pivot turns, the currents flow—grasp the meaning of seven returns, govern and supplement the Three Fields, circulate the Hundred Vessels. Without movement, one cannot restore life—thus, *The Returning is the Dao’s movement*.

The Dao is formless and soundless. Only the yielding can subdue the rigid; only the weak can subdue the strong. Thus, one must steadfastly guard the feminine and stillness, like an infant before it speaks. Moreover, nothing in the world is as yielding and weak as water—water is surpassed only by *qi*, and *qi* is surpassed only by the Dao. The yielding and weak of Dao-*qi*-spirit-water penetrates metal and stone, melts the sharp, grinds the hard—nothing it cannot enter. Thus, *The yielding is the Dao’s use*.

Mr. Lin says: "Returning is the Dao’s movement—the spirit emerges and is immediately drawn back. Yielding is the Dao’s use—concentrating *qi* to achieve softness."

All things under Heaven are born of *yǒu* [有], and *yǒu* is born of *wú* [无].

Mr. Lin says: "Only after Heaven and Earth exist do all things emerge—thus, things are born of *yǒu*. Yet who gave birth to Heaven and Earth? At their origin, they emerged from the Great Emptiness—thus, they are born of *wú*. Through movement, stillness, strength, and yielding, we speak of the beginning of *yǒu* and *wú*."

Mr. Lǚ says: "All things under Heaven—*yǒu* and *wú* generate each other, alternately emerging and returning, without end. Without *yǒu*, one cannot reveal *wú*; without *wú*, one cannot generate *yǒu*. All things are born within Heaven and Earth—thus, they arise from *yǒu*. Heaven and Earth take form within the Great Emptiness—thus, *yǒu* arises from *wú*. When *yǒu* is born, it returns to non-being; when form is born, it returns to formlessness. The mutual generation of *yǒu* and *wú* is like a cycle, and the meaning of returning is profound indeed.

The Sage deeply comprehends this principle. Observing water, one does not watch its flow but its return-flow. Observing wood, one does not watch its blossoms but their withering—thus, though things differ in myriad ways, the Dao is evident upon sight.

Yet Heaven and Earth are the parents of all things, and humans are the most refined of all things. What is *yǒu* in us is form; what is *wú* in us is spirit. The embryo forms our shape—*yǒu* born of *wú*. The Hundred Spirits arise from the body—*wú* born of *yǒu*.

Thus, true *yǒu* is not *yǒu*; true *wú* is not *wú*. Those who grasp this meaning naturally harmonize with true *yǒu* and true *wú*."

Mr. He says: "All things under Heaven are born of *yǒu*, and *yǒu* is born of *wú*—this is seeking the origin. Only after Heaven and Earth exist do all things emerge. Heaven and Earth are the first origin of all things. Before Heaven and Earth, there was the undifferentiated chaos—this too is the first origin of Heaven and Earth.

*Wú* generates *yǒu*; formlessness generates form. When *yǒu* is born, it returns to non-being; when form is born, it returns to formlessness. Beginning from *wú*, it disperses into *yǒu*; ending in *yǒu*, it returns to *wú*. Thus, the Dao’s simplicity does not ultimately disperse into things. Those who drift and forget returning are destined to lose strength and stray far from the Dao."

Mr. Li says: "All things under Heaven are born of *yǒu*, and thus *yǒu* is named the Mother of all things. *Yǒu* is born of *wú*, and thus *wú* is named the beginning of Heaven and Earth.

Chapter Forty-One: The superior man, hearing the Dao, strives and practices it. The mediocre man, hearing the Dao, is uncertain whether it exists or not. The inferior man, hearing the Dao, laughs at it—if he does not laugh, it is not the Dao.

Mr. Lin says: "To strive and practice it means that upon hearing, one must believe. To be uncertain whether it exists or not means to both believe and doubt, to regard it as both *yǒu* and *wú*. The most vulgar and coarse of men will simply laugh at it. Only when the lowest of men laugh at it does one truly see the Dao’s exalted nature.

Han Yu’s critique of literature states: 'If people laugh at it, one should take it as joy—how much more so for the Dao.'

Mr. He says: "The Dao is natural; those who practice it can attain it, those who hear it can speak of it. Those who practice it diligently are the superior among men. The common man seeks the Dao without sincerity or depth, wavering between existence and non-existence, clinging to thoughts and intentions—one in ten thousand may achieve it. As for the vulgar and shallow of the world, though they may appear modest and weak, they laugh at the void as absurd, yet their laughter is insufficient to expose the Dao’s greatness, for those who truly know the Dao are rare.

Mr. Lü says: "The superior man is endowed with pure and thick virtue, penetrating profound principles. Hearing the Dao, he instantly comprehends and believes without doubt, firmly resolving to practice it, fearing only that he cannot attain it. Thus it is said: *strive and practice it*.

The mediocre man is of moderate nature, capable of both high and low. Initially delighted, he later grows weary; at first diligent, he later becomes lazy. Hearing the Dao, he hesitates and doubts, unable to decide between acceptance and rejection. Though he wishes to advance, he lacks the resolve to act; though he wishes to retreat, he still hesitates. Thus it is said: *uncertain whether it exists or not*.

The inferior man is of impure and muddled nature, failing to grasp ultimate truth. Hearing the Dao, he deems it absurd and ridiculous, not only disbelieving but laughing and condemning it. Yet if the inferior do not laugh at it, how can it be called the pure and unadulterated Dao?

Therefore, it is said: *To establish speech, the Dao appears obscure; to advance the Dao, it seems to retreat; the great Dao appears common; virtue’s height resembles a valley; supreme brightness seems like shame; vast virtue appears insufficient; established virtue seems like theft; true substance seems corrupted; the greatest square has no corners; great vessels take long to form; great sound is rare in tone; great form is shapeless.*

Mr. Lin says: *To establish speech* means to found doctrine. The scholars of antiquity, in their teachings—from *The Dao appears obscure* onward—have spoken these words. These are merely rhetorical devices, not original to me but found in the ancients.

*The Dao appears obscure* means that only through obscurity does clarity arise. The previous chapter states: *Self-evident is not true clarity*, and again: *Not self-evident, hence clarity*. This is the same.

*To advance the Dao, it seems to retreat*—retreat is true advancement. *Ji* (夷) means level; the great Dao is level, without distinction. *Rú* (类) means same; it harmonizes with the dust, blending light with the mundane.

*Virtue’s height resembles a valley*—to be empty and not self-aggrandizing. *Supreme brightness seems like shame*—not dazzlingly distinct. *Vast virtue appears insufficient*—if one is self-sufficient, it becomes narrow. *Theft*—to desire to act but dare not. To establish virtue is to have purpose; to establish it through what one dares not do is how virtue is truly built. *True substance seems corrupted*—pure and unchanging substance, yet appearing as if it were ever-shifting. This too conveys the meaning of sufficiency without self-sufficiency.

*The greatest square*—this is the boundless void. Though the void has east, west, south, and north, who can perceive its corners? *Great vessels take long to form*—like casting a tripod; how could it be hastened? *Great sound is rare in tone*—among the sounds between Heaven and Earth, none surpass the wind and thunder, yet they are not always present. *Rare* means seldom seen. *Great form*—Heaven and Earth. The *Yijing* says: *No symbol is greater than Heaven and Earth*—who can fully perceive their form?

Mr. He says: *To establish speech*—the ancients who founded doctrine, as mentioned below, were all ridiculed by the world, yet the great Dao endured. *The Dao appears obscure*—great enlightenment is like no enlightenment. *To advance the Dao, it seems to retreat*—great courage is like no courage. *The great Dao appears common*—equality is like no equality. *Virtue’s height resembles a valley*—substance is like emptiness. *Supreme brightness seems like shame*—purity is like impurity. *Vast virtue appears insufficient*—having abundance yet not daring to exhaust it. *Established virtue seems like theft*—established in Heaven and Earth yet acting like one who seeks fleeting ease. *True substance seems corrupted*—substance like gold and stone, yet appearing to decay over time. *The greatest square has no corners*—no doors, no rooms. *Great vessels take long to form*—uncarved, unpolished. *Great sound is rare in tone*—not in tune with pitch pipes. *Great form is shapeless*—separate from color, separate from name.

The Dao is hidden and nameless. Only the Dao can be freely given and fully realized. Mr. Lin says: *The Dao is hidden and nameless*—it cannot be named. *To give* means to bestow upon others. Those with the Dao can give to others without possessing it themselves. *Realized*—the great fulfillment of the Dao, fulfilling self and others, and only then is it called great fulfillment.

「○ Master He says: *The Nameless*—cannot be pointed out and named, hence there are things like those described above that cannot be fully captured by words. Yet though the Dao is nameless, it exists and is truly good at lending and bringing to fruition. *Lending*—bestowing and applying through resonance, universally present in all beings, pervasively encompassing all things, like Heaven’s bestowal and Earth’s nurturing. The small fulfills the small, the great fulfills the great—what is there that is not the Dao?

The *Zhuangzi* says: The Dao transforms and lends to all things, yet the people do not rely upon it. It holds nothing aloft to claim fame, letting things rejoice on their own. Standing in the unfathomable, it wanders in the boundless—this is the Dao.

**Chapter 42:** *The Dao gives rise to One, One to Two, Two to Three, Three to all things.* All things bear yin within them and embrace yang, blending the *qi* to achieve harmony.

○ Master He says: *The void gives rise to Nature, Nature to the Dao, the Dao to One, One to Heaven and Earth, Heaven and Earth to all things.* All things arise from One and are fulfilled, receiving the subtle and wondrous *qi* and transformations. Whether it is beings or things, turning away from cold toward warmth, from darkness toward light, from evil toward good—all are expressions of *bearing yin and embracing yang*. *Embracing yang* is the same as *embracing One*, the Dao’s breath of life and its transformations. Man shares with Heaven and Earth as the threefold realm, yet among all things, he is the most responsive. Hence, man is Heaven’s trust of *shen* (spirit), Earth’s bestowal of *jing* (essence), and the *chong* (intermediary) and *he* (harmonious) *qi*’s nurturing. Man is precious in guarding his *qi*, preserving his essence, and cherishing his spirit—thus standing with Heaven and Earth, his means of sustaining the *chong* and *he*, to be modest rather than full. The *Classic of Documents* says: *Fullness invites loss, modesty invites gain*—this is the Dao of Heaven.

○ Master Lin says: *One*—the Supreme Ultimate. *Two*—Heaven and Earth. *Three*—the Three Realms. All things arise from the void. The Dao is the origin of all things, the principle of Nature. Once the Three Ultimate realms are established, then all things are born. The birth of all things depends on bearing and embracing yin and yang *qi*, moving through the void’s harmony. This is how harmony comes to be.*

○ Master Lü says: *The Dao gives rise to One Qi, One Qi to Heaven and Earth, Heaven and Earth to all things.* The reason the Dao gives rise to One is that the Supreme Ultimate was yet undifferentiated, a pure and undivided One Qi. *One gives rise to Two*—meaning that clarity and turbidity were divided, and thus Heaven and Earth took their places. As the *Yijing* says: *The Great Ultimate generates the Two Principles*—this is correct. *Majestic and resplendent it emerges from Heaven; solemn and austere it issues from Earth.* The two intermingle to form harmony, hence the order of humanity is achieved. *One yin, one yang*—this is the Dao. Saying *bearing and embracing*—they cannot be separated for even an instant. What can be separated is not the Dao. Those who cultivate and nourish understand that the moon is the mother of all yin, and the sun is the father of all yang. Therefore, those who bear and embrace should delight in absorbing Heaven’s light and chew upon the harmonious *qi*, letting the Heavenly Essence fill and overflow, the two luminaries intertwine, and all spirits rest in peace. Entering and exiting the primal transformations, they soar in pairs to the *Yuqing* (Jade Purity).

○ Master Dong says: *For all living things, their backs face stillness*—belonging to yin’s quiet nature. *Their mouths, noses, ears, and eyes reside at the front*—belonging to yang’s dynamic nature. Hence it is said: *bearing yin while embracing yang*. Like plants, they turn their backs to cold and face warmth. The *chong* (intermediary) *qi* moves between them, and when spoken of generally, the spirit is yang while the substance is yin. The spirit moves within—this is *embracing*, while the substance appears without—this is *bearing*. The *qi* is yang in motion and yin in stillness, then balanced by the harmonious center, blending within form and substance, the mechanism upon which the spirit rides.

What man hates most are *孤寡不谷* (loneliness, widowhood, and barrenness), yet kings and nobles take these as their titles. Hence, some things are diminished to gain, while others are gained to diminish. Master He says: *Kings and nobles who claim loneliness, widowhood, and barrenness*—do so by adopting the guise of being unsupported and unassisted. This is their name for self-humility and preservation.

The three titles are what man most detests, yet they are also what man uses to teach. Those in high station fall, the arrogant perish—this is the natural course. Yet within loss there is gain, and within gain there is loss. This is the changing nature of things. Hence, the Sage acts as the master of the Three Realms, holding the *chong* (intermediary) and containing the harmony, willingly enduring what man hates, willingly receiving what things diminish. How does he know that those who humble themselves are not exalted, those who retreat are not surpassed? Evil is not always evil, loss is not always loss. The *Classic* says: *The Dao is void yet its use is inexhaustible; fullness is its opposite.* The *Yijing* says: *What is great cannot be filled*—hence it is received with modesty, for modesty is the opposite of fullness.

○ Master Lin says: *What man hates most*—nothing compares to *孤寡不谷* (loneliness, widowhood, and barrenness), yet kings and nobles take these as their titles. This is also an analogy for the Sage’s self-humility and self-abasement. Its meaning is that Heaven, Earth, and man all arise from the void. All things take yin and yang as their foundation, yet their means of birth are all the harmonious *qi* of the void. Those who study the Dao should embody this meaning, thus valuing the ability to be empty and without. In the world, some things seem to diminish yet actually bring gain, while others seem to bring gain yet actually bring loss. The principles of loss and gain are not fixed—like the moon, when full it wanes, this is gain turned to loss. When it wanes, it must wax again, this is loss turned to gain. What man teaches, I also teach.*

The overbearing and forceful shall not meet a proper end—I shall take this as my teaching father.

Master He says: In *The Golden Back Inscription* it is written: *"The overbearing and forceful shall not meet a proper end."* It further states that those who delight in victory shall inevitably encounter their match, thus showing that the ancients all took this as a teaching, and I too do not seek to differ—I take this as my teaching as well. Moreover, the one who serves as the teaching father is because it is simple and easy to grasp.

The teaching father is the mother of the Dao—nothing else. Heaven, Earth, and all things arise from the One Dao, taking the void as their essence.

○ Master Li says: Whatever teachings man offers are nothing but the pursuit of goodness—I too teach in this way.

○ Master Wu says: This speaks of the principle of using softness. *Liang* (梁) also means "strong." To block water with wood, to bear beams with wood—both are called *liang*, taking from their strength. "Not meeting a proper end" means not meeting a good death, as in Zilu’s case, who walked resolutely until Confucius said he would not meet a proper end, and indeed he later died in the disaster of Kong Yue.

The "teaching father" is like the root of teaching—father refers to that which is supreme and unexcelled above all else.

Man’s teachings instruct in the use of softness—I too teach this. Those who are overbearing and forceful, unable to embrace softness, shall inevitably fail to preserve themselves. The benefit of overcoming others with forcefulness brings the loss of not meeting a proper end—this is what is meant by "gain turned to loss," and it is the foremost principle for teaching others. Hence, it is called the teaching father.

○ Master Ni says: Laozi often likens the Dao to a mother, yet here it is called father. The mother governs nurturing, while the father governs teaching. For one who takes the role of teacher, it is fitting to be revered and stern as a father.

**Chapter 43**
The softest thing under Heaven
Ranges freely over the hardest thing under Heaven.

「There is nothing that enters the *Wujian* [無間, "utterly seamless" or "void of gaps"], and thus I know that *Wuwei* [無為, "non-action"] brings benefit. Master Lin says: The firm may be easily broken, while the soft endures forever. To act with the utmost softness between the firmest things is like water’s ability to penetrate stone. *Wujian* refers to the absence of seams or fissures. *Wuwei* [無有, "void of form"] is the same as *Wuwei* [無形, "formlessness"]. As in the human body, the *Rongwei* [榮衛, "nourishing and defensive *qi*"] circulate between the seams, and the *qi* and meridians flow through them. Even in the gaps where there is no fissure, sunlight and moonlight enter—these are all cases of *wuwei* [無有, "void of form"] entering *wujian* [無間, "seamlessness"].

○ Master He says: Under Heaven, nothing is softer than *qi* [氣], and *qi* is nothing softer than the Dao/Way. The Dao/Way’s softness is its ability to envelop Heaven and Earth, to penetrate all things. Laozi once spoke thus to Guan Yin, explaining that the Dao/Way and *qi* are soft in this manner. The *qi* of Heaven and Earth, though as firm as metal and stone, penetrates everywhere. The Dao/Way is the same: its origin is subtle yet simple, its culmination vast yet firm and enduring. Thus, the utmost softness is not weakness, and the utmost firmness is not strength—like the cascading waters of Mount Tai eroding stone, or the hemp rope severing the *danji* [單極, "single pillar"]. This is of the same kind.

To "drive and range" [驰骋] is the *Zhuangzi*’s meaning of "riding Heaven and Earth, ranging over all things." One who can wield the Dao/Way to govern existence moves from the utmost firmness to the original softness, from the original softness to the primordial *Yuan* [元, "origin"]. As in the *Liezi*: "The body of *Liezi* is in harmony with the heart, the heart with *qi*, *qi* with spirit, and spirit with the void." By this, one sees that the Dao/Way is utterly seamless in its operation, and its subtlety is profound. Then one knows the benefit of *wuwei* [無為, "non-action"], where non-action is the return to the Dao/Way, stillness is the dissipation of *qi*, and the root of life is preserved by entering the *wujian* [無間, "void of gaps"]. One may attain this through silent contemplation.

○ Master Lü says: Under Heaven, nothing surpasses water, yet even water is not entirely without form. Some things seem formless but enter into form—this is *qi*. *Qi* is inherently soft, and even though *qi* is soft, when nourished to its utmost firmness and strength, no one can resist it. To govern firmness with firmness, if it does not break, it will shatter. To govern firmness with softness, neither the softness is worn away nor the firmness is harmed. The Dao/Way’s ultimate softness is the same as *qi*. To range over the utmost firmness means that *qi* can penetrate joints and open passages—thus, the softest thing under Heaven ranges over the hardest thing under Heaven. When formless enters form, the formless is never exhausted, and the formed never sated. Only the *qi* of the Great *Fan* [梵, "pervading, transcendent"] is without form or substance, and thus it can envelop Heaven and Earth without boundary, and cling to the finest hairs without interiority. The sage embodies this, and his virtue/power is thus realized.

When one sees the Dao/Way clearly, nothing is impassable—this is how one governs all under Heaven through divine transformation, likening the ultimate person to the *Qi Mu* [氣母, "matrix of *qi*"], refining and drawing forth the *Taihe* [太和, "Grand Harmony"]. Divine clarity resides within him, unrestricted in its coming and going, profound beyond measure. Thus, it is said: "There is nothing that enters the *wujian* [無間, 'void of gaps']." The sage of *wuwei* [無為, "non-action"] dwells before the *Xiangdi* [象帝, "Archetypal Sage"] and in the midst of the *Taiji* [太极, "Grand Polarity"]. The Dao/Way’s wondrous application truly lies here. The sage follows it, standing without instruction, sitting without debate, moving with emptiness and returning with substance—nothing is impossible. How could this be a minor supplement?

The ultimate person practices *wuwei* [無為, "non-action"] and does nothing, yet his body is not weary. He forgets self and others, yet his essence is not diminished. He does not desire the Dao/Way, yet the Dao/Way naturally returns to him. Thus, it is said: "I know that *wuwei* [無為, 'non-action'] brings benefit."

The teaching of *wuwei* [無為, "non-action"]—that is, the benefit of non-action—is rare under Heaven. Master Lin says: The first two sentences are all analogies. By this, one knows that *wuwei* [無為, "non-action"] is not the same as *wuwei* [無不為, "doing nothing"]. Non-action as teaching, and spontaneous action without effort—these are all the wondrous applications of the Dao/Way, and the people of the world cannot comprehend them. Thus, it is said: "The world rarely attains this."

Benefit [有益] means having practical efficacy. ○ Master He says: What is meant by "non-action as teaching"? Like Heaven and Earth possessing great beauty yet not speaking, the four seasons having clear laws yet not debating, and all things having established principles yet not explaining—when sincerity is felt, what need is there for words? What is meant by "the benefit of non-action"? Like using a boat to travel on water, or a sled to traverse mountains, or making fields from high ground and ponds from low ground—the transformations of simplicity, what need is there for action? This is all due to focusing *qi* to achieve utmost softness, and reaching the essence of non-action. The Dao/Way thus operates and transforms.

Thus, the world rarely attains this. Teaching without words, gaining without seeking—one who preserves sincerity and embraces the Dao/Way will have the world follow naturally. Who else could possibly aspire to this?

**Chapter 44**
Which is closer to me: name or body?
Which is more abundant: body or wealth?
Which is more harmful: gain or loss?
Master Lin says: Name and wealth are external things, of no benefit to my body. Thus, even if one gains or loses them, what harm could they truly cause? Yet those who do not know the Dao/Way often trouble themselves over these matters.

○ Master He says: *Zhuangzi*’s Bo Yi died for his name beneath the slopes of Shouyang, while the robber Zhi died for profit atop the mounds of Dongling. Though their deaths differed, both perished in vain, harming their true nature. The zealous martyr for name, the greedy one for profit—both sacrifice their bodies, and though they die without regret, they never realize that their bodies are heavier than all under Heaven, let alone that dying for name or profit could be a burden. One who knows the Dao/Way regards even the body as not truly his own—how much more so for beautiful names and beautiful profits, which are external things!

The unknowing try to compare name with their life’s fate, saying that name is like a flower, while the body inherently has name. If the name is high and the body is endangered, then is the name not distant from the body? They compare wealth with their life’s fate, saying that wealth is like a resource for the body. Yet even if wealth is abundant and the body is harmed, is not wealth then lesser than the body?

Thus, it is said:
*"Sheng wo ming zhe sha wo shen"* [生我名者殺我身, "Those who give me life through name kill my body"],
*"Yi wo huo zhe sun wo shen"* [益我货者损我神, "Those who enrich me with wealth harm my spirit"].

At the beginning, when there is neither gain nor loss, it is like water before waves. But when loss comes after what has already been lost, harm arises naturally—who could compare this to the peace of original non-existence?

Who could escape harm after what has already been lost?

○ **Master Lü** says: To prioritize the body over name and value the body above wealth is still not to forget oneself. But he who forgets himself does not even possess the body—how much less does he possess name and wealth!

Indeed, name without substance is hollow, and substance without name is true. The noble man toils night and day, pondering good and ill, yet even his form grows thin. The wealthy man exhausts his body with labor, competing for fame and hoarding wealth, yet cannot enjoy it all—his form becomes but an alien vessel.

Yet the sage values the body as the foundation for all under Heaven, not through forgetting himself, but by making all under Heaven know that name is insufficient for intimacy and wealth is insufficient for abundance. Thus, **Bò Yì** starved to death beneath Chief Yang’s slope, a sacrifice to name. **Zhóu Jī** died upon the Eastern Ridge, a sacrifice to profit.

Between name and wealth, which is more abundant, which is less? To sacrifice the body for name, the world will call him a **gentleman**; to sacrifice the body for profit, the world will call him a **villain**. Gain brings joy, loss brings sorrow; covetousness of gain forgets loss, and greed for profit forgets righteousness. This is like using **Duke Sui’s pearl** to shoot a thousand-foot-high sparrow—though one may strike the bird, the pearl is already lost. Truly, what is lost is heavy, what is gained is light—how lamentable!

The disease lies in desiring what one cannot obtain; when one has already gained but fears losing it, the disease is even worse than the loss itself. Only when one attains **equanimity between having and lacking**, and accepts both gain and loss with balance, does true peace begin.

Therefore, **excessive love leads to great expenditure**, and **hoarding wealth brings heavy loss**. ○ **Master Lín** says: Love clings to something, so one must inevitably exhaust mind and strength in seeking it—love grows greater, so does the exhaustion. This speaks of name. Greed leads to hoarding, and when loss comes one day, it will be great. If one hoards nothing, one loses nothing; if one hoards little, one loses little. Only through much hoarding does one invite much loss—these two lines illuminate the next three.

○ **Master Hé** says: To crave fine names is called **excessive love**; name is but a guest to substance, so the greater the name, the greater the mental strain. To dwell on rare wealth is called **hoarding**; profit is the catalyst for chaos, so the thicker the wealth, the greater the loss of what is truly valuable.

○ **Master Wú** says: Love refers to love of name; great refers to the body. Comparing name to the body, the body is greater than name. Those who admire name love it excessively, yet love of the body is not as fervent as love of name—this will inevitably drain the mind and shorten life. Thus, it is said: **great expenditure**. Hoarding refers to hoarding wealth; great also refers to the body. Comparing wealth to the body, the body is more substantial than wealth. Those who crave wealth hoard it excessively, yet those who value the body do not weigh it as heavily as those who value wealth—this will inevitably lead to the loss of life and premature death. Thus, it is said: **heavy loss**.

To know sufficiency is to avoid disgrace; to know restraint is to avoid peril—thus one may endure long.

○ **Master Lín** says: Only he who knows sufficiency does not fall into self-disgrace; only he who knows restraint does not court danger—thus he may endure long. These three lines are timeless wisdom, never exhausted by ages past or future.

○ **Master Hé** says: To know sufficiency means recognizing the proper measure in what one has already gained in name and profit, so that the body may early withdraw from strife—what disgrace could there be? To know restraint means refusing even a hair’s breadth of what one ought not to possess in name and profit, so that the body may remain hidden—what peril could there be? Only then may one endure long.

○ **Master Lü** says: A contented heart seeks nothing beyond itself, so there is no disgrace. A still mind extinguishes all entanglements, so there is no peril—thus one may endure long, may endure forever.

○ **Master Wú** says: To know the fixed measure within oneself, then one is sufficient without greed, and does not fall into disgrace through reckless actions or loss of integrity. To know that external things bring no true benefit, then one restrains without seeking, and does not court peril through depletion of life or premature death—thus one may endure long.

○ **Master Lǐ** says: Name and wealth, gain and loss—all are matters of **artifice**. They never endure long. Only to know sufficiency and restraint may one endure long.

**Chapter Forty-Five**
Great fulfillment seems like a lack, yet its use is inexhaustible.
Great abundance seems like emptiness, yet its use is endless.
Great rectitude seems like bending, great skill seems like clumsiness, great eloquence seems like stammering.

○ **Master Lín** says: Fulfillment brings lack; great fulfillment is always as if lacking, so its use is inexhaustible. Abundance brings emptiness; great abundance is always as if empty, so its use is endless. The previous chapter states: "Hollow brings abundance, worn-out brings renewal"—this is the same meaning. Great rectitude is always as if bent; what seems crooked is straight, what seems curved is whole. Great skill is always as if clumsy; it does not boast of itself. Great eloquence is always as if stammering; it does not force speech.

○ **Master Lǐ** says: The previous chapter speaks of knowing sufficiency to avoid disgrace, so it follows with *great fulfillment seems like a lack*—virtue in excess appears as deficiency, life in excess appears as poverty. Great fulfillment as lack, great abundance as emptiness, even to the point of seeming bent, clumsy, or stammering—all are virtue in excess appearing as deficiency. Its use is without end.

○ **Master Wú** says: To fulfill as fulfillment, to be abundant as abundance, to be straight as straight, to be skillful as skill, to be eloquent as eloquence—this is small. But to lack and not be fulfillment, to be empty and not be abundance, to bend and not be straight, to be clumsy and not be skillful, to stammer and not be eloquence—this is the greatness of fulfillment, abundance, straightness, skill, and eloquence.

Agitation conquers cold, stillness conquers heat—**clearing and tranquility** are the true norm for all under Heaven.

○ **Master Lín** says: Agitation’s peak is cold, stillness’s peak is heat—agitation and stillness are but the two forces of **yin and yang**. When **qi** stagnates in one extreme, it becomes illness; only **Dao’s** clearing and tranquility, neither having nor lacking, neither moving nor still, is the true norm for all under Heaven.

○ **Master Hé** says: The Dao’s norm is **clearing and tranquility**, and it is only through **clearing and tranquility** that greatness is attained. Agitation conquers cold, yet when **yang** reaches its extreme, **yin** arises; stillness conquers heat, yet when **yin** reaches its extreme, **yang** arises. All extremes arise from one-sided dominance. But when Heaven is clear and Earth is tranquil, this is the supreme norm for all under Heaven—neither dominance nor extremity—yet it is vast and enduring. The Classic says: *"If one can always maintain **clearing and tranquility**, all under Heaven will naturally return to this."*

**Laozi’s *Zhāngyì* (Collected Commentaries), Volume 6 complete.**
**Laozi’s *Zhāngyì*, Volume 7.**
**Jūjiāng Wēi Dàyǒu’s *Collection*, Chapter 46.**

*"When the Dao prevails in the world, even swift horses are turned aside to spread manure.
When the Dao does not prevail, warhorses are bred in the suburbs."*
