**Master Lǚ** says: In times of prosperity, the common people flourish, the seas are at peace, weapons are laid aside, and the people diligently till the land, competing in agriculture and sericulture. Their manure enriches the fields—though fine horses exist, they serve no martial purpose. How could this not be called the Dao?

In times of decline, all affairs falter, calamities arise, and the strong oppress the weak. Boundaries are contested, and the seeds of war take root. Warhorses gallop across battlefields, blocking the flow of **yin and yang** and stunting the growth of all things. How could this not be called the absence of the Dao?

For the body is but a microcosm of the world. The *Nánhuá Jīng* (Zhuangzi) speaks of wild horses and dust—thus, horses symbolize **qi**.

**Yang Zi** says: **Qi** is like a horse that carries one toward good or evil. Indeed, **qi** is the Dao, and the Dao is **qi**. From the One **qi**, all things arise; where **qi** lingers, things grow soft and weak, and where it departs, things harden and die.

*"However"*—the word *"however"* (却) means to reverse, *"walk"* (走) means to circulate. Circulating refined **qi** upward against the flow nourishes the brain’s blood and fertilizes the roots, thus nourishing the body. Hence: *"When the Dao prevails in the world, even swift horses are turned aside to spread manure."*

When the Dao does not prevail, one abandons roots for external pursuits, the mind agitates, and breath races upward—through the upper orifices, it disperses in great waves; from below, the **jing** (essence) leaks away in a steady drip. Daily, the heart battles, and the six evils (of excess) arise at the borders.

○ **Master Hé** says: When a ruler of the Dao governs the world, he does not use military might to dominate others but instead prioritizes agriculture and grain. Thus, even swift horses are turned aside to fertilize the fields, never neglecting the inner for the outer.

The Classic says: *"Remove the filth from one’s ancestral home, and all will be restored."* To spread manure is to mend what has decayed.

In a world without the Dao, warhorses are bred in the suburbs—
*Stallions prance in the boundless wilds.*

The suburbs originally refer to lands where horses are raised; if such lands obstruct farming, it is a warning.

○ According to **Master Wú Chéng’s** annotated edition, the original text reads: *"When the Dao prevails in the world, even swift horses are turned aside to spread manure [with carts]."* In an age of the Dao, there is no mutual invasion, so fine horses are not harnessed to war chariots but instead pulled lowly manure carts to fertilize the fields. Other commentaries omit the character *"cart"* (车), but **Huì’ān’s** *Júzǐ Yúlù* (Zhu Xi’s Dialogues) includes it. **Zhang Heng’s** *Dōngjīng Fù* (Eastern Capital Eulogy) states: *"Swift horses are turned aside to pull manure carts,"* using Laozi’s full line. **Master Wú’s** edition is well-reasoned—scholars must not overlook it.

No greater crime exists than desiring what one should not,
No greater fault than grasping what one desires,
No greater calamity than failing to know sufficiency—
Thus, the sufficiency of *"knowing sufficiency"* is eternal sufficiency.

**Master Lín** says: Wars all begin from the seed of desire. Once desire takes root, when will it ever be enough? Only by attending to what is essential does one avoid crime, calamity, and fault.

Only those who regard insufficiency as sufficiency remain truly sufficient. This further illuminates the previous chapter’s meaning: *"Knowing sufficiency brings no disgrace."*

○ **Master Wú** says: *"Crime"* refers to transgressions, *"fault"* to misfortune, and *"calamity"* to disaster—yet calamity is graver than fault. To covet the Rampart Pass and still long for Shù is the failure to know sufficiency.

○ **Master Lǐ** says: *"Crime," "fault,"* and *"calamity"* all stem from desire. Hence the conclusion: *"Knowing sufficiency, one is always sufficient."* For the contented poor find joy in poverty.

**Chapter 47.**
*"Without stepping outside, one knows all under Heaven."*

**Chapter 48.**

*"Without peering through the window, one perceives the Dao of Heaven.*
*The farther one goes, the less one knows.*
**Master Hé** says: Laozi spoke to Wenzi: *"Qi and spirit arise from within; spirit and qi move with Heaven. This speaks solely of how spirit alone can foreknow."*

○ **Master Lǐ** says: *"Without stepping outside, one knows all under Heaven"*—this means those who follow the Way know the inner and not the outer. Knowing the inner while ignoring the outer means knowing nothing; seeing the inner while ignoring the outer means seeing nothing. But if one only knows the outer and sees the outer while forgetting the inner, then one’s knowledge and perception are shallow and narrow.

○ **Master Wú** says: The principles of all things and affairs under Heaven are already complete within oneself. Thus, even without stepping outside, one knows them all. The Dao of Heaven is the root of all principles; it is grasped through inner observation, not like external, tangible things that must be glimpsed through a window. Those who do not realize that all is already within themselves must travel far to know even a single matter. Hence, the farther one goes, the less one knows—how could one ever know all under Heaven without stepping outside?

○ **Master Lín** says: Though Heaven and Earth are vast, human nature and the principles of things are fundamentally one. Even without stepping outside, one may know them. Though yin and yang transform, their patterns endure through the ages—visible even without peering through a window. If one insists on seeking them externally, how much can one truly know from where one’s feet tread? How much can one truly see with one’s own strength? The more one labors, the more one’s mind grows dim. Thus it is said: *"The farther one goes, the less one knows."*

Therefore, the Sage acts without moving yet knows, sees without looking yet understands, does without doing yet achieves. **Master Hé** says: One does not guess at others through conjecture but knows them through the Way. One does not probe Heaven through speculation but perceives it through the Way. Others are myself; myself is Heaven. If one must wait to step outside and peer through a window, then one abandons the mind for the external. When spirit is insufficient, how can knowledge and perception suffice? What relation does this have to the Way? The *Yijing* says: *"No thought, no action. Still and unmoving, yet responsive—thus one attains the patterns of all under Heaven."* Who but the most divine of all under Heaven could comprehend this? It further says: *"Only the divine moves swiftly without haste, arrives without traveling."* This is the Way of not acting yet reaching, not seeing yet knowing, not doing yet achieving.

○ **Master Lín** says: The Sage arrives without moving, understands without seeking, achieves without doing—such is the Way’s function. The *Yijing* says: *"Swift without haste, arriving without traveling"*—this is its meaning.

○ **Master Wú** says: It is not that one does not travel to places, but that one arrives at them all as if present. Thus one thoroughly knows all under Heaven’s affairs. It is not that one does not observe things before understanding their principles, but that one perceives the Dao of Heaven without peering through a window. *"To do without doing"*—this verifies the previous two sentences. Only because one does not act yet knows all matters, does not observe yet penetrates the root, does one achieve without effort.

**Chapter 48.**
*"The more one studies, the more one gains.
The more one follows the Dao, the more one diminishes.
Diminish again and again,
until one reaches non-action.
Through non-action, nothing remains undone."*

**Master Hé** says: A thousand days of studying wisdom cannot compare to a single day of studying the Dao. It is not that one studies the Dao to learn, nor that the Dao teaches itself. Learning expands knowledge and perception; only through accumulation does it take form. The Dao, however, transcends knowledge and perception—it is originally complete. Learning seeks the Dao; extensive study leads to simplicity. Through diligent practice of non-action, one awakens to the true Dao. First, one gains before diminishing; then, one enters emptiness. Having diminished again and again, even emptiness is emptiness—only then does one align with the true Dao. This is the wondrous realm of non-action.

Laozi spoke to Wenzi: *"Non-action does not mean resisting what must respond or remaining unmoved when stirred. It is not rigid stagnation but flowing with principle while acting, seizing the moment to establish merit, entrusting to others while achieving results—this is non-action yet nothing remains undone."*

○ **Master Lǐ** says: *"Without stepping outside, one knows all under Heaven"*—this is inner knowledge. *"The farther one goes"*—this is external affairs. Hence, it follows as a lesson in study. *"The more one studies, the more one gains"*—this refers to scholars seeking increase. *"The more one follows the Dao, the more one diminishes"*—this refers to those who follow the Way diminishing in all things. The *Yijing* says: *"Mountain over water: Diminishing. The superior person restrains anger and blocks desire"*—this is precisely it. Until there is no anger to restrain and no desire to block, until one reaches non-action, then all under Heaven will be transformed, and the world will turn toward it.

○ **Master Lǚ** says: The perfected one diminishes again and again, until distinctions vanish, still and unmoving, reaching non-action. They preserve the true nature of their essence and embody the origin of the Way. They harmonize with Heaven and Earth in virtue and share in creation’s function. Then they refine the Great Primordial *qi*, extract the golden essence from the elbow’s end, seal the womb and breathe inward, reverse the winds and blend them, irrigate the primal spring—all this is non-action yet nothing remains undone.

○ **Master Ní** says: *"The more one studies, the more one gains"*—this values breadth. *"The more one follows the Dao, the more one diminishes"*—this values simplicity. First, one broadens before narrowing; first, one gains before diminishing. Without gain, where would one find simplicity? To gain without diminishing turns breadth into confusion. Thus, study is the ladder to the Dao, and the Dao is the destination of study. Confucius said: *"Much learning and knowing—this is the gain of study. To thread it all with one principle—this is the diminishing of the Dao."* Having diminished, one diminishes again—this is the utmost diminishing. When diminishing reaches its end, there is nothing left to do. Though there is nothing to do, in truth, nothing remains undone. This is why non-action is precious. If one stops at mere non-action, unable to do nothing yet achieve everything, then one falls into emptiness—this is not the Dao.

*"To take all under Heaven, one must do nothing.
But when action arises, it is insufficient to take all under Heaven."*

He Shi said: *"To act without attachment, to let the mind dwell in non-doing—even if the world is vast, what room could my mind have for it? The sages of old who took all under Heaven did so through the natural Way. The yellow-roofed halls of Yao were not of his making; Shun and Yu held dominion without claiming it—all acted in accordance with their non-doing. If one acts upon the world, then one has attachment. The Classic says: 'To act is to ruin it; the world is a divine vessel that cannot be acted upon.' To act without acting, and the world is governed—this is it. This is why ancient sages did not exchange the Way for all under Heaven, yet always spoke warnings against taking the world, deeply cautioning those who did not know that they would prize the world above the Way. How could they know that non-action and non-doing are the natural Way?"*

Lin Shi said: *"To take all under Heaven, one must act without attachment. Those with attachment lose it. The Three Dynasties’ possession of the world—what room did they have for attachment? Though they spoke of wartime customs as metaphor, their words suffice as warning. Non-doing and doing are but non-attachment and attachment."*

Wu Shi said: *"Non-doing is to have nothing to attend to—this is non-action. Those who speak of taking the world mean only non-action. When virtue is abundant, the world naturally returns to it. If one must exert will and force, engaging in deliberate action, how could one ever take the world?"*

Li Shi said: *"If one rashly acts, the people grow disordered and difficult to govern. Hence, it is said one cannot take the world with such action."*

**Chapter 49**
The sage has no fixed heart, taking the hearts of the people as his own.

Lü Shi said: *"Heaven originally has no attachment to things; through things, Heaven’s heart is revealed. Thus, it is empty yet able to receive, still yet able to respond—like a mirror reflecting forms, whether fair or foul; like a valley echoing sounds, whether loud or soft. Initially, there is no other heart—what fixed heart could there be?"*

Gong Qing said: *"There is but one Way in the world, one heart in the sage. Hence, he likens it to himself. Silent and tranquil, he responds yet remains settled, neither leading nor following, blending his light with dust, harmonizing with all things—yet never differing from them. Thus it is said: the sage has no fixed heart, taking the hearts of the people as his own."*

Li Shi said: *"The previous chapter spoke of taking the world through non-doing, hence follows with the sage’s lack of fixed heart. By 'no fixed heart,' it means adapting to circumstances without opposing the nature of people and things. Thus, the people follow the sage’s words, actions, and transformations, and the world unites in one heart."*

He Shi said: *"The sage’s heart is too vast—what fixedness could it have? His fixed heart is but the heart of the world."*

Zhenghe Annotation: *"The sage’s heart is empty yet able to receive, still yet able to respond—like a mirror reflecting forms, whether fair or foul; like a valley echoing sounds, whether loud or soft. What fixedness could there be? To the virtuous, I am virtuous; to the unvirtuous, I am also virtuous—virtue is virtue. To the trustworthy, I trust them; to the untrustworthy, I also trust them—virtue is trust."*

Lü Shi said: *"Good and evil contradict each other; truth and deceit mock each other. Good and evil, truth and deceit—these are the common distinctions of the world. Thus, those who love virtue, the sage does not defy their nature but aids them with supreme virtue, ensuring they fulfill their goodness. If there are those who are unvirtuous, he also treats them with virtue. Their virtue or lack thereof lies with them, yet my way of treating them with virtue never wavers. All conduct is without exception virtuous, and the world cannot but submit to its transformation—how profound the virtue is! Those who sincerely love trust, the sage does not quench their resolve but aids them with great trust, ensuring they fulfill their sincerity. If there are those with insincerity, he also treats them with trust, yet my way of trusting them never changes. All emotions are without exception trustworthy, and the people firmly and surely trust—virtue reaches its peak. The perfect person transcends praise and blame, forgetting truth and falsehood. The beautiful he treats as virtuous; the ugly, he also treats as virtuous—constantly saving things without abandoning any. The trustworthy he trusts; the untrustworthy, he also trusts—constantly saving people without abandoning any."*

He Shi said: *"Generally, to the virtuous I am virtuous; to the unvirtuous, I do not necessarily treat them as virtuous. To the trustworthy I trust them; to the untrustworthy, I do not necessarily trust them—this is the common heart, not yet the affair of virtue as virtue, trust as trust. The sage’s virtue aligns with Heaven and Earth: Heaven and Earth cover all without partiality, the sun and moon shine their light without fail. The sage’s heart is unified, merging self and other. Thus, he treats the unvirtuous as virtuous, the untrustworthy as trustworthy. Though good and evil contradict, truth and deceit mock, I treat them all with the natural Way. The Classic says: 'Refine the well-ordered, also refine the unordered; advance the good, to lead the ungood—what is good, what is ungood?' The saying goes: 'Do not counterfeit, do not presume upon the untrustworthy—what is trust, what is deceit?' 'Sincerity shines clearly; observe below and transform.' This is it. From *Zhuangzi*: 'His love is one, his non-love is one; his oneness is one, his non-oneness is one. His oneness aligns with Heaven; his non-oneness aligns with humanity—this is it.'"*

Lin Shi said: *"Virtue or lack thereof lies with them, yet I always treat them with virtue, initially holding no discriminating heart. The Master said: 'If one’s resolve is set on transformation, there is no evil.' This aligns with it. Trust or lack thereof lies with them, yet I always treat them with trust, holding no doubting heart. The Master said: 'Do not presume upon the untrustworthy'—this is the same meaning. When it says, 'I also treat them as virtuous, I also trust them,' it does not mean making the unvirtuous virtuous or the untrustworthy trustworthy. It is merely responding with a heart of non-attachment."*

The Sage’s presence in the world is marked by humility—he muddles his heart for all under Heaven, while the people all fix their ears and eyes upon him, and the Sage treats them all as infants.

 Lü Shi said: *"The Sage embodies the Dao and harmonizes with transformation, without fixed intentions, without certainty, without rigidity, without self. He encompasses all things, muddling his heart to align with their dust, following their nature to align with their transformations. He does not initially presume to take anything upon himself; when all under Heaven share one heart, they are indistinctly unified, without distinctions of good or evil, trust or falsity, truly one without duality. He responds to them with the Dao, hence it is said: 'The Sage’s presence in the world is marked by humility—he muddles his heart for all under Heaven.'

 Lin Shi said: *"'Humility' means not being self-satisfied; the Sage has no pride or self-sufficiency, so he always bears this sense of unease. 'Muddling his heart' means a heart that is undifferentiated yet without sharp edges—this muddled heart is why there are no divisions of virtue or lack thereof, trust or lack thereof. 'Fixing their ears and eyes' means people all direct their sight and hearing toward the Sage, yet the Sage treats them all as infants, hence it is said: 'He treats them all as infants.' This is the Sage’s intention of never abandoning anyone."*

Chapter Fifty.

**Birth and Death, Entry and Exit**

Lü Shi said: *"The Dao gives rise to one qi; qi transforms and takes form, form transforms and gives rise to life. Life is the gathering of qi—all things emerge from the Machine. Life transforms and brings death; death is the dispersal of qi—all things return to the Machine. What has form returns to the formless; what lives returns to the lifeless. Things have birth and death, but their nature knows neither gain nor loss. Birth is the beginning of creation, death the transformation of yin and yang. This is the realm where all things meet their end yet do not depart from the Machine.

Those who depart from the Machine—
their exit is not joyful,
their return is not regretful.
To be born and die,
to die and be reborn—
without preference, without aversion.
The cultivator refines himself,
taking the pure yang, without substance, as the qi of life,
and the turbid yin, congealed and dreg-filled, as the qi of death.

Man draws upon the harmonious qi of the void—
neither turbid nor pure—
yet in life he ascends to the pure yang and descends into the turbid yin.
If yang is pure, he becomes an Upper Immortal;
if he descends into the turbid dregs and ascends to the pure yang,
if yin is pure, he becomes a Lower Ghost.

If one ingests the Six Qi,
nourishes the Original Essence,
absorbs the mist and dew—
these are all transformations of Heaven’s breath,
formless and without substance,
thus able to transcend the spirit and align one’s lifespan with the two solar terms.
This is called dying and being reborn.

But the people of the world consume grains and relish the five flavors—
substantial and dense,
they steal their True Qi,
scatter their pure yang,
and sink into the turbid dregs.
This is called being born and dying."*

Wu Shi said: *"To exit is to be born; to return is to die.
Exit means emerging from the non-existent into the existent;
return means dissolving from the existent back into the non-existent.
The *Zhuangzi* says: 'All things emerge from the Machine, return to the Machine.'
Again: 'Their exit is not joyful, their return is not regretful.'
And: 'There is an exit, there is a return.'
All take exit as birth, return as death."*

Lin Shi said: *"'Birth and death, entry and exit'—these four characters are the chapter’s guiding thread.
To exit is to be born, to return is to die;
to exit is to transcend and depart,
to return is to be lost in delusion.
Only those who follow the Dao can return and exit.

The followers of life—thirteen in number.
The followers of death—thirteen in number.
Man’s life is also thirteen steps into the realm of death.
Why so?
Because of the excess of life’s vitality.

Lü Shi said: *"Yin and yang never depart from the Great Dao,
creation and transformation never depart from the Five Phases.
The harmony of presence and absence forms Heaven and Earth.
They emerge from the Machine to be born,
return to the Machine to die.
Few follow the Dao; many defy it.
Thus, those who cultivate life
do not gaze recklessly,
do not listen recklessly,
do not speak recklessly,
do not walk recklessly—
in stillness and motion, serene and self-nourishing—
these are the followers of life.

Those who court death
let their eyes wander over garish colors,
their ears indulge in wanton sounds,
their mouths crave what is un-Dao,
their feet tread forbidden paths—
in stillness and motion, they drown themselves,
destroying their own vitality—
these are the followers of death.

Moreover, the Way of life and death,
though divided into two,
still knows action without knowing rest,
knows speech without knowing silence,
knows thought without knowing forgetfulness,
knows advance without knowing retreat—
and thus exhausts itself in the realm of death.

The Way of life and death,
when spoken of as thirteen,
three for each of the four—
the Way of neither life nor death
lies in the emptiness of Oneness.
This is why it is not spoken of;
one must awaken to it themselves.

As for the saying of thirteen,
though many have expounded it before and after,
no definitive conclusion exists.
Yet a few interpretations are somewhat acceptable.

An ancient Immortal said:
*'Within yang, thirteen symbols manifest;
within yin, six or seven seek no synthesis.'*
This refers to the numerical completion of water and fire.

Heaven One gives rise to Water,
Earth Six completes it;
Earth Two gives rise to Fire,
Heaven Seven completes it.
Six and Seven—
these are the heart and kidneys,
the water and fire of Kan and Li,
the roots of humanity.
Spirit dwells within them;
the heart houses the spirit,
the kidneys house the essence.
In all daily interactions of the mundane and the sage,
spirit is never absent.

The *Huangting Jing* says:
*'One’s spirit in the body must not be lost.
Spirit residing in the body—this is the follower of life.
Spirit departing from the body—this is the follower of death.'
Water and fire can both sustain life and destroy it—
are they not the followers of life and death?

When the red serpent stirs,
the spirit’s brilliance follows swiftly,
responding faster than grains,
erupting from the Machine—
is this not the realm of death?

Thus it is said:
The followers of life—thirteen in number.
The followers of death—thirteen in number.
Man’s life is also thirteen steps into the realm of death.

*Liezi*, pointing to a skull, said:
*'This one overindulged in pleasure;
this one overindulged in nourishment.'
Warm clothing, heated rooms,
delicious wine and rich grains,
ingesting minerals and stones—
though they nourish excessively,
they harm life instead.
Better to quiet the heart,
disdain flavors,
nourish the spirit-grain—
though it seems to slight life,
it can prolong it."*

Li Shi said: *"Water’s completion is six,
fire’s completion is seven.
The gentle and weak—followers of life;
the strong and great—followers of death.
The gentle and weak can restrain anger and suppress desire;
the strong and great are enslaved by anger and desire.
To restrain anger is to subdue fire;
to suppress desire is to elevate water.
When water and fire are harmonized, life follows—
thus it is said: the followers of life.
To indulge in anger is to lose clarity;
to indulge in desire is to stir the waves of suffering.
When water and fire conflict, death follows—
thus it is said: the followers of death.

Man’s life depends on water and fire;
to step into the realm of death is to let them conflict.
In the Dao of the Elixir,
when water and fire are harmonized,
the Holy Embryo congeals."*

Lin Shi said: *"If cultivation follows the Way,
one may achieve longevity and clear vision.
If cultivation does not follow the Way,
one perishes with all things."*

「The term *徒* refers to one’s kind or category. *动之死地* is not the *动* of motion and stillness, but rather the *动* of action and cessation. *之* means "to go," and *死地* means "the place of death." *夫何故* is an interrogative phrase asking, "Why is this so?"

*生者* is what sustains my life;
*生生者* is what nourishes my life’s vitality.
If one nourishes life excessively—
this is why, upon taking action, one hastens toward the place of death.
This embodies the meaning:
the external body may persist while the self is gone,
having nothingness before one can attain fullness,
emptiness before one can achieve completeness.

○ **Master He said:** "The followers of death and life each number thirteen. Various schools have differing views, but they merely stretch the numbers to fit their own interpretations. In truth, the upper limit of longevity is a hundred years, while the middle and lower limits vary, and contentment with wealth, ease, and peace are all blessings—these are the followers of life. Short-lived, untimely demise, illness, poverty, and frailty are extremes—these are the followers of death. Yet even before discussing virtue and vice, the first two categories each receive their fate from Heaven. Who can equalize them? This is not yet the crux of the matter. But if one is foolish and unworthy, clinging to life while losing reason, creating unnatural agitation to hasten one’s own demise, they not only become a follower of death but also rush toward the place of death—this calamity lies within humanity itself.

*The place of death* refers to those who act in accordance with vice yet do not embrace virtue. In generalizing about the world as described earlier, three out of every ten individuals fall into these categories, not necessarily adhering to the previous figures. Yet the followers of death already number thirteen, and those who hasten toward the place of death number another thirteen—six out of ten are doomed to death. The followers of life number only three—how dire!

If one seeks to preserve life and avoid death,
only one in ten is a virtuous person.

*The Zhuangzi* states:
*"A man’s life is the gathering of his qi;
when gathered, it is life; when scattered, it is death.
If death and life are merely categories,
what need have I to fear them?"*
Thus, it is clear that the true affliction lies in the *place of death*, not in being a follower of death.

○ **Master Wu said:** *之* is akin to "to go" or "to hasten." *生生* seeks to sustain life itself, while *厚* means excessive fixation—whether through Daoist elixir practices that stray from proper alignment or through alchemical preparations that overdo the measures. Their intent is to hasten life, yet in doing so, they only hasten toward the place of death, for their obsession with life does not conform to the natural Dao.

Indeed, those who excel in preserving life
walk on land without encountering rhinoceros or tigers,
enter battlefields without being struck by weapons or armor.
The rhinoceros has no place to lodge its horn,
the tiger no place to set its claws,
and weapons no place to find their mark.
Why is this so?
It is because they have no place of death—
their hearts are empty, and thus harm cannot touch them.

○ **Master Lin said:** "It is said that the ancient masters of life-nourishment, though wandering in deep mountains, never encountered rhinoceros or tigers; though entering the midst of armies, they were never struck by weapons or armor. This is because they had no fixation—thus, things could not harm them. The rhinoceros could not charge, the tiger could not attack, and weapons could not wound—all because they had no fixation. *The Zhuangzi* also speaks of entering water without drowning, entering fire without burning—this is the same principle.

Why is this so?
Again, a question arises:
things cannot harm them because they embody emptiness and nothingness,
and thus have no place of death.

○ **Master Wu said:** *摄* means "to govern" or "to oversee," as in *摄政*—to temporarily manage life as though it were a realm to be governed, without claiming it as one’s own. Dwelling in the realm of emptiness and stillness, presenting as gentle and weak, they are solid and unknowing like wood or stone, yet innocent and desireless like a babe. Even when confronted by fierce beasts or wicked men, they are untouched by harm.

For their impurities are dissolved,
their spirit and *qi* serene and tranquil—
like wind, like shadow—
nothing can grasp or seize them.
There is no substance in them that can die,
and even if harm comes their way,
how could it find purchase to wound them?

○ **Master Li said:** "Those without the place of death
are those who forget life and death."

○ **Master He said:** "Rhinoceros and tigers are spoken of as the most formidable of beasts—rhinoceros, the wild ox, weighing a thousand *jin*, with a single horn.

**Chapter Fifty-One:**
*The Dao* gives birth to life;
*virtue/power* nourishes it;
things take their form from it;
circumstances bring it to fruition."

Thus, all things reverence the *Dao* and hold *virtue/power* dear.

The *Dao*'s eminence and *virtue/power*'s nobility—
none command them yet they ever abide naturally.

**Master He said:** "The heavens cover with the *Dao* and all things are born;
each thing receives its *qi* from the heavens.
The earth bears with things and all things are nourished;
each thing receives its substance from the earth.
Thus, all things born possess their own forms and colors,
not awaiting external carving or refinement.
Yin and Yang intermingle, the four seasons succeed one another,
mutually giving life and taking it—
their *circumstances* thus come to fruition.
Who could resist them?

For form has its time of decline,
and *circumstances* have their time of collapse,
yet *Dao* and *virtue/power* endure in heaven and earth,
high and profound, eternal and unceasing,
unbroken and undying.

The *Yijing* says: *"Qián* governs with authority, *Kūn* conceals with capacity—
thus the *Dao* is supreme in eminence, with none to rival it.
*Virtue/power* is supreme in nobility, its height unmatched.
Though things differ in myriad ways,
none can compare to this.*

*Dao* and *virtue/power* transcend *circumstances* above,
yet move within form and *qi* below—
nothing lacks them, no time does without,
filling heaven and earth as the Eastern Cloth,
its fundamental essence universally acknowledged.
Not from deliberate decree,
nor from human contrivance—
its birth and nourishment are ever natural.
What is worthy of reverence and nobility
is also ever natural.

Yet the order of birth, nourishment, and formation—
though *Dao* and *virtue/power* and things and *circumstances* may differ,
they are ultimately spoken of as one *Dao*.

**Master Lin said:** "The *Dao* is natural and without;
all things conform to the void and thus are born,
hence it is said: *'The Dao gives birth to them.'*
*Virtue/power* then has its account,
hence it is said: *'Virtue/power nourishes them.'*
Nourish implies existence.
Things then have form,
hence it is said: *'Things take their form from it.'*
*Circumstances* imply correspondence,
hence it is said: *'Circumstances bring it to fruition.'*
Yin and Yang’s mutual pairing,
the four seasons’ mutual succession—
these are all *circumstances*.
'None command them yet they ever abide naturally'
is akin to saying: 'None make it so yet it is so.'
If there were no agency behind them,
they would be ever naturally constant.
Reverence and nobility speak of their transcendence above all things.

**Master Li said:** "The *Dao* originally has no form,
yet through its birth and nurturing of heaven and earth, form becomes visible.
The *Dao* originally has no name,
yet through its sustenance of all things, names begin to arise.
All things with form receive their mandate from heaven,
their shapes from earth,
their *qi* balanced and harmonized—
all are under the *Dao*'s influence,
hence all things reverence the *Dao* and hold *virtue/power* dear.
Thus, the *Dao* gives birth, nourishes, lengthens, and matures them,
covers and sustains them.
It births without possession,
acts without reliance,
lengthens without domination—
this is called the Mysterious *Virtue/power*.

**Master He said:** The *Zhuangzi* says: "The *Dao* is the common origin of all things;
those who lose it die, those who gain it live,
hence the *Dao* gives birth and nourishes them.
As the Way of Spring, it lengthens and nurtures them—
as the Way of Summer.
It brings to fruition and matures them—
as the Way of Autumn.
It sustains and covers them—
as the Way of Winter.
The *Gēngsāng Zǐ* says: 'Spring *qi* unfurls and all grasses are born,
just as Autumn arrives and all treasures are formed—
how could this not be so if they are gained?
The Great *Dao* has already gone forth—
this is it.
What *Dao* is this?
The void and natural *Dao*,
able to birth all things at their beginning,
yet also to shepherd and conceal all things at their end.
Heaven and Earth in motion and stillness—
without convergence and dispersion, they cannot arise or fade.
The *Gēngsāng Zǐ* thus reveals the Great *Dao* in Spring’s birth and Autumn’s fruition,
while Laozi unifies the arising and the covering in one *Dao*.
The *Yijing* says: 'It manifests in benevolence,
it conceals in utility—
thus is it.'

Heaven and Earth act without intent—
though they give birth, they do no harm,
yet claim no ownership;
though they act without refusal,
they do not boast of their deeds;
though they nurture without end,
they do not lord over others.
The seasons’ course—
when their work is done, they withdraw.
The Great Emptiness, profound and silent,
who could force a name upon it?

The Mysterious *Virtue/power*—
this is the Heavenly *Virtue/power*, the *Dao*.

**Master Lin said:** 'Lengthening and nurturing,
bringing to fruition and maturing,
sustaining and covering—
all speak of what comes after birth and existence.
Yet within heaven and earth,
endless birth and nurturing arise—
all from the power of transformation.
Yet transformation neither possesses nor claims ownership,
nor does it rely to boast of deeds,
though it lengthens all without a master’s intent—
this is why it is called the Mysterious and Marvelous *Virtue/power*.
The Mysterious *Virtue/power* is transformation itself.'

**Chapter Fifty-Two:**
The world has a beginning,
which serves as the world’s mother.
Having known its mother,
one knows its children;
having known its children,
one returns to guard its mother—
thus, no peril befalls the body.

**Master Lǚ said:** The *Liézǐ* speaks of *Tàiyì* (the Supreme Simplicity),
*Tàichū* (the Supreme Primordial),
*Tàishǐ* (the Supreme Beginning),
and *Tàisù* (the Supreme Purity).
*Tàiyì* is before *qi* appears,
*Tàichū* is the beginning of *qi*.
Now, the *Dao* originally had no beginning—
it envelops heaven and earth externally,
fills the cosmos internally,
formless and nameless,
the source from which all things draw their origin.
When it takes on names,
then things gain their means to be born.
There is a primal mixture formed,
before heaven and earth were born—
thus it is the world’s beginning.
It births and nurtures heaven and earth,
lengthens and sustains all things—
thus it is the world’s mother,
and all things are thus formed.

Take, for instance, the world within a single body:
its beginning stems from the Three Primordials nurturing *virtue/power*,
the Nine *qi* forming its shape.
Without the Three Primordials’ nurturing,
without the Nine *qi*’s guidance,
it could not be born—
thus it is the beginning-mother of a single body.
Hence it is said: *'The world has a beginning,
which serves as the world’s mother.'*

Having obtained its *qi*,
it becomes the mother of all limbs.
One must know its children—
the children are the spirit.
Having made the spirit the Original Spirit,
and the *qi* the Original *Qi*—
when the mother remains, the children endure;
when the mother departs, the children weep—
for the *qi* can birth the spirit,
while the spirit can subdue the *qi*.

The *Danjing* calls them *jūn-chén* (Lord-Subject),
or also *mǔ-zǐ* (Mother-Child).
The ancient True Persons say:
'When child and mother first meet, their affection springs like spring;
when Lord and Subject unite, their joy clusters like happiness.'

Within the form, Mother and Child dwell,
*Qi* guards the spirit, the spirit guards *Qi*;
in mutual affection they cling and blend,
united as one, forming the embryonic sage—
thus harmonized with Heaven, their essence complete.

Hence it is said:
Having obtained its Mother,
then know its Child,
Having known its Child,
then guard its Mother—
thus the body perishes not.

○ Lin Shi says:
*The world has a beginning,*
*which serves as the Mother*—
this is the Mother of all named things.
The Mother is *Zàohuà* (Creation),
the Child is all things.
Knowing Creation while also knowing all things,
knowing all things yet still knowing Creation—
for without *Wu* (Non-Being) there could be no *Yǒu* (Being),
while *Yǒu* arises from *Wu*.
Those who know *Yǒu* must not fail to know *Wu*—
to seek *Wu* with desire is to glimpse its mystery,
to seek *Yǒu* with desire is to grasp its limits.
This too is the meaning.
*"Thus the body perishes not"*—
meaning that so long as one abides thus,
there shall be no peril throughout life.

○ Dong Si Jing says:
Humans receive *Qi* to be born,
*Qi* as the Mother;
the spirit resides within *Qi*,
thus one must have the Child guard the Mother,
so that *Qi* may focus and the spirit be at ease.

○ He Shi says:
The Classic states:
*"Non-Being was the beginning of Heaven and Earth,
Being was the Mother of all things"*—
this is the meaning of the passage’s Beginning and Mother.
The *Taìshǐ* (Supreme Beginning) is merely *Wu* and *Yǒu*.
From *Wu* to *Yǒu* is the Mother,
as in *"The Dao gives birth to One"* is the way.
From *Yǒu* to *Yǒu* is the Child,
as in *"One generates Two, Two generates Three,
Three generates all things"* is the truth.

To **塞其兑** (*sè qí yuè*),
to **闭其门** (*bì qí mén*),
thus the body labors not its whole life.
To open **兑** (*yuè*),
to advance its affairs,
thus the body cannot be saved its whole life.

○ Lü Shi says:
*Qián* (Heaven) and *Kūn* (Earth) are the gates of the *Yì* (Changes),
opening and closing with regularity—
from them all hexagrams emerge.
The mechanism of birth and transformation
follows virtue’s measure without deviation.
*Yuè* (兑) means delight,
it means dispersion—
the pathways by which the ears and eyes
are drawn to colors and dust.
If one **塞** (*sè*) the love and delight
of sight and hearing,
then the gates of calamity and fortune are shut—
naturally, the body knows no labor.

Should one open the gates of love and delight
yet fail to **塞** (*sè*) them,
then the cravings of desire shall grow—
throughout life, peril and ruin shall be unavoidable.

By this analogy of oneself:
*Yuè* (兑) is the mouth,
the gate is the eyes—
constantly sealing the mouth,
then the Five *Qi*’s within solidify,
and the Original Essence does not dry.
Constantly dimming the eyes,
then the inner realm does not emerge,
and the outer realm does not intrude—
in stillness, supreme equanimity,
united with the Dao.

The *Shēntóng Qìè* (Parametric Agreement) says:
Ears and eyes are one’s treasures,
keep them sealed, do not let them shine.
Do not speak with the *Yuè* (兑) mouth,
nor seek the subtle with the coarse.
This is what is meant by:
**塞其兑, 闭其门**—
thus the body labors not its whole life.

Should one open the gates of delight
yet fail to **塞** (*sè*) them,
advance the affairs of desire
yet fail to **闭** (*bì*) them,
then external things shall entice,
and inner passions shall stir—
*Qi* shall dissipate, fluids shall leak,
and the spirit shall scatter.
Its spirit shall wither and die,
and none shall be able to save it—
thus it is said:
**开其兑, 济其事**—
throughout life, the body cannot be saved.

○ Lin Shi says:
*Yuè* (兑) is the mouth,
in the body there is a mouth,
in the home there is a gate—
all are metaphors for the place
from which all things emerge.

**塞其兑, 闭其门**—
to conceal *Yǒu* within *Wu* and not reveal it.
*"Thus the body labors not"*—
meaning success without toil.
To open **兑** (*yuè*)—
to emerge and put it to use.
To advance its affairs—
to use it and seek further benefit.
*"Thus peril and ruin shall be unavoidable."*

○ He Shi says:
The gate—
though ancient commentaries point to the mouth,
this is not rigid—
anything that has emergence and intrusion
may be called a gate.
To **闭其门** (*bì qí mén*)—
to obscure the mind with the Great Dao,
so that external thoughts do not enter,
and inner thoughts do not escape.
As in *Zhuāngzǐ* (Chuang Tzu):
*"If one’s heart rejects it,
then Heaven’s gate shall not open."*

Indeed, **塞兑闭门**—
to enter *Wu* and extinguish *Yǒu*,
for the Child to know the Mother,
is to return to the beginning,
and thus throughout life,
its application shall require no labor.

Should one open **兑** (*yuè*) and advance its affairs,
then with every thought and movement,
one shall stray from the original,
and ruin *Wu* while losing *Yǒu*—
as the Mother gives birth to the Child,
and entrusts it in myriad forms,
the Child grows strong while the Mother weakens,
the wheel turning endlessly—
throughout life, salvation shall be difficult.

○ Li Shi says:
**塞兑闭门**—
the inner realm does not emerge,
the outer realm does not intrude—
thus the body labors not,
and is governed without effort.
To open **兑** (*yuè*)—
the inner realm emerges,
the outer realm intrudes—
thus the body cannot be saved,
and the True Dao is forever lost.

*"Perceiving the small is clarity,"*
*"Guarding the soft is strength."*
Using its light,
then returning to its clarity—
no harm shall come to the body.

This is called **袭常** (*xí cháng*).

○ Lin Shi says:
What one perceives is vast,
yet if one can gather and make it small,
then it is supreme clarity.
What one governs is rigid,
yet if one can retreat and guard the soft,
then it is supreme strength.
Thus, not self-asserting is clarity,
not self-aggrandizing is longevity.

Light is the application of clarity,
clarity is the essence of light—
to use its light and return it to clarity,
then there shall be no blame.
To know its application yet not know concealment,
is the way of inviting harm.
**袭** (*xí*) means concealment,
**常** (*cháng*) means unchanging—
**袭常** (*xí cháng*) means
to conceal its application and not exhaust it.

○ He Shi says:
All who can be great yet cannot be small,
strong yet cannot be weak,
clear yet cannot be obscure,
then they abandon the root for the branches—
the Child generates another Child,
endlessly drifting, forgetting to return—
what then of the Mother?
This is the way of inviting harm.

The Beginning is the Dao of constant *Wu*,
the Mother is the Dao of constant *Yǒu*—
from *Wu* arises *Yǒu*,
from *Yǒu* returns to *Wu*—
this is the unchanging Great Dao.

It is also said:
*"Using light yet returning to clarity—*
the sage shines without dazzling."
*"Observing deep fish is an ill omen."*

○ Lü Shi says:
The reflection of water
illuminating things is called light,
its essence is called clarity—
to use its light of external illumination,
then to withdraw and reflect inward,
returning and concealing it within,
is to grasp its essence.

The spirit, when overused, is exhausted.
To gaze upon deep fish is an ill omen—
this is the harm of expending light outward while neglecting the self.

If one can contain the light and blend with the world,
then no harm befalls the self.
This is called grasping the constant light’s use
and returning to the constant essence of clarity—
thus it is said: *"Cling to the constant."*

**Chapter Fifty-Three**
*"If I were suddenly to know,*
walking the Great Dao,
I would fear only *shī*."*

○ **Wu Shi says:**
*"I"* refers generally to all people, not Laozi speaking of himself.
*"Jié"* sounds like *jié*—as in *Mencius*’ *"Jiérán yòng zhī ér chéng lù"* (to act swiftly and complete the path).
The word *"zhī"* cuts off *shī* as in *"The Analects’* *‘wú shī láo’* (without bestowing favors),
and *Mencius’* *‘shīshī cóng wài lái zhī shī’* (the ostentatious bestowal of favors from without, boastful and grand).
The sage’s Preface: Yanzi was *"having-yet-as-if-not-having,"*
*"being-yet-as-if-empty"*—
without bestowing favors, just as Laozi’s use was.
He magnified what he feared—
this chapter speaks of those who do not know the Dao,
who only seek to boast,
so that if such a person were suddenly to know,
and wished to walk the Great Dao,
he would fear only *shī* and dare not act.

○ **Li Shi says:**
*"Fear only shī"*—those who recognize the law fear it.

○ **Chái Shi says:**
All who walk outside the Great Dao are to be feared.

The Great Dao is most level,
yet people prefer byways.

○ **Wu Shi says:**
Byways are narrow paths,
contrary to the Great Dao.
The truly humble, withdrawing, is the Great Dao—
its way is flat and easy to walk,
yet the boastful and ostentatious are the byways.
And people love to tread them.

○ **Lü Shi says:**
The Great Dao is a broad, open thoroughfare,
level and unobstructed—wherever one goes, it is passable.
Though distant as thousands of miles,
one must begin with the step beneath one’s feet.
The sage fears overstraightness,
hesitating before action—
does he value swift arrival or seek near-term gain?
The world values swift arrival,
so they take shortcuts.
Yet they stumble, confused, unaware of their destination,
moving backward, acting contrary—
the farther they stray from the Dao,
the less chance they have of ascending.
The Dao of refining and nurturing is level as a whetstone,
easy to know and easy to walk.
A narrow-minded scholar, stuck in a corner,
wants quick results,
so he wanders through side doors,
falls into crooked paths,
and does not realize it.
Thus it is said:
*The Great Dao is most level,*
*yet people prefer byways.*

○ **Hé Shi says:**
The Great Dao is level like a great road,
yet if one abandons it for crooked paths,
one becomes deeply lost.
The court is well-ordered,
the fields lie fallow,
the granaries are empty,
yet they adorn themselves in fine silks,
wear sharp blades at their sides,
gluttonously indulge in food and drink,
hoard wealth—
this is called *thieving boastfulness.*
How can this be the Dao?

○ **Wu Shi says:**
This speaks of boastful deeds—
the court is not swept clean,
the fields lie untended—
the people are forced to labor without rest,
their strength drained, their stores depleted.
They adorn themselves with fine ornaments,
fill their tables with rich foods,
plunder their treasuries for wealth—
this is like a thief,
proud of his spoils,
displaying them outwardly.
Like Xia’s Jie and Shang’s Xin—
how could one who knows the Dao do such things?

○ **Lín Shi says:**
The Great Dao is level,
yet people seeking it do not walk the straight path,
preferring crooked byways.
It is like a ruler who orders his court splendidly—
palaces and towers built in splendor.
Though the court is beautiful,
the fields lie fallow,
the granaries are empty,
yet they adorn themselves in embroidered robes,
wear sharp blades,
overindulge in food and drink,
hoard wealth,
striving for riches and strength—
this is like a thief boasting of his prowess.
How long can this last?
This is a parable:
Those who do not know the Great Dao
yet boast of their cleverness and learning—
these are the ones who love byways.

「How could one know the **至道** (*Zhìdào*, the Ultimate Dao/Way)?
That is why it is said: *"This is not the Dao!"*

○ **Master He says:**
*"朝甚除"* (*cháo shèn chú*—this is "clearing") refers to **修治** (*xiūzhì*, cultivation and rectification).
The **传** (*transmission*) states: *"Remove the dung from the ancestral hall"*—and again, *"clear the path"*—both meaning **cultivation and rectification**.

**Chapter Fifty-Four:**
*"The wise builder does not uproot,
the wise embrace does not sever;
sacrifice to the ancestral spirits never ceases."*

○ **Master Lü says:**
Those who skillfully establish a state by the Dao/Way
stand alone above all things,
unchanging through all ages,
soaring beyond all things,
yet never reaching an end.
If one establishes oneself by this Dao/Way
and governs the world by this Dao/Way,
holding fast to its **中** (*zhōng*, central harmony),
embodying its **常** (*cháng*, constancy),
and anchoring it in the nameless **朴** (*pǔ*, primal simplicity),
then one will not be swayed by the transformations of things.
Blessings extend to future generations,
virtue grows ever broader,
its efficacy ever greater—
unshakable indeed.

But if one relies on **根技** (*gēnjì*, rooting in skills and artifices),
**氣** (*qì*) will be exhausted,
and the **青** (*qīng*, vital essence) will leave the **木** (*mù*, body).
If **炁** (*qì*) decays,
**精** (*jīng*, essence) will be exhausted,
and **神明** (*shénmíng*, spirit and luminosity) will depart from the body.
Thus, the **至人** (*Zhìrén*, Perfect Sage) firmly cultivates deep roots,
standing alone in the vast wilderness,
the realm of **無何有** (*wúhéyǒu*, the boundless),
endless and unyielding—
immovable, never uprooted.

Those who skillfully embrace the people by the Dao/Way
ride the harmony of Heaven and Earth,
command the subtleties of the **六氣** (*liùqì*, Six Qi),
and the scholars of the world
settle into **一心** (*yīxīn*, singular purpose).
Thus, the people naturally gather in devotion.

Cultivating **一己** (*yījǐ*, oneself alone)
is to embrace the **元** (*yuán*, primal origin) and guard the **一** (*yī*, singularity),
preserving **神** (*shén*, spirit) and returning **精** (*jīng*, essence),
as the winds of **回風** (*huífēng*, circulating breath) blend in the **丹田** (*dāntián*, elixir field).
There will be no severance, everlasting.

That is why it is said:
*"The wise embrace does not sever."*

But those who skillfully establish and embrace—
is it not that they do more than just remain unrooted and unsevered?
Their virtue and grace flow through all levels,
filial piety and compassion extend to their descendants,
and their lineage endures without limit—
is this not beautiful?

○ **Master He says:**
In the **《庄子》** (*Zhuāngzǐ*, Chuang Tzu),
the state of **"建德之国"** (*Jiàndé zhī guó*, the state built by virtue)*
has citizens who are simple and unsophisticated.
Thus, those who speak of **善建** (*shànjiàn*, wise establishment)
do not add to virtue—
then people will also stand with them, never uprooted.

The **经** (*classic*) says:
*"Embrace the One as the model for all under Heaven,"*
so those who speak of **善抱** (*shànbào*, wise embrace)
do not stray from the One—
then people will not abandon their hearts and be severed.

It is not merely in oneself,
but descendants will also rejoice and inherit it,
their sacrifices stretching far into the future.

Those who observe the Dao/Way
do not selfishly act for their descendants alone,
but establish teachings for the ages,
not speaking only to those who dwell in mountains and valleys.
It is said:
*"Great virtue must gain its rewards—rank, position, longevity—
and the ancestral temples will enjoy them,
the descendants will preserve them."*

○ **Master Lín says:**
The builder who does not establish virtue
will be uprooted,
the embrace that does not hold simplicity
will be severed.
But if one establishes virtue and embraces simplicity,
then there will be no uprooting or severing.

For households with descendants,
sacrifice will never cease.

The Dao/Way gives birth to the **一** (*yī*, One),
the One generates the **二** (*èr*, Two),
the Two gives rise to the **三** (*sān*, Three),
and the Three produces **萬物** (*wànwù*, all things).
All things are born yet never exhausted—
just as descendants perpetuate a family.

These three sentences are parables,
speaking of the Dao/Way’s enduring presence
though it seems without limits.

Cultivate it in oneself—
then virtue is **真** (*zhēn*, true).
Cultivate it in the family—
then virtue has **余** (*yú*, surplus blessings).
Cultivate it in the village—
then virtue is **長** (*cháng*, enduring).
Cultivate it in the state—
then virtue is **豐** (*fēng*, abundant).
Cultivate it in the world—
then virtue is **普** (*pǔ*, universal).

○ **Master Lü says:**
The Dao/Way of cultivating oneself
in every action, every response,
every adaptation and transformation—
it never fails to begin with oneself.
Refining **氣** (*qì*) and nourishing **神** (*shén*),
few desires and little self-interest,
all falsehoods exhausted—
then one becomes a **真人** (*Zhēnrén*, True Sage).

Cultivate it in the family—
then the father is kind, the son filial,
the elder brother friendly, the younger respectful,
and the nine clans harmonious.
Accumulating virtue with surplus,
joy flows into future generations.

Cultivate it in the village—
then the aged are honored,
the ignorant are taught,
and trust flows upward and downward.
Virtue endures for ages.

Cultivate it in the state—
then all officials fulfill their duties,
calamities do not arise,
and all treasures flourish in abundance.
Things are filled and complete.

Cultivate it in the world—
then there is governance without teaching,
transformation without words,
harmony with the Great Way,
and even common households may be ennobled.
Virtue spreads far and wide.

If one cultivates in family, village, state, and world—
speaking of virtue as surplus, enduring, abundant, and universal—
only in cultivating oneself
does one speak of virtue as **真** (*true*).
This is what **《庄子》** (*Zhuāngzǐ*) means:
*"The Dao’s true virtue is for cultivating oneself,
its lingering traces and scattered benefits
are for governing the world."*

The root lies in oneself,
the branches extend outward—
the principle is complete.

○ **Master Lín says:**
Cultivating oneself—
virtue is real and without falsehood.
Cultivating the family—
virtue accumulates with surplus blessings.
Cultivating the village—
virtue is what the village holds in honor.
Cultivating the state—
virtue extends to more people, ever greater.
Cultivating the world—
virtue reaches ever more widely.

○ **Master Wú says:**
The Dao’s true virtue is for cultivating oneself,
its lingering traces and scattered benefits
are for governing the people.

The **家** (*jiā*, family) is beyond oneself,
within the nine clans.
The **鄉** (*xiāng*, village) is beyond the family,
within the extended neighborhood.
The **國** (*guó*, state) is beyond the village,
within the national borders.

The **天下** (*tiānxià*, world) is beyond the state,
within the four seas.
Those who cultivate in family, village, state, and world—
speaking of near and far,
each person cultivates their own virtue.

Yet is this something taught to all?
No—
*"I act without action,
and the people are naturally transformed."*

○ **Master He says:**
Within, in oneself and the family,
cultivating by this Dao/Way
brings true virtue, accumulated strength, and lasting power—
and even surplus remains.

Without, in village, state, and world,
cultivating by this Dao/Way
makes the source deep and the flow long—
abundant and universal.

Thus, observe oneself by oneself,
observe the family by the family,
observe the village by the village,
observe the state by the state,
observe the world by the world.

How do I know this of the world?
By this.

○ **Master Lín says:**
Take my own body,
and you can observe others’ bodies.
Take my own family,
and you can observe others’ families.
Take my own village,
and you can observe others’ villages.
Extend this to state and world—
all the same.
The Dao’s applications are universal.»

「Thus, the principle by which one observes is the Dao/Way itself—
by observing through the Dao/Way, nothing in the world fails to align.

○ **Master Lǐ says:**
When applied to cultivating oneself,
observe the heart and examine the nature—
a settled heart ensures the body’s cultivation.
When applied to governing the state,
observe the people and reflect upon oneself—
if the people are transformed, then the state is well-governed.
If there is even one unvirtuous act in the world,
then it is due to one’s own governance and transformation being flawed.
The *Shū* [Classic of Documents] states:
*"If the people have faults,
they lie with me alone."*
This is the meaning.

When one looks inward at all the hearts within,
there is even a speck of dust or attachment,
then it is my own innate nature that remains unclear.

○ **Master Dǒng says:**
The method of observation is not distant—
why seek it elsewhere?
Though the divisions of things may differ,
their root is singular.
Observing by this principle,
nothing fails to align—
this insight must resonate beyond mere words.

---

**Chapter 55 of *Jìyì* [Collected Meanings] in *Zhēnchūn Dàodé* [True Purity Daoist Virtue]**
*"The depth of virtue/power is like that of an infant."*

○ **Master Lǐ says:**
*"Virtue/power"* here means
to hold it in reserve without revealing it.
*"Depth"* is the utmost degree—
when virtue/power is held to its utmost,
it is like that of an infant.

○ **Master Hé says:**
A true man of virtue/power
dwells in its depth, not its shallowness—
thus, profound virtue/power is the affair of the great.
Preserving the essence and safeguarding the qi,
grasping the vital breath and harmonizing the flow—
the great indeed does not lose
the heart of the infant.

○ **Master Lǐ says:**
*"The depth of virtue/power is like that of an infant"*—
it means not losing the heart of the infant.

○ **Master Wú says:**
One who contains within himself
the utmost depth of virtue/power
is like a newborn babe.
The previous section states:
*"Specializing in the vital qi to utmost softness,
able to be like an infant,
constant virtue/power does not depart,
returning again to the infant."*
Mencius said:
*"The great man does not lose the heart of the infant."*

○ **Master Sū says:**
When Laozi speaks of *Dàodé* [Daoist Virtue],
he often compares it to an infant—
meaning its essence precedes its application.

○ **Master Lǖ says:**
The sage embraces the Dao/Way and holds virtue/power within,
deep, profound, and pure—
without action or affairs,
his will undivided,
his spirit concentrated,
like that of an infant.
The infant is taken from
its initial birth color as a metaphor.

Again, it is said:
*"The infant of the highest realm is a clay pellet,*
a name of the Celestial Sovereign."
Naturally holding within,
true and pure without dissipation,
like the infant before it knows of worldly ways.
Poisonous creatures do not sting,
ferocious beasts do not seize,
raptors do not strike.

○ **Master Lǐ says:**
*"Poisonous creatures do not sting,
ferocious beasts do not seize,
raptors do not strike"*—
meaning nothing can harm him,
also implying *"enter water and not be wet,
enter fire and not be scorched."*

○ **Master Wú says:**
Poisonous creatures—
bees and wasps of their kind—
sting with their tail ends.
Ferocious beasts—
tigers and leopards—
seize with their claws and paws.
Raptors—
hawks and falcons—
strike with their wings and talons.

○ **Master Dǒng says:**
The complete man of the world
is harmed by nothing.

○ **Master Hé says:**
Profoundly containing virtue/power,
not revealing it outwardly,
harmony exists between oneself and all things—
thus, nothing can harm him,
and nothing can harm others.

○ **Master Lǐ says:**
The infant’s heart is pure,
harming nothing,
and nothing can harm it.

○ **Master Sū says:**
The infant’s heart is without intent,
and figures without heart cannot oppose it—
how then could they harm it?

○ **Master Lǖ says:**
The perfect man’s spirit is whole,
his Dao/Way profound—
piercing metal and stone,
sitting he vanishes, standing he endures,
driving dragons and subduing tigers,
commanding ghosts and controlling demons.
Poisonous creatures dare not unleash their sting,
ferocious beasts cannot employ their claws and teeth,
raptors cannot exert their talons and wings.
Though his bones are frail and sinews soft,
his grasp is firm—
before knowing of union between male and female,
yet his essence is already stirring—
the utmost of essence.
Crying all day yet his voice never breaks—
the utmost of harmony.

○ **Master Lǐ says:**
The infant’s bones are most frail,
his sinews most soft,
yet his grip is firm—
before knowing of male and female,
yet his essence is already stirring—
his essence and qi are full.
Essence, the infant’s vital root.
Though he cries all day,
his throat is not hoarse—
his heart knows no joy or anger,
his qi is inherently harmonious.
Hoarse, a reversal of breath.
The infant’s purity and singularity are fixed—
thus he can be so.
Yet those with the Dao/Way are also so—
it is simply that their hearts do not move.

○ **Master Hé says:**
Essence—
the infant’s yin.
At birth, sinews and bones are unlabored,
soft and supple—
yet his grasp is firm,
while his yin and yang already surge.
Not from knowing of male and female desire,
but from the abundance of his essence and qi.
Yet one with virtue/power
preserves essence, qi, and spirit—
accumulating to the point of transforming sinews, bones, and form—
approaching this likeness.
Hoarse—
a broken voice—
though he cries all day,
his voice does not break—
from the completeness of his harmonious qi.
Yet one with virtue/power
respects without delight,
is insulted without anger—
approaching the likeness of the natural harmony.

○ **Master Lǐ says:**
Unknowing of male and female distinction,
yet his essence is already stirring—
his essence and qi are whole.
Though stirring, he is without intent—
thus he preserves his utmost essence,
crying all day yet his voice does not change—
from the harmony of the vital breath.
Cultivate the body by this principle,
preserve its purity and wholeness—
thus it is called
the depth of virtue/power.

*"Knowing harmony is constancy,
knowing constancy is clarity,
enhancing life is auspicious,
moving the heart to stir the qi is force."*

○ **Master Lǐ says:**
Harmony is the preservation of pure qi.
Knowing this utmost harmony,
one can endure without change.
Knowing this principle of enduring,
one can be said to be clear in the Dao/Way.
Life cannot be enhanced by force—
seeking to enhance it brings calamity.
Auspicious—
auspiciousness is the same as *xiáng* [auspicious].
The saying goes:
*"What auspiciousness is this?"*
meaning the intent of this *xiáng* character.
To move the heart and stir the qi—
this is the will affecting the breath.
Force is violence—
violence is not the Dao/Way—
thus, moving the heart to stir the qi is force.»

「○ **Master Hé says:**
The sun and moon never cease their course,
yet they bring spring to all things—
how can they remain constant?
It is harmony.

Good and evil, beauty and ugliness—
they do not dwell in the spirit storehouse,
how can they be so clear?
It is constancy.

Human life is bounded—
to seek enhancement brings disaster.
Huìzǐ said: *"How can one have a body if one does not enhance life?"*
The *Zhuāngzǐ* says: *"Words do not harm the body by their own good or evil;
constancy follows nature without enhancement."*

Again, it says: *"Excess is enhancement."*

Thus, *"There is no way to enhance life"*—
this is the virtue/power of transcending attachment to life.

Like eating in excess or indulging in superfluous actions,
this would be the auspiciousness of monstrousness and wickedness.

The will is the master of the *qi*,
and the *qi* is the essence that fills the body—
if the will stirs the *qi*,
then the *qi* in turn agitates the will,
becoming fierce and narrow in its violence.

○ **Master Lǚ says:**
Harmony is nourishing oneself with uprightness,
from the beginning free of the calamities of straining or deviating.
How could one harm oneself by such violence and lose constancy?

Having known harmony,
one never loses what one preserves—
calm and unified without change,
one aligns with the constancy of Heaven and Earth.
Having known the Dao/Way of constancy,
one awakens to the singular,
neither ancient nor modern,
illuminated and boundless,
sharing radiance with the sun and moon.

Thus, *"Knowing harmony is constancy,
knowing constancy is clarity."*

Yet, if called divine,
how could one not be burdened by life?
If one does not enhance life according to principle,
disaster will surely come—
thus there is ruin in clinging to delusion.

If called *qi*,
how could one not be swayed by the will?
When *qi* and blood act wildly,
violence grows ever more extreme—
thus one is drowned in perversity.

If one does not know the Dao/Way of constancy,
life’s disasters will come.
To gather *qi* in utmost softness,
to embrace the principle of life—
now, if the will instead enslaves the *qi*,
and the *qi* becomes bold and violent,
how could this be the Dao/Way?

Only those who know the Dao/Way
follow nature without enhancing life.

When things grow strong, they age—
this is called *"not the Dao/Way"*.
*"Not the Dao/Way"* brings early demise.

○ **Master Lǚ says:**
When things grow strong, they must age,
when the will is violent, disaster follows—
yet the Dao/Way uses softness and weakness to preserve virtue/power.
If one instead takes strength and violence as the will,
this is *"not the Dao/Way"*—
thus, *"not the Dao/Way"* brings early demise.

Among all things, humans are the most refined—
there is life, then youth,
youth, then vigor,
vigor, then aging,
aging, then death—
these are all those who do not know the Dao/Way
and cannot escape the workings of *造化* [Creation-Transformation].
If one could seize the mechanism of Heaven and Earth,
grasp the subtlety of *阴阳还返* [Yin-Yang return and reversal],
then form could avoid birth,
spirit could avoid change—
one’s fate lies within oneself,
no longer bound to Heaven and Earth.

If one has not yet attained this,
how could one avoid growing strong?
Thus, *"When things grow strong, they age"*—
this is called *"not the Dao/Way"*,
and *"not the Dao/Way"* brings early demise.

○ **Master Wú says:**
Constancy is like that of an infant—
then there is no strength,
how could there be aging?
If one does not age,
how could one perish?

○ **Master Hé says:**
Those who cultivate virtue/power wisely
understand that discernment lies in early action—
their harmony remains unshaken by external affairs,
thus their virtue/power is complete and their spirit undiminished,
the reason they embody the depth of virtue/power—
they do not see the three shifts of youth, vigor, and aging.

**Chapter 56:**
*"The wise do not speak,
speakers lack wisdom."*」

「Block its mouth, close its gate,
dull its sharpness, resolve its chaos,
soften its radiance, blend with the dust—
this is called *Mysterious Unity*.

○ **Master Lín says:**
*"The wise do not speak"* and *"speakers lack wisdom"*—
this means the Dao/Way cannot be contained in words.
One must block its mouth and close its gate,
and hide it in secrecy,
must dull its sharpness and grind it smooth,
so that it has no jagged edges,
must resolve its chaos and harmonize it,
so that it is not disordered,
must soften its radiance and blend with the dust,
so that it does not dazzle or reveal itself—
this is the *uttermost Mysterious* and *uttermost Marvelous*,
the principle of Unity that is naturally so.
○ **Master Hé says:**
Knowing the Dao/Way is easy,
but speaking of it is difficult.
To know and not speak—
this is Heaven.
To know and yet speak—
this is humanity.
The ancients were Heavenly yet not human.
The Dao/Way is naturally born,
those who practice can attain it,
those who hear can speak of it.
Those who know the Dao/Way, because its depth and subtlety,
do not speak of it.
Those who do not know the Dao/Way, because its complexity,
cannot comprehend it.

To block its mouth is to take pleasure not in the Dao/Way,
but in what is contrary to it.
To close its gate is to follow not the Dao/Way,
but what is contrary to it.
To dull its sharpness is to break the jagged angles of danger and height.
To resolve its chaos is to escape the entangling web of false views.
To soften its radiance is to guard wisdom as if one were foolish.
To blend with the dust is to forget contrivance and mingle with the common.

These six things must be done in accordance with the Dao/Way,
then one returns to the True and preserves the body,
can penetrate its Mysterious virtue/power,
and associate with Heaven—
thus it is called *Mysterious Unity*.
○ **Master Lǚ says:**
The subtleties of creating the Dao/Way
do not align with the functions of yin and yang,
transcending the limits of form and number.
Above, it associates with the Originators of transformation,
below, it befriends those who dwell beyond life and death,
without beginning or end.
Without external attachments to things,
how could one be close to them?
Without seeking what is evil,
how could one be distant from them?
Without seeking in utility,
benefit cannot intrude upon one.
Without a mind toward things,
harm cannot reach one.
High rank and great wealth
cannot stir one’s heart—
this is not valuing honor as nobility.
To be stained with shame and disgrace
cannot alter one’s resolve—
this is not valuing defilement as lowliness.
One can preserve life and nourish form,
this is the Dao/Way’s *supreme height and extremity*.
Even the world cannot attain it—
thus it is the most noble.

○ **Master Wú says:**
If I have attained *Mysterious Unity*,
then I cannot be close or distant,
beneficial or harmful,
noble or lowly to others.
Grace, though like that of parents,
is forgotten by people when given—
thus I cannot be close to them.
Distant like a passing traveler,
yet people do not recognize or keep their distance—
thus I cannot be distant from them.
External fame, rank, wealth, and goods
cannot truly benefit others—
thus I cannot be beneficial to them.
External life, death, calamity, and fortune
cannot truly harm others—
thus I cannot be harmful to them.
Power, though like that of a ruler,
is treated familiarly by people—
thus I cannot be noble to them.
Insignificant like a commoner,
yet people cannot belittle me—
thus I cannot be lowly to them.
All these six things,
others cannot do,
yet I alone can—
thus I am the most noble in the world.

**Chapter 57:**
Govern the state with uprightness,
employ the strange in warfare,
acquire the world with non-action.
How do I know this is so?
From this.

○ **Master Lǚ says:**
Uprightness is the Dao/Way’s constancy,
the strange is its variation,
non-action is its truth.
To govern with uprightness is to fulfill the Dao/Way of ruling,
to employ the strange in warfare is to respond to the changes of the time.
The ancient sages established foundations that could endure and grow great,
not originally intending to use warfare.
When forced to act, there were campaigns and battles—
thus, governance is uprightness,
and warfare is the strange.

The world is a divine instrument that cannot be forced.
Those who force it ruin it,
those who grasp it lose it.
To acquire the world is always through non-action.
When there is action,
it is insufficient to acquire the world.
Only those who embody the Dao/Way remain serene and non-acting,
following things naturally,
thinking nothing, deliberating nothing—
even if they do not acquire the world,
the world naturally returns to them.

Speaking of oneself,
when the heart is balanced and the will steadfast,
when *qi* and blood flow harmoniously,
then external evils do not intrude,
and the natural divine realm is serene and settled—
the luminous light of Heaven shines forth,
just as the practice of governing the state
in accordance with the Dao/Way
brings peace to the borders.»

If the Six Bandits have not been eliminated and the Seven Emotions not dispelled,
then my body’s afflictions still linger.
When dusty thoughts arise and karmic fires blaze,
this is the time of *no choice*—
to wield arms without resorting to slaughter is impossible,
to unleash killing intent without striking is impossible—
just as the four barbarian tribes and eight frontier tribes
invade China in turn,
and the Six Armies cannot restrain themselves.

Thus it is said:
*"To govern with virtue/power, to use the strange in war,
to acquire the world through non-action."*
But how can one know the intentions of Heaven and humanity?
It is seen through the affairs of governing the state,
of wielding arms,
and of acquiring the world.

○ Master Wu said:
*"Virtue/power"* refers to laws, statutes, and prohibitions—
to correct what is incorrect is its meaning.
The disciples of Guan Zhong and Shang Yang
governed with *virtue/power.*

The sovereign’s governance of the state,
however, takes *cultivating oneself and harmonizing the family*
as its foundation,
and does not rely on laws, statutes, and prohibitions
to establish *virtue/power.*

*"The strange"* refers to stratagems and deceits—
its meaning is cunning yet not upright.
The disciples of Sun Bin and Wu Qi
employed *the strange* in war.

The sovereign’s use of war,
however, takes *relieving the people and punishing crimes*
as its intent,
and does not prize stratagems and deceits
to achieve *the strange.*
Thus *the strange* may only be applied to war,
but not to governing the state,
and *virtue/power* may only be applied to governing the state,
but not to acquiring the world.

*Non-action* refers to the Three Sovereigns’ governance of non-doing—
like Heaven which does not speak,
yet the four seasons proceed and myriad things flourish,
without expecting humanity’s obedience,
yet the world has none who do not obey.
Thus only *non-action* can acquire the world.

○ Master Lin said:
*"To govern with virtue/power, to use the strange in war—*
this speaks of governance requiring administration,
and war requiring deceit.
Both are acts of deliberate intent.
But to act without acting,
this can win the world’s heart.
Hence it is said:
*‘To acquire the world through non-action.’*
I do not know why deliberate intent is unworkable,
while non-intent is effective—
this is the Dao/Way of it.

The world abounds in taboos and prohibitions,
yet the people grow ever poorer.
Humans multiply sharp tools,
yet the state grows ever more corrupt.

The people multiply in cleverness and artifice,
and strange things arise in abundance.
Laws and decrees grow ever more explicit,
yet thieves and robbers abound.

Master Lü said:
*"The cause of the people’s poverty and wealth,
longevity and early demise,
lies in their own nature,
without the ruler’s interference.
If the net of prohibitions is dense and strict,
the people grow ever more cautious and evasive,
their actions falter, and their livelihoods decline,
day by day growing poorer and more destitute.
Thus, the Sage’s engagement with the world
is rooted in *non-doing* as the true foundation,
while *doing* is but a responsive measure.
Whether doing or not doing,
the Sage remains steadfast in the foundation,
like one amid the clamor without resentment,
entangled in affairs yet without vexation—
this is the true foundation,
where *non-doing* and *non-thought* are constant.*

Moreover, the heart is like a sovereign,
while *qi* is like the people.
Excessive prohibitions unsettle the heart,
and the primal *qi* is scattered and diminished—
hence it is said:
*"The world abounds in taboos,
and the people grow ever poorer."*

Sharp tools are but instruments of authority,
the ruler’s axes and hatchets—
how can subjects wield them?
Even a single man’s tool,
when shared by all,
is like reversing the *Tà’ē* hilt
and giving the handle to another.
Once authority is surrendered,
the state falls into chaos.
As for oneself,
if the heart cannot remain pure and undivided,
but is instead controlled by the seven emotions and six desires,
how can clarity and wisdom not grow dim?
Hence it is said:
*"The people multiply in sharp tools,
and the state grows ever more corrupt."*

Clever artifice is but refined craftsmanship—
when rulers cannot refrain from action,
and their minds turn to licentious excess,
the people fashion intricate carvings,
ornaments of gold and jade,
strange and wondrous things,
to curry favor and indulge their desires.
For oneself,
if one cannot abandon worldly attachments
and instead drowns in sensory pleasures,
day by day growing deeper in indulgence,
then the Great Simplicity is dissipated,
and the Dao/Way is lost.
Hence it is said:
*"The people multiply in cleverness and artifice,
and strange things arise in abundance."*

Laws and decrees are but instruments of punishment and instruction—
if they are excessive and harsh,
those who manipulate words and laws will thrive in their wickedness,
while the people cannot bear the burden.
The small result in theft of wealth,
the great in theft of the state itself.
For oneself,
if one is ensnared by petty laws,
clinging to trifling rules,
while external distractions multiply,
the heart grows restless and disordered—
plundering one’s true nature,
robbing one’s innate purity—
such is the chaos wrought.
Hence it is said:
*"Laws and decrees grow ever more explicit,
yet thieves and robbers abound."*

Master He said:
*"The world abounds in taboos,
and the people grow ever poorer"—
this speaks of the state’s many restrictions.
Mountains and marshes each have their keepers,
markets and passes each have their tolls,
taxes without measure,
the people burdened and weary.
As when Yanzi answered Duke Jiao of Qi,
‘Every touch incurs a prohibition,
the people cannot endure it’—
is this not the case of excessive taboos making the people ever poorer?
Fewer prohibitions mean peaceful livelihoods.
The Classics say:
‘The state’s sharp tools
should not be displayed to the people.’
If the people multiply in sharp tools,
then the state grows ever more corrupt—
for fear they will seize the ruler’s authority.
Hence it is said:
‘The craftsman should not indulge in excessive cleverness,
for his tools will then be complete yet undecorated.’
If the people multiply in clever artifice,
strange things arise in abundance—
this is to fear the corruption of simplicity and virtue.
If the ruler issues no harsh decrees,
his affairs proceed without disturbance,
but if the net is dense and the laws severe,
wickedness cannot be contained,
and the people will turn to thieves and robbers—
this is to fear the overreach of laws.
Thus one understands further:
‘My non-doing, and the people transform themselves,
my love of stillness, and the people align themselves,
my non-interference, and the people grow rich,
my absence of desire, and the people return to simplicity.’
The ancient sages did not deceive me.

Master Li said:
*"Excessive taboos,
excessive sharp tools,
excessive clever artifice,
excessive laws and decrees—
all are unnatural.
Fewer taboos, and the people prosper,
abolish schemes of gain, and the people transform,
