reduce taxes and levies, and the people grow rich,
guide them with virtue/power, and the people return to simplicity.
Hence the sages say:
‘I do not act, and the people transform themselves,
I love stillness, and the people align themselves,
I do not interfere, and the people grow rich,
I have no desires, and the people return to simplicity.’

Master Lü said:
‘The Venerable Lord is the progenitor of ten thousand teachings,
yet still he upholds the words of the ancient sages—
“Guarding through humility,
not daring to reveal oneself.”
Thus, having state and people,
he makes no artificial constructions,
never failing to govern through non-doing,
so the people fulfill their natural lives,
and the common folk transform themselves.

The alchemists of the True One,
treat myself as the spirit,
and the people as *qi*.
Spirit condenses through non-doing,
primal *qi* transforms into fluid,
fluid into blood,
blood into sinew,
sinew into bone,
bone into marrow,
marrow into essence,
essence into the Mysterious Pearl,
the Mysterious Pearl into Golden Elixir.
This is what the *Yīnfǔ Jīng* calls
‘ten thousand transformations born from the body.’
Hence it is said:
‘I do not act, and the people transform themselves.’

Moderate use and cherish the people,
reject ornamentation and uphold substance—
never failing to prioritize frugality,
the people grow rich in their livelihoods,
and their homes prosper naturally.
The alchemists of the True One,
constantly without desire, endure long life—
enduring long life, *qi* fills the Three Fields within,
hence it is said:
‘I do not interfere, and the people grow rich.’

Peaceful and still, unspeaking,
simple and undisturbed in self-cultivation—
never failing to uphold the root of virtue/power,
the people embrace Heaven’s completeness,
and their customs become pure and correct.
If the human spirit is restless and agitated,
then *qi* becomes coarse and abrupt,
but if the spirit is tranquil and at ease,
all gates flow harmoniously,
and the primal *qi* aligns itself.
Hence it is said:
‘I love stillness, and the people align themselves.’

Without desire, empty and boundless,
venerating the root and discarding the superficial—
never failing to pursue simplicity,
the people rise in aspiration,
and their nature returns to simplicity.
The alchemists of the True One,
with the primal spirit serene and undisturbed,
desire unarisen,
*qi* refined and spirit condensed,
form the embryo and take shape—
hence it is said:
‘I have no desires, and the people return to simplicity.’

Master He said:
‘*I do not act, and the people transform themselves*—
if one acts, it ruins them,
and the people will not transform.
*I love stillness, and the people align themselves*—
if one agitates and loses it,
the people will not align.
*I do not interfere, and the people grow rich*—
if one seeks contention, one invites calamity,
and the people will not grow rich.’"

I have no desires, and the people return to simplicity—
if one harbors many desires, one brings many burdens,
and the people will not return to simplicity.
This is the essential Dao of supreme governance.

In ancient times, Master Guai excelled in governing with the Yellow Emperor’s teachings.
What he spoke with Cao Pi of was solely:
*"Governance values stillness and clarity, and the people will settle themselves."*
Expanding upon this principle,
Cao Pi grasped the method,
and as Chancellor of the State of Qi for nine years,
he brought about great harmony.

Master Li said:
*"Non-action and non-desire,
non-interference and love of stillness—
all are uprightness.
To govern with this,
the seas will be tranquil and the rivers clear;
to practice the Dao with this,
the Way will flourish and the times will prosper;
to cultivate oneself with this,
one’s *qi* will solidify and the spirit will condense.
When one is upright, ten thousand are upright;
when one heart is upright, ten thousand transformations are upright;
when one body is upright, ten thousand affairs are upright.
The meaning of uprightness is vast indeed!"*

**Chapter Fifty-Eight**
When governance is obscure and unadorned,
its people are pure and unspoiled.
When governance is scrutinizing and exacting,
its people grow deficient.

Fortune and misfortune lean upon each other,
misfortune and fortune lie hidden within each other—
who can know their limits?
What is upright may become strange,
what is strange may become upright;
what is good may become perverse,
what is perverse may become good.
The people’s confusion has long been thus.

Master Lin said:
*"Obscure and unadorned"* means not contriving cleverness.
*"Scrutinizing and exacting"* means excessive fussing.
*"Pure and unspoiled"* means self-contentment.
*"Deficient"* means insufficiency.
This also conveys the idea:
where there is intent, there is harm;
where there is no intent, governance is self-regulating.

Misfortune and fortune are not constant;
they lean upon and conceal each other—
who can know their ultimate end?
Uprightness is fixed,
but what is without uprightness?
It speaks of their endless leaning and concealment,
impossible to define.

In all under heaven,
what is strange may become upright,
what is upright may become strange,
what is good may become perverse,
what is perverse may become good—
benefit and harm, right and wrong—
all follow this pattern.
This too is the meaning of misfortune and fortune leaning upon each other.
The world’s people are confused and do not know it,
merely dividing strange from upright,
perverse from good—
their confusion is not of one day’s making.

Master Li said:
The previous chapter spoke of non-action and non-interference,
letting the people transform themselves—
a governance of broad tolerance.
Thus it follows with *"governance is obscure and unadorned,"*
meaning:
when the ruler is tolerant and expansive, the people are sincere and genuine;
when the ruler is overly active, the people grow dull and violent.
*"Obscure and unadorned"* is tolerance,
*"scrutinizing and exacting"* is meticulousness—
tolerance wins the people,
rigid law without flexibility harms them.
Misfortune and fortune lean upon each other,
uprightness and strangeness alternate,
good and perverse oppose each other—
this is the natural order.
How can one not take heed?

Cultivating fortune is not as good as avoiding misfortune,
pursuing uprightness is not as effective as avoiding perversity.
Though uprightness is good,
if one arrogantly claims extraordinary virtue,
it becomes perverse.
When uprightness is forced to be upright,
it ceases to be upright.
When goodness is forced to be good,
it ceases to be good.

Master Lü said:
The ancients aligned with Heaven’s movements,
their governance broad and natural,
letting things unfold without interference—
ever unadorned and obscure,
thus their customs were pure and unspoiled.
Those who cultivated the True Way
embraced the boundless spirit,
vast and tranquil,
forgetting sight and sound,
without distinction—
their breathing deep and pure,
and the Dao of transcendence was achieved.
Thus it is said:
*"When governance is obscure and unadorned,
its people are pure and unspoiled."*

Later rulers, with their rigid and hasty governance,
used laws to bind the people,
their scrutiny penetrating like a fishing net—
yet they still deemed it insufficient.
The people’s loss of their nature
was never more severe.
Thus they constantly stumbled into prohibitions,
their refinement and decay scattering,
and customs daily declined.

Those of shallow learning or just beginning the True Way,
not yet attaining Dao’s subtlety,
their *qi* and spirit not yet condensed,
their minds entangled in affairs,
obsessed with minute scrutiny,
praising cleverness and deceit—
if they govern themselves thus,
they gasp and strain,
their true essence departs,
and this is not the Way of nurturing life.
Thus it is said:
*"When governance is scrutinizing and exacting,
its people grow deficient."*

Moreover, the origins of chaos and order,
the sources of misfortune and fortune—
these have existed since antiquity,
arising from human actions.
When misfortune hides within fortune,
if fortune reaches its peak
and one cannot practice humility and fear,
but instead indulges in arrogance and excess,
there has never been one who did not invite misfortune.

When fortune lies hidden within misfortune,
if one, amid calamity and hardship,
suddenly abandons past errors
and inwardly reflects,
then what was once hidden becomes fortune.

The Dao of Heaven and Earth has periods of rest and activity,
the revolutions of sun and moon have phases of waning and waxing—
this is the pattern of leaning and concealment,
misfortune and fortune arising in sequence,
their limits unknown.

Those who hold the world
should not seek to govern it with clarity and activity,
but instead let it unfold naturally,
neither coveting wealth nor rejoicing in longevity,
neither honoring prominence nor shaming poverty.
Thus misfortune and fortune lean upon each other—
what concern are they to me?

Those who cultivate the True Way
do not seek fortune first,
nor invite misfortune.
They forget self and other—
how could misfortune and fortune’s leaning burden them?

Thus it is said:
*"Fortune and misfortune lean upon each other,
misfortune and fortune lie hidden within each other—
who can know their limits?"*
The movements of yin and yang govern life and death,
stasis and motion determine auspiciousness and calamity,
uprightness and perversity, goodness and perversity—
who can define them?

Uprightness is the orthodox path,
strangeness is a deviant route,
goodness is innate nature,
perversity is an aberrant kind.
Rooted in a single mind,
they branch into four—
not the harmony of a single *qi*.

Those who claim to uphold uprightness
must be sages with deliberate intent,
yet cunning and deceit
only harm what is upright.
To forcefully assert uprightness,
omens and portents arise—
they harm what is good.

Goodness and perversity, strangeness and uprightness,
alternate in their influence,
human nature is not singular,
lost between perversity and uprightness—
their confusion has endured for ages.

Take those of mediocre or low station who have heard the Dao:
they may suddenly conceive a spark of goodness,
yet lack the resolve to stand alone.
Confused by appearances,
in the blink of an eye,
errant thoughts arise—
and uprightness becomes strangeness,
goodness and strangeness become perversity.
As Zhuangzi and the Sage said:
*"The divine and strange transform into rot and decay."*

The wisdom of the Sage is hard to fathom,
even spirits and gods cannot discern it—
how much more so for the common flow?

「Thus, people have long been mired in delusion and folly—
it is not only a recent affliction.
Therefore it is said:
*When there is neither uprightness nor perversity,*
*uprightness becomes strangeness,*
*goodness becomes perversity.*
The people’s confusion,
its duration has indeed been long.

Hence, the Sage is square yet does not sever,
clean yet does not wound,
direct yet does not indulge,
bright yet does not dazzle.

**He Xian** says:
*Severing is harming,*
*wounding is injuring.*
Square yet not severed—
easy to harm.
Clean yet not wounded—
easy to injure.
I use square and clean,
leading by my own virtue,
to let transformation unfold naturally—
I would never do harm to others or injure them.

*Indulging is excess,*
*dazzling is ostentation.*
Direct yet not indulgent—
easy to flaunt.
Bright yet not dazzling—
easy to blind.
I use direct and bright,
to show the Dao to the people,
to let transformation unfold naturally—
I would never indulge in self-aggrandizement or self-display.

*Moderate yet not extreme*—
an ancient complete phrase,
as seen in the *Annotations of the Plenitude of Rites* (*Liji* 注).
Those who alter *wounding* to *filth* are incorrect.
In *Zhuangzi*, *clean* and *wound* share similar meanings—
*refined and polished*.

**Dong Shi** says:
*Square in conduct yet round in virtue,*
*so there is no confusion of severing or wounding.*
Clean—
to contain without harm.
Indulging—
to spread without restraint.
The great directness,
though appearing bent, does not indulge,
bright yet not dazzling—
restoring its clarity.
These four are the essence of governance.

The Sage alone maintains tranquility and non-action,
using square, clean, direct, and bright principles within—
orderly yet imperceptible to people.
He lets all under heaven dwell in their natural states,
unentangled by one-sided extremes—
thus avoiding severing, wounding, indulging, and dazzling.
Then, how can misfortune or blessing arise?

**Chapter Fifty-Nine: Governing People and Serving Heaven, Nothing Exceeds Frugality**

**Lü Shi** says:
The Sage’s governance,
with non-action and non-interference,
saves resources and restrains expenditure—
then the granaries are full,
the people prosper, and customs flourish.
Then, preserving vitality and cherishing spirit,
unifying the heart and purifying the self,
to manifestly honor the sacrifice to the Supreme Sovereign—
both people and gods are in harmony.

Moreover, the Sage’s **cleverness and wisdom**,
his **movement and stillness**, his **thought and contemplation**—
these are the inner fulfillments of his mind.
His **bending and stretching**, his **bowing and rising**,
his **appearance, voice, and color**—
these are the outer fulfillments of his body.
The two must harmonize,
naturally aligned—
the key lies in each attaining its proper measure.
This is the meaning of **frugality**.

Cultivating the inner
is the way to govern people—
governing oneself to govern others.
Cherishing the **primordial essence** (*yuan* 元)
is the way to serve Heaven—
serving one’s own Heaven.

Heaven is the **cerebral palace** (*nao gong* 脑宫),
pure Yang radiant and vast,
dwelling in the **Upper Nine** (*Shang Jiu* 上九),
above which there is no Yin.
From the **Jade Capital Gate** (*Yujing Guan* 玉京关) upward,
all is Heaven.

Frugality is **preservation and cherishing**.
Cherishing vision nourishes the spirit,
cherishing hearing nourishes the **void** (*xu* 虚),
cherishing speech nourishes the **qi**,
cherishing rest nourishes **harmony** (*he* 和),
cherishing the brain nourishes the blood—
thus it is said:
*Governing people and serving Heaven,*
*nothing exceeds frugality.*

**He Xian** says:
The ruler stands at the juncture of Heaven and humanity.
Whatever is used to serve Heaven above,
and govern people below,
often seeks from without—
yet does not seek within the self.
The **Grand Historian** (*Sima Qian* 司马迁) placed Dao first among the Six Schools,
taking its **singular focus** (*shen* 神) as the **Great Dao** (*Dadao* 大道),
saying *"movement aligns with the formless"*—
this is harmony with Heaven.
Saying *"sufficiently nourishing all things"*—
this also nourishes the people.
Again, he said:
*The essence of what humans bear is spirit,*
*what they rely upon is form.*
If **vitality** (*jing* 精) is overused, it is exhausted;
if **spirit** (*shen* 神) is overused, it is fatigued.
When spirit departs, death ensues—
thus the Sage treasures it.

Treasure—
this is the meaning of **frugality**.
Frugality is the utmost **parsimony**.
The Sage guards the inner yet does not guard the outer.
When he speaks of **restraint** (*jie* 节),
**cherishing** (*ai* 爱),
**preservation** (*shou* 守)—
all are frugality’s intent.

Not frugal with the mouth leads to excessive speech and depleted **qi**.
The five flavors, if indulged, impair vitality;
not frugal with ears and eyes leads to the five sounds confusing the ears,
the five colors confusing the eyes—
as it says,
*"To indulge one’s desires*
*fatigues the spirit."*
Thus, **frugality in vitality, qi, and spirit**—
this, on the Dao’s path,
can be **guest of the Nine Purity** (*Jiu Qing* 九清),
and below,
can bless all living beings.

**Wu Shi** says:
Sound—
the essential **guardian of form** (*liu xing xi qi* 留形惜气),
the **vital screen** (*yao wei* 要卫).

**Li Shi** says:
Governing people and serving Heaven,
nothing exceeds **beginning with frugality**.
Speaking of the state,
it means **reducing punishments** and **lightening taxes**.
Speaking of the self,
it means **restraining speech** and **moderating drink and food**.
Speaking of the heart,
it means **limiting thought** and **cutting off vision and hearing**,
preserving the heart and nurturing nature—
thus one can serve Heaven.

Frugality alone is called **early restoration** (*zao fu* 早复).
Early restoration is **heavily accumulating virtue** (*zhong ji de* 重积德).
Heavily accumulating virtue—
nothing cannot be overcome.
Nothing cannot be overcome—
its limits are unknown.
Its limits unknown—
one can have a state.

**Lü Shi** says:
Moreover, **frugality**—
cherishing and nurturing the spirit,
diligently practicing moderation with constancy—
like nature, unchanging;
like virtue, unshifting.
Restraining expenditure and storing resources,
self-sufficient in frugality—
while wisdom and tranquility mutually nourish,
desires do not stray outward.
Nature restores itself from within—
as when one cherishes form,
preserves spirit,
honors qi,
and stabilizes the root.
Spirit, brain, and blood—
not wasted or scattered.
Early to restore the original and return to the essence—
thus it is said:
**Frugality alone is early restoration.**

Cultivating the inner—
early frugality leads to restoration,
then **qi softens** and **harmonizes**,
all spirits find ease,
restoring one’s original nature—
not swayed by external things.
Deep and distant—
thus it is said: **early restoration.**

Called **heavily accumulating virtue**—
yet **heavy accumulation** means
**overcoming oneself to return to propriety** (*ke ji fu li* 克己复礼),
**cultivating the Dao with benevolence** (*xiu dao yi ren* 修道以仁).
Never indulging in **ostentation or commercialization** (*xuan yu* 眩鬻),
Heaven and humanity trust and obey—
no thing dares defy.
Thus, the four quarters **reverently emulate**,
nothing resists or conquers—
hence the saying:
*Heavily accumulating virtue,*
*its limits are unknown.*」

「Thus it is said: **Heavily accumulating virtue**, then nothing fails to be overcome.
And when nothing fails to be overcome,
the myriad things return to their transformations,
virtue and Dao are boundless—
where shall it not prevail?
Those who refine within,
great virtue abides with ease,
for they do not contend—
what then can they not overcome?
Reaching the One without resistance,
true constancy never alters.
Attuning to the Ten Thousand Ages yet never aging—
who can fathom its limits?
Thus it is said: **Heavily accumulating virtue**, then its limits are unknown.

When its limits are beyond knowing,
one’s body follows yet precedes itself;
the world joyfully extols it without wearying.
Enjoying the Ren of Yao,
delighting in the transformations of Yao,
his realm is preserved with ample surplus.

Those who refine within,
embody the boundless Dao,
and nurture virtue without end—
a realm that can be secured
is like a body that can endure.
Thus it is said: **Its limits are unknown**, yet one can have a realm.

○ Master He says:
**Heavily accumulating virtue**—
then gathered water becomes the sea,
gathered qi becomes the spirit.
Nothing fails to be overcome—
then it responds to all things in transformation,
adapting to every circumstance without fail.
When its limits are beyond knowing,
it aligns with the Great Dao,
mysterious and profound—
if rulers truly follow this Way,
they can secure their realm and endure in lasting peace.

○ Master Lin says:
This chapter speaks of **early restoration**—
when one is frugal, it returns to the root,
reverting to the origin and restoring the essence.
When virtue reaches this point,
it grows ever more abundant and flourishing.
**Heavily accumulating** means ever-increasing virtue.
The foundation of a lasting realm—
this is the Way of deep roots and firm bases,
of enduring life and long vision.

○ Master Lü says:
At first, one cultivates nature to return to virtue,
now one accumulates virtue to enter the Dao.
As the foundation of a realm,
it can nourish the people abundantly,
bestow blessings upon the land and its altars,
and span the eternal ages.
Comparing it to the body—
the foundation of a realm is qi,
and form is the basis that receives qi.
Qi is the root of form,
born from spirit and consciousness,
while form arises from qi.
Thus, Fu Xi attained it to inherit the **Qi Mother**,
for spirit is the child of qi,
while qi is the mother of spirit.

Form cannot exist without qi—
it has no foundation.
Qi cannot govern form—
then what can be its master?
When qi disperses, the body decays;
when qi remains, the body endures long.
Thus it is said: **The foundation of a realm**, can endure long.
The ruler who accumulates virtue,
governs humanity and serves Heaven,
nurtures the realm and cares for the people—
plants his roots in **non-action**,
firmly secures his base in **clarity and tranquility**,
and the altars of soil and grain stretch far.
Thus it is called the **Way of enduring life**.
Ever ruling with constant illumination,
thus it is called the **Way of long vision**.
One’s own root is the brain,
one’s own base is the navel.
Deep roots draw upon the **Original Qi** to nourish the brain—
the **immortal’s saying** goes:
*To attain non-aging, return essence to nourish the brain.*
Then, sealing the pivotal mechanisms,
closing the gates of desire,
hiding the **Original Essence** in the **obscure palace**,
and forming the **fetal breath** in the **Cinnabar Court**—
above red, below black,
left blue, right white,
within the yellow radiance of the center,
lies the **Yellow Court’s treasure**,
right at the navel—
this is called **firmly securing the base**.
Now, to cultivate **enduring life**,
first one must **secure the base**,
breathing in harmony with **Great Unity**,
returning the spirit and taming the qi,
with **fetal breath** continuous and abiding in the navel,
**enduring life** becomes as constant as Heaven and Earth,
and **long vision** shines like the sun and moon.

The **immortal’s saying** declares:
*To attain enduring life, one must first achieve long vision.*
**Long vision** is **inner vision**.

○ Master Lin says:
This chapter uses governing a realm to illustrate **nourishing life**.
If one can be frugal in nourishing life,
then one can deepen the roots,
firm the base,
attain enduring life,
and achieve long vision.

○ Master Su says:
Using **frugality** to govern the people,
one can secure a realm.
Using **frugality** to serve Heaven,
one plants deep roots and secures the base.
The ancient sages
preserved their **nature and life’s constancy**,
not wasting the inner through external excess—
then their roots are deep, their bases firm,
and nothing can uproot them.
Though **enduring life** and **long vision** may follow,
**frugality** alone is supreme in governing people and serving Heaven.

---
**Chapter Sixty: Governing a Great Realm, Like Cooking a Small Fresh Fish.**

○ Master Lü says:
Governing a great realm—
one must not **harass** it, for harassment brings disorder.
Cooking a small fresh fish—
one must not **disturb** it, for disturbance makes it rot.
Thus, the adept ruler
cooks a fish no larger than a palm,
adjusting the **water and fire**,
letting it cook itself—
then the fish remains whole.
If one disturbs it, the fish is damaged,
and it spoils in the pot.

The adept governor
harmonizes his **policies and teachings**,
letting the people find their own way—
then the people are naturally at ease.
If one disturbs them, the people are harmed,
and they scatter from the realm.
Thus, the **Way of governance values clarity and tranquility**,
the realm depends on the people,
the body depends on qi.
Loving the people and governing the realm—
then the realm prospers.
Loving qi and governing the body—
then the body endures long.
Hence this illustration is set forth.

The method of **loving the people and conserving qi**
is like cooking a small fresh fish—
do not stir it,
for agitation wastes and ruins it.

○ Master Li says:
Governing with **rectitude**,
one’s policies are **profound and subtle**.
Governing people and serving Heaven—
all are the **meaning of governance**.
A small fresh fish—
a tiny fish.
Using it as an analogy,
if one stirs it, it disintegrates—
speaking of governing the world,
if one disturbs it, chaos ensues.

○ Master Lin says:
Cooking a small fresh fish—
if one stirs it, it breaks apart.
Governing a realm—
if one disturbs it, disorder arises.
**Clarity and tranquility, non-action,**
peace and stillness without disturbance—
this is the **Way of governance**.

---
**Governing the world with the Dao, the spirits lose their power.**

○ Master Lü says:
The sage is **divinely illuminated**,
the master of all things.
Though compelled to govern the world,
he employs the **Way of non-action** to rule it.
Thus the winds and rains come in due measure,
floods and droughts do not arise.
Human hearts are **purified and harmonized**,
and the spirits and gods all submit to **illuminated virtue**.
The spirits, content in their **hidden realms**,
dare not emit their **mysterious sounds**,
nor display their **divine transformations**.
If one governs the world within one’s own body
with the Dao,
then **serenity and simplicity** mutually nourish,
the **divine abode** is **calm and settled**,
inner **Heavenly Light** radiates,
**evil spirits** are **extinguished**,
and how dare the **malevolent ghosts** show their **divine transformations**?

○ **Master Wu says:**
"Governing" is the same as "descending."
"Ghosts" refer to the **qi of Heaven and Earth**—**divine and numinous** forces, **portents and omens**.

Humankind’s **qi** flows as one with the **qi of Heaven and Earth**.
The sovereign who follows the **Dao** descends upon the world with **serenity and simplicity**,
does not disturb or harm the people,
so the people’s **qi** becomes **purified and harmonized**,
filling the **Heavenly and Earthly realms**,
mutually resonating in **correspondence and response**,
and the **qi of Heaven and Earth** does not dare to act in **contrariety or perversity**,
so its **ghostly portents** do not become **malevolent omens** to bring calamity.

○ **Master He says:**
To govern the world with the **Dao** is to **divinely illuminate** the people and bring them **serenity**.
As *Zhuangzi* says, the sage descends upon the world only when compelled,
and nothing surpasses **non-action**.
**Non-action** means the people find **tranquility in their natural lives**,
acting without interference, yet ever **spontaneously**,
and nothing can harm them.

○ **Riverbank Master** [河上公] says:
When one holds office by **Dao and virtue** and governs the world,
the **ghosts** dare not display their **spiritual essence** to offend humanity.
It is not that their **spiritual essence** is absent—
rather, they cannot harm those who live in **natural harmony**.
Nor is it that they are unable to harm—
the sage also does not harm them.
**Master Lü says:**
It is not that the **ghosts** lack **divine transformations** and **mysterious sounds**,
but rather that the sage maintains his **proper station**,
and the **ghosts** ought to **reverently praise** the **grand beauty of Heaven and Earth**,
**assisting and harmonizing** with **illuminated virtue**,
fittingly bestowing blessings,
and not merely refraining from **malevolent omens** and **mysterious transformations**
to harm the people.

Moreover, the **Daoist sage** does not allow the **ghosts** to display their **divine transformations**,
for they cannot harm those who abide in **natural harmony**.
Thus it is said: *"It is not that the ghosts lack spiritual essence—
rather, they cannot harm those who live in natural harmony."*
When the **spiritual essence** is **settled in rectitude**,
it does not harm the people,
for it submits to the **illuminated virtue** of the sage,
retiring into **hidden realms** and not daring to reveal its **portents and omens**.

The sage pities even a single individual who has lost his **proper place**,
never failing to consider the means to **rescue and sustain** him.
Just as a father nurtures his child,
though he wraps and swaddles him,
his care is ever **vigilant and tender**—
how could he harm him?
This is like the **Daoist sage** abiding in **natural harmony**,
where not only the **ghosts** dare not offend,
but the sage himself does not harm them either.
The sage, who **honors the Dao** and **values virtue**,
would never harm those who follow the **Dao**!

Thus it is said: *"It is not that the spiritual essence cannot harm—
rather, the sage does not harm them."*

○ **Master Wu says:**
The reason the **ghosts** do not become **portents and omens** is not that they are incapable—
though they can become **portents and omens**, they do not act in **malevolence** to harm humanity.
Again, the **ghosts'** portents and omens** do not harm humanity not because they are inherently so,
but because the sage can **purify and harmonize** the people’s **qi** without harming the **qi of Heaven and Earth**,
so the **qi of Heaven and Earth** also remains **purified and harmonized** and does not harm humanity.
The terms **"ghosts"** and **"spiritual essence"** both refer to the **qi of Heaven and Earth**,
two names yet one reality.

○ **Riverbank Master** [河上公] says:
The **ghosts** are not without **spiritual essence**—
they simply cannot harm those who live in **natural harmony**.
When both sides do not harm each other,
**virtue and power** mutually return to their source.

○ **Master Lü says:**
Humans are governed by the **yang**, the **ghosts** by the **yin**.
Humans **preserve their natural lives**,
while the **ghosts** **fulfill their divine spirits**.
Both sides, in **reciprocal delight**, do not harm one another,
leading to **Heaven’s clarity and Earth’s peace**,
**harmony between yin and yang**,
**abundant harvests each year**,
**no creatures maimed or lost**,
**no flaws in the state**,
**no plagues among the people**,
**no malevolent omens from the ghosts**—
thus **virtue and power** mutually return to their source.

○ **Master Lin** [林氏] says:
**Hidden realms** manifest as **yin and yang**,
while **illuminated governance** is the **sage’s proper station**.
These two aspects inherently do not harm each other,
so their **virtue and power** mutually return.
This means **Heaven and Earth** follow their **natural Dao**,
and the sage also follows the **natural Dao**,
each preserving their own **virtue and power** without encroachment,
hence the saying: *"They mutually return."*

**Not harming** means **not encroaching**.
This passage first presents a general statement,
then elaborates on the **Way of the Three Powers**—
all of it is about **non-action**.
To govern the world with the **Dao** is to encompass the **Three Powers** [Heaven, Earth, humanity].
All things beneath the **Great Void** [太虚]
are governed by the **Dao**:
**Heaven** fulfills its role of **covering and nurturing**,
**Earth** fulfills its role of **bearing and sustaining**,
and the sage fulfills his role of **teaching and guiding**.
When each fulfills its own role without encroaching,
all attain their **Dao**.

○ **Riverbank Master** [河上公] says:
The **ghosts** and the **sage** both do not harm each other.
When neither harms the other,
humans are governed by the **yang**,
while the **ghosts** are governed by the **yin**,
humans **preserve their natural lives**,
and the **ghosts** **fulfill their divine spirits**—
thus **virtue and power** mutually return.

○ **Master He** [何氏] says:
The ancient people dwelled in the **indistinct void** [混茫],
and with the world they **dissolved in tranquility**.
At that time,
**yin and yang** knew **stillness**,
**ghosts and gods** did not disturb,
the **Four Seasons** followed their **proper rhythms**,
and all things were **unharmed**.
Though creatures had **awareness**,
they **did not apply it**—
this was called **"Absolute Unity"**,
where **non-action** and **spontaneity** prevail eternally.
This is the case.

**Chapter 61:**
*"Great states are the lower streams [of the world],
the **confluence of all things**,
the **yin of the world**.*
*The **yin** always overcomes the **yang** by **stillness**,
and **stillness** is the **yin**’s way.*

○ **Master Lin** [林氏] says:
Borrowing the **principle of great states and small states**,
he illustrates that the **Daoist sage** ought to **be humble and still**,
not teaching people to **submit themselves** to gain victory.
Since the **Three Dynasties** onward,
there have been those who **seize states**,
so he uses their observations as an analogy.

*"Lower streams"* means to **place oneself in a humble station**.
The people of great states,
if they **humble themselves**,
can **unite the world’s confluence**,
just as the **yin** overcomes the **yang** by **stillness**.
To **humble oneself** is to **abide in stillness** as the **Dao**,
hence the saying: *"Stillness is the yin’s way."*

○ **Master Lü** [吕氏] says:
The reason the **seas and rivers** can be the **"sovereigns of all valleys"** [百谷]
is that they **excel in humility**.
All streams **converge at the river’s source** [朝宗],
just as great states, through **humility and inclusivity**,
can **unite all people** without exception.
The **Classics** say:
*"When Heaven and Earth’s **qi** converge, all things are born.
When the **Dao of humanity** converges, **achievements and merit** are fulfilled."*
This is what is meant by *"confluence."*

The establishment of **one body** is the **mirror of a state**.
To **forget one’s body** is to **order all limbs**,
To **empty one’s heart** is to **return to harmony with the **qi****.
The **emptiness and nothingness** are where **sages dwell**,
Which can be called a **great state**.

Because it **does not contend**,
The **world cannot contend with it**.
Even the **essence of all things**,
When **converged and united**,
Is like the **many waters rushing to the lowlands**.
Hence the saying: *"Great states are the lowlands."*

The **confluence of the world**,
The **yin of the world**,
Does not initially **hold the heart to victory**,
Only through **stillness** does it always prevail.
To **prevail through stillness** is to **abide in humility**,
Hence the **world’s confluence** returns to it.

**Yin is still, yang is dynamic**,
Softness is still, hardness is active.
Hardness is **overcome by softness**,
Activity is **subdued by stillness**.
The **Classics** say:
*"Know the **heroic**,
Guard the **receptive**,
And you will be the **valley of the world**."*
This is indeed the **virtue of humility and abasement**,
Which ensures **no undertaking fails**—
**Victory is inherent in oneself**.

**Heaven’s One** generates **water**,
In humans, this is **precision**.
**Earth’s Two** generates **fire**,
In humans, this is **divine spirit**.
**Divine spirit** is the **yin within the yang**,
Hence it is called **receptive**,
Also the **yin of the world**.
To **know the heroic and guard the receptive**,
To **focus the **qi** and cultivate softness**,
To **refine yang with yin**,
Is the **mysterious and wondrous application**,
Hence **yin always prevails through stillness**.

**Stillness** attracts **all movements**,
**Humility** draws **all people**—
This is the **key to cultivating the **True Dao****.
Hence, **great states humble themselves to small states**,
And **gain the small states**.
**Small states humble themselves to great states**,
And **gain the great states**.
Sometimes **humility leads to gain**,
Sometimes **humility brings gain**.

**Master Lin** says:
To **gain through greatness** is called *"gain"* (以取),
To **gain through smallness** is called *"while taking"* (而取).
These two phrases are **most profound**.

**Master Lü** says:
To **serve small states with greatness**,
Only **benevolence** can achieve this.
To **serve great states with smallness**,
Only **wisdom** can achieve this.
Those who **serve small states with greatness** are **rejoicing in Heaven’s Dao**,
Those who **serve great states with smallness** are **fearing Heaven’s Dao**.
The **rejoicing in Heaven’s Dao** know **Heaven’s command**,
The **fearing Heaven’s Dao** **preserve themselves**.

**Master Wu** says:
**Great states**, originally **above small states**,
Do not **rely on their exalted status** but **humble themselves below small states**,
Thus **attracting the small states’ joyful submission**.
**Small states**, naturally **below great states**,
**Gladly abide in humility** and **bow to the great states**,
Thus **earning the great states’ favor**.
*"Humility leads to gain"* means **great states** can **humble themselves to gain small states’ joyful submission**,
*"Humility brings gain"* means **small states** can **humble themselves to earn great states’ favor**.

**Master He** says:
**Great states humble themselves to small states**,
Like **Tang serving Ge**,
Or **King Wen serving the Kun Yi**.
**Small states humble themselves to great states**,
Like **King Tai serving the Xi Yu**,
Or **King Gu of Yue serving Wu**—
Such examples.

**Master Li** says:
**Superiors humble themselves to inferiors**,
**Inferiors humble themselves to superiors**,
**Superiors yield to inferiors, inferiors comply with superiors**—
Thus **virtue and Way return to their confluence**.
**Cultivating oneself** with this principle:
The **heart grows more humble**, the **Way grows more exalted**,
The **body grows more retiring**, the **virtue grows more refined**.
**Great states** only **seek to embrace and nurture all people**,
**Small states** only **seek to be included in service**.
If both **fulfill their desires**,
Then **greatness is fitting to abide in humility**.

**Master Lin** says:
The **intent of great states** is only **to embrace and nurture all people**,
The **intent of small states** is only **to carve and inscribe themselves** in seeking service.
Both must **abide in humility**—
Only through **humility** can each **fulfill their desires**,
Thus the **greatest understanding of the Way** must **favor humility and abasement**.

**Master Wu** says:
When **great states humble themselves to small states**,
It is only **to embrace and nurture the small states**.
When **small states humble themselves to great states**,
It is only **to seek inclusion in service**.
If both **abide in humility**,
Then **great and small each attain their desires**,
Yet the **small ones** are naturally **beneath others**,
And **do not fear their inability to humble themselves**,
While the **great ones**, not naturally **beneath others**,
**May fear their inability**—
Hence it is **specifically emphasized** that the **great ones should abide in humility**.

**Master Lü** says:
When **great states humble themselves to small states**,
It is only **to annex and embrace the small states’ people**,
Making them **their rulers and leaders**.
When **small states humble themselves to great states**,
It is only **to offer proper rites** and **reverently serve the great states**,
Seeking **aid and support**.
Yet **great and small** each **fulfill their proper roles**,
**Mutually holding firm** without **encroaching or provoking**,
**Content in their dwellings**,
**Rejoicing in their customs**,
And **small states naturally submit in softness**—
This is the **natural order**.

**Great states** practice **humility and abasement**,
Hence they **should bend and yield** to **preserve completeness**,
Making **great and small adaptable**,
Thus **attracting the world’s confluence**.

**Master He** says:
This chapter **begins with "Great states are the lowlands"**,
And **ends with "Greatness is fitting to abide in humility"**,
Though **great and small states are discussed together**,
The **primary focus is always on the great states**.
The **small ones** easily **abide in humility**,
This **requires no instruction**,
The **great ones**, prone to **excess and pride**,
Find **humility difficult**—
Even with instruction, they **may not comply**.
Hence it is **repeatedly emphasized**,
In alignment with the **same Way as the *I Ching*** (*易*).

**Chapter Sixty-Two**
The **Dao** is the **mysterious core of all things**.

The **virtuous** hold **Dao** as their treasure,
While the **unvirtuous** rely upon it for **safeguard**.

**Master He** says:
The **Dao** molds and refines all things,
Polishes the formless,
Silent and unmoving—
The **Great Dao** is **mysterious and obscure**,
Deep, vast, and boundless,
Unattainable from without,
Unfathomable within—
This is what is called the **mysterious core of all things**.
Like the **inner sanctum of a hall**,
Not glimpsed in haste,
As *Zhuangzi* says, the **Dao** is the **shared source of all things**,
Hence this chapter first speaks of **treasure**, then of **nobility**.

The **Dao** does not distance itself from people—
People distance themselves from the **Dao**.
Though there is a distinction between **virtuous** and **unvirtuous**,
Even the **unvirtuous** rely upon it in hidden ways for **security**.

**Master Wu** says:
The **mysterious core of all things** refers to what is **most honored**—
*"Olymp"* (奥) is the **west-south corner of a hall**, where the **noble** reside,
Thus *"Olymp"* signifies **nobility**.
The **nobility of the Dao** is like this.
**Treasure** is what the **virtuous** value—
When the **virtuous** turn toward the **Dao** and cultivate it,
They gain **esteem from others**,
Hence it is said: *"The treasure of the virtuous."*
When the **unvirtuous** turn toward the **Dao** and repent,
They may yet **preserve themselves**,
Hence it is said: *"The safeguard of the unvirtuous."*
**Graceful speech** can be **traded in the market**,
**Noble conduct** can **elevate others**.
Why should the **unvirtuous** be **cast aside**?
Thus, when establishing the **Son of Heaven** and appointing the **Three Ministers**,
Even with **jewel-encircled jade and four-horse chariots** offered first,
It is **not as valuable** as **sitting and advancing this Dao**.

**Master Li** says:
**Graceful speech** can be **traded in the market**—
Sufficient to **please the multitude**,
**Noble conduct** can **elevate others**—
Sufficient to **rise above the crowd**.
If a person is **unvirtuous**, why **abandon them**?
To establish the **Son of Heaven** and appoint the **Three Ministers** is to **make them rulers and teachers**,
To **cultivate the people below**,
So that the **unvirtuous** follow their **transformations**.
**Jewel-encircled jade and four-horse chariots** are **not truly noble**—
Not as noble as **sitting and advancing this Dao**,
Cultivating oneself to **settle the people**,
Leading with **respect and humility** so they **do not contend**,
Displaying **virtue and righteousness** so they **upright their conduct**,
Demonstrating **approval and disapproval** so they **know prohibitions**—
Thus the **Sage** always **rescues people with virtue**,
And **never abandons anyone**.

**Master Chai** says:
The **Dao’s flavor** is **beneficial**—
Like **treasure brought to market**,
Hence *"graceful speech can be traded in the market."*
**Virtue’s weight** is **irresistible**,
Hence *"noble conduct can elevate others."*
If a person is **unvirtuous**, why **abandon them**?
Teach them to **transform**—
How dare one **abandon them**?
Thus, establishing the **Son of Heaven** and appointing the **Three Ministers** is to **assist in advancing this Dao**.

**Master Wu** says:
The **virtuous** gain **esteem from others** through the **Dao**—
Their **praiseworthy words** are **lovable**, like **fine goods sold in the market**,
Their **exalted deeds** are **worthy of veneration**, rising above others.
The **unvirtuous** preserve themselves through the **Dao**—
Fearful of **punishment**, few in **guilt**, their **bodies remain whole and secure**,
Thus even the **unvirtuous**—
How could the **Dao** ever **abandon them**?
**Jewel-encircled jade**—a **jewel held in both hands**—
**Four-horse chariots**—a **single team of horses**,
**Jewel-encircled jade and four-horse chariots** are **the height of nobility**,
Yet they are **not truly noble**.
Not as noble as **advancing this Dao**—
**Jewel-encircled jade and four-horse chariots** are **not as noble as this Dao**,
Hence all things **hold it in highest esteem** as their **mysterious core**.

**Master He** says:
Not to **glory in jade, gems, or chariots**,
But to **hold advancing this Dao as noble**.
Why did the **ancients** hold this **Dao** in such **nobility**?
Was it not because **seeking it brings attainment**,
And **guilt is pardoned**?
Thus it is **noble for all under Heaven**.

**Master Lin** says:
Moreover, why did the **ancients** hold this **Dao** in such **nobility**?
Because **seeking it brings attainment**—
The **Dao** is **within oneself**,
To **practice virtue** depends on **oneself**, not others.
To **pardon guilt**—
A single **virtuous thought** can **correct errors**,
Even the **wicked**, through **fasting, purification, and bathing**, may **offer sacrifice to the Most High**.
It is not said, *"As the *Poetry Classic* does not say?"*
The **Dao** knows **no distinction between wise and foolish**—
**Awakening brings attainment**,
This is why it is **truly noble**,
Hence it is said: *"Thus it is noble for all under Heaven."*

**Master He** says:
Without a single person **not gaining what they desire** or **gathering what they seek**,
All their **pursuits are fulfilled**.
The **Noble One** delights in **attaining the Dao** and **rests in virtue**,
The **petty person** delights in **attaining desires** but **shames unvirtue**,
Even those who are **unvirtuous** yet **turn their hearts toward the Dao**,
**Shift toward virtue and distance guilt**—
Unaware, they do so.
This is how those **who embody the Dao** gain **great use in the world**,
And it also **reveals the Dao’s efficacy**—
The more **noble**, the more **esteemed**,
Thus it is said: *"The ancients held this Dao in nobility"*—
Not of **this present age alone**, but **throughout antiquity**.
Thus it is.

**Chapter Sixty-Three**
**Act without acting**,
**Manage without managing**,
**Savor without savoring**.

**Master Lü** says:
The **Sage acts without purpose**,
Yet **trusts in things’ natural self-so**,
Thus **without acting, nothing remains undone**—
How could the **heart** ever **cease moving**?
**Manages without interference**,
Yet **follows things’ natural affairs**,
Thus **without managing, nothing remains unattended**—
How could the **body** ever **cease laboring**?
**Savors without indulgence**,
Yet **follows things’ inherent flavors**,
Thus **without savoring, nothing remains unsavored**—
How could the **mouth** ever **cease craving**?
When the **heart is unmoved**, all **wonders are visible**,
When the **body is unlabored**, **spirit is undiminished**,
When the **mouth is unindulgent**, **essence is undrained**—
The **Perfect One** **acts in cultivation and nourishment**,
Yet **does not engage in reckless deeds**,
**Attends to True Refining**,
Yet **does not involve themselves in worldly affairs**,
**Savors in virtue and morality**,
Yet **does not indulge in carrion and rank odors**,
Hence it is said:
**Act without acting**,
**Manage without managing**,
**Savor without savoring**.

**Master Lin** says:
Only when **acting without acting** can one **do all things**,
Hence *"Act without acting."*
Only when **managing without managing** can one **gather all affairs**,
Hence *"Manage without managing."*
Only when **indulging without indulging** can one **truly know flavor**,
Hence *"Savor without savoring."*

**Master Li** says:
**Act without acting**—
This **unfolds the mysterious core of all things** from the previous chapter.
**Act without acting**—
**Nothing remains undone**,
**Manage without managing**—
**All affairs succeed**,
**Savor without savoring**—
Its **flavor is profound and enduring**—
These three are the **Dao’s mysterious core**.

**Great and small, much and little,**
**Repay resentment with virtue,**
**Plan for difficulty in its ease,**
**Achieve the great through the minute.**

**The world’s difficult tasks**
**Must begin in simplicity,**
**The world’s grandest deeds**
**Must arise from the fine.**

**Master Lü says:**
When dealing with the formed,
There are great and small,
When bound to the formed,
There are much and little.
Though things are boundless in measure,
Their utility is rooted—
Respond without hoarding,
Entrust to innate constancy.
When great is needed, be great,
When small is needed, be small,
When much is needed, be much,
When little is needed, be little.
All things differ, yet all return to One.

All are governed by the **Dao**,
Repaid with **virtue**—
If no one harbors deep malice,
What cause for resentment remains?

The **Perfect Person’s** **Way** is vast,
His **primordial simplicity** minute,
Though his actions are many,
Their essence is few.
Great and small contain the **Great Void** within a square inch,
Much and little encompass all things in **One Reality**,
In harmony with all things, all things become one with me—
What is there to resent?

Thus it is said:
**Great and small, much and little,**
**Repay resentment with virtue.**

As for right and wrong, beauty and ugliness,
Resentment and anger, gratitude and **virtue**,
All arise from subtle beginnings,
Start in ease, and end in difficulty,
Begin in the fine, and culminate in the grand.
If one plans at the time of initial ease,
Then at the end, there will be no difficulty.
If one acts at the time of first simplicity,
Then afterward, it will surely be grand.

Thus, the world’s difficult tasks
Must begin in what is easy,
Desiring to act in ease
To plan for the weighty and great.
The world’s grandest deeds
Must arise from the minute,
Desiring to act in the minute
To remove it.

One’s own difficult task is **cultivating the True**,
One’s own grand task is **walking the **Way**.
Nothing is more difficult than **cultivating the True**,
Yet if one approaches it as if it were easy,
One’s resolve will be firm and unshaken—
What difficulty remains?
Nothing is greater than **walking the **Way**,
Yet if one treats it as a minor matter,
And persists without ceasing,
Accumulating the fine to reach the grand—

**Master Lin says:**
Those who can be great must also be able to be small,
Those who can be much must also be able to be little,
Those who harbor resentment must repay with **virtue**,
Those who plan for difficulty must first embrace ease,
Those who achieve the great must first attend to the minute.
From *"Savor without savoring"* onward,
All are metaphors.
Difficult tasks must begin in ease,
Great deeds must arise from the minute—
This is merely elaborating the **Dao’s** intent.

**Master Liu says:**
Some say to Confucius:
*"You advocate repaying resentment with directness,
Yet now you say to repay it with **virtue**—why?"*
Indeed, **Laozi** teaches people
To dwell in gentleness and weakness,
To align with Heaven’s course,
And to avoid contention—
Thus averting Heaven’s punishments
And warding off human calamities.
If one repays resentment with directness,
How could resentment ever cease?
At that time, Confucius spoke in provocation,
Yet nothing surpasses repaying with **virtue** as good.

Thus the **Sage** never seeks to be great,
And so can achieve the great.

**Master Lü says:**
The **Sage** does not act in what is difficult or grand,
Yet does nothing and attends to nothing,
Simple and easy, simple and knowable—
Thus can he establish foundations that endure and grow grand.
The **Sage** cherishes **virtue** and **power**,
Knows himself without revealing himself,
Loves himself without esteeming himself,
Never seeks to be great.
Within the square inch, in the realm of true nature,
There is nothing he does not forget,
Nothing he does not possess—
Serene and boundless,
Yet all beauties follow him.
Thus it is said:
*"Therefore the **Sage** never seeks to be great,
And so can achieve the great."*

**Master Li says:**
When the **Sage’s** deeds are fully realized,
He does not claim greatness himself—
Heaven does not claim to be Heaven;
What makes Heaven is things.
The **Sage** does not claim to be the **Sage**;
What makes the **Sage** is the people.
Because he does not claim to be the **Sage**,
He can become the **Sage**.

**Master Lin says:**
The **Sage** does not claim greatness yet remains humble and lowly—
Thus he achieves the great.

He who makes light promises will lack trust,
He who takes many things lightly will face many difficulties.
Thus the **Sage** still regards them as difficult,
And so in the end faces no difficulty.

**Master Lü says:**
He who makes light promises lacks sincerity,
And reckless responses will lead to deceit.
He who takes many things lightly is negligent in affairs,
And reckless action will lead to difficulty.
Even the **Sage’s** talents find difficulty in minute, easy matters,
Let alone failing to dare act lightly in all things—
Thus, in the **Way**, he is diligent and perseveres,
And in the end faces no difficulty.

Light promises lead to few fulfillments,
Many easy things lead to many difficulties—
Thus it is said that first embracing difficulty leads to attainment,
For difficulty and ease complement each other.

**Master Li says:**
Difficult tasks are easy to complete,
Easy tasks are difficult to complete—
Easy tasks must not be taken lightly,
For if they are, people will neglect them,
Thus the **Sage** still regards them as difficult.
What is gained with difficulty is lost with difficulty—
If one does not act lightly in all things,
Then in the end there will be no difficulty.

**Master Lin says:**
Those who make light promises often overcommit,
And so must fail in trust.
Those who treat matters with a careless heart
Often fail to complete them—
This is also metaphorical language.
But by adding *"the man who"* [夫],
The meaning shifts again.
Earlier, it was said *"easy"* [易],
For fear people would view it with a lighthearted attitude,
Thus turning it to say:
*"Easy" is not "light and easy"—*
The **Sage** still treats it with a difficult mindset,
And so reaches a state of no difficulty—
Let alone others.

This intent means:
When earlier it spoke of *"easy,"*
It referred to **non-action** and **non-affairs** being easy to practice,
Not that *"easy"* means acting lightly.

**Master He says:**
Empty promises bring real disaster—
He who makes light promises will lack trust indeed.
Words spoken without reproach.
Many easy things lead to many difficulties—
The **Sage**, dwelling in **non-action** and savoring **non-flavor**,
Penetrating the **Way** beyond form,
Gives rise to **being** from **non-being**,
And difficulty and ease complement each other.
Adapting to things without being bound by them,
Yet able to flexibly complete all things without omission—
Thus it is said:
*"There is nothing he does not forget,
Nothing he does not possess."*
This reveals the **Sage’s** heart—
Neither **being** nor **non-being**.

**The Complete Compilation of the *Dao De Jing* (Volume Nine, *Xu Jiang Wei Da You* Collection) – Chapter 64**

Its ease is easy to maintain,
Its unseen signs are easy to plan against.
Its fragility is easy to shatter,
Its subtlety is easy to disperse.

Act before things arise,
Set things in order before they fall into disorder.

**Master Lü says:**
The ancient rulers of the world—
When governing, they faced disorder as their counterpart,
When securing peace, they faced peril as their counterpart.
When the state was at peace, it was easy to maintain,
But when it leaned toward danger, it became difficult to defend.
Thus the *Book of Zhou* says:
*"Establish governance before disorder arises,
Preserve the realm before danger manifests."*
For like a sprout not yet strong,
Its fragility is easy to shatter,
And remorse not yet settled,
Its subtleties are easy to disperse.

Take from oneself—
In the prime of youth, when the body is at ease,
It is easy to practice self-cultivation.
The rewards are seen swiftly,
For when sickness has not yet manifested,
One’s cultivation and refinement are easy to plan and achieve.
But when desires grow excessive,
Spirit weakens and vital essence is depleted,
Once illness strikes, it flees like passing waves.
If nurturing is done properly,
Qi harmonious and spirit undisturbed,
Even minor ailments are easy to treat.

Thus it is said:
*"Its ease is easy to maintain,
Its unseen signs are easy to plan against,
Its fragility is easy to shatter,
Its subtlety is easy to disperse."*
Moreover, the person yet to fall ill
Is easy to heal,
The state yet to face danger
Is easy to strategize for.
If one prepares defenses beforehand,
Then disasters not yet formed—
How could they take root?
If one makes plans in advance,
Then troubles not yet visible—
How could they arise?

Therefore, the **Sage** does not fear many difficulties,
But fears the absence of difficulty.
For calamity and fortune are inherently intertwined—
If one attends to their latent origins,
Calamity may turn into fortune.
But if one neglects them once they manifest,
Fortune may shift into calamity—
Beyond remedy.

Thus, the **Dao** of regulating the body and harmonizing the heart
Is to preemptively guard against emotions and desires,
To balance and adapt the spirit and qi,
And prevent them from falling into disorder.

**Master Li says:**
Referring to the meaning of the previous chapter,
*"Its ease is easy to maintain"*—
When undertaking deeds, the sincere seeker of **True Dao** (*Xiu Zhen Zhi Shi*)
Rectifies the heart before thoughts arise,
And restrains emotions before they are provoked by external things.
Then the heart is easily corrected,
And emotions easily severed.
But if one governs affairs after they have fallen into disorder,
And wards off disasters after they have already sprouted—
Is that not difficult?

**Master Lin says:**
When peace prevails,
Maintaining it is easy.
But once danger arises,
It becomes difficult to hold firm.
When troubles are yet unseen,
Planning against them is clear and easy.
But once they take form,
They become hard to counter.
Fragile yet not yet strong—
Attack it, and it is easy to shatter.
But once it grows firm,
It is hard to attack.
Thus, one must act before things arise,
And govern before they fall into disorder.

A tree thick enough to embrace,
Grows from a tiny sprout.
A nine-tiered tower,
Rises from a single basket of soil.
A journey of a thousand miles,
Begins with a single step.

**Master Lü says:**
If one does not preemptively guard against troubles,
Nor block evil at its roots,
It is like planting a tree.
When it first sprouts,
One may uproot and sever it—
How easy!
But once it reaches a thickness of a hundred feet,
How can one cut it down?

Again, if one builds a tower,
Starting with a single basket of soil,
One may kick it into place—
How difficult could that be?
But once it rises to nine tiers,
Towering a hundred feet high,
How can one demolish it?

Again, if one embarks on a distant journey,
Beginning with a single step,
One may turn back at once.
But once it stretches a thousand miles,
Endless and vast,
How can one return?

Thus it is said:
*"A tree thick enough to embrace,
Grows from a tiny sprout.
A nine-tiered tower,
Rises from a single basket of soil.
A journey of a thousand miles,
Begins with a single step."*

**Master Li says:**
Small things accumulate into greatness—
Beyond remedy.
Thus the *Yijing* warns:
*"Treading on frost, expect solid ice."*
The commentary states:
*"A subject slays his ruler,
A son slays his father—
This is not the work of a single morning or evening.
Their origins lie in gradual corruption,
From failing to discern early."* (*Yi Bian Zhi Bu Zao Ye*)

**Master Lin says:**
A tree thick enough to embrace,
Its growth must begin from a sprout.
A nine-tiered tower,
Its construction must start from a single basket of soil.
A journey of a thousand miles,
Must begin with the first step.
All these examples
Speak to the **Dao**-seeker’s need to understand the critical moment (*ji*).

He who acts will fail,
He who grasps will lose.
Thus the **Sage** practices **non-action** and avoids failure,
Lets go and avoids loss.

**Master Lü says:**
If one acts out of differentiation,
One disrupts **nature’s** spontaneity.
If one clings to desires and gains,
One loses **spirit’s** essence.
Better to rest in what is at ease,
And guard what is naturally held—
Then one governs without knowing one governs,
Acts without seeing one acts.

The **Sage** perceives where things fail—
From having acted.
If one does not act, how could there be failure?
Where intentions falter—
From having grasped.
If one does not grasp, how could there be loss?

If one governs the body through action,
Then form is strained and spirit taxed,
Mind and soul grow weary.
If one clings to things,
Then one becomes fixated in one corner,
Never departing from the myriad attachments,
Far from the **Way**.

The **Sage** has never acted nor grasped—
Pure and unadulterated **True Dao** (*Zhen Zhen*).
Thus, enduring ten thousand kalpas without failure,
No form to grasp—
How could there be loss?

**Master He says:**
The **Sage**, when undertaking deeds, attends to their beginnings,
And ensures both good beginnings and endings.
He never dares to act lightly,
Nor cling to what he sees.
For he harbors the subtle and profound,
Embraces simplicity and purity,
And does not dare stir up the contentious hearts of the world.
He follows **principle** (*li*) in his actions—
What does he do?
He aligns with the times in his deeds—
What does he grasp?
Names follow their own destinies,
Affairs unfold naturally—
How could he fail or lose?

The people, when they act,
Often falter just as things are nearing completion.
If one is cautious at the end as at the beginning,
Then there will be no failed affairs.

**Master Lü says:**
Though the world strives for good deeds,
None lack a beginning,
Few achieve an end.
All grow weary from the start,
Begin with joy but end in weariness,
Near completion yet then collapse—
This is because their resolve is not steadfast,
And thus **True Dao** does not solidify.
Vital essence leaks away,
Hence it is said:
*"Qi depletion, essence leakage—
This is not one’s own form."*

The **True Person** (*Zhìrén*) acts with undivided resolve,
From beginning to end, unchanging—
All spirits align of their own accord,
Who could bring him to ruin?

**Master He says:**
Common folk who seek to act
Deem diligence as incompetence,
Those who cling to rigidity
See adaptability as unprincipled.
Thus their affairs fail when nearly complete—
This warns against failing to *palindromically* (*shènzhōng-rúshǐ*) persevere,
Lest one begin with vigor but end in few achievements.

Hence the Sage
Desires without desiring,
Does not prize hard-to-obtain wealth,
Learns without learning,
Corrects the errors of the multitude,
To aid the natural unfolding of all things,
Yet dares not act.

**Master Lü says:**
The True Person is not without desire—
He desires what others do not,
Does not trade his nature for things,
So what need has he for rare treasures?
He is not without learning—
He learns what others do not,
Does not take deception as his mind.
All that the multitude errs in,
He returns to correct—
Thus it is said:
The Sage desires without desiring,
Does not prize hard-to-obtain wealth,
Learns without learning,
Corrects the multitude’s errors,
To reach the point of neither aiding life
Nor prolonging it,
Following things in their natural course,
With no room for private interference.

The **Dao** can be a parent,
**Virtue** (*dé*) a friend—
With **Dao** and **virtue** supporting his form,
In every matter, he acts spontaneously—
How could he dare to contrive?

**Master Wu says:**
The Sage’s desire is to *not desire* as desire,
His learning is to *not learn* as learning.
Hard-to-obtain wealth—
What others desire, he does not prize—
This is to not desire what is desirable,
Hence it is said: *"Desire not-desire."*
What the multitude pursues, he does not pursue;
What the multitude dismisses with a shrug, he returns to—
This is to not learn what others learn,
Hence it is said: *"Learn not-learning."*
All these—*not desiring, not learning*—
Are because the **principle** (*lǐ*) of all things
Is to act without action, naturally.
Thus I too act without action,
In harmony with all things, as spokes unite in a wheel.

**Master He says:**
This chapter first speaks of acting before action,
Then of those who act failing at completion,
And finally of daring not to act.
So is acting correct, or not acting?
Both are correct—
Is it not said:
*"Act without action,
And nothing remains ungoverned."*

**Master Lin says:**
What the multitude does not desire, the Sage desires;
What the multitude prizes, the Sage does not prize—
This is metaphorical speech.
What the multitude does not learn, the Sage learns;
What the multitude passes by without a glance, the Sage turns back to—
*Return* (*fǔ*) is also metaphorical speech.
The Sage, thus acting in all things,
Holds a heart of *daring not to act*,
Only then can he aid the natural unfolding of all things.

---
**Chapter 65**
The ancients who excelled in the **Dao**
Did not enlighten the people—
They sought to make them simple.
The people are hard to govern
Because their cleverness is excessive.

**Master Lü says:**
In ancient times, those who governed well
Gathered the people with **Dao**,
Opened their innate nature to the heavens,
Rectified their hearts and sincerely their intentions,
Seeking to guide the people to simplicity,
Their **virtue** and bearing overflowing,
All day like the unknowing,
So the people followed them their whole lives
Without knowing why.
Those who govern well with **Dao**
Do not display their brilliance to dazzle the vulgar,
In harmony with the unknowing,
Embracing simplicity alone.

**Master Wu says:**
In the ancient times, they governed without action,
The people were pure and unknowing.
In later times, they governed with action,
The people became artful and deceitful—
Hence their difficulty in governance.

**Master Lin says:**
Cleverness is a burden to the **Dao**.
The Sage’s teaching of others
Always seeks to obscure their cleverness,
Lest they burden themselves—
Hence it is said:
*"Not to enlighten the people,
But to make them simple."*
The more cunning and skillful the people,
The harder they are to govern—
Thus governing with cleverness
Becomes the ruin of the state.
Not governing with cleverness
Is the state’s blessing.
Know these two principles—
This is called **Mysterious Virtue** (*Xuándé*).
**Mysterious Virtue** is profound and distant,
Opposed to things—
Only then does one reach **Great Harmony** (*Dàshùn*).

**Master Lin says:**
Governing with cleverness
Becomes the ruin of the state—
For if superiors and inferiors all rely on cunning,
Chaos ensues, and treacherous ministers and rebellious sons
Seize power and rely on strength—
What more could there be?
This is not called governing with cleverness—what is?
If one governs the self with cleverness,
The spirits grow unsettled—
How could one not steal one’s **true qi**?

**Master Lü says:**
If the cleverness of bows, crossbows, and snares increases,
Birds grow disordered above.
If the cleverness of hooks, bait, and nets increases,
Fish grow disordered in the water.
If the cleverness of deceit, poison, and sophistic arguments—
Distinctions of *precedence, slipperiness, hardness, whiteness, impurity, sameness, and difference*—increases,
The vulgar are bewildered by debate.
If superiors govern with cleverness,
Then inferiors respond in kind—
Thus superiors and inferiors become entangled in chaos,
And treacherous ministers and rebellious sons
Seize power and rely on strength—
What more could there be?
This is not called governing with cleverness—what is?
Moreover, in ancient times,
Those who ruled nations
Used knots and markings on the ground—
Their governance was subtle,
Their people pure—
Without governance, they governed themselves,
Thus opening the people’s **innate nature** (*tiān*),
Yet still not as opening the **Heaven’s nature** (*tiān*).
This is not called not governing with cleverness—what is?
If one governs the self with cleverness,
The spirits grow unsettled—
How could one not steal one’s **true qi**?

---

Born without relying on cleverness,
This is called nourishing tranquility with cleverness.

When cleverness and tranquility mutually nourish each other,
Then the myriad spirits become balanced and harmonious,
And the ten thousand *qi* all blend in unity—
Who, if not those who forget the form and embrace the Dao,
Could attain this?

Thus it is said:
To govern a nation with cleverness is to ruin it,
To govern without cleverness is to bless it.
For those who employ cleverness corrupt governance and harm the people,
While those who do not employ cleverness cultivate governance and nourish the people—
These are the models and standards by which to measure.

Having known these models yet still not using cleverness,
To bring this people to wealth and longevity,
Following Heaven’s principles,
And aligning with Heaven’s virtue—
This is called knowing these two models.

To always know these models
Is called the **Mysterious Virtue**.

The **Mysterious Virtue** is profound beyond measure,
Distant beyond sight,
So subtle and refined that it cannot be detected.
Yet observe its application—it seems contrary to things,
But examine its principle—it converges with the same end as things,
Entering the realm of natural spontaneity without resistance.

The **Perfect One** stands in opposition to the world and its myriad affairs,
Yet returns to simplicity and purity,
Aligning with the Great Harmony—
Great Harmony means no obstruction.
Thus it is said:
The **Mysterious Virtue** is profound and distant,
Contrary to things,
Only then does it reach the **Great Harmony**—
The ultimate harmony.

○ **Master He** says:
The Sage does not govern cleverness with cleverness,
Nor does He wander with the world in the realm of contrivances and schemes,
But governs cleverness with simplicity,
Drawing it back to the Dao of ease and straightforwardness—
How could this not be so?
Thus it is said: *The Sage*.

If cleverness exists without the Dao,
Then the world falls into great chaos.
How do we know this is so?
When the cleverness of bows, crossbows, and snares abounds,
Then birds are disturbed in the sky.
When the cleverness of nets, traps, and hooks abounds,
Then fish are disturbed in the water.
When the cleverness of deceit, poison, and sophistry—
The debates of *jié* and *huá*, the distinctions of firm and white,
The arguments of impurity and purity, of same and different—abounds,
Then people are bewildered by discourse.
Thus the world often falls into great chaos,
And the fault lies in the love of cleverness—
This has been so since the Three Dynasties.

To govern a nation with cleverness is to harm the people,
To govern without cleverness is to bless them.
To know these two models is also a standard—
This is the fine law for those who rule.
To always uphold this fine law,
Then the virtue becomes profound, deep, and distant.

Thus it is said:
Though Heaven and Earth are vast, their transformation is uniform,
Though myriad things abound, their governance is one,
Though the people are countless, their ruler is singular.
The ruler originates from virtue and is completed by Heaven—
Thus it is said:
The ancient Sage who ruled the world did so with *wuwei*,
For his virtue was that of Heaven itself.

To stand contrary to things—
They rely on their cleverness,
I rely on the **Mysterious Virtue**,
They rely on harm,
I rely on blessing—
Opposite yet never conflicting.
Straight speech as if reversed,
This is the **Great Harmony**—
The ultimate harmony.
How could it lie in private scheming or cleverness,
In clashing with things like blades,
And yet be unable to stop?

○ **Master Li** says:
*Wuwei* is broad and generous,
Bringing peace to the world—
This is the people’s blessing.
*Youwei* is meticulous and strict,
Dominating the people—
This is the nation’s ruin.
The difficulty in governing the people lies in their many affairs,
Thus the Sage governs the nation with *wuwei* and tranquility,
So that the clever may dare not act.
Though contrary to things,
This reversal penetrates natural spontaneity—
The people follow their nature,
Reaping a hundredfold blessings,
The world attains peace,
And the **Great Harmony** is fulfilled.
To exhaust this Dao is to embody the **Mysterious Virtue**.

**Chapter Sixty-Six**
The reason the rivers and seas can be kings of the myriad valleys
Is that they excel in humility.
Thus they become kings of the myriad valleys.

○ **Master Lü** says:
The rivers and seas reign over the myriad valleys
Through humility and lowliness,
Through emptiness and receptivity,
Through *wuwei* and seeking nothing—
And the hundred rivers naturally flow to them.
From this, we see that humility as Dao is great indeed.
In the human body, the lower *dantian* at the navel is the *qihai* (Sea of Qi),
The origin of the five *zàng* and six *fǔ*’s vital breath,
The source of the thirteen thousand five hundred breaths,
The ancestor of the twelve meridians.
If one diligently guards the *qihai* without interruption,
Then the *qi* of the hundred passes naturally converges there.

○ **Master He** says:
The rivers and seas do not resist the smallest streams,
Thus they achieve their greatness,
Naturally embodying the majesty of ruling the myriad valleys—
Yet this greatness is not forced.
Water has no division of east and west,
Only division of high and low.
The sea resides below, and thus the myriad valleys naturally pay homage.
The Classic says:
*"The Dao in the world is like rivers and valleys to the seas."*
What Dao is this?
The Sage-King knows to embrace the yielding to guard the strong.

○ **Master Lin** says:
The waters of the myriad valleys all flow to the rivers and seas,
And though the rivers and seas are the exalted kings of the valleys,
They yet reside below the myriad valleys—
This is a metaphor borrowed from things,
Expressing the intent of humbling oneself to be exalted by others.
Thus the Sage, desiring to rise above others,
Must first speak in humility.
Desiring to lead others,
Must first place himself behind them.

「Thus, when the Sage stands above the people, they do not feel burdened;
When he leads before them, they do not feel harmed.
Hence, his virtue is pushed forward without ever being exhausted.

○ **Master Lü** says:
The Sage embodies the Way of humility and yielding,
And though he desires to stand above others,
He must first humble his words to them,
For he knows that arrogance invites loss.
Thus, his speech is modest and gentle,
His reputation is one of solitude and simplicity,
And by yielding to all things,
His virtue grows ever more exalted,
His great treasure ever more secure—
Naturally, others push him forward,
And he comes to stand above them.
When he wishes to lead all things,
He must first humble himself in speech and retreat in body,
Placing himself behind others.
For he carefully follows the Dao,
Never forcing himself before others,
Thus, he is pushed forward by all things
And comes to lead them.

Hence it is said:
*"When the Sage desires to stand above others,
He must first humble his words to them.
When he desires to lead others,
He must first place himself behind them.
Thus, he stands above others without burdening them—
And they follow him lightly.
He leads before others without harming them—
And they benefit greatly from him.
Not burdening, so not slighted;
Not harming, so not resisted.
Benefit and harm, lightness and burden—
These arise naturally.
How could there be room for scheming in his heart?
Thus, the world willingly embraces the Yellow Emperor’s
Clear and tranquil transformation,
And never tires of it—
For his humble and lowly virtue reaches far,
And the world joyfully pushes him forward without exhaustion.
The learned and noble naturally revere him,
Standing above others, leading them,
His brilliance concealed yet exalted—
A result of his virtue.
What exhaustion could there be?"*

○ **Master He** says:
The Sage’s heart is like the boundless void,
His Dao like the heavens and earth—
He awaits the world with gentle stillness.
What desires could he have?
To stand above others, to lead them—
He shows them what is worthy of imitation,
Speaking in accord with their nature.
What scheming could the Sage have?

People all seek blessings,
Yet he alone remains complete and unassuming—
Thus, when he stands above the people,
They do not feel burdened,
And those who press themselves below
Are exalted by others.
People all seek substance,
Yet he alone embraces emptiness—
Thus, when he leads before them,
They do not feel harmed,
And those who injure themselves by rushing forward
Are led by others.

He entrusts the small to contain the great,
His resolve weak yet his actions strong,
Ever wide and accommodating toward all things,
Never diminished by others.
What others naturally dislike and resent
Becomes their joyful endorsement—
No longer weary of his teaching.

○ **Master Wu** says:
*"To humble his words"* means to lower and bend his speech,
Not to exalt it.
*"To place himself behind"* means to retreat his body,
Not to advance it.
Yet these are all the Sage’s supreme virtues of modesty and yielding—
Not that he harbors any intent to stand above or lead others.
The reader must not let the words obscure the meaning.

○ **Master Lin** says:
The Sage does not first desire to stand above or lead others
And then act accordingly.
His intent is simply this:
Even the Sage, though he desires to stand above or lead others,
Still acts in this way—
How much more so others!
Because he does not contend,
The world cannot contend with him.

○ **Master Lü** says:
The Sage always treats his body as empty,
His heart as non-being.
Once his mind and form are settled,
Who could contend with him?
The *Classic* says:
*"You alone do not boast,
And thus none can contend with your ability.
You alone do not vaunt yourself,
And thus none can contend with your achievements."*
This is what is meant.

The Perfect One forgets his ears and eyes,
Discards his liver and gall,
Dissolves all attachments,
Erases all distinctions of self and world,
Harmonizes with the natural course,
And flows with all things—
Who then could contend with him?

○ **Master He** says:
Human hearts and the principles of the Dao
Can be moved by the Way, not by force.
Because he does not contend,
The world cannot contend with him.
What he upholds is sparse,
Yet those who submit to him are many—
This is the natural order.

Laozi once spoke to Wenzi:
*"The Sage who desires to be honored by others
Must first honor others.
The Sage who desires to humble others
Must first humble himself."*

**Chapter Sixty-Seven**
All under heaven say I am great—
Yet I seem unworthy.
Only because I am great
Do I seem unworthy.
If I were worthy,
Long ago I would have been insignificant.

○ **Master Lü** says:
The Dao is vast and formless, nameless,
Filling all things.
Though it exists, it seems as if it does not—
Its likeness to all things is nonexistent.
This is why it is great.
If it were to resemble any thing,
The Dao would itself be a thing—
And how could it then be great?

○ **Master Dong** says:
If it resembles anything,
It would be identical to one thing—
How could it then be great?

○ **Master He** says:
The Sage holds himself in profound virtue,
Not desiring to be seen as honored in the world.
Thus, the world regards him as boundlessly great,
Without resemblance to anything.

Only the great are indistinct and obscure—
No one can name them.
If they resemble anything,
They are merely petty in conduct.

○ **Master Lin** says:
One manuscript adds *"the Dao"* after *"I"* and *"also"* after *"insignificant"*—
Both are errors.

I have three treasures, which I hold fast.
The first is *compassion*,
The second is *frugality*,
The third is *not daring to lead the world*.

○ **Master Lü** says:
If a person embraces the Dao and governs himself
As one would hold a treasure,
He must surely cherish and guard it without loss.
The three practices he follows are:
To embody benevolence and widely bestow kindness,
Nurturing all living beings—this is *compassion*.
To use resources moderately and enrich others,
