Not exhausting what belongs to all—this is *frugality*.
To never initiate action,
Harmonizing without instigating,
Daring not to lead—this is *not daring to lead*.

The Perfect One who preserves these three treasures
Extends broad relief to all suffering,
Clothes and shelters all things—
This is the utmost of *compassion*.
Frugality in hearing and seeing preserves the spirit from agitation,
Frugality in speech preserves the *qi* from depletion,
Frugality in desires preserves the heart from disturbance—
This is the supreme treasure for nourishing life.
Able to place himself behind others,
His humility naturally elevates him—
Thus it is said:
*"I have three treasures, which I hold fast—
The first is compassion,
The second is frugality,
The third is not daring to lead the world."*

○ **Master He** says:
The Sage does not regard jade and pearls as treasures,
But treasures only the Dao—
Which Dao?
The Dao of *compassion, frugality,* and *not daring to lead*.»

「A common man brandishing his sword and glaring fiercely—
This is the way of one who delights in bravery,
The *qi* of petty men seeking self-aggrandizement,
Yet I treasure *compassion* as my jewel.

To barricade one’s door with small vessels and fill it with wealth—
This is the way of one who delights in abundance,
The shallow vision of petty men in their self-indulgence,
Yet I treasure frugality as my jewel.

To pursue fame and gain, to vie with others—
This is the way of one who delights in leading,
The boastful arrogance of petty men calling it skill,
Yet I treasure *not daring to lead* as my jewel.»

○ **Master Wu** says:
*"Preserve"* means to hold fast without loss,
*Compassion* means gentle kindness, mercy without rigidity,
Frugality means moderation in small matters, restraint without excess,
*Not daring to lead* means humility and retreat without reckless advance—
Thus, though great, he seems not to excel.
For *compassion* is why he can be brave,
Frugality is why he can be expansive,
*Not daring to lead* is why he can be a master of affairs.

Now if one abandons *compassion* for mere bravery,
Abandons frugality for mere expansiveness,
Abandons humility for mere advancement—
Then death is certain!

○ **Master Lü** says:
To know humility and gentleness as superior to rigidity,
To follow the natural order of sentient beings—this is *compassion*—
Thus one may speak of bravery.
To know moderation and frugality as noble,
To use them without depletion—this is frugality—
Thus one may speak of expansiveness.
To know placing others before oneself as the way to restrain oneself and return to propriety,
To give only to the virtuous—
Thus one may become a master of affairs.

For rigidity is easily broken,
But *compassion* and gentleness are invincible.
*Compassion* is bravery without rigidity,
Frugality cherishes vital *qi* and essence,
Preserving spirit and clarity without exhaustion,
Thus my Dao grows ever broader.
I never lead others,
So my virtue is joyfully extended without end,
And I become the sovereign of the Dao’s instruments.
Thus it is said:
*Compassion* is why he can be brave,
Frugality is why he can be expansive,
*Not daring to lead* is why he can be a master of affairs.

Yet the world today does otherwise:
They discard *compassion* and virtue,
Pursuing mere bravery and righteousness,
Draining their *qi* and risking their lives,
Unable to preserve frugality in their use,
Exalting luxury and excess,
Wasting resources and harming the people,
Only to invite ruin and scarcity,
Lacking the heart for humility and retreat,
Yet harboring the will for rigidity and strength,
Delighting in taking the lead,
Shaming to follow others—
These are not the arts of longevity and greatness,
But tools for self-destruction.

Thus it is said:
Now if one abandons *compassion* for mere bravery,
Abandons frugality for mere expansiveness,
Abandons humility for mere advancement—
Then death is certain!

○ **Master Lin** says:
*"Instrument"* is form,
To *"become a master of affairs"* is to *"take form"*—
All things that take form upon the earth,
I make them my masters.
Thus it is said: *"become a master of affairs."*

Now people discard *compassion* and use only their bravery,
Discard frugality and use only their expansiveness,
Discard humility and use only advancement—
This is not the way to preserve life,
Thus it is said: *"Then death is certain!"*

○ **Master Wu** says:
*"Abandon"* means to not use.
To not use the treasures of *compassion*, frugality, humility, and retreat,
To employ rigidity as bravery,
Excess as expansiveness,
Reckless advance as leadership—
Then one will not be able to preserve life,
But will become a follower of death.

For *compassion* in war brings victory,
In defense brings security.
When Heaven intends to aid him,
It shields him with *compassion.*

○ **Master Lü** says:
Even *compassionate* armies entering enemy lands
Are seen as protectors by the people,
Spared the crops and graves,
Not plundered nor burned—
Thus all rejoice in them.
Why must one array soldiers to fight,
And only then achieve victory?
Heavily barred doors and nightly guards
Are the ways of defense and security.

Thus the Dao of Heaven rewards the virtuous and punishes the licentious,
The virtuous are naturally shielded by Heaven,
Their hearts and wills opened,
So they may be *compassionate* toward all things,
And when *compassionate* toward all things,
The world protects them.
The Perfect One can defend *compassionate* bravery against the world’s entanglements,
Then no thousand demons dare approach,
And his Dao grows ever more secure.
For Heaven’s Dao knows no favorites,
It always aligns with the virtuous.
Thus it is said:
*Compassion* in war brings victory,
In defense brings security,
When Heaven intends to aid him,
It shields him with *compassion.*

○ **Master Lin** says:
*"War"* is interaction with things that stir the heart,
Just as *Zhuangzi* says:
*"To engage with others is to clash—
Daily, the heart struggles within itself."*
*"Defense"* is like *Zhuangzi*’s *"preserving pure* qi*"—
If a man takes *compassion* as his master,
Then externally he can conquer things,
Internally he can preserve himself.
The earlier words on the three treasures,
Here I expound upon one—
To be *compassionate* is to embrace the other two within.

○ **Master Wu** says:
*Compassion* is the Dao of life,
The virtue of *ren*,
The foremost of the three treasures,
Thus below this, I speak solely of *compassion*—
The other two treasures are contained within it.

*Compassion* is what all men naturally love,
Like a father and mother—
Who would have a son who opposes his parents,
Who attacks his parents?

Thus, to wage war with *compassion*
Is to make men unwilling to resist—
This is true victory.
To defend with *compassion*
Is to make men unwilling to assault—
This is true security.

Even if there are raiders who come to attack or plunder,
Those who aid their parents are many,
And so they will surely be victorious and secure.
Or if human strength is insufficient,
Heaven will also aid and rescue them,
Not allowing them to be defeated and scattered.
Heaven’s aid comes because they are able to be *compassionate* and rescue others.

○ **Master Li** says:
The single character *compassion*
In war brings victory,
In defense brings security—
*Ren* is invincible,
This is true bravery.

**Chapter 68:**
The good *scholar-official* does not rely on force,
The good warrior does not grow angry,
The good victor does not quarrel,
The good user of men places them below himself.

○ **Master He** says:
Gongyi Bo was renowned for his strength among the feudal lords,
King Xuan of Zhou invited him with elaborate rites to inquire of his prowess,
Observing his frail appearance.
The king asked: *"How is your strength?"*
Gongyi Bo replied: *"My strength can break the leg of a spring grasshopper,
Sever the wings of an autumn cicada."*
The king said: *"My strength can split the hide of a rhinoceros,
Drag the tail of an oxen of nine thousand pounds—
Yet I still find you weak.
If your strength is renowned throughout the world, what is it for?"*
Gongyi Bo replied: *"Noble is your question, my lord!
My teacher, Shangqiuzi, had strength without rival in the world,
Yet even his six relations did not know of it—
Because he never used his strength.
Now my name is known to the feudal lords,
This means I have gone against my teacher’s instructions,
And displayed my own abilities.
Yet my fame does not come from my strength itself,
But from my ability to use my strength wisely."*

「Thus, the skillful in serving as the lower party are not aggressive—
This principle is clearly demonstrated in practice.
In *Zhuangzi*: the Market-South Comilou played with balls,
Yet the two families’ feud remained unresolved;
Sun Shu’ao lay content with his jade staff and feathered fan,
Yet the people of Ying cast down their weapons.
These are those who do not rage yet excel in battle,
Who do not contend yet triumph in victory.

Han Xin untied Guangwu Jun’s bonds,
Facing east to acknowledge him as a teacher,
Seeking strategies for Yan and Qi—
This is the skillful use of others as the lower party.

The ancient adept at being a warrior,
Did not seek to be fierce and strong;
In battle, he did not yield to anger or rage,
Yet victory over the enemy came without clashing strength—
For the Dao/Way was preserved within him.
Thus, some say, *"I excel in forming arrays!"*
*"I excel in waging war!"*—
This is a grave offense.

When a sovereign cherishes virtue/power of benevolence,
The world finds no rival.

○ The Wu School says: In ancient times, chariot warfare was practiced,
Where a warrior (*shi*) meant three armored soldiers on a chariot—
The left wielded the bow, the right the spear,
The central one drove the chariot, controlling the flags and drums,
All desired to be strong and aggressive in battle.
Seventy-two foot soldiers served beneath the chariot,
And when war was imminent, their courage was roused,
Their will to contend with the enemy sharpened,
So they might take victory.

But the benevolent way of wielding armies
Does not regard this as skillful.

Warriors do not wish to be strong and aggressive,
In battle, they do not seek to overwhelm with rage—
Victory over the enemy does not depend on matching strength,
Weapons are not drawn—
The enemy will yield themselves.
This is the wisdom and ability of the ancients.

○ The Lin School says: The excellence of these four lies
In the art of non-contention.
This is called the virtue/power of non-contention,
This is called the strength of using others,
This is called the harmony with Heaven’s ancient principle.
The He School says: The virtue/power of non-contention
Is benevolence without reliance on force.
The strength of using others
Is bending collective strategy to collective force.
Harmony with Heaven’s ancient principle
Is that Heaven’s Dao/Way does not contend yet excels in victory,
And has endured since antiquity—
The Way reaches its utmost.

○ The Lin School says: Because of its virtue/power of non-contention,
It can harmonize with Heaven,
It can bend collective force,
And govern the world—
Since antiquity, none have surpassed it.
Thus it is called the ancient principle.

○ The Li School says: The virtue/power of non-contention,
The strength of using others,
Both are rooted in benevolence and mercy—
Thus it is called harmony with Heaven.
By cultivating oneself thus,
One’s form is preserved and life prolonged.

○ The Chai School says: Only by aligning with the Dao/Way
Of the ancient sages can one stand at Heaven’s utmost—
Thus it is called harmony with Heaven’s ancient principle.

**Chapter 69: On Wielding Armies, there is a saying:**
*"I dare not act as the aggressor, but only as the respondent.
I dare not advance an inch without retreating a foot."*

The He School says: *"I dare not act as the aggressor, but only as the respondent"*
Is what is called *responding armies*—
Thus, *"relieving chaos and punishing tyranny"* is called *righteous armies*,
And armies of righteousness are invincible.
When one rises not by choice but necessity,
This is called *responding armies*—
And armies that respond will prevail.

To fight over petty grievances,
To bear grudges and refuse to yield in anger—
This is called *armies of resentment*—
And armies of resentment will be defeated.

To covet another’s land and treasures—
This is called *greedy armies*—
And greedy armies will be broken.

To rely on the might of one’s state,
To boast of the multitude of one’s people,
And to wish to intimidate the enemy—
This is called *proud armies*—
And proud armies will perish.

These five principles Laozi once spoke to Wenzhongzi,
Now he says: *"I dare not act as the aggressor, but only as the respondent"*
Means the aggressor strikes first,
While the respondent answers afterward—
Not by choice.

*"I dare not advance an inch without retreating a foot"*
Means to advance when opportunity is seen,
To retreat when difficulty is known—
The utmost of caution,
Yet not cowardice.
To fear in battle yet succeed through careful planning.

○ The Lü School says: Those who spoke of military strategy in antiquity
Must have regarded the aggressor as the first to act,
And the respondent as the second to answer.
The aggressor creates the situation,
The respondent counters the enemy.

Moreover, the Sage’s armies are deep in strategy, subtle in deployment—
Though enemies come, we sit and watch their movements,
Adapting as they change,
Pressing only when forced,
Rising only when necessity demands.
If one acts lightly, this is called *reckless with the enemy*.

Generally, those who have intent to contend
May not necessarily win,
While those without intent to contend
May not necessarily lose—
Strategy and timing depend on circumstances.

The Perfect One responds to the world,
With one glance, one listening,
One word, one smile—
A thousand threads of interaction,
All called *magical illusions*.

The resolution of victory in battle
Lies in not daring to act as the aggressor first,
But maintaining a posture as the respondent,
Ever dwelling in stillness and composure,
With spirit unshaken—
Advancing an inch means meeting resistance,
Retreating a foot means avoiding contention.
Hence the saying: *"On wielding armies, there is a saying:
I dare not act as the aggressor, but only as the respondent,
I dare not advance an inch without retreating a foot."*

Thus, in movement there is no formation,
In repelling there are no arms,
In drawing there are no enemies,
In grasping there are no soldiers.

The He School says: The usual state of armies is
Movement has formation,
Repelling has arms,
Drawing has enemies to face,
Grasping has soldiers to hold—
Yet here all are absent,
As though without hands to strike or fists to raise,
As though the enemy passes by like a teacher on a pillow or bed,
Bending without battle—
This is why it is so.

*Eran* (扔), means to draw or lure—
As in repelling with arms yet *eraning* them to lure,
*Eran* and *cai* (才) share the same sound in ancient texts,
With meaning akin to *waving the enemy away*.

○ The Master of the River Above said:
If they persist in wrongdoing,
They become thieves to the world—
Yet even if I wish to execute them,
No proper formation can be made.
Though I wish to rage in anger,
It is as though I have no arms to repel.
Though I wish to draw and lure them,
It is as though there are no enemies to draw.
Though I wish to grasp and hold them,
It is as though there are no soldiers to seize.

○ The Lin School says: These are all feints of cowardice and weakness
To deceive the enemy’s calculations.
*Eran* means to draw,
To lure the enemy to the teacher’s forces.

Thus, wielding armies in this way
Is the path to true victory.

To act lightly against the enemy and boast in arrogance,
One is certain to meet ruin and defeat.

○ The original text by Wang Bi retains *eran* (扔).

No calamity is greater than reckless contempt for the enemy—
Reckless contempt nearly loses all one holds dear.

When armies clash in opposition,
The compassionate will prevail.

The He School says: Do not ask *"what harm?"*—
The peril is immense.
This is the warning against reckless contempt for the enemy,
The greatest of calamities.»

The Sage holds compassion as his treasure,
And Heaven will rescue him—
Guarding him with compassion.
Now, when facing the enemy and lightly wielding one’s blade,
To seek victory over the world—
This is to first destroy one’s own Dao/Way’s treasure.

Hence, when two armies meet,
The compassionate and humble will prevail.
Thus it is said: *"View your soldiers as beloved children,"*
Thus they can die together.
To wage war with compassion is to win—
A single word moves the three armies,
Like one who embraces a babe and sucks a boil,
Yet soldiers and their fathers fight to the death—
Who then would dare oppose?

This passage states that armies cannot be lightly used,
And yet not using them is their greatest application—
There is a way beyond mere military force.

○ The Wu School says: He who leads the three armies,
When confronted with battle, fears—
He does not dare to lightly scorn the enemy.
To lightly scorn the enemy is to lightly engage in war,
And thus to bring slaughter upon oneself while losing one’s compassionate treasure—
No calamity is greater.

Though war has not yet been entered,
Yet once the heart holds even a trace of contempt for the enemy,
There is the gradual risk of killing and losing one’s treasure—
Hence it is said: *"Nearly lose my treasure."*

*Ant* means to raise,
The compassionate reveal their Dao/Way’s virtue/power,
And Heaven and humanity aid them to victory.

○ The Su School says: When two enemies raise their armies against each other,
And I act only out of necessity,
Then I bear pity and mercy for the slain—
Compassion is revealed, and Heaven and humanity assist in my顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺顺

The Way is known by none, practiced by none.

Master He said: What is the Way? It is clarity and stillness, simplicity and ease above all. The Way is inherently clear, yet people muddy it; inherently still, yet people agitate it; inherently simple, yet people complicate it; inherently easy, yet people make it difficult.

The Mysterious Sage speaks: To me, it is most easy to know and to practice; to others, it is impossible to know or to do. The method differs not—it is simply a return to its origin.

To speak of strength and rigidity is difficult; to speak of gentleness and weakness, is it not easy? To speak of ornate adornment is difficult; to speak of plainness and simplicity, is it not easy? Yet because it is so easy to know, people fail to know it; because it is so easy to practice, people fail to do it—because it is too near.

Liezi could lift a single autumn hair and hear the thunder—these were the words of one who knew the Way.

Master Li said: The previous chapter spoke of the compassionate prevailing, meaning that benevolence is invincible. Thus, it follows with "most easy to know, most easy to practice," yet the world cannot know or practice it—truly, their faith is insufficient. Even those with faith cannot act—truly, their strength is insufficient.

Master Wu said: The teachings of Laozi instruct one only in gentleness, weakness, and humility. His words are most easy to know, his deeds most easy to practice. Yet as the age declines and customs corrupt, none in the world can know their worth, nor practice gentleness, weakness, and humility.

Words have their source, deeds have their master.
Only those without knowledge fail to recognize me.
Few know me, thus I am noble.

Master He said: Laozi’s two phrases—"words have their source, deeds have their master"—yet he repeatedly speaks of discarding words, of doing without doing, saying, "You have lost your origin." Though eloquence may abound, it is not as good as few words. This is so.

Hold fast to the essential, grasp the root—then you will know how to know, how to practice, how to make it easy.
There is nothing else: broad knowledge is difficult for the few; many revere this, and thus knowing becomes easy. Broad practice is difficult for the restless; many submit to this, and thus acting becomes easy.

Yet the teachings of the Way have three aspects: what is most easy is near; what is unknown is subtle; and what is without knowledge is forgotten. Thus, the Way, though easy to know, reaches the state of being unknown.

The Sage’s deeds are such: I transcend the realm of recognition and knowledge, who then can measure me?
Guan Yinzi could forget the Way only to possess the Way. He also said: If someone asks me, I do not even know myself—how could I know what they seek? This is without words, without deeds, an inner knowing that embraces the Mysterious.

I do not even know myself—how could others know me? If people can know the Way, what is there to value?
Thus, those who attain the Way fear being known by others, yet first fear knowing themselves. Self and others both forgotten—clouds and mist vanish together.

The most noble is the Way. The great sound is rare in voice—why seek knowledge at all?
Yet the difficulty and ease of knowing are not worth discussing.

Master Lin said: Among many words, there must be the supreme word, hence "words have their source." All deeds in the world have the Way as their master, hence "deeds have their master." Since the world lacks those who know the supreme word and the supreme Way, none recognize me, hence "only those without knowledge fail to recognize me."

Thus, the Sage wears coarse cloth while harboring jade.
Master He said: Liezi dwelled in the fields of Zheng for forty years, unrecognized. Even the king and ministers regarded him as common folk. The virtuous man’s noble bearing appears foolish—thus, the Sage is like one wearing coarse cloth while harboring jade: externally unassuming, yet internally possessing the most noble treasure. *Pò* (褐) is coarse hemp cloth, the garment of the lowly, hence "wide and ample."

Master Lü said: The Sage has no name; the Perfect Man has no self; the Divine Man has no brilliance. Though his robes are worn and his sandals threadbare, he nurtures his integrity beneath them. His heart is like jade and stone, accumulating the utmost treasure, concealing its radiance, never displaying it. He silently nurtures the embryo of the Way—thus, "the Sage wears coarse cloth while harboring jade."

Master Lin said: The Sage’s Way suffices within and does not manifest without—like wearing coarse cloth while harboring jade. Thus, people cannot see it.

**Chapter Seventy-One**
To know what is unknown—this is supreme.

「To not know what one knows—this is a malady.
Only by recognizing this malady as a malady can one avoid it.
The Sage does not suffer from this malady because he recognizes it as a malady, hence does not suffer from it.

Master Lin said: When one attains supreme knowledge yet behaves as if ignorant, this is the highest Dao.
When one presumes to know within the realm of the unknowable, this is the sickness of learning the Dao.
If one can recognize this presumption as a malady and treat it as such, then this malady will be cured.
The Sage’s way of not suffering from it is that he recognizes this presumption as a malady and treats it as such—this is the most wondrous chapter.

Master Li said: "To know what is unknown" is easy to understand when linked to the previous chapter: The Sage knows all things yet does not presume to know—this is true knowledge.
The common folk know nothing yet presume to know—this is false knowledge, hence "malady."
If one knows that false knowledge is a malady and treats it as such, then the malady of false knowledge will be cured.
The Sage does not suffer from this malady because he recognizes false knowledge as a malady and treats it as such—thus he does not suffer from it.

Master Lü said: How profound and wondrous is the Dao! It is vast and silent, deep and unfathomable—
It cannot be grasped by intellect, cannot be discerned by perception,
Cannot be measured or estimated, cannot be approximated or debated.
Knowledge is external to it; ignorance is internal to it.
Knowledge is shallow; ignorance is profound.
The Sage perceives the purity of *qi*—his inherent nature is lofty and bright.
Within, he harbors true knowledge; all things are naturally awakened.
Only then can he attain true ignorance, not boasting of himself—
This is called true knowledge and true perception,
For he knows the unknowing and the unperceiving.

Moreover, the common folk do not know the essence of the Dao,
Yet they know the coarse aspects of things.
They do not know the ultimate of the Dao,
Yet they know the trifling ends of things.
They are enslaved by schemes and deceived by phenomena,
Their minds filled with cunning, vainly seeking grandeur,
Yet they truly do not know the Dao.
They exhaust their spirit, strain their thoughts,
Using elaborate words to extinguish essence, using superficiality to drown the heart,
And forcefully argue and justify, claiming to know—
This is the malady of *virtue/power*.

The noble ones attain the Dao and clarify truth;
They possess it yet remain humble,
Sealing their lips and tongues,
Preserving and concealing it, never daring to reveal it.
They always act as if ignorant—how much more so when they do not know the Dao—
Yet they indulge in lofty, boastful debates.
Debaters are not virtuous; knowers are not profound; the profound are not knowers.

Master He said: The *Zhuangzi* states: "There have been those who knew through hearing, but never those who knew through ignorance."
Hence, deep knowledge without presumption is the highest in the Dao.
In the realm of the unknowable, to forcefully presume knowledge—
This is the malady of learning the Dao.
The *Zhuangzi* says: *Taicheng* asked *Wuqiong*, "Do you know the Dao?"
He replied, "I do not know."
Again, he asked *Wuwei*, "I know the Dao."
He asked *Wuji* (the beginningless), and he replied,
"I do not know its depths; knowledge is shallow.
I do not know its interior; knowledge is external."
*Taicheng* knew and sighed, saying:
"To not know is to know; to know is to not know—
Who knows the knowing of not knowing?"

**Chapter Seventy-Two**
When the people do not fear *威* (威), the greatest fear has arrived.

Master *He Shang Gong* (河上公) said: *威* (威) is harm; people cannot but fear small harm.
If they do not fear small harm, great harm will arrive.

Master Lü said: The *junzi* (君子) has three fears:
Fear of Heaven’s decree (*天命*),
Fear of great *virtue/power* (大人),
Fear of the Sage’s words.
If one embodies constancy and adapts to change,
Preserving the principles of life and death,
Then within, true essence is manifest and fills all without.
Anxiety and sorrow cannot penetrate it—
What the multitude fears, one cannot but fear.
The foolish do not know Heaven’s decree,
Seeking only grandeur and excess,
Bearing harm and relying on power,
Boasting of their virtue and ability.
The *Zhuangzi* (*Nanhua Jing*) says:
The great foolish never awaken;
The great confused never enlighten.
Do not say that minor evil causes no harm—
If one neglects it and does not repent,
Then when harm accumulates over time,
Even human *威* cannot be added,
And Heaven’s *威* will surely come.

Master He said: The ancients said,
"To fear *威* as one fears illness is the highest for the people;
To follow *威* as one follows a flowing current is the lowest for the people;
To see *威* and think of *威* is the middle for the people."
Thus, the human heart must never lack something to fear.
Confucius said: The *junzi* has three fears—
Fear of Heaven’s decree,
Fear of great *virtue/power* (大人),
Fear of the Sage’s words.
The petty do not fear Heaven’s decree,
They are familiar with great *virtue/power*,
And scorn the Sage’s words.
Great *virtue/power* refers to the sternness of rulers and parents,
As well as the great *virtue/power* of the worthy—
These are indeed worthy of fear, not to be trifled with.
The Sage’s words: to violate his precepts,
One cannot escape punishment.
Their bodies are indeed worthy of fear, not to be scorned.
Yet among the three fears, fear of Heaven’s decree is paramount.
Heaven’s decree is the natural principle bestowed upon all things by Heaven.
To fear it, every thought and word is under the gaze of Heaven and Earth, ghosts and gods—
None dare to play or neglect it.
The Dao is the shared source of all things; those who attain it live, those who lose it die.
The people receive the essence of Heaven and Earth to live,
The capable take blessings, the corrupt take calamity—
Thus, the *junzi* fears *yì* (义), the petty fear *xíng* (刑).
Both fear Heaven.
If one does not fear this *威*, then there is a greater *威*.
The *Yì* says: "If evil is not accumulated, it is not enough to destroy one’s life."
The petty presume minor evil causes no harm and do not abandon it,
So evil accumulates and cannot be concealed,
Guilt becomes great and cannot be absolved,
And they are punished to death—*凶* (凶), is this not the great *威* that comes from not fearing *威*?

Master Lin said: Those who do not fear *xíng* (刑) will often meet it.
Do not narrow one’s dwelling, do not weary of one’s birth—
Thus, one does not weary.
Master *He Shang Gong* said: The heart should dwell in the Divine (*神*), broad and gentle,
Not narrow and rigid.
The reason people are born is to possess spirit *qi*—
If one neglects its Dao, indulging only in its superficial manifestations,
This is called harming the root and wearying the Divine.»

Only those who do not weary of *spirit-qi* can wash their heart clean of defilement,
purify their mind of filth, and remain undisturbed by desire—
then *spirit-qi* abides within them without weariness.

Master *He* said:
When one dwells with the Dao, they can inhabit the boundless dwelling of the world,
so one must not narrow their own abode.
Otherwise, even a room will seem empty and void,
and discord will arise between wives and mothers-in-law.

When one is born through the Dao, they can nurture all that heaven and earth bring forth,
so one must not weary of their own birth.
Otherwise, the mind will lack celestial wandering,
and the six passions will vie for dominance.

Only those who take joy in the Dao and do not weary within themselves
will not be seen as weary by heaven above or by people below—
thus, none will weary of them.

Master *Lin* said:
*Residence* is the realm of boundless dwelling,
*birth* is the principle of enduring life and long vision.
All people narrow their own abode and weary of their own birth,
unable to rest in retreat while pursuing advancement,
unable to observe the void while bewildered by the manifest—
this is self-narrowing, self-wearying.

*The void* is a warning against such conduct, saying it must not be so.

Only those who do not weary can endure long and be at peace—
thus, it is said: *therefore, none weary.*

Master *Dong* said:
*Narrow* means confined, *weary* means to abandon.
*Wu* (无) should be read as *wu* (毋).
*Residence* is the foundation of nature—
to dwell in the world’s boundless abode is to merge with the Great Void.
*Birth* is the gathering of *qi*—
to contain the utmost essence of the Great Unity is to harmonize with the creative force.

Only the narrow-minded cannot be spoken to of the Dao.
If one does not drown in petty techniques
but instead embodies the void in reality,
they will abandon the manifest to cling to the void,
and be obscured by annihilation,
thus wearying of their own birth.
Therefore, one is told: *Do not narrow, do not weary,*
so that scholars may know that nature is the leader of *qi*,
its vastness boundless—
one must expand and fill it to preserve its greatness.

*Birth* is the Dao’s manifestation, its use inexhaustible—
one must cherish and nurture it to fulfill its purpose,
thus nurturing *qi* to perfect life,
exhausting nature to attain the Mandate,
and never harboring the mind of abandonment—
this is how one embodies strength without force,
and softness without weakness,
long life without greed for life,
and life not as something lowly.

Thus, one dwells with life and the Dao,
and the Dao never abandons them.

Therefore, the Sage knows themselves without revealing themselves,
loves themselves without esteeming themselves—
thus, they discard the former and take the latter.

Master *He Shang Gong* said:
*Self-knowing* is to discern one’s own gains and losses;
*not revealing oneself* is to conceal virtue and beauty within.
*Self-loving* is to cherish one’s body and preserve *jing-qi*;
*not esteeming oneself* is to reject high honor and fame in the world.
*Discarding the former* means discarding self-revelation and self-aggrandizement;
*taking the latter* means embracing self-knowledge and self-love.

Master *He* said:
The Sage who knows themselves does not dare obscure right and wrong, beauty and evil,
while self-revelation breeds the mind of self-sufficiency.
Self-love ensures one does not act lightly toward safety and danger, retreat and advance,
while self-responsibility stirs the arrogance of pride—
this is how the distinction between fear and non-fear is made.

Between the one and the other—what to discard, what to take—
how can one not know what to choose?

The *Collected Commentaries on the True Classic of the Dao* concludes here.

**Chapter Seventy-Third:**
*"Courageous in daring brings death; courageous in not daring brings life.
This pair—sometimes beneficial, sometimes harmful—
what the Heavenly detests, who knows its cause?
Thus the Sage finds it difficult.*

Master **Lin** said:
*Those courageous in daring will surely harm themselves;
hesitating before matters is courage in not daring.
Life comes from preserving oneself,
while daring brings harm and not daring brings benefit—
these two are clearly understood.
Heaven detests fullness and favors humility,
so the daring are not only reviled by men,
but also by Heaven.
Yet few perceive its cause,
hence the saying: "who knows its cause?"
The Sage finds it difficult because
this Dao is hard to grasp.*

Master **He** said:
*The overbearing will not meet their end,
those who love victory will surely meet their foe—
this is daring, the warning of death.
Hesitating before matters, planning carefully to succeed—
this is courage in not daring, the Way of life.
This is why the saying holds:
the noble will prosper, the petty will perish.
Yet these two—sometimes beneficial, sometimes harmful—
those who do good often meet fortune,
while those who do ill may sometimes escape harm.
From the first part, prosperity and misfortune
depend on good and evil,
like the interplay of light and shadow.
From the latter, calamity and blessing
seem not of our own making.
Ordinary minds thus conclude:
Heaven’s preferences and dislikes are inscrutable,
its causes are unfathomable,
its deeds are untrustworthy.
The Sage reveres Heaven’s command,
cultivates their virtue to await its unfolding,
never daring to apply ordinary reasoning lightly.
Only knowing Heaven does not aid the reckless,
but demands caution—
how could it lack cause?*

Master **Li** said:
*Continuing from the previous chapter:
the people do not fear authority,
so how can authority use death to frighten them?
If the people were always afraid of death,
and yet someone dared to act strangely and perversely,
I could seize and kill them—
who would dare?*
Master **Lin** said:
*This chapter explains that distinguishing good and evil,
preferring or rejecting by one’s own will—
all this stems from ignorance of the Dao,
so the ruler is likened to those who enforce punishment.
The ruler merely uses death to frighten the people,
yet the people never truly fear death.
If the people truly feared death,
then those who acted strangely and perversely
I could seize and punish—
then none would dare.
But now, the strange and perverse are killed,
yet the people’s offenses grow daily—
then how have the people ever feared death?*

Master **Wu** said:
*"Strange" means "unorthodox."
If the people were always afraid of death,
and yet the unorthodox and perverse still acted wickedly,
I could seize and kill them—
then who would dare?
But even if wicked men are killed,
those who dare to act wickedly do not cease—
this means the people are foolish and do not know to fear death.
Though those who do evil must surely face punishment,
they still fear nothing.
How can the ruler use death to frighten them,
yet casually take lives?*

Master **He of the Yellow River** said:
*Laozi laments that the kings of his age
do not first cultivate Dao and virtue to transform the people,
but instead rely on punishments.
Those who merely enforce punishments,
when they kill, are like carpenters replacing the master—
few escape harm to their own hands.*

**Chapter Seventy-Fourth:**
*"If the people do not fear death,
how can you threaten them with death?
If the people were always afraid of death,
and yet someone acted strangely and perversely,
I could seize and kill them—
who would dare?"*

Master **Lin** said:
*This chapter states that distinguishing good and evil,
preferring or rejecting by one’s own will—
even to the point of excess—
all this is not of the Dao.
Thus the ruler is likened to those who enforce punishment,
explaining that punishment is merely used to frighten the people with death,
yet the people never truly fear death.
If the people truly had fear of death,
then those who acted strangely and perversely
I could seize and punish—
then none would dare.
But now, the strange and perverse are killed,
yet the people’s offenses only multiply—
then how have the people ever feared death?*

Master **He of the Yellow River** said:
*Laozi grieves over the times when kings
do not first uphold Dao and virtue to transform the people,
but instead begin with punishments.
Those who merely take the place of the executioner,
when they kill, are like apprentices replacing the master carpenter—
few escape injury to their own hands.*

Lin Shi said:
*The killer is the Creator. Between Heaven and Earth, whether one does good or evil, there is always one who holds the authority over life and death. Those who should be killed are killed by the Creator itself, hence the saying that there is always one who holds the authority over killing. For a state to be overly concerned with punishment, it is as if one were substituting for the Creator in wielding the authority to kill. Just as a clumsy artisan replacing the master carpenter in cutting wood will rarely avoid injuring their own hands, this is a metaphor within a metaphor. This passage arises from the times’ excessive fondness for killing, yet its intent is not to entirely abolish punishment. Heaven’s retribution for guilt must simply be without malice.*

Wu Shi said:
*If one does not use killing to instill fear in the people, then should those who commit evil not be killed? The one who holds the authority to kill is Heaven itself. Only Heaven can kill, just as only the master carpenter can carve wood. For a man to presume to replace Heaven in killing is like one who is not a carpenter attempting to carve wood in the master’s place. Those who substitute for the carpenter will inevitably injure their hands, and by analogy, those who substitute for Heaven in killing will inevitably harm themselves. Without human calamity, there must be Heaven’s punishment.*

He Shi said:
*Though punishment may be wielded by one person, it is not the private domain of any single individual. There is always one who holds authority over punishment and killing. The ruler, in governing, should not presume to take this role upon themselves. Who, then, is the one who holds the authority to kill? Above, there is one who operates in the unseen—Heaven’s retribution for guilt, the Five Punishments, and their proper applications. Below, there is one who operates in the manifest—*as the *Shu* says: *"The officers regulate the people within the bounds of punishment."* The ruler, holding the reins in between, enforces punishment only when necessary. When such necessity arises, they uphold Heaven’s decrees and entrust the matter to the proper authorities, demonstrating fair governance to the people: *"The people have their own guilt; what concern is it of mine?"* Killing is the domain of Heaven, of the officers—not mine. Who would dare blame their ruler for this? If this is not followed, it defies Heaven’s order, misuses official punishment, and arrogates its authority as one’s own—just as if the master carpenter should step aside while another, bloodied and exhausted, takes their place in cutting wood. Would this not bring harm?*

*The ruler, with the heart of Heaven and humanity, desires goodness, and thus the people become good. Why then the need for killing? If a ruler governs thus, the world will shift toward virtue and away from guilt, all cherishing life and valuing death. They will welcome the great harmonizing *qi*, this being the unspoken intent of the Sage.*

**Chapter Seventy-Five**
*The people’s hunger arises from their rulers taking too much in taxes. Thus, they hunger. The people are difficult to govern because their rulers act excessively. Thus, they are difficult to govern.*

Lin Shi said:
*"Too much in taxes"* refers to the ruler taking excessively from the people. *"Their rulers act excessively"* means those in governance overuse schemes and stratagems. When the ruler is greedy for profit, the people grow hungrier. When the ruler favors cleverness, the people grow harder to govern.

Wu Shi said:
*"Food"* refers to what the ruler takes from the people, while *"taxes"* are what the people contribute to sustain the ruler’s consumption. If the ruler takes too much from the people, the people grow poor and hungry. If the ruler governs with schemes and stratagems, the people respond with deceit and trickery, making governance difficult.

Lü Shi said:
*The people are the foundation of the state. Of the Eight Governments, food is the priority. Though the discussion of food may seem trivial, the Minister of Agriculture oversees it as a matter of great consequence. If this is mismanaged, harm to the people will be severe. The Sage allotted land, exhorted officials, and ensured they did not disrupt the seasons. Taxation was regulated to avoid overburdening the people. The *Nine-Tenths* law was relaxed, and the *Tenth* tax was established—prioritizing what was lightest. If, however, the ruler disregards the people’s proper seasons, drains their strength with extraordinary demands to satisfy insatiable desires, the people will suffer under the crushing weight of taxes. Exhausted and emaciated, they will show signs of famine, the wilds will echo with the cries of the starving, bandits and thieves will multiply, and deceit will flourish unseen within the state. The body relies on *qi* for the people’s vitality; when *qi* is depleted, the spirit weakens. Now, speaking of their hunger is to say their *qi* is insufficient. This is why they cannot abandon speech and desire—hence, *"The people’s hunger arises from their rulers taking too much in taxes. Thus, they hunger."* In ancient times, the people lived in peace, content with their customs, their fields yielding enough to eat, their weaving enough to wear. Who would say they were difficult to govern? But if the ruler acts with excess, desire, and interference—imposing dense nets of laws, harsh decrees, and convoluted governance—they will only agitate the people, who cannot endure such disturbance. Thus, they are difficult to govern. It is like one’s own breath: difficult to purify but easy to foul, easy to coarsen but difficult to refine. How much more so when the ruler’s actions agitate it further?*

*The people’s indifference to death arises from their excessive pursuit of life. Only those who do not cling to life are wiser than those who prize it.*

Lin Shi said:
*Generally, those who love themselves too much risk losing their lives. Overindulgence in food and drink can bring illness—this is one example. Overvaluing self-preservation and excessive care for life lead to reckless schemes to secure it, hence *"the excessive pursuit of life."* They risk their lives in pursuit of it, hence *"indifferent to death."* To forget the self is to preserve the self, hence *"those who do not cling to life are wiser than those who prize it."* To prize life is akin to the previous chapter’s *"increasing life"*—the excessive pursuit of life. *"Prize"* here means *"excel."**

Wu Shi said:
*"Indifferent"* means *"easy."* *"The excessive pursuit of life"* refers to an overattachment to living. *"Wiser"* means *"excel."* To prize life is to hold it in high regard, the same as the excessive pursuit of life. When one’s attachment to life is heavy and self-preservation is overdone, they may seek not to die yet instead make death easy. The perfected do not fail to cherish life, but they follow nature, harboring no contrivances. If one does not cling to life, their body remains unharmed—this excels over those who prize life with their hearts, yet paradoxically make death easy.

He Shi said:
*"Those who do not cling to life are wiser than those who prize it."* This is what is meant by *"harmonizing with the Creator"* and *"wandering in the unified qi of Heaven and Earth."* They forget their liver and gallbladder, abandon their ears and eyes, reversing and returning to the beginning and end without knowing where to start or stop. Lost in the vastness beyond dust and filth, they drift effortlessly in the realm of *wuwei*, unharmed by things. This surpasses those who excessively cherish life—through this, all under Heaven preserve their lives. Is this not wondrous?

**Chapter Seventy-Six**
*"The living are soft and weak; the dead are rigid and strong.*
*All things—grasses and trees—are soft and brittle when alive; when dead, they wither and decay."*

Lu Shi said:
*"The Dao is the master of the spirit; the spirit is the master of qi; qi is the master of the body. When the Dao abides, the spirit abides; when the spirit abides, qi abides. Thus, when a person is born, they contain the primordial *yuanhe* qi and embrace the true unity of essence. Their form is whole, their spirit flourishes, and the myriad *jing* converge at the origin, flowing and ceasing not. Hence, their softness and weakness are evident.*

*When they die, the *yuanhe* qi disperses, the true unity of essence is exhausted, the form decays, and the spirit departs. Then the hundred limbs grow rigid and strong. Hence it is said: ‘The living are soft and weak; the dead are rigid and strong.’*

*Consider this: when grasses and trees are born, the qi of Heaven and Earth flows within them, and the yin-yang qi nourishes them without. When spring qi stirs, the nourishing rain falls abundantly, and sprouts unfold in lush splendor. Yet when frost and wind blow, killing qi follows, and they wither, their branches and twigs decaying.*

*The enlightened see life and death as the abiding or fading of the Dao; the unenlightened see vitality and decay as the gathering or scattering of qi. Hence it is said: ‘All things—grasses and trees—are soft and brittle when alive; when dead, they wither and decay.’"*

Wu Shi said:
*"When a person lives, their flesh and skin are soft and supple, capable of bending and stretching. When dead, their flesh and skin grow cold and rigid, unable to bend or stretch. Grasses and trees, when alive, have pliable stems; when dead, their stems wither and harden."*

Lin Shi said:
*"Soft and weak, rigid and strong—these are metaphors. Laozi’s teaching emphasizes valuing softness, thus using the birth and death of humans and plants as illustrations. Therefore, those who are rigid and strong are companions of death, while those who are soft and weak are companions of life."*

Lu Shi said:
*"Where yang qi resides, even wood can be bent and grass bound. Where yang qi departs, even water can congeal and ice shatter. Thus, the luminous yang qi is the root of life. Softness and weakness are the sprouts of things. Those who seek rigidity must guard it with softness; those who seek strength must preserve it with weakness. Now, rigidity cannot nurture harmony—it opposes things. How could it avoid harm? Softness and weakness do not lose the mean—they move with things, thus preserving life."*

He Shi said:
*"The soft and weak of the world—the Dao and qi—are what permeate Heaven and Earth and embrace all things. Thus, those who align with life must have the Dao and qi within them; those who align with death must have the Dao and qi depart from them."*

Lin Shi said:
*"Companions—here meaning ‘kindred.’"*

Wu Shi said:
*"The earlier text speaks of the forms of humans and plants—when alive, they are soft; when dead, they are rigid. Extending this principle, one sees that those whose conduct is rigid and strong do not attain death properly—they are companions of death. Those who are soft and weak preserve life well—they are companions of life. Hence, strong armies do not conquer, and strong trees are felled. The strong and mighty are placed below; the soft and weak are placed above."*

Lu Shi said:
*"Arms are instruments of violence and conflict, clashing with opposing forces. Relying on strength and despising the enemy is unrighteous. To act unrighteously and yet presume victory—how could it succeed? Thus it is said: ‘Strong arms do not conquer.’ Small trees are soft and weak, but when they grow strong, they become thick enough to encircle with both hands. If wood is thus, how much more so for humans? How much more so for nations? Hence it is said: ‘Strong trees are felled.’ The strong and mighty are bold warriors; the soft and weak embody the Way of the gentleman. Those who rely on force see strength as victory; those who rely on virtue see softness as victory. Thus, there is an eternal victorious Way called softness, and an eternal defeated Way called rigidity. Alas, the world’s people rely on force, not virtue, forgetting the root and chasing the branches. The enlightened observe this with the Dao, and thus know that softness overcomes rigidity—this is the necessary truth."*

He Shi said:
*"Sharp teeth break; the soft tongue survives—this is the way. ‘Felled’ as in encircling a tree with both hands—things grow old when they reach their peak; this is called *not the Dao*. Why do strong arms fail to conquer? Because they rely on the might of their nation, boast in their people’s numbers, and seek to intimidate enemy states—these are *proud armies*, and proud armies perish. The strong and mighty are placed below; the soft and weak are placed above—not merely an analogy with trees, but the application of softness to govern the world, which is the Way of the sovereign. To arrogantly exalt oneself is but the shortsightedness of a well-frog. Can those in high station not take heed? It is not only Daoists who uphold humility and weakness."*

Lin Shi said:
*"Those who rely on the strength of arms will not conquer. Young trees are soft and weak; when they grow old and rigid, they reach a thickness where they can be encircled with both hands—then they wither. Thus, those who understand the Dao value softness and weakness as superior, and rigidity and strength as inferior."*

Wu Shi said:
*"‘Felled’—the encircling of a tree with both hands."*

Dong Shi said:
*"This chapter speaks of how softness and weakness preserve the harmonious qi."*

**Chapter Seventy-Seven**
*"The Way of Heaven is like drawing a bow: the high are lowered, the low are raised, the excessive are diminished, the deficient are supplemented."*

「Lü Shi said:
*"The Dao of Heaven follows the Way of Dao, sharing its function: it accomplishes without action, transforms without speech. The four seasons rely on it to unfold, and all things await it to flourish. It never strays from its course, yet never fails in its impartial, upright principle. Hence, it is said: ‘The Way of Heaven is like drawing a bow.’*

*Now, the artisan who crafts a bow—without slaying or sparing, without favor or coercion—shapes it by compass and square, guided by true measure. If the string is too high or too taut, he relaxes and softens it; if too low or slack, he draws and tightens it. What is in excess is trimmed away, what is lacking is filled in, so that the bow may be bent and the arrows may fly.*

*The ruler’s Way should emulate Heaven’s: to suppress the strong and support the weak, to diminish excess and supplement deficiency, to loosen and tighten, to refine and temper—so that every stroke finds its mark, and precision is the Dao. To balance gain and loss, to apply measures justly, to act as outer and inner counterpart—this aligns with Heaven’s function.*

*Heaven’s principle is grasped in the heart and executed by the hand, hence the saying: ‘The high are lowered, the low are raised, the excessive are diminished, the deficient are supplemented.’*

○ *Li Shi said:*
*"Continuing the previous chapter’s meaning of strength and weakness—diminishing excess, supplementing deficiency—that is the Way of Heaven.*
*Those who are strong and great must be diminished; those who are weak must be benefited."*

○ *Dong Shi said:*
*"Heaven’s Way is without partiality, all must conform to moderation.*
*Heaven’s Way diminishes excess and supplements deficiency. But human ways are otherwise: we diminish deficiency to serve excess."*

○ *Lü Shi said:*
*"To act without doing yet hold in esteem—that is Heaven’s Way. To have doing yet remain humble—that is humanity’s Way.*
*Heaven’s Way is to govern through transformation; humanity’s is to administer through ritual. Heaven, when in excess, diminishes; when lacking, bestows. This cannot be grasped by those with intent, nor approached by those without intent—it responds naturally and acts in silence.*

*But humanity is not so. Our nature strays from balance, our emotions are divided; we prefer the high and despise the low, serve the full and discard the empty, unable to align with Heaven’s principle of diminishing and supplementing. Thus, we are far from the Dao."*

○ *Lin Shi said:*
*"Heaven, in its treatment of all things, is ever thus.*
*Yet why does humanity’s Way differ? It seeks to diminish others while benefiting itself, to take from the world’s deficiency to fill its own excess—this is contrary to Heaven’s intent.*
*Who can sacrifice their own excess to nourish the world? Only those who embody the Dao."*

○ *Lü Shi said:*
*"Moreover, excess cannot be left untrimmed, deficiency cannot be left unsupplemented.*
*Thus, those who embody the Dao do not only love their own kin nor only cherish their own children—they love the world’s children as their own. In their regard for others, they are heavy; in their regard for themselves, they are light. Who but the sages could achieve this?*
*Hence the saying: ‘Who can sacrifice their own excess to nourish the world? Only those who embody the Dao.’"*

○ *Lin Shi said:*
*"Only those who embody the Dao can diminish my excess to nourish the world."*

○ *Li Shi said:*
*"Only those who embody the Dao act in accordance with Heaven, diminishing artificiality while benefiting true nature, diminishing abundance while supplementing scarcity, diminishing self while benefiting others, diminishing excess and supplementing deficiency—diminishing again and again until it reaches the state of non-action.*
*Hence, the sage acts yet does not rely on his deeds, achieves yet does not claim his accomplishments, and does not desire to be seen as virtuous. He does not boast of his own excellence or seek self-satisfaction; though his deeds are done and his affairs settled, he does not dwell upon them. He does not wish for the world to know his virtue—if his virtue were manifest to all, then he would be esteeming himself, which is not the way of the Dao."*

○ *Lü Shi said:*
*"The sage’s wisdom is all-encompassing, his mind adapts to all things. Though he benefits all things, he does not claim righteousness; though his grace extends to all ages, he does not claim benevolence. He has no intent to praise himself, acting yet not boasting of his skill, nor seeking self-fulfillment. When his deeds are accomplished and his affairs settled, he does not dwell upon them—he does not wish for others to know his virtue. If his virtue were made known to the world, then he would be esteeming himself, which is not the way of the Dao."*

○ *Lin Shi said:*
*"Though the sage acts in the world, he does not rely on his deeds; though his accomplishments are realized, he does not claim them; though his conduct is the most virtuous, he does not wish to be seen for it. This is the Dao’s principle of continual diminishment—until one diminishes even the act of diminishment."*

○ *Wu Shi said:*
*"The sage’s achievements can cover the world—this is his excess. Yet he does not rely on his own ability, as though he had none; he does not claim his own accomplishments, as though he had none; he does not desire to display his ability as superior to others. All of these are acts of diminishing his own excess."*

**Chapter Seventy-Eight**
*"In the world, there is none softer or weaker than water, yet none better at overcoming hardness and strength—nothing can replace it."*

○ *Lü Shi said:*
*"The things of the world vary, yet none surpasses water in its extreme softness and weakness. It levels mountains, erodes stone, clears blockages, overcomes hardness and strength, benefiting all things and entering all things. It follows the dictates of man—whether confined or released—and adapts to the shape of its vessel, whether square or round. It transforms into vapor, into clouds and rain, into frost and snow. Hence, Heaven first generates water, from which springs the ceaseless flow of sources and streams, vast and boundless, though winding a thousand times or turning a million times, it never ceases to be water. This is the embodiment of the marvelous unity of the harmonious qi.*

*Yet in its application to all things, water can be strong or weak, rigid or soft—thus it can overcome all things, approaching the Dao, and nothing can replace it.*

*In the body, there is none softer or weaker than the essence of Hua Tu (华池灵液). The perfected one circulates and directs it, reaching every place. Sometimes they rinse and swallow it to irrigate the five viscera; sometimes they manipulate and transport it to reach the three pass points. Even to the point of permeating the lower back’s aperture (透尾闾), passing the spine’s interstices (过夹脊), flowing upward against the current (泝流直上), and supplementing the brain (而补於脑)—none but the divine water could achieve this."*

○ *He Shi said:*
*"Water can be directed east or west to flow. In the world, there is none more supremely soft and weak than water. It erodes the stone of Tai Mountain (穿太山之石), breaches a thousand-golden floodbank (决千金之堤), and overcomes hardness and strength—this is an unalterable truth from ancient to modern times."*

「○ Lin Shi said: Water is supremely weak yet can overcome hardness and strength—none in the world can rival it. The thousand-golden floodbank (千金之堤) is undone by the leak of an ant’s burrow (蚁穴之漏), thus proving that weakness can triumph over strength, and none can surpass water in this.

Weakness triumphing over strength, softness overcoming hardness—none in the world does not know this, yet none can practice it.

○ Lü Shi said: Water extinguishes fire, yin subdues yang; the tongue is soft yet the teeth are hard, yet when the tongue survives, the teeth perish. None in the world does not know this, yet it is a common saying among the vulgar.

The Dao/Way of Heaven does not contend with things, yet thus overcomes them. Common folk always desire to contend with things, yet few can overcome them.

Indeed, those who know yet cannot practice it cannot achieve their aims in the world; those who know and can practice it may achieve their aims in the world.

○ He Shi said: In the *Liezǐ* (《列子》), it is said that there are unchanging ways of victory and unchanging ways of defeat. The unchanging way of victory is called *softness* (柔), the unchanging way of defeat is called *strength* (强). These two principles are easy to know, yet people do not recognize them. Master Huangshi (黄石公) instructed Zhifang (子房), yet only spoke of *softness overcoming hardness* (柔胜刚) and *weakness overcoming strength* (弱胜强). This is a principle easy to know yet not understood, and even those who know it cannot practice it—only the sage can know and practice it.

○ Lin Shi said: Weakness overcoming strength, softness overcoming hardness—like water, this is plainly visible, yet none in the world can practice it. The supreme Dao/Way lies in those who can be soft and weak, yet none can practice this.

○ Li Shi said: Softness overcomes hardness, weakness overcomes strength—all know this yet none can practice it. Why? Because they refuse to humble themselves. Hence, the sage says:

*"To bear the filth of the state (受国之垢) is to be called its sovereign (社稷主).
To bear the misfortunes of the state (受国之不祥) is to be called the King of the world (天下王).
To speak straight yet reverse the meaning (正言若反)."*

○ Lü Shi said: The sage’s virtue aligns with Heaven and Earth. Heaven and Earth are supremely vast, encompassing all things great and small, coarse and fine, and their likes and dislikes. The sage is supremely exalted, and all—wise and foolish, noble and ignoble—turn to him for support.

Again, the sage can endure filth and conceal it, tolerate insults and embrace chaos, thus aiding the Dao/Way of *ren* (仁) and *yi* (义), of softness and weakness overcoming strength. Then the people revere his virtue and do not scatter. He can perpetually serve as the sovereign of the state (社稷主).

The sage practices humility and submission, following Heaven’s course. Wars, calamities, droughts, and locust plagues are all the fate of *effort* (劫运), which Heaven sometimes uses to admonish people. If the sage does not lay blame upon others but instead takes it upon himself, then the Four Seas will return to *ren* (仁), and the Six Directions will find their home in his heart. Thus, he can become the King of the world (天下王).

Hence it is said: *"To bear the misfortunes of the state (受国不祥) is to be called the King of the world (天下王)."*

The vulgar regard bearing filth and misfortunes as something to be reviled, yet the sage regards them as something to be rejoiced in. Rejoicing in what others revile may not necessarily bring fortune; reviling what others rejoice in may not necessarily bring disaster.

To speak straight yet reverse the meaning (正言若反)—this is because the vulgar conceal their own faults while exposing others’, whereas the sage acknowledges his own faults while concealing others’. Thus, their words diverge; only the sage’s words are truly upright, hence *"to speak straight yet reverse the meaning."*

○ He Shi said: The term *"orphans, widows, and the destitute"* (孤寡不谷) is used by kings and nobles, all of which reflect the principle of *softness overcoming hardness* (柔胜刚). This is the true principle of the world, yet people do not know or practice it. When they hear the sage speak of *softness overcoming hardness*, they think it contradictory, yet it is truly the correct teaching.

○ Dong Shi said: This chapter states that softness and weakness, when able to contain, become what all things turn to. The seventy-ninth chapter says: *"To reconcile great grievances (和大怨) must leave some remnant grievance; how can this be called a good Dao/Way?"*

○ Lin Shi said: Only when one forgets both恩 (恩) and怨 (grievance) does one know the supreme Dao/Way. If a person harbors great怨 (grievance) against me yet I still desire to reconcile it, though I have no lingering resentment, I still recognize that it is a grievance. Then this heart is not yet transformed.

Though it is said that I can reconcile it, my heart is not yet transformed, so the remnant grievance remains. How can this be called a good Dao/Way? This is indeed a profound statement, yet it is also an analogy.

○ Lü Shi said: Grievances and enmities have already run deep, and the chasm between enemies is already vast. Even if one ultimately concedes what is right and wrong, affirming what is wrong and denying what is right, repays them with virtue, and straightens them with righteousness, how could there ever be no lingering regret?

It is better to forget both恩 (恩) and怨 (grievance), and let the Dao/Way reconcile and transform them. Then the grieved will not need reconciliation before they are already reconciled.

Biqiuzi (碧虚子) said that knowing of a grievance yet reconciling it is not as good as having no grievance to reconcile. If one reconciles and repays grievances with laws and governance, how can this be called a good Dao/Way?

○ Li Shi said: Building upon the previous chapter’s principle of *hardness and softness*, to reconcile with恩 (恩), though grievances may be resolved, the heart remains unchanged. How can this be called a good Dao/Way? It is better to blame oneself and forget both恩 (恩) and怨 (grievance). When恩 (恩) and怨 (grievance) are both forgotten, the people will naturally feel gratitude and respond with loyalty.

Hence, the sage holds the *left contract* (执左契) yet does not demand repayment from others.

○ Lin Shi said: The *left contract* (左契) is like the written agreements of today. One party holds the left half, the other holds the right half, hence the term *left contract*.

If this contract is held by me, then the other party must fulfill their obligations. Though the sage holds this contract, he does not demand repayment from others, but instead forgets and transforms.

○ Lü Shi said: In ancient times, people tied knots in ropes to make agreements, yet the people did not deceive. They split wooden tablets to make contracts, yet the people did not violate them. Thus, the sage harmonized human Dao/Way with Heaven’s Dao/Way, and human heart with Heaven’s heart. Hence, holding the contract on the left (执左契) signifies agreement, and combining it on the right (合之于右) signifies trust. Not demanding repayment from others also corresponds with Heaven’s will. Here, there is no blame; there, there is no fault. Above and below are in harmony, and there is no blame for grievances. Hence it is said: *"The sage holds the left contract yet does not demand repayment from others."*

○ Wu Shi said: *"The sage holds the left contract yet does not demand repayment from others"* means he has no expectations of things.

The *contract* (契) is a wooden tablet split in two, with each party keeping one half to demonstrate sincerity. When one demands repayment of money or goods from another, it is called *responsibility* (责). The contract has left and right halves: the left half is held by the party who gives the money or goods, and the right half is given to the party who comes to take them. Master Lin of Lichuan (临川王氏) said: The *Shiji* (《史记》) states: *"He held the left contract to demand repayment"* (操左契以责事), and the *Liji* (《礼记》) states: *"Those who offer land and residences hold the left contract"* (献田宅者操左契). Thus, it is known that the left half is held by the party who receives responsibility.»

Thus, the virtuous govern through trust, while the unvirtuous demand clarity.

The Dao/Way of Heaven favors none, yet always aids the good.
Master Lin said: The virtuous hold the contract, yet seek nothing in return. The unvirtuous, however, demand only clarity.
*Simply* (彻) means to make clear.
It is like when people today say, *"Let us settle this clearly"* (必与之讨分晓).

Those who govern through virtue are the good—Heaven, without favor, aids such people, hence it is said: *"Heaven always aids the good."*

Master He said: The contract is a binding agreement, like written covenants or pacts.
The virtuous gain the Dao/Way and many allies—like the contract held within, merging without demand. The unvirtuous lose the Dao/Way and have few allies—even if they forcefully demand clarity from others, who would answer them? *Zhuangzi* despised those who sought to force connections—this is the case.

If one truly possesses virtue and morality, why would Heaven alone not aid them? The august Heaven favors none, only virtue does it support. Thus it is said: *"I do not know those who do good, nor those who do evil."* Accumulated goodness attracts benevolent qi; accumulated evil attracts malevolent qi. Accumulated goodness summons divine assistance—Heaven’s Dao/Way naturally aids the good.

Master Li said: The contract is a pledge of trust; *simply* (彻) means to make clear.
The virtuous hold their pledges, while the unvirtuous demand only clarity—knowing only to judge by law, yet unaware that such strictness would leave no people. Those who govern through virtue preserve sincerity, aligning with the people’s hearts.

Master Lü said: The *Classic of Documents* states: *"The august Heaven is impartial, aiding only virtue."* The *I Ching* says: *"Heaven aids them—auspicious, with no misfortune."* Heaven’s Dao/Way never distinguishes between giving or taking; doing good generates benevolent qi, doing evil generates malevolent qi—naturally corresponding.
For the supremely virtuous, harmony arises naturally, favoring only the good—why demand contracts or responsibility from others? This is the way of the ancient past.

**Chapter Eighty: Small States, Few People**
In small states with few people, let there be weapons numbering in tens and hundreds, yet unused. Let the people value life so deeply that they do not wander far.

Master Lin said: Small states with few people—like Mencius’ saying that even a hundred *li* of land could be used to gather lords and unify the world. Laozi meant that those who embody the Dao/Way, if granted even a small state and few people, would govern them simply. Let there be *shí-bǎi* (什伯) formations—like today’s neighborhood watches—where each person has usable skills, yet does not seek personal gain. Thus, all act like gentlemen, content in retreat, valuing life so deeply that they do not wander far. The common people fear doing wrong and remain settled in their homes.

Master He said: Small states with few people are not inferior to vast lands and many subjects. Governing by the Dao/Way means even smallness can illuminate greatness.
*Shí-bǎi* refers to communal tools—according to a Western Han decree prohibiting officials’ residences from storing *shí* (什, ten) items, Preface Master Yan (颜师古) noted: *"Five men form a *wǔ* (伍), ten form a *shí*—sharing tools, hence called *shí-wǔ* equipment, or *shí* goods, like today’s military units where ten form a team, sharing supplies and logistics."* Ten make a *shí*, a hundred make a *bǎi*—calculable numbers. If the people have *shí-bǎi* tools yet do not use them, there is no need for travel or storage, sparing labor and taxes—this is the ideal of *"no affairs where there are none."*

States, whether small or vast in Heaven and Earth, thrive with non-action and falter with interference. If the people once fled lightly for their lives, now that they cherish life, none would stray—who would compel them?

Master Wu said: Ten make a *shí*, a hundred make a *bǎi*—*shí-bǎi* tools are heavy implements shared by many. *"Unused"* means no scheming or greed, so heavy tools serve no purpose. *"Valuing death"* means treating life as precious and nurturing it. *"Not wandering far"* means living and dying in the same place, never relocating. Laozi sought to reverse the decline of the Zhou, restoring the simplicity of antiquity. Since large states with many people are hard to govern, he spoke modestly: if even a small state with few people could be governed thus, the people would have no desire for the outside, content within.

Though there are boats and carriages, they are not used. Though there are armor and weapons, they are not displayed. The people return to using knotted cords for record-keeping.

Their food is sweet, their clothing fine, their dwellings secure, their customs joyful.
Neighboring states are close enough to hear the sounds of roosters and dogs, yet the people die of old age without visiting one another.

Master Lin said: Though there are boats and carriages, they are not used for distant journeys seeking profit. Though there are armor and weapons, they are not displayed for reliance on force to win. Abandoning written contracts, they return to using knotted cords—reverting to primordial simplicity. Sweet food, fine clothing, secure homes, and joyful customs: neighboring states are near, roosters crow and dogs bark, yet the people die of old age without traveling—each self-sufficient, seeking nothing.

Master He said: Boats and carriages serve desires—now that the people have few desires, what need is there to ride them? Armor and weapons are used for conflict—now that the people have no conflicts, why display them? Knotted cords were used before writing—now to revive them is to return to ancient purity. Sweet food and fine clothing come from tilling and weaving—no hunger, no cold. Secure homes and joyful customs: their movements are slow, their awareness serene, content and at ease. Neighboring states are close, roosters crow and dogs bark, yet none go nor come—seeking nothing from the world, they age and die. This flourishing of ancient customs comes from the ancient sage’s governance.

Master Wu said: This speaks of having myriad tools yet not using them. Boats, carriages, armor, and weapons—none are things a single person can employ alone; these are what is meant by "myriad tools." To have nothing to ride upon, nothing to display—this means they are not used. Without need for travel, there is no use for boats and carriages; without strife, there is no use for armor and weapons. When the people are pure and their affairs simple, even the ancient system of knotted cords could be revived. Though writing replaced knotted cords, it too would not be used.

Not only are myriad tools unused, but the food they eat is deemed sweet, the clothing they wear deemed fine—self-sufficient, nurturing life with what they have. This is what is meant by valuing death. They find contentment in their dwellings and joy in their customs—this is to say they do not migrate far. Those who value death will age and die in the place of their birth; who would lightly abandon it for distant lands?

When the people cherish their native soil, even neighboring states—visible to the eye, their roosters crowing and dogs barking audible to the ear—though so near, none go to visit, let alone migrate far.

**Chapter 81**
*"True words are not beautiful; beautiful words are not true.
The virtuous do not argue; those who argue are not virtuous.
The wise are not learned; the learned are not wise."*

Master Lü said: Words of truth are plain and unadorned, like water—water is bland yet enduring. What is not beautiful is because its essence is bitter. Beautiful words are sweet and nourishing, pleasing to the ear, like fine wine—yet wine’s sweetness fades quickly. What is untrue is hollow beneath its ornamentation. Hence, *"True words are not beautiful; beautiful words are not true."*

Those who are truly virtuous value action over eloquence, holding to simplicity. Those skilled in argument prize persuasion, entangled in right and wrong, yet lacking true understanding. Hence, *"The virtuous do not argue; those who argue are not virtuous."*

Those who grasp the Dao understand principles and penetrate to their roots, seizing the essential—this is to be unified in one, completing all things. Why seek breadth? The broadly learned are versed in things, immersed in affairs, their substance obscured by ornamentation, their minds drowned in multiplicity, seeking only what is right—yet unaware that excess leads to confusion. Hence, *"The wise are not learned; the learned are not wise."*

Master Lin said: True words lack adornment; what is adorned is not true. *The Zhuangzi* conceals itself in splendor, taking broad knowledge as skill, boasting of it—this is not the way of the wise.

Master Li said: *"True words are not beautiful"*—loyal words are harsh to hear. *"Beautiful words are not true"*—flattering speech is deceitful. True words are plain and unadorned; false words are embellished. Those who truly follow the Dao make no distinctions, hence do not argue. Those skilled in argument are the cause of strife.
